# The Rooms We Borrow

# Chapter 1

The auditorium was cold enough to raise a rash of gooseflesh along Tiana's forearms, which would have been a mercy if the rest of her had not been sweating through the lining of her dress. Graduation always pretended to be about youth and futures and a clean ceremonial crossing. In practice it was also about evidence: who came for you, who had flowers, and who knew how to dress for a room like this without looking as if they had spent the bus ride praying nobody would stare too long.

She sat in the third row of graduates with the cap pins biting her scalp and three gold cords warming the back of her neck. The folding chair dug a ridge into her thigh. On the other side of the aisle a girl from student government kept smoothing the skirt under her gown as if the fabric could still matter now that everybody was dressed in the same blue polyester tent. Up in the stands, families leaned over the rail with phones already out. Men wore jackets the color of sand. Women wore church hats and white linen and perfume that had made it from parking lot to bleacher without dying in the heat. Little cousins swung their legs and dropped program booklets to the floor.

Four seats in the family section, two rows up from the center aisle, stayed empty.

Tiana looked at them, then looked away. She had folded the programs on those seats into sharp rectangles so the usher would know somebody was coming. Now the paper just looked stranded, four white flags of a claim nobody had shown up to defend.

Her phone hummed once against her thigh.

`On d way. Traffic dumb.` from Delroy.

Nothing from Marcia was worse. Delroy could text anything and mean none of it. Marcia only texted when she was forced into a machine she did not trust.

Onstage the principal adjusted the microphone and smiled the wide exhausted smile of a man who had been saying the same good-luck sentences all week. His face gleamed under the lights. Beside the podium stood the guidance counselor, a narrow woman in a mint suit whose left sneaker gave a tiny rubber squeak every third step because the sole had come half loose. She held a milk crate full of scholarship packets and donor envelopes. She kept knocking the stack square with the side of her hand, not from nerves exactly but from habit, as if every piece of paper in a school had to be made to behave before it could do damage.

Tiana had marked the program at home. Her own name appeared four times: English Department Award, Social Studies Medal, Principal's Scholar, state university scholarship recognition. She had pretended the black pen circles were just organization, not appetite. If the family had gotten there on time they would have seen it when they opened the booklet. They had not gotten there on time. So the wanting stayed private for another ten minutes.

The principal began the dean's roll call. The gym smelled like hot extension cords and old varnish and the sweet damp of too many people pressed into one room. Somewhere behind the bleachers a baby started crying with the offended, insulted howl of a creature brought against its will into fluorescent ceremony. Everybody laughed politely. Tiana did not. Her eyes kept lifting to the side doors.

She knew the script already. If Delroy came in first he would enter like he had been invited to improve the room. If Marcia came in first she would have apology on her face before she even found the seats, though she had probably taken two buses from Coral Gables and changed shoes in the back of one. Micah, if he had been dragged along at all, would perform indifference so hard it circled into theater.

Her row stood for the processional of honors graduates. Caps shifted. Tassels brushed cheeks. Tiana moved when the others moved, walked when the row in front of her walked, smiled when the photographer stationed by the fake ficus tree lifted his hand. She knew how to perform competence even when nobody had given her enough room to make it comfortable.

When the vice principal said her name the first time, the room offered a clean clap and nothing else.

Tiana Campbell, English Department Award.

She crossed the stage with the small fixed smile she had practiced in mirrors and dark bus windows. The English teacher waiting by the certificate table wore her reading glasses on a bead chain and smelled faintly of lemon oil, the way old classrooms smelled after somebody had tried to scrub ambition into the desks. "I told you Baldwin would take you somewhere," she whispered as she handed over the cream paper.

Tiana smiled harder. "Thank you, Ms. Prado."

Offstage again, she sat, set the certificate on her lap, and kept her face still while the applause drained away. Ms. Prado had meant well. Teachers always thought they were giving you a ladder when sometimes they were only giving you a prettier name for distance.

The doors at the back of the gym banged open during the science awards.

Sound arrived before Tiana turned. Delroy's stage whisper carried like a church tambourine. "Excuse me, excuse me, this section? No, man, I've got it." Metal seats knocked. Somebody muttered. A few heads twisted in a single, practiced motion that told Tiana they did not usually have to wonder which family the disruption belonged to.

Her family came down the bleacher steps in a compressed, embarrassed cluster. Marcia first this time, one hand on the rail, one hand clutching the strap of a black purse so hard the tendons stood out in the back of her wrist. She had changed out of her work shoes but not out of the day; the lilac blouse she saved for church had a damp crescent between her shoulder blades, and her lipstick had worn off in the center of her mouth. Delroy followed in dark slacks and the white button-down he called his good shirt, though the cuffs had gone shiny with years and one collar point never sat right. Micah came last, lanky and irritated in a tie he had plainly knotted from YouTube and already loosened, his dress shoes unlaced because they were blistering him and he did not care who knew it.

Tiana felt the heat rise from her collar all the way to her scalp.

She also felt relief, which was the insulting part.

Delroy spotted her immediately and lifted one hand in triumph. It was not a discreet wave. It was an announcement. His grin flashed so fast and bright she nearly laughed, stupidly, before the woman in the pearl drop earrings in front of him turned, saw the whole family at once, and performed a tiny startled smile that might have been kindness and might have been social mercy. Tiana hated her for it and hated herself more for noticing.

The principal kept talking through the interruption. Metal seats finished clanging. Programs rose again. Heads turned back toward the stage by courtesy rather than grace. Marcia found Tiana's eyes across the distance and pressed her lips together in a look that said later, later, I'm sorry, I came.

The vice principal reached the social studies medal, then the principal's scholar recognition, then the state scholarship acknowledgments in quick succession, and Tiana went up and back and up and back until the carpet runner on the stage felt familiar under her heels. By the third trip Delroy had lost any respect for auditorium scale.

"That's my daughter," he said, not shouting exactly, but not far enough from it to save her.

Several people laughed. One of the donor women near the front smiled toward the bleachers. Micah, seeing the whole thing could not be undone, leaned into it with the ease of a boy who always preferred the wound he chose himself. He put two fingers in his mouth and whistled once, sharp and indecently loud. An usher half rose. Delroy clapped Micah on the shoulder, proud of a crime he had not committed. Marcia closed her eyes long enough to swallow it, opened them, and started clapping anyway.

Tiana accepted the scholarship certificate from the guidance counselor and shook hands with a man from the local alumni association whose watch looked more expensive than her mother's monthly electric bill. The envelope had the University of Florida logo in blue and orange on the corner. It should have felt like a door opening. In her hand it felt only heavier than paper had any right to be.

Back in her seat, she could hear the family now that they were in place.

"You missed the first one," Micah whispered, still breathing a little hard from the bleacher steps.

"We missed two," Marcia said under her breath. "If you had put your shoes on when I told you to, maybe we would have missed only one."

"Nobody was looking at my shoes."

"They're now."

Delroy leaned forward between them. "Look how she's collecting them, though. Like she owns the place."

Tiana kept her eyes on the stage. Pride from him always came wrapped in performance, but it still reached her, and that reach was part of what made him dangerous. He could make love feel like a crowd had witnessed it.

When commencement finally ended and the gym broke into scraping chairs and shouting and flashes of phone light, Tiana tried to move fast, the way she always moved after events like this, before people could braid congratulations to pity. Families flooded the floor. A woman with a camera bag shouted for her nephew to stop blinking. Somebody's grandmother in a yellow suit sat down right there in the aisle and fanned herself with the program because her feet had swollen past patience. Teachers got trapped in circles of gratitude. The air-conditioning lost the fight it had been pretending to win all evening.

"Graduate!" Delroy spread his arms as if he needed a larger body in order to contain her success. He kissed Tiana's forehead, leaving a trace of aftershave and sweat. "Come, let me see all of them."

She handed him the certificates because refusing would have been ruder than the room could afford. He fanned them out badly, mixing the pages, reading half the headings aloud and half inventing the rest.

"English, scholarship, principal scholar. You hear that?" He pitched the last line toward nobody and everybody. "Principal scholar. She was too bright for this house from the beginning."

"Don't say that in front of the child like it's a compliment," Marcia said. But she was smiling when she took the papers from him and stacked them properly. Her hands were callused and careful. She read every line. She always did.

Micah had found the basket of butter mints from the donor table and was emptying them into his pocket with a thief's solemn concentration. "If she gets one more certificate they have to let us park in Coral Gables."

"You don't drive," Tiana said.

"That's why I said us."

She laughed before she could stop herself. Micah grinned with the raw pleasure of somebody who had successfully dragged his sister down from her ledge, however briefly. He had a blister peeled open at the back of his heel. He kept lifting one foot and then the other, pretending it was dance rather than pain.

The guidance counselor appeared at Tiana's elbow carrying the UF packet and a clipboard. Up close, the mint suit had a loose thread at one cuff. "There you are. I was trying not to lose you in the stampede."

"Sorry." Tiana straightened without thinking. Around school adults who could alter her future, her spine always seemed to know its job before the rest of her did.

"Don't apologize for being popular on your own graduation day." The counselor smiled at the family and then at the packet. "This is the housing and final aid packet from Gainesville. Your spot is provisionally held, but the $225 dorm deposit has to be paid by August first or the reservation releases to the next student. Do not miss that date."

She tapped the orange card visible through the top of the folder. Two hundred and twenty-five dollars. August first landed in Tiana like something dropped into water, the ripple running wider than the room.

Marcia shifted closer. "What is that?"

The counselor, helpful and brisk, turned toward her. "Housing confirmation. The university just needs the $225 in on time and the file should keep moving."

Marcia's fingers stalled on the packet edge.

Tiana took it before the woman could say anything else, folding the orange card out of view. "It's fine. It's just the dorm paperwork."

"Just?" Micah said. "Listen to rich girl."

"Shut up."

The counselor had already moved on to another graduate, her left sneaker squeaking away through the crowd. Delroy touched the Gators logo with one finger and whistled low, softer than Micah's earlier blast, almost reverent. "University of Florida. You hear that, Marcia?"

Marcia heard everything; that had never been the issue. Her eyes rested on the edge of the packet a beat too long, then lifted to Tiana's face. There was pride there, yes. There was also the first click of arithmetic beginning behind it.

"We'll take a photo first," she said. "Then we can talk at home."

Tiana's stomach tightened. She hated that her first response to we can talk at home was not warmth but inventory. Home meant numbers. Home meant what things cost after people stopped congratulating you.

They took pictures under the painted school seal. Delroy insisted on one with all the certificates visible. Micah kept making ugly faces at the last second until Marcia pinched the soft skin under his arm hard enough to correct his spirit. An assistant principal with a comb-over and nicotine-colored fingers held Delroy's phone and took six crooked shots, muttering "One more" every time Delroy demanded better lighting from a building that had no interest in flattering poor people.

In the fifth photo Tiana looked happy enough to fool almost anybody. Her cap sat straight. Her smile did not show the clench behind it. Marcia stood beside her with one hand flattened against the small of her back, not possessive, not even especially tender, just steadying. Delroy leaned in too close, proud as brass. Micah, finally cooperating, held the bundle of certificates like he was the bodyguard of paper.

For a moment Tiana let herself stand inside that version of the family, loud and late and underprepared and hers.

Then she felt the stiff corner of the dorm packet against her palm again.

On the walk to the bus stop, she slipped it behind the certificates so her mother would not keep seeing the orange card.

***

By the time they reached Little River the night had thickened into its usual wet grip. Even the dark felt humid. Music leaked from a car idling too long at the corner. A rooster on the next block, confused by streetlight, offered one rusty half-crow and then thought better of it. Tiana carried the certificate bundle and the packet and her cap. Delroy carried nothing except his running commentary about how half the graduates on that stage had not looked half as ready for the world as his daughter.

"You see the one with the red shoes?" he said. "That boy looked frightened of a stapler."

"Everybody looks frightened of something," Marcia said.

"Not my girl."

Tiana almost answered. She did not.

Their path to the apartment ran along the side of the decaying stucco house the landlord rented out front. The side yard smelled of hot dirt, old frying oil, and the sour wetness from the garbage bins. Gravel shifted under their shoes. The motion light over the converted garage clicked on a second after they turned the corner.

Then Tiana saw the paper on the door.

It was stapled crooked to their door at eye level, a hard square of municipal red against the peeling cream paint. It was not pink or orange. It was red like a hand where somebody had been struck. Black block letters stamped across the top under a city seal. There was a case number and a folio number and the kind of language that did not need a raised voice because it had already decided who was smaller.

She stopped so abruptly Micah bumped into her shoulder.

"What now?" he said, and then saw it. "Damn."

Delroy stepped ahead of them. "What's this foolishness?"

He reached to tear it down.

Marcia caught his wrist before his fingers closed on the page. "Leave it."

"It's on my door."

"That's exactly why you leave it."

The motion light hummed overhead. In the front house a television blared laughter at a joke nobody near the Campbells could hear. Mosquitoes found the exposed skin at Tiana's ankles. She read what she could without moving closer.

NOTICE OF VIOLATION.

UNPERMITTED OCCUPANCY.

COMPLY OR VACATE WITHIN NINETY DAYS.

Below that, smaller paragraphs. References to unsafe electrical modifications, illegal conversion, inspection authority, daily fines. There was a blank line for owner of record and another for responsible party. No line at all for the people sleeping inside.

Delroy laughed once. It came out wrong, too dry. "This is for the landlord. They post paper on everything in this city."

Marcia moved close enough to the page that her shadow swallowed half of it. She read with her head tilted slightly, the way she read medicine labels and bus schedules and all the other small print that could punish you for assuming. Tiana watched her mouth move once over the phrases without sound. Her mother's hand still held Delroy's wrist, not tightly now, just there.

"It says the unit has to be vacated if the violation is not corrected in ninety days," Marcia said.

"By the owner."

"You think code enforcement is going to argue semantics with us while we stand here with our pots and shoes?"

"Marcia."

"I'm reading what's on the page."

That shut him up for three seconds. Tiana counted them because the quiet felt expensive.

Micah leaned sideways to read around Marcia's shoulder. "Unpermitted occupancy sounds like they caught us existing wrong."

Nobody answered. He tried a grin anyway. "Maybe we can tell them we're very permitted. Spiritually."

Tiana almost told him to stop. Then she looked at his face under the hard porch light and saw he had gone shiny with sweat above the lip. He was frightened. The joke was what he was using instead of saying that.

Marcia released Delroy's wrist and flattened the page against the door where the humidity had started to curl one corner. "Ninety days from when?"

Tiana found the date stamped near the bottom. "Three days ago."

Marcia inhaled through her nose. "So less than ninety."

"Still plenty of time," Delroy said quickly. "You know how these people do things. They send paper first. The landlord pays somebody. Maybe he fixes one wire, maybe he knows a man at the office. It doesn't mean men are coming tomorrow with a truck."

"It doesn't mean they aren't," Marcia said.

He shook his head, annoyed already, which was always how his fear dressed itself before it admitted anything. "You go straight to funeral."

"And you go straight to fairy tale."

The words did not come out loud, but they landed loud enough.

Tiana looked back at the red notice. The city seal sat at the top like a judge's face. All that crisp authority for a room that could barely fit their lives now, let alone after it had been converted into evidence. She thought of the auditorium, the donor envelopes, the guidance counselor's squeaking sneaker, the orange card inside the packet pressed under her arm. Two pieces of paper in one night, both demanding money by a date certain if she wanted the future to remain available.

Delroy yanked his keys from his pocket and shoved the door open. Warm air rolled out, thick with bleach, fried onion, and the mildew they kept trying to outrun with open windows and luck. The apartment was one long compromise: bed in one corner, Micah's mattress folded against the wall, kitchenette shoved under hanging cabinets that never closed properly, bathroom door swollen from old damp. A standing fan turned its head with a click at each end of the arc, distributing heat rather than fighting it.

On the table by the hot plate sat the little grocery-store cake Marcia had bought that morning and hidden in the fridge behind eggs. Congratulations Graduate, the icing read in blue loops already sweating at the edges. Tiana had forgotten it existed. One corner had started to slump in the heat, and the cake looked too small for the table, too hopeful beside the red notice in Marcia's hand.

Marcia came in behind them with the red notice still in her hand. She set her purse down, found the one working pen by the sugar jar, and pulled over the back of an electric bill envelope to write on. "We need the landlord's number."

"I've got it."

"Then call him."

"Tonight?"

Marcia looked up. "You think tomorrow will make this prettier?"

Delroy spread his hands. "It's after ten. If you call a man at this hour over a city paper, all you do is let him know you're panicking."

"I'm not panicking." Her voice stayed level, which landed harder than a shout would have. "I'm counting. Ninety days means June, July, August. Tiana needs school money in August. Rent is eleven-fifty before that. If this fool puts us out to save himself fines, we don't have someplace ready to go. So I'm counting."

Nobody reached for the cake. Micah stopped chewing at the sink. Delroy's jaw worked once before he looked back down at the notice. Tiana kept her face neutral. She did not know what school money Marcia was imagining. Books, probably. Supplies. Some basic mother estimate of the cost of a child leaving. Not the actual number: $225 due by August first, an amount small enough to sound manageable and vicious enough to turn ugly once it had to be produced all at once in a house already chasing $1,150 rent.

Micah had already cut himself a corner of the cake with a butter knife and was eating it standing by the sink. Blue frosting ringed his thumb. "If we get evicted before I finish summer, do I still have to go to school or is homelessness like a district exemption?"

"Don't play with your mouth," Marcia said automatically.

"I'm trying to innovate."

"Take a plate if you're going to eat."

He did not. He licked the frosting off his thumb and reached for another bite, hunger making him bold enough to risk her irritation. Tiana was suddenly grateful for the stupidity of cake, for the ordinary appetite of it. If Micah could still steal dessert while a red notice sat on the table, the world had not ended in front of her. It had only tilted.

Delroy took the paper from Marcia and scanned it more aggressively than carefully, as if force of personality could extract a different meaning from printed text. "This says owner of record right here. It isn't talking to us."

"Where do you think we live?" Marcia asked.

"On his property."

"In the unit they say is illegal."

"Half Miami is illegal if you ask the right office."

"And half Miami can still lose a room."

He slapped the notice lightly against his palm. "You want me to do what? Throw everybody into the street tonight because the city found red paper?"

"I want you to stop talking as if refusing to understand something is the same as fixing it."

That hit clean. Tiana heard it. Delroy heard it too. His mouth tightened at one corner.

"I understand enough," he said. "I understand that a man who can't pay code fines fast wants tenants to panic faster so he can clear the place cheap. I understand that landlords lie. I understand that city offices move like old people taking stairs. We have time."

"Time for what?"

"For me to talk to him. For him to sort it out."

"Sort it out with what money?"

Silence came again, and this time it was packed.

Delroy put the notice down. "You don't know what he has."

Marcia let out a small sound through her teeth, not quite a laugh. "No. And you don't know either. That's why I'm making a list."

She started writing on the envelope in her small exact print.

Landlord.

Church room?

Agency extra shifts.

Ask Sonia about cousin in Broward.

Boxes.

Tiana watched the list arrive. This was what her mother did when fear threatened to go soft around the edges and become useless. She gave it nouns. She gave it errands. She pinned it to paper before it could spread.

"Mummy," Tiana said, keeping her voice light, "it's one notice. Maybe Daddy is right and it takes a while."

Marcia did not look up. "Maybe. That'd be nice." She underlined Boxes twice and kept writing.

Delroy went to the sink, ran water into a glass, and drank it too fast. A little escaped the corner of his mouth and ran into his beard. He wiped it with the back of his hand, irritated at the evidence of thirst. "I'm calling the landlord in the morning. Early. Before he starts ducking me."

"Call code enforcement too," Marcia said.

"For what? So they can put our names on another list?"

"We're already on one."

Micah pointed his butter knife at the red notice. "Maybe Tiana can write them a letter. She wins all these essay prizes. She can say, Dear City of Miami, congratulations, you have discovered poor people."

"Give me that knife before you stab the air to death," Marcia said.

He handed it over and finally took a plate. His face had gone slack with teenage boredom, but Tiana knew him too well. He was watching every sentence. He always acted like a room had to earn his attention right before proving he had not missed anything.

The fan clicked. Outside, somebody revved a motorcycle up the block and let it idle back down. The front-house television audience laughed again through the wall. Their lives kept happening, which felt like one more insult.

Marcia looked up from the envelope. "Tiana, what exactly did the counselor give you?"

The question came so neatly it almost sounded harmless.

Tiana felt the packet against her ribs as if it had grown corners sharper than cardboard. "Just housing stuff. Checklists."

"Anything with a date on it?"

Delroy turned at that. Not sharply. Just enough.

She made herself shrug. "It's summer. Everything has a date on it."

Marcia held her eyes for a beat longer than comfort allowed. Then she nodded once. "Bring it to me tomorrow. We'll see what is what."

Tomorrow bought her one night.

"Okay," Tiana said.

Micah, because he could smell pressure like rain, broke in before the silence could harden. "If she gets a dorm, can I have her side of the room? I can put up that poster you hate."

"You don't have a side of the room now," Tiana said.

"Exactly. I'm a man with a vision."

Delroy snorted despite himself. Marcia did not smile, but some of the set left her mouth. Micah saw that too and pushed his luck, lifting his paper plate like a toast. "To higher education and illegal occupancy."

"Boy," Marcia said.

"I'm saying there is range in this family."

This time even Tiana laughed, unwillingly, the sound leaving her before she could inspect it. It lasted only a second. The notice remained on the table. The list remained on the envelope. Nothing had changed. Micah grinned over his paper plate with frosting bright on his lip. Delroy let out a breath that almost passed for a laugh. Marcia did not tell either of them to stop. Cruelty and humor were still using the same butter knife.

Later, after the cake had been eaten in uneven strips and Micah had folded his mattress out and Delroy had made two more calls that went to voicemail, Tiana took her folder into the bathroom and locked the swollen door.

The overhead bulb buzzed. The mirror above the sink showed her graduation face half dismantled: lipstick faded, edges of makeup broken apart by sweat, one side of the cap-flattened hair already rebelling. She set the folder on the closed toilet lid and opened it carefully, as if the paperwork might startle and fly.

The certificates sat on top, then the commencement program, then a flyer for Bright Futures renewal instructions, and then the university packet.

Inside was the orange housing card, bright as hazard tape.

Housing reservation confirmation. Advance rent payment of $225 due by August 1 to secure dorm assignment.

The line below smiled while it threatened.

Failure to submit payment by the deadline may result in cancellation of your space.

Tiana read it twice. By the second pass her thumbnail had bent the card's corner white. Nobody in Gainesville was going to call and ask what happened. They would clear the bed, hand the key to somebody whose money had arrived on time, and keep moving.

Tiana ran her thumb over the embossed edge of the school seal until it left a pale dent in her skin. In the other room she could hear her parents through the thin wall, their voices low because Micah was trying to sleep. Low did not mean gentle.

"You should've told me earlier the landlord was getting inspected," Marcia said.

"I didn't know it reached this stage."

"That's not what I asked."

"I heard things. People hear things all the time."

"And you did nothing with them."

"I'm doing something now."

"Now the red paper is on my door. On the same door I bleach every Sunday because the paint comes off on your hand. In the same room I make hold all of us."

Two hundred and twenty-five dollars was less than a quarter of the $1,150 rent and still the kind of number that could split the apartment open, because it had to arrive clean and on time. Tiana slid the orange card back into its sleeve. She could walk out there now. She could put it on the table beside the violation notice and let both truths sit in the same heat. Her August would meet their ninety days. Her mother's pen would start scratching again. Her father would rub both hands over his face and call it manageable. Micah would lie still on the mattress, pretending not to listen.

She closed the packet instead.

The night's arithmetic was ugly. They could survive celebration or dread. They could not lay both deadlines open on the same table and still breathe.

She tucked the university papers beneath the certificates, then under her immunization records and test scores, deeper in the folder where official things went to wait their turn to ruin her. When she came out, the apartment was mostly dark. Delroy sat on the edge of the bed with his elbows on his knees, looking at nothing. Marcia lay on top of the covers in her slip and bra with the red notice on the pillow beside her, reading it again as if a second pass might reveal a mercy clause hidden in the code sections. Micah had one arm flung over his face and one bare foot sticking off the mattress, a butter-mint wrapper still trapped against his wrist.

Tiana slid her folder under the bed.

The fan turned. The notice rustled once under Marcia's hand, then again as she folded it smaller. Somewhere outside, a bus sighed at the corner and pulled away.

In the dark, her mother began counting days under her breath, not for drama, just to make the number real enough to work on.

Tiana kept August silent.


# Chapter 2

The light-bill money rode under Marcia's left bra strap all through service, the folded cash warming against her skin until the paper edges softened and curled. By the second hymn it felt less like money than like a hot leaf pasted to her body, something fragile that would tear if she breathed wrong. She kept one hand flat on her pocketbook anyway. The pocketbook held the tithe envelope, two safety pins, a pack of peppermint she had meant to ration for Micah, and the red copy of the code notice she had slept beside, folded so small it could almost pass for something manageable.

Church was full because people liked to look at a child who had done well and then look at the people who had made her. Tiana sat straight in the pew with her graduation hair wrapped down into a neat puff, the gold clip at one side catching the weak light from the high windows. The girl had changed out of the dress from Friday night, but the room was still treating her as if she were carrying a crown in her lap. Women leaned in to squeeze her hand. Men nodded at Delroy with that public pride fathers got to wear cheaply. Micah sat on the aisle in a shirt that had belonged to a cousin first and then somebody from church before it reached him. He kept two fingers hooked under the collar and tugged when he thought Marcia was not looking.

She was looking at all of them. Church made that seem holy. Everybody else was looking too.

The sermon was from Matthew, the bit about wise men building on rock and foolish men on sand. Pastor Green had a gift for making old warnings sound as if God had typed them that morning and slipped them under your own door. Every time he said house, Marcia saw the warped side wall of the garage apartment and the thin crack above the bathroom window that Delroy kept calling cosmetic. Every time he said storm, she thought of mildew under the kitchenette sink and the way rain already needled in under the back threshold if the wind leaned hard enough from the east. She kept her face arranged. Beside her, Delroy said amen in his big public voice, warm enough to fill three seats.

His hand rested on Micah's shoulder for part of the sermon, loose, fatherly, easy. Anybody watching would have seen a family steadied by faith and a daughter's good news. Marcia had dressed them for exactly that: her own blue church suit let out half an inch at the hip but still hanging clean, Delroy in the tan shirt that made him look less worn than he was, Tiana pressed and composed, Micah scrubbed hard enough behind the ears to count as obedient. There was no place in the arrangement for the city's red warning or for the electric bill due Tuesday or for the fact that the money for one of those things was now warming in her bra because she had not yet decided whether she was going to save the room they lived in or the plan that might get them out of it.

At offering time, Sister Paulette slipped down the aisle with the basket balanced on one palm. She always wore too-bright coral lipstick and always carried a funeral-home fan in her Bible, no matter whose face was printed on it. Today the fan was tucked into the top of her blouse, the dead man peeking over lace. Her left heel clicked late because the rubber cap had worn off again. When she paused at Marcia's row, she smiled at Tiana first.

"Scholar girl," she whispered. "You look nice, eh. Gainesville soon make you too fine for we."

Tiana gave the church smile she had learned young, soft enough to read as humble and careful enough not to promise anything. "Morning, Sister Paulette."

"Morning? It nearly afternoon." Paulette laughed and pinched Tiana's forearm, affectionate and measuring in the same touch. Her eyes flicked once over Marcia's suit, Delroy's collar, Micah's shoes. "Marcia, you must feel proud."

"I do," Marcia said. "By God's grace."

That answer was smooth enough to satisfy church. It did not tell Paulette that pride felt expensive in Marcia's body, that it scraped against fear until both things turned raw.

The basket came. Marcia put in the tithe envelope. The other money stayed where it was, damp and private.

By the time service ended, her slip was sticking to her back. The sanctuary had old air-conditioning units mounted high on the wall, but they mostly circulated the perfume, starch, and body heat. Outside, the church steps flashed white under noon sun. Women opened umbrellas against weather that was not rain. Men loosened ties. Children kicked off church shoes and got rebuked before the shoes could scuff too far under parked cars.

The congratulations came in a line.

Brother Henry, who smelled permanently of hair tonic and gasoline, shook Delroy's hand and said, "You have to start charging the neighborhood now. Two smart pickney in one house, that is luxury."

Delroy laughed. "Then I need luxury money, boss. You know where them keeping that?"

Everybody laughed because Delroy was good at turning lack into performance. He could say broke and make it sound like a temporary misunderstanding.

Sister Paulette came back with a square of bun wrapped in napkin for Tiana and one for Micah. "For the scholar and the one coming after," she said.

Micah took his and muttered, "Yes, ma'am."

"You hear how nice him can sound?" Paulette said to Marcia. "At church only."

"That's already a miracle," Marcia said, and the women around her made pleased church noises, because a mother joking lightly at her son was the sort of thing everybody knew how to hold.

Then Paulette lowered her voice half an inch, which in church was the same as stepping closer. "And Delroy finding enough work?"

There it was. Never naked, never rude. Always wrapped in concern the way cheap cake got wrapped in extra icing.

Marcia kept her smile. "He's managing."

Paulette nodded as if she had been given a number she could use later. "God makes way."

God, Marcia thought, and women who remembered everybody else's business better than their own blood pressure medicine schedule.

She had just taken a paper cup of warm fruit punch when she saw Yvette under the almond tree beside the fellowship hall. Yvette was still in her usher blouse, white enough to show the shadow of her bra through the back. She had kicked off her low heels and stood in nude stockings on the packed dirt, one foot flexed up on its toes to ease a cramp. Her patent Bible case lay open on the folding chair beside her. Inside it, tucked under a worn New Testament and a packet of receipts, was the black-and-white composition notebook that held the hand. Yvette's thumbnail was split down the center from work and had a strip of clear tape over it to keep it from snagging on fabric. She was writing with the slow, careful script of a woman who knew numbers had to stay legible longer than feelings did.

Marcia felt the money against her skin as if it had suddenly turned sharp.

Yvette looked up and gave her the small nod women used when money had to pass between them without announcing itself to every cousin and prayer partner within earshot. "You have a minute?"

Marcia handed her fruit punch to Micah. "Hold this."

"If you come back with no punch, I'm drinking all of it," Micah said.

He took the cup with his left hand. His right stayed knotted inside the bun napkin, and when he answered he kept the right corner of his mouth behind the rim as if the punch needed guarding already. Marcia caught a puffiness there, some small darkening she could not name from three feet away. He wiped at it fast and gave her a look that said not here.

"Try it."

He smiled out of the unhurt side of his face, half challenge, half plea. It made him look younger than the trouble waiting for him.

Marcia crossed the yard. From the church steps she could hear Delroy still talking, still charming. Brother Henry said something too low to catch. Delroy answered louder, "That city thing? Man, city always sends red print first. They trust a stamp more than a person. Monday I go there and settle it."

Marcia kept walking. If she stopped now she would have had to choose between humiliating him in public and swallowing him again.

Under the almond tree, Yvette fanned herself with a supermarket circular. The circular made a tired slapping sound in the air. Close up, Marcia could see the salt line dried at Yvette's hairline where her wig had shifted in the heat. There were flour smudges on one cuff. Yvette had probably baked before dawn again, sweet bread for the church table, one more thing turned into money or obligation.

"Rough morning?" Yvette asked.

"Morning is morning."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer I have."

Yvette's mouth twitched. She was one of the few women in church who could hear a dry line and not take offense. "You good for today?"

Marcia could have said no. She could have said the light bill was due, the city had stapled a countdown to her door, Micah had grown a restlessness in his face she did not trust, Delroy was talking like a man with two plans when he had not yet shown her one. She could have said her chest felt packed with crumpled demands, every one of them asking for cash. But saying no to the hand did not only mean saying no to one Sunday. It meant asking to be carried by the circle instead of carrying her place in it. It meant watching the July draw move away from her. It meant admitting, to Yvette and then to everybody else soon enough, that the one rescue she could still picture had started slipping.

So she hooked two fingers inside the top of her blouse, reached under the bra strap, and pulled the folded money free. The cash came out damp from her skin. That shamed her more than the amount.

For one stupid second she thought of the refrigerator light. She thought of the fan on the milk crate beside their bed. She thought of chicken in the freezer softening into pink water if Florida Power decided Tuesday meant Tuesday. Then she put the bills in Yvette's hand anyway.

Yvette did not count in front of her. Poor women knew better than to total up a sister's dignity in public. She slipped the money into an envelope already marked with Marcia's name, licked her thumb without thinking, then remembered the taped nail and winced.

"You're still good for July," Yvette said.

"I need to be."

"Everybody needs to be. That's why the hand exists."

The notebook opened on Yvette's knee. Marcia watched her own name receive its small mark of continuation. The scratch of pen on cheap paper gave her a mean kind of relief. Nothing had improved. The apartment was still on the city's clock. The electric bill was still unpaid. But the line beside her name was not blank, and blankness was how disaster announced itself first.

Yvette tore off Marcia's receipt from the envelope flap and handed it over. "Keep this. And tell Tiana from me, don't let them big college people bleach all the sense out of her."

"I'll tell her."

Yvette looked past Marcia toward the steps where Tiana stood surrounded by women. Her face softened. "That girl looked good Friday. Real good. I cried and it wasn't even my child. My husband laughed at me."

A whole life sat inside that sentence, one Marcia had no room to ask about. Most of them lived like that, skimming the top of one another's days and hoping the weight underneath held.

"You all right?" Yvette asked then, quieter.

The church yard shimmered. Delroy was still making people laugh. Micah had taken a bite of bun and kept the napkin near his mouth as if he were guarding crumbs, pretending not to eavesdrop on adult trouble. Tiana stood with her shoulders polite and tired while three women advised her about dormitories they had never seen.

Marcia tucked the receipt into her purse. "I'll be all right when July comes."

Yvette gave a short laugh that was almost a cough. "Then let July hurry."

Marcia turned back toward her family. She could feel the absence of the light-bill money now more clearly than she had felt its presence. It had left a damp patch cooling against her skin.

On the steps, Delroy reached for her elbow as if nothing in the world were fraying. "You ready, baby? Brother Henry saying his cousin know somebody down by code enforcement. All these people love to make thing sound worse than it's."

Marcia looked at his face, still open from performance. Men liked the feeling of help before help had happened.

"Then let your somebody call before they tack another threat to my door," she said.

His smile held, but it tightened at the edges. "I said I'm dealing with it."

"You said."

Micah made a little sound into his cup, a swallowed laugh or warning, and Tiana looked down at her shoes. Marcia adjusted her hat, turned her church face back on, and walked toward the car. If she started with Delroy there, on church steps with half the congregation breathing close, the whole yard would eat it before she got to the point. She had already spent the electric money. She did not have extra strength to spend too.

The garage apartment hit them with trapped heat when she opened the door. Church sweetness dropped off all four of them at once. Shoes went one way, pocketbooks another. Delroy unbuttoned his collar before the door even latched. Micah headed for the bathroom with his phone speaker leaking a thin mechanical beat. Tiana set down the foil pan of leftover bun Sister Paulette had pressed on them and lifted the edge of the clear vinyl table cover to slide something underneath.

Marcia saw the flash of a heavy white page, UNIVERSITY OF FLORIDA in blue across the top, then it disappeared under old takeout menus, a church anniversary flyer, and a faded photograph of Micah at eight missing his two front teeth.

"What are you hiding there?" Delroy asked lightly.

Tiana did not look up. "Nothing. Just school stuff."

"More scholarship thing?"

"Mostly deadlines."

"Deadlines love you bad."

"Apparently."

Delroy laughed and went to wash his face. He did not hear the way her answer stopped short. Marcia did. She heard more than she had strength to sort. She also meant to stop Micah when he came back out and ask what had happened to the side of his mouth. The phone got there first.

Her phone buzzed from inside the purse. She almost let it keep buzzing. Then she looked at the screen and saw the local school number with a little triangle beside it, voicemail already waiting.

The timestamp read Friday, 4:52 p.m.

While Tiana had been walking across a stage, while Delroy had been waving like the whole county was there to see his daughter, and while Marcia had been trying to hold her family inside one good public room, the school had already been reaching for her other child.

She pressed play.

A woman's voice filled the kitchenette, nasal and tired, the kind of voice that sounded as if it had spent years being patient with other people's children and had finally gone thin from the labor of it. "Mrs. Campbell, this is Dean Robles calling regarding an altercation involving your son, Micah Campbell, on school grounds this afternoon. At this time he is not to return to campus until a parent conference has been held. We have scheduled that meeting for Monday morning at 8:15 with administration and the school resource officer. Please contact me if that time creates a hardship."

The phrase sat there: creates a hardship.

As if hardship were something a person penciled in when convenient.

Micah came out of the bathroom in one of his old school polos, wiping his face on the hem. The right side of his lower lip had gone thick, and the first two knuckles on his right hand were skinned raw where the water had taken off the dried blood but not the sting. One button was missing from the shirt. He saw the phone in Marcia's hand and stopped. Tiana's eyes lifted once from the table.

"Micah," Marcia said. "Come here."

"Ma, I was going to tell you."

"No. You were going to let Monday arrive and then watch me get surprised in clean clothes."

He leaned against the fridge instead of coming closer. He had grown into that habit lately, making furniture carry the disrespect so he could pretend his own body was innocent. "It wasn't even like that."

"Then tell me what it was like."

Delroy came back from the sink rubbing water through his hair. "What happen?"

Marcia held up the phone. "Your son is not to return to school till conference."

Delroy's face shifted, not yet alarm, still looking for the joke that might reduce the thing. "For what?"

"That's what he's about to explain."

Micah rolled his eyes toward the ceiling. "It was a fight."

"I gathered that much from the word altercation. Use your mouth properly now."

He pushed off the fridge and paced two steps in the narrow space between table and stove. "It wasn't no random fight. Them boys kept on."

"Which boys?"

"Brandon and that next one with him, Luis."

"Kept on with what?" Marcia asked.

Micah shrugged, too hard. "Same stupidness."

"Do not give me same stupidness. Give me the thing itself."

He glanced toward Tiana, then away. That embarrassed look, quick and hot, frightened her more than the anger. Micah liked anger. He knew how to wear it. Shame moved on him like clothes borrowed from somebody bigger.

"They were doing Daddy's voice," he said finally. "Since Friday morning. The whole long vowel thing. 'Miiiicah, come ya, bwoy.' That foolishness."

Delroy snorted once. "So because they copy accent, you mash up the place?"

Micah swung toward him. "It wasn't just that."

"Then say the rest," Marcia said.

He dragged a hand over his face. "At lunch Brandon asked if I had goat in the container. I told him suck himself. He kept on. Said Tiana only got all that clap because white people like island sob stories. Then he asked if Mummy still cleaned up after everybody at school same way she clean old people house."

Dean Robles's voicemail kept leaking tinny out of the phone in Marcia's palm. The refrigerator motor kicked hard under it, rattling the salt shaker by the stove. Delroy stopped with his fork halfway up. Tiana looked down so fast her lashes hit her cheek. The bathroom tap kept ticking because Delroy never shut anything fully. Marcia felt the insult arrive there in her own kitchen, laid down between the plates where her children could watch it sit.

"And then?" she said.

Micah's mouth flattened. "Then he did the accent again. Said, 'Go back a yard with all that black ass island shit.' Loud. In front of everybody."

Tiana sucked air through her teeth before she could stop herself.

Micah heard it and got meaner to cover what was in him. "So, yeah. I hit him. First one caught his mouth. Second one landed wrong and split my hand when Coach Everett yanked me off him."

"With your hand?" Marcia asked.

"What else I was supposed to use, a hymn book?" He held up the fist like evidence. The knuckles were puffing around the cuts already, shiny and ugly. "Then Dean Robles marched me to the office, took my backpack, sat me under that freezing vent with a melting ice pack, and wrote emergency removal on a yellow slip like I brought a damn gun to school."

Delroy laughed once, quick and wrong.

Marcia turned on him. "Do not laugh."

"I'm not laughing at you."

"Then do not laugh at all."

Micah crossed his arms. "He deserved it."

"That's not the same thing as it helping you."

"So I should have just stood there?"

"I didn't say that."

"You're about to."

Marcia put the phone down very carefully on the counter. If she slammed it she would only add one more broken thing to the room. "Listen to me good. Tomorrow morning that school does not care how clever Brandon was with his mouth. They care whose fist they can write down. They care whose name fits the box they've already built. You already know whose name that will be."

Micah's face twitched. She had him there. The trouble with boys his age was that truth reached them and pride reached them faster.

"I'm not saying sorry like I started it," he said.

"I didn't say say sorry. I said use sense."

"Same thing."

"No," Tiana said quietly from the table. "It's not."

He rounded on her at once because sisters were safer to fight than systems. "Nobody asked you."

"Then stop talking like you want to get suspended forever."

"I said nobody asked you."

Marcia saw Tiana's fingers flatten the vinyl until her knuckles went pale. The girl's face stayed cool. Lately Marcia kept clocking how much silence Tiana could turn into a weapon.

Delroy leaned against the sink. "If the boy call him out his name like that, he's not wrong to defend himself."

"And when the school stamps ten days on him, you defending that too?" Marcia asked.

"You don't know it's ten days."

"I know schools. I know black boys. I know what an office can do."

Micah laughed without humor. "Yeah. An office. A stamp. Everybody in this house get to lose their mind over whatever desk wants next."

Nobody answered that because the room was already crowded with deadlines.

Marcia picked up the phone again and replayed the message, this time with the volume lower, as if smaller sound might make the thing less public. The words administration and resource officer landed harder on second hearing. Resource officer meant the cop with the tucked-in stomach and the gun on campus like children were a border to patrol.

She would have to miss part of Monday morning with Celia. She would have to call Elena and ask, which meant Elena's pause, Elena's little courteous silence while class considered inconvenience. The school had already reached into tomorrow's wages and squeezed.

"You'll come with me at 8:15," she said to Micah. "You won't go in there smiling. You won't go in there slouching. You'll answer what they ask and you'll leave your little smart mouth outside the office door."

"What if they ask if he called me that?"

"Then you tell them."

"And if they say I should have told a teacher?"

"Then you tell them he had time to speak because the teachers let him."

"And if they still side with him?"

Marcia looked at him. She wanted to lie. Mothers earned that temptation every day. But Micah had already reached the age where a child could tell when comfort was being served in place of fact.

"Then we deal with what they do," she said. "We don't hand them extra."

He dropped into the chair by the door with a thud that shook the table hard enough to rattle the forks. Tiana steadied her water glass without looking up.

She said nothing. One emergency at a time was not wisdom. It was simply all poor rooms could hold.

She untied her head scarf, folded it once, and went to the sink. "Move. I've got to season chicken."

Nobody moved fast enough, which was another Sunday truth.

She made the Sunday pot because not making it would have meant the bad news had won before evening. The chicken was not the nice kind, only backs and a few thick neck pieces from the freezer, but if you browned them hard with enough onion, enough thyme, enough scallion crushed under the knife handle, they could smell like care from the front door. She washed the pieces in lime and salt till the water ran cloudy, then clear, then cloudy again because cheap meat always carried some hidden extra with it. She rubbed in browning, allspice, garlic, black pepper, and the last spoon of Grace jerk paste scraped from the jar with her finger.

Heat climbed the kitchen walls. The apartment always seemed to shrink when a proper meal was underway, as if the steam took up square footage. Tiana shredded cabbage at the counter, long controlled strokes, not wasting movement. Micah peeled potatoes with his left hand because the right one had swollen across the knuckles, and every few strokes he flexed it like he could bully the pain smaller. He still took meat out with the skin. Marcia slapped his wrist once and made him start smaller. Delroy sat at the table with the newspaper sports section spread before him, though she had never in her life seen him read the sports page straight through without stopping to talk at somebody.

"Read that paper one more time and maybe money will fall out," she said.

He looked up, wounded and amused together. "I'm staying out your way."

"You're in my way while sitting down."

"That's marriage. You agreed."

Micah barked a laugh. Tiana tried not to.

For a few minutes the room found its old rhythm. Oil hissed. Onion went sweet. Delroy told a story about one deacon nodding off during sermon and waking only to shout amen three seconds late, which made Micah nearly drop the potato peeler. Tiana said Pastor Green had probably noticed and upgraded the man's salvation points accordingly. Even Marcia laughed once when Micah imitated Sister Paulette's click-heel walk and said her rebukes arrived with built-in percussion.

The laughter hurt and helped. A room could bruise you and keep you alive in the same hour.

She got the rice and peas going in the big pot, thin coconut milk stretched with water because the can had to last. The red peas rolled, starchy and softening. She sliced a sweet pepper fine enough that every spoonful might catch some color even if no one got much meat. On a good Sunday, she would have fried plantain too. Today there were two left on the counter, both going black at the edges. She cut them lengthwise so each person could have a few slices and no one would have to say out loud that there was not enough for greed.

Delroy folded the paper and tapped the clear table cover with one finger. "What is this under here now?"

Marcia did not turn. She knew the answer before Tiana spoke.

"Just my stuff," Tiana said.

"What stuff?"

"School things. Thank-you letters. End-of-year stuff."

"You're telling me school still isn't done with you after graduation?"

Tiana gave a little shrug. "Apparently they like deadlines as much as you do stories."

"That's rude," Delroy said, not sounding offended.

"It's accurate," Micah muttered.

Delroy grinned at him. "See there. Brotherly support."

Marcia set the spoon down harder than she meant to. "Support would look good tomorrow morning too."

The spoon struck enamel with a bright hard click. Tiana's knife stalled in the cabbage. Micah dropped the peeler into the sink hard enough to ring the basin. "I said I'm going."

"You also said you should have been allowed to punch the boy."

"Because I should have."

"This again," Tiana said.

"You hush," Micah shot back. "You're over there with your own problem and want to manage me."

Tiana's head lifted slowly. "I'm not managing you. I'm saying maybe don't make things worse on purpose."

"For who?"

"For yourself, maybe."

He laughed at that, a fifteen-year-old laugh sharpened on humiliation. "That's cute. Everybody wants me to act right so the room stays pretty."

"The room was never pretty," Marcia said. "It still has to function."

Delroy held both hands up, palms out. "All right. Enough. The boy is heated."

"He's not soup. Stop talking like temperature is the problem."

That earned her a look from Delroy, half annoyance, half admiration. He liked when she was sharp until the sharpness pointed directly at him.

She plated the food. She laid down rice first, then spread the gravy wider than the meat, then added cabbage, then two thin strips of plantain each. She watched all three children, yes, even Tiana though the girl would have hated the word child applied to her now. Micah went for the biggest neck piece on instinct. Tiana's hand paused over the ladle and withdrew, letting him take it. Delroy said grace too quickly, as if God might accept speed for sincerity.

For two minutes there was only eating. The first sounds were always practical: spoon on plate, a little hiss when somebody took in food too hot, Micah sucking gravy from his teeth, Delroy asking for pepper sauce before he had swallowed. Marcia loved them most at tables and least at tables. Hunger made everybody honest. So did the distribution of portions.

"This is good," Tiana said after a while.

"It's food," Marcia said.

"It's good food."

"She wants extra plantain," Micah said.

"I want you to mind your plate."

"That means yes," Delroy said.

"That means if any of you keep talking, next Sunday all I make is boiled green banana and attitude."

"You cook attitude excellent already," Delroy said.

Micah laughed with his mouth full. Even Tiana smiled into her plate. Marcia let the line stand because she had meant it and because the room needed one more minute of being itself.

Then Delroy wiped his fingers on the napkin and said, "Tomorrow, after the school thing, I'll go by code. Brother Henry's cousin says the man who actually signs off doesn't settle in till ten. If I rush down there at eight, all I get is some bored clerk telling me come back when the right desk opens."

Marcia looked up. "After?"

"Yes, after. I told you."

"You told me on church step. That's not the same as doing it."

"I can't be in two place one time."

"I've got to lose Monday morning to that school already, come back to work, and Tuesday's light bill still wants paying. Don't hand me after like it's some clean piece of day."

Micah looked from one parent to the other the way people watched tennis in waiting rooms. Tiana kept her eyes on her rice, but Marcia saw her listening so hard the muscles in her hand had gone careful around the fork.

Delroy sat back. "I'm not telling you fairy tale. I'm telling you how those offices run. One woman stamps it, the next man sends you upstairs, then somebody tells you the case man's on lunch. If Henry's cousin can point me straight, I skip the foolish laps. That's me trying to help, not dodge."

Marcia laughed then, one ugly short sound. "While you explain the office, I'm here stretching gravy wider than meat. The red slip is still in my purse. Micah still has conference at eight-fifteen. I need the thing done before they tack another warning to my door."

"You hear red slip and start talking like the bulldozer's already backing down the alley."

"Because ninety days gets small fast in one room."

"Half the city gets tagged like that. You go in, you talk, you fix what they want."

"Then let half the city come split that room with us and see how automatic it feels."

Micah looked down fast. The apartment had only one room to begin with. Everybody in it knew exactly what room she meant: the garage, the thin walls, the air mattress wheezing at dawn, Tiana's corner with the milk crate desk and the school certificates clipped to the string lights. Ordinary did not need explaining in houses like theirs. Threat did not either.

Delroy pushed his plate a little away. His shame always showed up like this, not in silence but in extra argument. "You talk like I enjoy this. You know how many times a man can go downtown, sweat through his shirt, smile at people who don't even look up, and still come home with the same damn runaround? I'm trying not to waste a run. I'm trying not to let them make me look stupid."

"Then don't waste it. But don't feed me charm while I slice plantain into slivers and pretend that counts as enough."

Micah sucked his teeth. "Can we not do this over chicken?"

"You can get up from the table if you don't like dinner talk," Marcia said.

"And go where?" he asked before he could stop himself.

Nobody answered because there was nowhere in the apartment for a dramatic exit. The bathroom was too small to slam impressively, and outside the back door the mosquitoes would have made mockery of any teenager's suffering in under a minute.

Tiana reached for her water glass and nudged the table with her wrist. Under the clear plastic, the corner of one white page showed again. This time Marcia could read the top line. It said UF Housing Contract.

The words sat under old menus and a church flyer advertising Youth Explosion Night, hidden so plainly they almost dared somebody to notice. Tiana saw Marcia looking and put her glass down over the line, casual as a card trick.

Delroy did not see any of it. He was still looking at Marcia as if the argument between them had begun in that moment and not years ago in smaller, cheaper versions.

"So what you want tonight, then?" he asked. "You want me to ride down there, find the building dark, and argue with one security guard and a mop bucket? Tomorrow's when the office opens. That's the plain truth."

"What I want is for you to talk like Tuesday is already in this room, because it is. That light bill's still unpaid. That city warning's still in my purse. I don't have room left for tomorrow-talk."

"And I want every stamped thing in this house to stop walking in here bigger than me. School calling, city threat, light bill, now whatever Ti's hiding under the table. A man gets tired of ink running his life."

Micah snorted. "That ship sail, Daddy."

"You're very funny for somebody with conference in the morning," Marcia said.

"I'm serious."

"That's the problem," Tiana said softly.

Everybody heard her. Her voice had slipped out before she dressed it up. Delroy turned to her.

"What problem?" he asked.

Tiana took one second too long to answer. Marcia could nearly see the girl sorting which truth she could afford. "That we keep calling it tomorrow like that changes the date. No, that's not even what I mean. I mean the words change, but it's always later. After the meeting. After somebody calls somebody. After the money shows up. Then the real deadline gets here and everybody acts like it came early."

Delroy blinked at her, then reached for a joke and missed. "Well. University words now."

"It's not university words."

"Then what is it?"

She glanced down at the glass over the contract and then back at her plate. "Nothing. Eat your food."

Marcia felt the sentence like a small hard seed dropped into her palm. The word was as false as Micah's same stupidness earlier, only polished smoother.

Micah shoved back from the table. "I'm done."

"Bring your plate to the sink," Marcia said automatically.

He did, though the plate hit the stainless steel too hard and sent gravy freckles up the backsplash. "If that dean asks me what happened, I'm saying exactly what he said. Every word."

"Good," Marcia said.

He looked surprised.

"Say it. Let them hear their school through your mouth. But after that, stop performing. Do you understand me?"

He rolled his jaw once. "Yeah."

"And if they ask whether you hit him?"

"I'm not lying."

"Good again."

That confused him more than anger would have. He picked up the dish towel and wiped his fingers on it despite knowing better. Marcia slapped at his wrist with the serving spoon. He yelped, laughed, and finally carried himself to the bathroom, muttering about tyranny.

The bathroom door knocked shut behind him. Tiana set the cups inside one another with more care than they needed. Delroy lowered his head and cleaned his plate in three big bites, either proving a point or hiding inside appetite. "Food nice," he said, standing.

"It was nice before the speech too," Marcia said.

"Everything with you is speech when I open my mouth."

"Then try opening your hands."

He looked at her long enough that Tiana busied herself stacking cups. In another room, in a bigger life, that look might have held flirtation or memory. Here it mostly held history worn down to friction.

"I'm going to smoke outside," he said.

"Take the red slip with you and smoke that too."

His nostrils flared. Then, because Tiana was there and because even now he preferred exit to humiliation, he gave a little shrug and went out the back door.

The screen door gave a soft slap. The mosquitoes got first right of him.

Tiana stayed at the table. She gathered the cups, then stopped with one hand under the clear vinyl. Marcia pretended to rinse plates while watching in the dark shine of the kitchen window. Tiana slid the housing contract deeper beneath the plastic, tucked now between the church flyer and an old Chinese takeout menu from the year before, the corner aligned so neatly it nearly disappeared.

"You know I saw it," Marcia said.

Tiana's shoulders went up, then down. "It's the housing contract."

"I know what it's called. What does it want?"

Water dripped from the plate rack onto the counter in slow fat clicks. Tiana kept her hand under the vinyl another second, as if the contract might still hide if she pressed hard enough. "Signed and back with the deposit by August first," she said. "If they don't get both, they release the room. Somebody else gets the bed and the move-in slot."

Marcia turned off the tap. "And you were going to leave that under a takeout menu till August walked in?"

Tiana looked at her then, not cool anymore, just tired and cornered. "I was going to wait till there was money attached to the sentence."

Marcia dried her hands on the dish towel slowly. July rose in her head at once, the partner payout she had already spent Tuesday's current to protect. She kept that thought in her own mouth. "Don't decide by yourself what I can carry."

"I'm not protecting you," Tiana said. "I'm trying not to watch Daddy promise one more fix with both hands empty."

For one quick second Marcia saw the child still in the girl, the one who used to bring permission slips three days early and stand by the sink until they were signed. Then the look closed. "I'll wash the pot later," Tiana said.

"No. Go rest your head."

"I'm not tired."

"You look tired."

Tiana almost smiled. "That's rude."

"Everything true sounds rude these days."

The girl took that and went to the bed area, carrying the cups. Marcia heard the rustle of sheets, the click of Tiana's phone charger, Micah already back there arguing with somebody through a headset in a voice softer than the one he used on family. Delroy's lighter scratched outside, then caught.

Marcia opened the top cupboard and took down her own partner notebook, the small one where she kept the envelope stubs and dates because trust was easier to survive when duplicated. Yvette's torn receipt went inside. The June line was marked paid. The handwriting looked thin and stubborn on the page.

Then she opened the drawer by the stove and took out the light bill.

The red band across the top shouted in a font that was almost cheerful. She laid it beside the notebook on the table. Under the clear plastic, the edge of Tiana's housing contract still showed if a person knew where to look. There were three claims there now: Yvette's thin promise of July, the light company's red threat of Tuesday, and August first waiting under Tiana's name. They all wanted the same hand, the same wallet, the same woman to answer first.

Outside, Delroy laughed at something on his phone, some man joke floating up warm through mosquito hum. From the bed area, Micah said, "Nah, he wish," and Tiana told him to shut up if he was going to keep lying that loudly. Their voices tangled, sharp and ordinary. It was family noise. It was life noise. It made the room sound held together when it was mostly held up.

Marcia stacked the notebook on top of the bill, then stood there with both palms resting on the covers as if she could press the whole stack into mercy. The electric money was already in Yvette's hand. The city's red slip still sat folded in her purse. Micah's meeting was at 8:15. Tiana had hidden an August first deposit line under the table where people usually stored coupons and church flyers.

Sunday had not stayed ordinary. It had only dressed itself that way long enough to get through the door.

She slid the notebook back into the cupboard and left the light bill flat in the middle of the table where Delroy would have to step around it if he slipped out before dawn. July had to come now. She had paid for that future with Tuesday's current, and there was no getting either one back by wishing.


# Chapter 3

By five-thirty the lot had already started smelling like diesel, bitter coffee in Styrofoam, and the minty grease men rubbed into knees they swore were fine. Pickup headlights cut across the chain-link fence and moved on. The men left standing in the wash of those lights all made the same small adjustment when a truck slowed: shoulders back, stomachs in, faces arranged into something between readiness and indifference. Nobody wanted to look desperate. Desperation scared off contractors almost as fast as age.

Delroy stood near the front without crowding it. He had put on the faded gray T-shirt from an old Coral Gables remodel because a shirt with a company name on it still suggested invoices, regular jobs, a version of himself who had keys to houses with alarm systems and women who handed him cold water in real glasses. The logo had cracked into pale islands across his chest, but it was better than showing up in a plain white tee like he had come only to carry other men's shit out to a truck.

His canvas tool bag sat by his sneaker, not too full. If it was heavy enough to advertise skill, it would pull on the bad shoulder and betray him before anybody asked a question. He had rubbed blue gel into the joint in the dark kitchen before leaving the house. The smell still rose off him each time he shifted. It wasn't pain yet, not exactly. It was the warning before pain, the hinge talking.

He had stepped around the light bill Marcia left flat on the table and gone out while she and the children were still asleep, though Marcia never really slept once worry took hold of a room. She lay still and listened to what money was not doing. The red notice on the garage door had changed the air inside the place. It had made the bathroom seem smaller, the hot plate meaner, the patched cracks in the wall less like poor people's decoration and more like evidence. Three months sounded like time if you said it fast. It sounded like already too late if you stood in the lot before sunrise with fifty men and watched how easily a city decided which backs were still worth renting.

A thin Honduran kid in a Dolphins cap kept calling home between trucks, checking on a daughter with fever. Each time voicemail picked up, he kissed his teeth and tucked the phone back into the waistband of his jeans. Two Haitian men stood beside Delroy in paint-splashed boots, one of them broad as a refrigerator, the other narrow and elegant, with a funeral-home tie folded neatly inside his back pocket so it would not catch dust before church that night. A Black American boy, maybe twenty, maybe younger, had his barber cape still looped from his backpack zipper and was trying to laugh his way through the wait, making jokes about the mosquitoes getting first pick today. Men answered when they felt like it. Mostly they watched the road.

A pool-service van nosed to the curb and stayed idling. The passenger leaned out, looked over the crowd once, and said, "Need two Spanish. Fast-fast." The broad Haitian stepped forward anyway. The passenger shook his head and crooked two fingers at the Honduran kid and another narrow boy behind him. "No, no. Not that Spanish. Lady hears island, she makes that blank face. I need house Spanish. Easy." When Delroy moved, the man cut a palm at him. "Not you either, bossman. Too much gray. She'll start hollering insurance." The van rolled off with both boys hanging off the back doors, already hired and already cut down to fit the job.

Nobody in the lot ever posted the rules. "Need two Spanish" meant the Honduran kid and not the Haitian. "Homeowner sees gray" meant Delroy and every other man the city had already started pricing out.

The first truck rolled in at quarter to six, a white F-150 with ladders clattering in the rack. The driver, a heavyset man with dried white caulk trapped in the hair on his forearms, leaned out the window and shouted in Spanish for six roofers. He scanned the yard as he spoke, already counting bodies before his words finished. Men moved toward him in a burst. Delroy stepped up too. Roof work was not his best thing, but he had done worse for cash.

"Boss, I can do it," he said. "You need somebody who knows how not to mash up a whole house."

The man gave him half a look. His eyes flicked to Delroy's face, then lower, to the tool bag, then to the shoulder Delroy had tried not to hold too carefully. "Need roof legs," he said. "Young. Spanish. No lessons."

"Spanish can learn from me in five minutes."

That got a snort from one of the boys climbing into the truck bed, but the driver had already pointed past Delroy. "You. You. Blue shirt. Move."

The ladder rack rattled off with six younger men and the Honduran kid hanging from the side rail, still dialing home one-handed as the truck pulled away. Delroy kept smiling half a second too long after it was gone. Then he bent, lifted his tool bag, and set it back down in a different spot as if location had been the problem.

By six-fifteen the lot grew louder. Men who had not been chosen yet started talking more because silence was beginning to feel superstitious. A man selling coffee from a cooler walked the fence line and shouted, "One dollar, black as your landlord heart." Delroy bought none. He had eight dollars in his wallet and needed those eight to remain whole until they had company.

Another contractor skimmed by in a red pickup calling landscaping at eighty cash. Delroy gave him half a step anyway. The driver laughed through the open window. "Nah, boss. Need yard dogs. Bent all day, no bitching." Delroy let that one go. Eighty for full sun and somebody else's bougainvillea thorns was insult money, and he was not yet ready to admit insult money had become real money. Three men climbed in anyway, their gloves already on. The truck rolled off with sod trembling in the bed, and Delroy hated how fast his pride started looking like a luxury item.

He shifted his weight and the shoulder answered now, a hot wire from collarbone to elbow. He rolled it once. Beside him, the elegant Haitian man with the folded tie said, not looking over, "Don't do that. They see."

Delroy glanced at him. Up close the man had a crescent scar in one eyebrow and smelled faintly of the clove gum he kept tucked in his cheek. "What, Miami's hiring by x-ray now?"

"Miami's hiring by any excuse."

The man got chosen ten minutes later by a stucco crew that needed two laborers and one man who could keep the younger fools from falling off a scaffold. He climbed in without gloating, only touched two fingers to the tie in his pocket like he was checking it was still there. Delroy hated him a little for leaving clean.

By seven the sun had burned the softness off the morning. Heat pooled in the blacktop and came back up through shoe soles. A box truck stopped. The man in it had a lizard tattoo crawling out of his collar and a cigarette burned almost to the filter between two fingers stained yellow. "House cleanout," he said. "Need strong back. No talking. Seventy-five and lunch if the owner don't act funny."

"How heavy?" somebody called.

"Heavy enough."

Delroy stepped forward before he had decided to. "Strong back right here."

The tattooed man looked at him, then at the kid with the barber cape, then back again. "You serious with that shoulder, old head?"

For one stupid second Delroy nearly asked how the man knew. Then he remembered himself rubbing it. He dropped the arm. "Shoulder's fine."

"Then quit petting it. I need a man who'll drag a fridge and shut up after."

The kid with the cape laughed so hard he had to slap his own thigh. Not cruel, exactly. Young. The worst kind of witness. Delroy felt the humiliation like heat under his shirt.

"Because I don't waste motion," he said.

"I need waste motion today." The driver jerked his chin at the younger boy. "You. And you. And the little joker too. Come."

The kid hopped into the truck bed and, to his credit, did not meet Delroy's eyes again. That almost made it worse. Delroy had become something polite people avoided looking at directly.

He kept his mouth in its half-smile and let the morning keep insulting him. Jobs like his were supposed to come later, by phone, through men who knew his work and did not need a cattle call to find it. That had been true in other years. The lot kept correcting him. Worse, the shoulder had stopped being private. When the next truck slowed, two men nearer the curb glanced at his arm before they looked at his face.

There was a time he would not have stood here at all. There had been kitchens in Kendall where he laid tile straight as sheet music. There had been bathrooms in the Gables where women twice Marcia's age called him Mr. Campbell and asked his opinion on marble, as if he had not learned most of what he knew on jobs where nobody wore a permit on their belt. He had spent good years making rich people believe their houses reflected taste instead of money. That work had thinned. Shoulder first, then age, then the little humiliations that multiplied until a man found himself in a lot with his jokes growing stale in his own mouth.

A dark blue van pulled in close to the curb. The passenger window came down. A pale Cuban woman in mirrored sunglasses leaned across the driver and spoke rapid Spanish. Delroy caught enough to understand they wanted two men to move appliances and one to patch drywall after. Then she switched to English for the numbers. "Condo job. Pay goes Friday when management clears it. Need one drywall man who can wait."

"Drywall I can do," he said. "Texture too."
