She looked straight at him this time. "Can you float till Friday, yes or no?"

The five dollars left in his wallet suddenly felt as loud as coins in a collection plate. "Today is Monday."

"Then you're not my drywall man," she said. "Management pays when management pays. I need somebody who can absorb that."

The driver barked a laugh. The woman did not. She only pointed to a younger man in a clean polo who said yes before the last word left her mouth. The van eased off with him and two movers inside. Delroy watched his own reflection shrink in the mirrored lens of her sunglasses and then disappear.

Even the work that fit his hands needed a man who could afford to wait to be paid.

By eight there were only leftovers in the lot now, the men nobody had matched themselves to yet, or who had once been matched and no longer were. An old Bahamian with swollen ankles. A wiry Jamaican who smelled of Wray and had started cursing every departing truck in detail. A Venezuelan father with a Hello Kitty lunchbox that had belonged, judging by the glitter sticker half-peeled off the side, to a child who had once cared very much what color a thing was. Delroy knew some of them by face, some by first name, some only by the shape of their defeats. They drifted apart as the morning advanced, ashamed of being seen with the last of the stock.

The Wray-smelling Jamaican came over and squinted at Delroy's shirt. "Did you ever do that Gables condo on Alhambra? Gray tile? Lady had a dog that looked richer than me?"

"Maybe," Delroy said.

"I thought I knew your face. Claude asked for you a couple weeks back."

Delroy kept his expression loose. "Claude asks for everybody when he's short a body."

"True enough." The man scratched under his hard hat, which he wore though nobody had hired him. "He said demo's coming."

"Demo's always coming."

The man gave a shrug that said suit yourself and moved on. Delroy kept his face easy, but the name tightened his throat. Last year he had watched Claude stop a plasterer outside the Chevron on Sixty-Second and ask, soft as prayer, whether Friday still meant Friday. Three laborers by the ice machine had heard every word while pretending to study lottery tickets. By Monday everybody in the lot knew the plasterer had taken advance money and gone missing for a weekend. Claude never had to shout if shame could travel for him. Delroy had managed, so far, not to become one of Claude's examples.

Nine o'clock came. The coffee seller was packing his empty cooler. The shade beside the fence had shrunk to a mean strip. Delroy stood a little longer because leaving too early felt like confessing the day was over, and he was not ready to tell the truth to himself before he told it nowhere else. Finally he bent for the tool bag again. This time the shoulder caught hard enough to blur one edge of his vision.

He straightened slowly and pretended he had only meant to retie his lace.

The coffee seller snapped his cooler shut. A truck on the avenue grumbled past without turning in. The canvas handle sweated in Delroy's palm while he stood there pretending for nobody.

***

He slung the bag across his left side and started walking toward the strip mall on the corner, where a Jamaican bakery sat between a tax office and a store that sold used phone cases nobody trusted. His stomach had begun to gnaw. He had left the house on one cup of sweet coffee and a lie to himself about how the day would go.

The bakery's air-conditioner had lost the war years ago, but stepping inside still felt like being let slightly off the hook. The place smelled of patties, frying oil, old flour, and the bleach somebody had sloshed across the tile after opening. A radio behind the counter muttered morning news over static. Delroy ordered one beef patty and a small cola, then did the math as the girl at the register rang him up. Three dollars gone. Five left.

He carried the plate outside and found Claude Brown sitting at one of the metal tables under the awning, legs stretched, reading glasses hanging on a cord against his chest though he was not reading anything. Claude drank from a pink insulated water bottle printed with fading cartoon mermaids. He had once announced to a whole domino table that cold water did not turn a man soft because the cup was girlish, and the phrase had been stupid enough to keep surviving in Delroy's memory. Claude looked up from his phone and grinned as if Delroy had arrived for an appointment.

"There he is," Claude said. "King of the no-call, no-show himself."

Delroy did not break stride until he reached the table. "If I was a no-show, why are you seeing me?"

"Because Miami humiliates a man in public if you wait long enough."

Claude laughed at his own line, a dry smoker's bark that ended in a wheeze he never treated with the inhaler clipped to his shirt pocket. He wore a lime-green fishing shirt too clean for the lot and jeans dusted red at the cuffs from some site he had visited without working. Claude liked staying one degree removed from grime. It let him talk like management without anybody forgetting he came from the same bottom shelf.

Delroy sat because standing would have made the encounter look accidental. "You came here to feed me or just admire the view?"

"Both, maybe. I saw you over there trying to sell luxury labor to men shopping clearance."

Delroy bit into the patty before answering. Pepper and hot grease hit his tongue. It almost helped. "What I've got, I've got. I can't turn twenty-five because a fool with a pickup wants cheap knees."

"No," Claude said. "But you can stop advertising that you know what a fair price looks like. That's what scares them."

The line landed too close to the truth. Delroy washed the bite down with warm cola. "You're always this cheerful before noon?"

"Only when the sermon already writes itself."

Claude tilted his phone so Delroy could see a list of names in the notes app, some checked off, some not. Delroy saw his own first name halfway down, unmarked. Claude locked the screen again before Delroy could ask why looking at that list felt so much like standing on a hook.

"What do you want?" Delroy said.

"See, that's why I like you. Other men round the thing with pride until hunger starts speaking plain. You at least come to the gate faster."

"I'm sitting at the table because the shade is free."

"Not free. Nothing here is free. Not even shade. Shade belongs to the bakery owner and the city permit and the man who poured this concrete." Claude leaned back and sipped from the mermaid bottle. "But all right. Since we're telling stories, let me tell one. I got a motel gut coming later in the summer, maybe end of July if the paperwork finally coughs itself into action. Airport side. Interior demo only. Drywall, cabinets, old bathroom tile, ugly carpet, all the sins. Two long days if the inspector doesn't start acting holy."

Delroy kept eating, though slower now. "And?"

"And I need bodies I don't have to chase across three neighborhoods once the dumpster lands."

"So hire bodies."

"I'm hiring bodies. I'm talking to one."

Delroy let the silence work a second. Claude watched him with amusement so patient it was almost affectionate. He said it in the old-work voice, the one that belonged to domino tables and half-finished roofs after rain. Delroy could almost smell white rum and wet plywood, and that made the hook worse.

"Demo pays trash money," Delroy said.

"This one pays better."

"Better meaning what?"

"Better meaning enough that you're still listening."

Delroy wiped a thumb across the flaky crust on his plate. His first instinct was to bargain, his second to joke, his third to pretend he had other options. Even now he could feel the old sequence lining up in him, smile first, price talk after, hand never still. "If the work is so nice, why are you scouting from a bakery table like a vulture with data?"

"Because good crews stay booked. Because my regular drywall boys don't want demo dust in their lungs. Because half the men in that lot can't show up two mornings in a row unless their mother threatens them. Pick the reason you like." Claude smiled. "And because I know you need money before your face starts telling the truth."

Delroy looked out toward the road. A bus sighed at the stop and opened its doors onto nobody. "Everybody needs money."

"Not like you."

There it was. Claude did not say it loud, but he never needed volume to make a thing public. The bakery napkin stuck to Delroy's damp thumb while the room at home came at him in flashes: Marcia at the sink before work, rinsing out the same two good food containers because buying new ones would be stupidity; Tiana crossing the room with her folder tucked under her arm like paper could force doors; the red notice nailed bright on the garage door, Micah joking about framing it so at least the government message would look like decor. None of that belonged at this table. Claude could smell household trouble the way sharks got described in bad documentaries. Delroy hated giving him anything to feed on.

"Say what you came to say, nuh," Delroy said.

Claude reached into his shirt pocket and brought out a narrow receipt book, the kind mechanics used. That more than anything made Delroy want to leave. A man ready with paper had already decided the conversation. Claude flipped to a blank slip, clicked his pen, and started writing before the terms were fully spoken.

"I can front you four hundred now," he said. "Advance against the demo. Not gift. Not friend-friend softness. Advance."

Delroy gave a short laugh. "Four hundred? You think I look that bad?"

"Worse. Four hundred is me being respectful."

Claude kept writing. He spoke as he moved the pen, each line a small nail. "When the motel job confirms, you answer on first ring. You show up wherever I say, when I say. First four hundred of your cut comes back to me before you buy a beer, before you buy a screw, before you carry home one pretty grocery bag to act like Providence visited the house. If you disappear, I don't go searching dramatic. I just remember. I let other men remember too."

He tore the pink copy free and slid the white original toward Delroy. On it he had written:

`Advance: $400`

`Worker: Delroy Campbell`

`Job: airport motel interior demo`

`Repay from first payout`

`On call when notified`

Under that was Claude's signature, wide and theatrical, and a space left blank.

Delroy stared at the paper. The itemization was almost funny. Pink carbon had smudged Claude's thumbprint near the margin. The slip looked neat enough for brake pads or an oil change, not four hundred dollars tied around a man's neck. Claude squared it to the table before nudging it closer, like straight edges could keep the stink off.

"You're carrying a receipt book now?" Delroy said. "What next, office downtown?"

"Don't mock efficiency. Efficiency is why I can help when help gets expensive."

"Help."

"Call it what soothes you. I don't care. Sign if you want the four."

Delroy did not touch the pen. "And if the job delays?"

"Then the debt delays. The debt don't vanish."

"Suppose I get something else before then."

Claude's grin widened, not kindly. "Then you better get something good enough that four hundred doesn't matter, because once my money's in your pocket, your calendar and my phone become cousins."

It should have made Delroy stand up. It should have pushed him back into the sun with his remaining five dollars and his injured dignity, sent him somewhere else. Instead he sat there with patty grease drying on his thumb while, across the street, a man dragged a split mattress to the curb and let it flop beside a busted fan. The four hundred on Claude's side of the table already had weight; Delroy could feel it in his chest before it ever touched his pocket.

Marcia's face arrived anyway, not shouting, never first. It would hold still too long. Then she would get exact. What lots did you hit. Who was hiring. Did you call Mr. Lewis from the Kendall backsplash. Is the shoulder worse. Her questions were never only questions. They came trimmed neat as bill stubs.

The red paper would still be on the door when he got home. Rent would still sit on the household like a heavy man refusing to move his feet. Micah still needed school shoes soon. Tiana would still be carrying that folder around like paperwork might get tired of humiliating her and finally behave.

His thumb pressed harder into the paper plate. Under that pressure the four hundred split itself clean: light bill first, then groceries, then one night where Marcia opened the cupboard and found something heavier than a can of tea and two dry onions.

"If I sign," Delroy said, "you don't bring my name to my front gate. Whatever's between me and you stays between me and you."

Claude leaned back and looked almost offended. "You think I like domestic theater? I hate people's children staring at me like I came to steal Christmas dinner. I come to the front gate only when a man starts acting forgetful."

"I'm not forgetful."

"You're charming. Sometimes that's the same disease."

Delroy nearly smiled. Claude saw it and pressed.

"Listen to me proper. I'm not doing you dirty. Dirty is leaving you in that lot till noon and then letting some cousin tell me later you're short on rent and too proud to say it straight. Dirty is pretending friendship means I should front cash and then beg for it back like a church sister collecting pledge. This stays clean. Terms now, peace later."

"Peace later for who?"

"For the man who shows up on demo day."

Claude tapped the pen against the receipt book. Not impatient. Certain. Delroy hated certainty in other men when his own had gone thin.

He took the pen.

The signature looked older than he felt until he saw it there. Delroy Campbell, the D tall, the y tail dragging. Claude tore off the second copy and folded the original into the receipt book. Then he reached into the cargo pocket of his jeans and pulled out four crisp hundred-dollar bills wrapped with a bank band.

That startled Delroy more than the paper had. Cash that clean meant Claude had expected this outcome before Delroy sat down.

"You're walking around with four hundred waiting on me?"

"Not waiting on you only. Waiting on whichever man lost the morning and still had enough sense left to know it."

Claude set the bills on the table but kept two fingers on them. "One more thing."

Of course there was one more thing. Delroy looked at the money and said nothing.

"You don't come to the motel trying to be foreman," Claude said. "You do the work in front of you. If I tell you to strip bathrooms, you strip bathrooms. If I tell you to load the dumpster, you load the dumpster. No speeches about how you used to lay tile for ladies with poodles. No shoulder drama unless the arm actually falls off."

"You're selling money or comedy?"

"Both. Since you enjoy comedy for free."

Claude released the bills.

Delroy put his hand over them. The paper felt cool despite the heat, smooth and almost refrigerated. The bank band pressed into his palm. Forty could go under the light bill. Rice could hit the table with a decent thump. Micah could paw through the breadbox and find more than heels. Tiana could open the cupboard and find something with weight in it. The question waiting behind all that was the same one. There was no clean story attached to these bills, and he was tired enough to take them anyway.

"All right," he said.

"All right what?"

Claude loved a witness, even when there were only flies and traffic nearby. Delroy pocketed the money before answering, forcing Claude to take the concession without ceremony. "All right, when the call comes, I answer."

"On first ring."

"If I'm in the bathroom, Claude, the second ring will still save civilization."

"You see? This is why receipts are needed."

Claude laughed again, coughed, took a hard pull from the ridiculous pink bottle, and then rubbed the center of his chest as if amused air had gone down wrong. For a second he looked his age, or older. Then the expression shut. He was back to being the man with names in his phone and work traveling toward him like weather.

"Spend it smart," he said. "Not flashy. Flashy attracts questions."

Delroy stood. "You're coaching me in deception now?"

"No. I'm respecting your marriage."

That one made Delroy look at him sharp. Claude raised both hands, innocent as a preacher in a tax scandal.

"I didn't say nothing," Claude said. "I know married men. Money appears in a house, women ask what tree bears it."

"My wife asks because she has sense."

"Exactly. So take some of that money home in pieces. Groceries. Light bill. Maybe one little thing for the boy so the story smells nicer." Claude shrugged. "Or tell the truth. Honesty is always available. Just not popular."

Delroy should have walked away then. Instead he said, "You ever tried minding your own blasted business?"

Claude grinned. "This is my business."

Outside the awning, the late morning had thickened into the kind of heat that made parked cars seem angry. Delroy slid the four bills into the side pocket of the tool bag under a tape measure and a set of dull drill bits. The bank band disappeared under steel and dust. He crossed to the family-dollar store two doors down and bought rice, a pack of toilet paper, and the cheap dish soap Marcia hated because it went watery halfway through the bottle. The white rice sack bulged against the thin plastic bag. The toilet paper made the whole load look domestic, harmless. He could set that on the table first and let the tool bag stay behind his leg.

By the time he cut down the alley behind the house, sweat had glued his shirt between his shoulder blades. The red notice still burned on the garage door. Water ran inside. Delroy hitched the tool bag farther behind his leg, balanced the rice where it would show first, and went in.

Marcia stood at the sink in work pants and a faded house T-shirt, rinsing out two plastic food containers until the lids squeaked under her thumbs. The apartment smelled of bleach, onion, and the wet concrete coolness the place kept in its floor even at noon. She looked at the groceries first, then at him.

"You found something."

"Little bit," Delroy said. He set the rice on the table like proof. "Patch job. Quick one."

"Where?"

"Hialeah."

Her eyes dropped to the tool bag. He tucked it against his calf. Marcia turned off the tap. "What kind of patch job pays for rice, toilet paper, and that nasty soap all before lunch?"

"The kind I said yes to."

He forced a laugh and pulled two twenties from his wallet, laying them beside the light bill on the table. "Put that toward FPL."

Marcia did not touch the money. "Who paid you, Delroy?"

He should have kept it simple. Instead he heard himself furnishing the lie. "Man from that Kendall backsplash finally called me. Wall crack by an exhaust fan. Ten-minute thing once I got there."

"Mr. Lewis."

Delroy hated how fast she found the name. "Yeah."

She looked at the untouched twenties, then at the bag still hidden behind his leg. "Mr. Lewis finally paid up on a Monday morning, in cash, and all you had time to buy was the soap I hate?"

"It was on the way."

"Mm."

The one syllable landed heavier than if she had flung the plate. She picked up the two twenties at last and slid them under the red-banded light bill, not because she believed him but because FPL didn't care what story money carried. Marcia going quiet was never mercy. It only meant whatever was owed would come due later. Delroy kept his hand on the tool bag and felt the other three hundred and sixty dollars sitting under the drill bits between them, already inside the house, already spoiling the room.


# Chapter 4

The dean's office was cold in the kind of mean way only school buildings managed in June, as if the air-conditioning existed less to cool anybody than to remind kids which rooms belonged to adults. Micah sat under a row of faded college pennants with his knees open and his jaw loose like none of this mattered. He kept his backpack hooked around one sneaker so the shaking in his leg did not travel into the whole chair.

Across from him, the trophy case shone with old football helmets and dust trapped behind glass. Every time the outer door opened, a rectangle of heat fell across the linoleum, then got swallowed by the office chill. Kids passed the doorway in clumps, loud and alive and headed somewhere that was not here. A couple of them looked in. Micah lifted his chin at them like he had chosen the chair and the wait and the white envelope clipped under his mother's thumb.

Marcia sat beside him in a navy blouse already darkened under the arms from the walk over. She had changed out of her church skirt and into work pants, but she still looked arranged, every button where it belonged, every edge under control. Her handbag sat upright on her lap. She held herself perfectly still, phone buried, questions spent. She had brought that silence from home and set it down between them like another folder she meant to open later.

That was worse than the talking would have been.

If she had wanted to cuss him out, he knew the shape of that weather. She would have gotten exact, then sharp, then loud enough to make the room understand she had not raised some wild little idiot for school people to pin to a bulletin board. Instead she sat with her back straight and her mouth fixed, as if whatever she needed from him would be collected later, in private, with interest.

Micah scratched at the healing split in his knuckle and stopped when she turned her head.

"Don't pick at it," she said.

Her voice was level, with no heat in it and no shelter either.

"I wasn't."

She gave him one look that said lie cleaner than that and turned back toward the closed door.

The office secretary worked behind a counter wrapped in fake wood grain, humming under her breath while she stabbed a stack of attendance printouts with a three-hole punch. The sound came down in hard little chunks, punching, lifting, punching again. Her nails were painted with tiny mangoes on a white base, bright enough to feel rude in this room. She called two students to the window, handled a pass, answered a phone, and never once looked at Micah like he was a child who had simply had a bad morning. He was already sorted, sitting there and waiting to hear the number attached to his name.

He rolled his shoulders like he was getting ready for practice, though the fight had not happened on any field where rules lived. It had happened between the science hall and the cafeteria, in that stretch where the floor always sweated and people moved slow because lunch had already started and everybody thought the day had made up its mind. The boy from world history had been on him for weeks, not every day, just enough to keep it hot.

Say something in Jamaican.

Where your boat parked, bro?

Ask your moms if she cleaned my auntie house in Gables too.

The first few times Micah had laughed because boys watched laughs closer than fists. Then the boy started doing the accent. Then he got bold enough to reach and pinch one of Micah's twists between two fingers and ask what kind of sponge he scrubbed his head with.

Micah had swung before he even felt himself decide.

He could still feel the slick snap of skin across his knuckles, the ugly satisfaction of not swallowing it this time, and the worse thing after that, teachers rushing in while the other boy shouted like he had been jumped for no reason at all. By Sunday evening the dean's voicemail had turned it into an incident. By Monday morning it was a hearing.

Marcia had listened to the message with one hand on the pot, spoon standing up in rice and peas, and said only, "You'll come with me in the morning." She had not asked whether the boy deserved it. She already knew the answer and knew it would not help.

Micah leaned back farther until the chair complained under him. "You think they gon' drag this all day?"

Marcia did not look at him. "Sit proper."

"I'm sitting."

"Don't try me in this place."

There it was, finally, a little edge. He should have felt relieved. Instead the words made the sweat gather at the base of his neck. He sat up and pressed his palms between his knees.

Outside in the hallway, a burst of laughter broke open and died. Somebody slapped a locker. A bell rang from somewhere deeper in the building, shrill and metallic, and the office swallowed that too. The secretary glanced up at the clock. Micah followed her eyes and wished he had not. They had only been there eighteen minutes.

Marcia pulled a folded tissue from her bag, blotted the shine off her upper lip, and put the tissue away so neatly it made him want to start something. She had called Miss Elena that morning, standing by the apartment sink with the faucet running to cover the conversation, and said she needed an hour, maybe two. Her voice had gone extra polite in that white-lady way it sometimes did, smooth enough to skate on. Micah had stood in the doorway tying his shoes and wanted to say he could go alone. He had not said it because even he knew that was bullshit.

The inner door opened.

The school cop stepped out first.

Officer Baines was broad through the chest and carried his radio high on his shoulder, a strip of silver unicorn glitter stuck to the side of it like one of his kids had decorated him and he had not bothered peeling it off all the way. He had a lunch cooler tucked under one arm and a damp half-moon at the collar from the heat outside. He looked at Micah, then at Marcia, then back at Micah with the calm face adults wore when they wanted a boy to do the math for himself.

Micah's skin pulled tight across his back.

The dean came behind him with a manila folder and a mug that said WORLD'S OKAYEST AUNTIE in cracked gold letters. Ms. Valdez was one of those women who always looked like she had stepped out of a car fifteen seconds ago: lipstick still there, blouse smooth, chain at her neck resting exactly where it should. She kept peppermints in a cut-glass dish on her desk for kids with shaky hands before exams, which did not make her soft. It just meant she liked order in more than one flavor.

"Mrs. Campbell," she said. "Micah. Come on in."

Micah stood too fast and banged his knee on the chair arm. The pain flashed white and stupid. He grinned like it was nothing and slung his backpack over one shoulder.

Marcia was already up, handbag in place, face arranged for company.

By the time Micah followed them through the door, his body had understood something his mouth was still refusing to say. If the cop was in the room, school had already decided what kind of story this was.

***

Ms. Valdez's office smelled like toner, peppermint, and the wet roots of the plant dying behind her file cabinet. A framed photo of two girls in matching soccer uniforms sat near the edge of her desk beside the district handbook. The girls were missing both front teeth and grinning so hard it looked painful. Micah hated that he saw them. The photo put a little family softness in the room, and he wanted the room hard.

He sat in the chair across from her desk. Marcia took the one beside him. Officer Baines stayed standing for a moment, thumbs hooked in his belt, then eased himself into the chair by the window with a little grunt like his knees were bothering him. Outside, the basketball courts wavered in the heat.

Ms. Valdez opened the folder. Paper slid against paper. "All right. We need to establish what happened Friday during fifth lunch and what needs to happen going forward."

Micah shrugged. "I already told the AP what happened."

"You're going to tell me now."

It came out of him fast because if he slowed down he would hear how bad it sounded in this room: the hallway, the comments, the hand in his hair, the swing, the other boy coming back at him, the teacher trying to get between them, the way everybody only really arrived once Micah had hit first and that became the whole movie.

He skipped the accent and let them imagine it themselves. That performance belonged to the boy, not to this room.

When he finished, Ms. Valdez tapped the corner of one witness statement against the desk until all the pages aligned.

"So to be clear," she said, "the other student made comments you found offensive."

"I didn't find them offensive. They were offensive."

Her eyes lifted. "And your response was to hit him."

"My response was telling him stop for like three weeks."

"That's not what I asked."

Officer Baines leaned back, chair creaking. "Soon as you swing, nobody in this building hears the first story, son. They hear the hit. You could've walked away before that."

Micah turned his head. "Yeah? That's what y'all say every time somebody decides I'm supposed to be cool with whatever."

"Micah," Marcia said quietly.

It was the first time she had used his name in the room, and the sound of it tightened him further. It came out as warning, not defense.

Ms. Valdez folded her hands. Her nails were short and bare. "What I have in this folder says you were verbally aggressive before the physical contact. I also have a teacher statement saying that when she intervened, you jerked away and said, quote, don't touch me."

"Because she grabbed me."

"She was trying to stop a fight."

"After it started."

"Because it had already escalated."

"Because nobody stops shit when it's just talking."

Ms. Valdez let that sit. Not because she was considering it, Micah thought, but because silence looked official on her. "Did you report those comments to any staff member before Friday?"

He laughed once.

Marcia's eyes cut to him.

"No, ma'am," he said, and hated how the words tasted. "I didn't report it."

"Why not?"

Because boys who reported things got looked at different after, and not by the people who could help them. Because the last time he told a teacher somebody kept calling him African booty scratcher in seventh grade, the teacher had smiled like she was all tired out already and said middle school boys could be unkind, then asked whether maybe he was giving as good as he got. Because every version of telling required him to stand there and explain that yes, he was Black and yes, his family was Jamaican and yes, those were different things to some people and no, he wasn't going to teach a lesson while somebody laughed.

He picked at the frayed seam of his backpack strap instead.

"Didn't think it mattered," he said.

Officer Baines blew air through his nose, a dry little huff that scraped the room raw. "It matters now."

Micah looked at the glitter sticker on the radio by the officer's shoulder, the silver horn half peeled, and wanted to rip it clean off. "Yeah. Now I got paperwork."

Marcia shifted beside him. The vinyl chair whispered.

Ms. Valdez reached for the district handbook and turned it so the page faced them. The paragraph had a yellow sticky note on it, already marked. "Under the code of student conduct, physical aggression resulting in injury and disruption of instruction carries a range from five to ten days out of school suspension, followed by behavioral probation."

Micah stared at the highlighted lines without seeing them.

"He got injured?" he said. "He had a busted lip."

"That is an injury."

"So my hand don't count?"

"Micah," Marcia said again.

He wanted her to say more than his name. Wanted her to tell them the boy had been begging for it in that slick safe way kids did when they knew adults would call it teasing. Wanted her to say that a hand in a Black boy's hair was not horseplay just because it happened at school under fluorescent lights. Wanted her to speak in that exact, cutting tone that could turn a whole kitchen quiet.

She sat with her hands folded so tight he could see the white at her knuckles, and said nothing.

That hurt him so cleanly he almost missed the next question.

"Is there anything you'd like to add, Mrs. Campbell?" Ms. Valdez asked.

Marcia looked at Micah then, really looked, and he saw what she was doing. Counting. If she said the wrong thing, if she came in hot, if she gave them even one sentence they could call combative, the number would land hard and final. She was trying to keep the floor from dropping through. He knew that. He knew it and still wanted blood from her.

She turned back to the dean. "I'd like to know how he'll make up the work."

For a second Micah thought he had heard wrong.

Ms. Valdez blinked once, maybe surprised, maybe not. "Teachers will provide assignments through the portal. He'll also need to meet with our intervention specialist before returning."

"How many days?" Marcia asked.

"Given the physical altercation, the language toward staff, and Micah's prior referrals for defiance and classroom disruption, I'm assigning ten days out of school," Ms. Valdez said. "That is the maximum suspension I'm authorized to issue at the building level."

Micah swung toward her. "Previous referrals?"

Ms. Valdez opened another page. "Three tardies. Refusal to surrender a phone. Leaving class without permission. Dress-code noncompliance after redirection."

"Man, you calling my hoodie and being late the same thing as this?"

"Taken together, yes. That's a pattern of oppositional behavior."

There it was: pattern. Ms. Valdez laid it between them as neatly as the handbook.

Micah laughed again, too loud. "Just write Black boy and stop wasting toner."

Officer Baines straightened. "Don't say that in here. All it does is give this room one more line on you."

"I'm watching. That's the problem."

"Micah." Marcia's voice sharpened, but only a little, like she was trying not to let it slip.

Ms. Valdez's mouth flattened. "And that's the conduct concern. You hear correction, answer challenge, and by then we've moved from behavior to discipline."

"Because half the time it's that."

"No," she said. "Half the time it's staff asking you to meet a basic expectation without turning the room into a contest."

He could feel the room narrowing. The plant smell stayed in the room, damp and rooty; the cold air kept needling him, and sweat dried in a peel under his arms. He knew the smart thing now was to shut up. Let Marcia finish her questions. Let the number stay at ten and not somehow become more than ten in the story people told about him later.

But the word pattern kept burning.

"Everyone else?" he said. "You mean the dude who gets to say boat-boy shit in the hall till I put my hands on him? That's everyone else?"

Ms. Valdez's voice thinned. "I can't discuss another student's discipline with you."

"Exactly."

Officer Baines leaned forward. The chair legs squealed. "Quit climbing over her. You want your point heard, say it once and keep your hands still."

"Then stop acting like I'm the only person that was there."

"You're making this worse."

Micah looked at him. Really looked. The sweat stain at the collar. The wedding band worn dull. The glitter unicorn still stuck to the radio like home had followed him into this room and hardened there. And still he sat in school colors with a gun on his hip, helping turn hallway stupidity into something official enough to stain the year.

"It was worse when I walked in," Micah said.

Officer Baines planted both feet and let the chair settle flat. Ms. Valdez set her mug down on a damp coffee ring, slid the witness statements aside, and pulled a suspension form from the back of the folder. The air-conditioning rasped over the file cabinets. Marcia kept her eyes on the handbook page instead of on him.

That was how it happened here: the pen clicked, paper slid across laminate, and his name got boxed in before anybody met his eyes.

Ms. Valdez began filling boxes. "Ten days out of school suspension, effective immediately. Behavioral probation through the end of the semester. Any further physical or verbally aggressive conduct will trigger recommendation for alternative placement."

The words kept coming, but Micah only caught pieces: portal, assignments, return conference. Zero tolerance was not the phrase she used because nobody used that anymore when they could say restorative and mean paper.

She slid the form across the desk to Marcia with a pen.

Marcia read every line before she signed. Of course she did. Her face gave nothing away. She signed once for receipt, once for acknowledgment, and slid the form back without letting the pen scratch.

"Can I get a copy?" she asked.

"Of course."

Micah stared at her hand until the secretary's printer outside started up again and the room remembered itself.

When Ms. Valdez handed him the packet, it was thick as a phone book and warm from the copier. His own name sat on the first page in block letters. MICAH CAMPBELL. He had seen his name on honor roll certificates before, on report cards, on the basketball roster. It had never looked this much like an accusation.

Marcia stood. "Thank you," she said, which was such a clean public lie it almost made him dizzy.

He got up slower. Officer Baines moved aside to let them pass. As Micah reached the door, the officer said, not unkindly, "Take the ten, keep your hands off people, and don't make another hallway mess into my paperwork."

Micah kept his mouth shut because every answer in him was ugly.

Outside in the office, the secretary lifted the copied pages from the printer and clipped them together. She handed them to Marcia with her mango nails and a sympathetic mouth she had probably practiced on parents all year.

Micah kept his face empty until they were back in the hallway.

Then he kicked the cinder-block wall once, hard enough to sting his toe through the shoe.

Marcia did not flinch. "Do that again and you'll buy me the paint."

He laughed because the alternative was worse. "With what money?"

The joke fell flat in the heat. Marcia's mouth tightened once.

Marcia tucked the suspension packet under her arm. "Come."

She said nothing on the way to the front gate. The packet stayed pinned under her arm, and each clipped step told him she was carrying the whole thing straight to work.

***

Outside, Miami hit him in the face full force with heat, bus exhaust, wet grass, and the metallic smell rising off the chain-link fence after too much sun. After the office air the day felt obscene. Boys in mesh shorts cut across the parking lot toward summer workouts. A teacher smoked beside her Corolla with her lanyard still on, staring into the distance like she had misplaced her life and expected it back by lunch.

Marcia stopped under the thin strip of shade by the bus stop sign and checked the time on her phone.

"I've got to get to work," she said.

Micah looked at her. "That's it?"

Her eyes came to him, tired and clear. "What you want me to say here?"

He wanted anything, something hot, something that sounded like he was still hers before he was the school's.

Instead he shrugged. "Nothing."

"Go straight home. You hear me?"

"Yeah."

"You're not on vacation. That packet better be open when I come in."

He nodded.

She looked at him one second longer, and there was something under the control then, something like fear or fury packed so tight it had stopped having edges. She could not spend it here. Not with buses groaning past and strangers half listening.

"Don't make me shame you twice in one day," she said.

Then she turned and walked toward the other stop, the one that would take her south to Miss Elena's house. Her steps were fast and clipped, fixed on the curb ahead. Micah watched till she got swallowed by a cluster of people and a county bus exhaling at the curb.

His own bus came ten minutes later, roaring hot, then cooling as soon as the doors folded shut. He tapped his Easy Card and went to the back, packet shoved in his bag like it might burn through the fabric if he held it. The seats were cracked blue plastic. The air smelled like old coins and somebody's coconut body spray. He took the window and watched the school slide away behind graffiti-tagged walls and palms bent ugly by the heat.

He tried on a hard little indifference and wore it badly.

Then two girls from algebra got on at the next stop. One glanced his way, saw the envelope corner sticking from his backpack, and looked too fast in the other direction. That was worse than staring. It meant she knew and wanted him to know she knew and was generous enough not to enjoy it in front of him.

Micah leaned his head against the vibrating window. The glass was hotter than skin. In the reflection he looked older and dumber at once. His twist near the right temple still stuck out where that boy had grabbed it. He tried to flatten it and made it worse.

"Damn," a voice said from the aisle. "They cooked you for real."

Dre dropped into the seat across from him, legs wide, grin already halfway out. He had one white lace and one length of bright yellow extension cord threaded through his left sneaker, and he kept a little black rat-tail comb tucked behind one ear like he was too busy being broke to let his lineup collapse. The inside of his lower lip was rubbed raw where his braces had been cutting him. He worried it with his tongue between words.

"Who said they cooked me?" Micah asked.

"Your face said it. Bag said it. Whole vibe said it." Dre whistled low. "How many?"

Micah looked out the window. "Ten."

Dre slapped the back of the seat. "Ten days? Boy, you basically graduated."

Micah snorted before he could stop himself. The laugh startled him. Since the office he had been holding his face in one hard shape, and Dre talking like suspension was a joke instead of a stain took some of the heat off his ears.

"See?" Dre said. "That's not even bad. That's freedom with homework stapled to it."

Freedom was not the word for ten days trapped in the apartment with that packet and Marcia's silence sharpening by the hour. Still, the idea opened something in him. No hall passes. No Valdez. No boys turning his life into a Jamaica joke and acting shocked when he hit back.

"Yeah, freedom. My mother's gon' kill me slow."

"Only if you sit in the house and let her." Dre leaned back, one arm over the seat top. "Come work."

The words hit before the details. Micah felt the math start anyway: bus fare, a cut, maybe something he could drop on the table so Marcia had to count him as useful and not just home. Even without a number attached, he wanted the shape of it.

Micah turned to him.

"Work where?"

"Dock with my cousin's people. Little river side, not the nice-boat shit. Real early. We unload coolers, stack bait, wipe down decks if the charter captains feeling fancy. Sometimes boxes come in off a truck and nobody asks too many questions. You show up, you move what they point at, you leave with cash."

He said cash the way other people said scholarship, like the word deserved room around it.

Micah stared. "You do that?"

"Sometimes." Dre shrugged. "Enough."

"Since when?"

"Since my grandma decided lights are more important than my phone bill, which apparently they are." He scratched his jaw. "Also my sister's birthday this weekend and I'm not buying her no gas-station teddy bear. She wants that mermaid cake from Publix with the blue icing that stains your teeth for two days. So. Men must labor."

It came out jokey, but Micah could picture it at once: Dre carrying some ridiculous blue cake into an apartment where everybody pretended life was not chewing through money fast as termites. That was the problem with Dre. He kept the joke on top, but there was always something heavier under it and you could hear it if you were not busy performing.

"What they paying?" Micah asked.

Dre's grin changed shape. There was less clowning in it now, more business. "Depends what boat, what truck, who owes my cousin a favor. Sixty if it's light. Eighty, ninety if it's nasty. More if you stay for the second run and don't whine about smell."

Micah tried not to show how hard the numbers hit him.

Ninety dollars was school shoes. Ninety dollars was groceries and maybe enough left for a fresh cut and a Metro reload. Ninety dollars was not having to ask Delroy for shit and hear a story instead of an answer.

"Smell like what?"

"Fish. Diesel. Bleach. Rich-people bait. Sometimes dead shrimp. Sometimes rotten plantain if produce came through first." Dre made a face. "Banana boats can go to hell."

Micah laughed again, smaller this time.

Dre watched him. "I'm serious, though. You got ten days. School already told you what they think. Might as well get paid while they meditate on your growth."

That was the first smart thing anybody had said to him since Friday.

The bus lurched through a light. Somebody up front started arguing with the driver about a transfer. A baby cried two seats over and got shushed with a bottle. Outside, auto shops blurred by, then a church with its marquee missing letters, then a stretch of warehouses where the sun glared off roll-up doors hard enough to make Micah squint.

"My moms ain't letting me just disappear before sunrise," he said.

"Then don't call it disappear." Dre shrugged. "Call it helping a man who tips cash. Call it character building. Y'all adults love that one."

"You stupid."

"And paid." Dre tugged the comb from behind his ear, ran it once through the front of his hair, and tucked it back. "Tomorrow. Four forty-five under the bridge by the river. If you don't come, I text the next boy."

Micah looked at him.

He should have said no on principle. He should have acted like fish guts and dawn were beneath him. He should have clowned Dre for smelling like bait and bilge water before first period half the time. That was the old script, the one where school still had enough of a hold on him that cash work looked temporary and embarrassing instead of clean.

Instead he said, "What I need?"

Dre's eyes brightened, but he kept his face cool. "Shoes you don't mind ruining. Gloves if you got them. If not, your hands belong to Jesus till payday."

"That's not a plan."

"It's Miami. It counts."

Micah nodded once.

Dre bumped his fist lightly against Micah's uninjured hand. "There he is."

The gesture was small and a little stupid, but it steadied something in him, a mean little readiness more useful than pride, the sense that maybe there were ways to move through a week besides sitting at a table under adult disappointment clicking through an online portal.

When Dre got off three stops later, he tapped the window twice and pointed two fingers at his own eyes, then toward Micah. Be there.

Micah watched him hop down to the sidewalk, yellow lace flashing, head already turned toward some other part of the day.

By the time the bus crossed into Little River, the packet in Micah's bag felt lighter than the thought of four forty-five under the bridge.

***

The apartment was hotter than outside. The little wall unit hummed and blew air that smelled faintly of bleach and damp towels, but it could not reach the corners. Tiana sat at the table with a folder open in front of her, highlighter cap between her teeth, one leg folded under her. She had papers spread in careful stacks weighted with a can of black beans.

She looked up when Micah came in. Her eyes went straight to the white packet under his arm.

"Well?" she asked.

He dropped his backpack by the couch-bed and shrugged. "They gave me some bullshit."

"How much bullshit?"

"Ten."

Her mouth tightened with the look she got when the world behaved exactly as she'd warned it would.

"Damn," she said.

He hated that from her more than he would have hated a lecture. "You could at least pretend they're idiots."

"I know they're idiots," she said. "That doesn't mean you didn't help them."

He opened the fridge, stared at a pot, a bottle of ketchup, half an onion in a baggie, and shut it again. "You always got some smart shit ready."

"And you always act shocked."

He almost snapped back. Then he saw the papers on the table, the underlined dates, the clipped seriousness on her face, and understood she was managing her own disaster over there. Different office, same fluorescent knife. The fight leaked out of him.

"Whatever," he said.

Tiana pulled the highlighter from her mouth. "Mummy said open the packet."

"Mummy says a lot."

"She also works harder than everybody in here."

He gave her the finger without turning around. She clicked her tongue and went back to the folder.

For an hour he moved around the apartment like there had to be a place in it where he could sit without being visible. There wasn't. The couch-bed was also the living room. The kitchenette was also the hallway. The bathroom was the only room with a door that shut all the way, and even that door had a swollen bottom edge that scraped the floor and left a gap wide enough for sound to leak through.

He took the packet into there and sat on the closed toilet lid. The paper was already curling from humidity. It laid out ten school days, probation, a parent conference required for reentry, and work posted to the portal. It was all written in that district language that pretended the page had no blood in it. Micah Campbell demonstrated aggressive and noncompliant behaviors. Micah Campbell will be expected to reflect. Micah Campbell may access support services.

He dropped the packet into the sink.

In the mirror he looked exactly fifteen: young, pissed off, caught, a kid with a hallway bruise and one stubborn twist standing up at the temple. He wet his hand and smoothed it down. It sprang back up.

He pressed a paper towel to the scab on his knuckle and watched the rust-colored dot bloom through. The sink smelled like old toothpaste and the lemon cleaner Marcia watered down to make it last. Through the wall he could hear Tiana's highlighter tapping the table in a rhythm that said she was irritated and trying not to be.

By late afternoon the neighborhood sounds changed. Children were outside. Somebody's speaker coughed up bass two houses over. Roosters carried on like rent was free for them. A bus braked at the corner. Delroy still wasn't back. Micah stretched on top of the made bed without taking off his shoes and stared at the ceiling stain above the fan while the heat pushed on his eyelids.

He must have slept for a little while because when he opened his eyes the room was dimmer and the apartment smelled like onions hitting hot oil.

Voices moved on the other side of the half wall.

"Ten days is ten days," Marcia was saying, low and tight. "Probation after that. One more foolishness and they start talking alternative placement."

Delroy made a sound in his throat. "For one fight?"

"Not one fight. One file. That is how they count these children."

Micah stayed still.

Pots shifted in the kitchenette. A cabinet door banged. Delroy's chair scraped the floor. He pictured his father leaning back like trouble was a thing he could charm if he gave it enough air.

"Miami school keep score like old men over dominoes," Delroy said. "One mark by your name and they sit up waiting for the next slap on the table. Boy can't feed them another point this week."

Marcia laughed once, no humor in it. "You tell him that. You, who think every rule is a suggestion till somebody nails it to your forehead."

There was silence. Then Tiana asked carefully from the table, "Can he make up the assignments?"

"They say he can," Marcia said. "Portal, packet, meeting. Always another set of steps like time belongs to poor people in bundles."

"I can help him with algebra," Tiana said.

Micah shut his eyes.

He wanted the apartment to shut up long enough for him to stay inside his own skin. Their voices kept dragging his life around like furniture while the rice steamed and oil popped and Delroy sighed like any of this was interesting instead of humiliating.

The wall unit kicked higher for one bright second, then settled back into its usual losing battle.

"And who watching him all day?" Marcia said. "I can't be here. You can't promise what you don't have. I'm not coming home to find he gone in the street because school decided to send him to me like extra laundry."

"I'm right here," Micah said before he meant to.

The room beyond the wall went quiet.

Then Marcia answered in a flat voice scraped clean by the day. "Yes. You are."

After that the oil hissed again, a spoon tapped the pot, and Delroy cleared his throat without saying anything. She had only enough left to confirm he was there.

Micah rolled off the bed and crossed to the plastic drawer unit by the TV stand. The top drawer stuck halfway, same as always. He jerked it open harder and rooted past charger cords, old mail, a remote with no back, domino score sheets curled at the edges.

The clippers were under a faded barbershop cape Delroy had picked up years ago from some cousin who had started cutting hair in a garage and then vanished to Broward. Silver body, black guard snapped on crooked, cord wrapped with electrical tape near the base. Heavy enough to mean something in the hand.

Micah had used them on Dre once, just to clean the neck. Then on two little boys down the block before church. Then on himself, badly. Marcia had called the back of his head a crime scene and fixed it with kitchen scissors while cussing under her breath. But people paid for a clean line if the neighborhood barbershop was booked and payday hadn't hit right.

He slid the clippers into the front pocket of his backpack.

From the kitchenette, Delroy started saying something about not making a bad week worse. Marcia cut him off. Oil hissed. A spoon hit the sink. Tiana's chair scraped back. The whole apartment kept happening three feet from every other part of itself.

Micah pulled his phone from the charger.

He stared at Dre's number for a moment, thumb hovering.

Then he typed: `What time again`

The reply came almost before he had locked the screen.

`4:45. Don't dress scary.`

Micah laughed under his breath. It was the first real laugh of the day. It felt wrong in his mouth and good anyway.

He shoved the phone into his pocket, zipped the bag, and pushed the suspension packet under the bed with the side of his foot until it disappeared into the dust and lost pens.

The clippers made a solid shape in the backpack now, more convincing than the stack of assignments, more useful than any explanation waiting on the other side of the wall.

When Marcia called that dinner was ready, he said, "Coming," and meant it only in the smallest possible way. By morning she'd be headed south to Miss Elena's house, and he'd be gone before she could start asking better questions.


# Chapter 5

By nine-thirty the next morning, Marcia had changed Celia Alvarez twice, once because the old woman had not made it to the bedside commode in time and once because she had, technically, made it there but not all the way down. The second mess took longer. Celia's knees had given with a soft folding that still startled Marcia, as if the body had stopped consulting the woman inside it. Marcia got one arm under her back, the other under the meatless thighs, and lowered her to the tile before the fall could become a crack and a hospital and all the other expensive humiliations rich families called complications.

The bathroom was colder than church silver. Elena kept the air so low in that house that Marcia's sweat never dried properly; it just cooled on her spine and sat there. Celia cursed the cold every morning and then refused the blanket because blankets made her look sick. This morning she had one heel hooked on the bath mat, her nightgown twisted over one breast, the skin on her legs stippled purple where blood had started giving up on distance.

"Don't make that face," Celia said from the floor. Her lipstick from yesterday had flaked into the lines around her mouth, a faded coral ghost. "If I wanted pity, I would've married better."

Marcia braced her and got her up in stages. "Hold the rail, Miss Celia."

"I'm holding the rail. The rail's not holding me."

The old woman still had the cracked hand mirror on the sink ledge, the same one with a spiderweb split through the corner. She reached for it before she reached for water. Marcia had to admire that, though admiration was not the word she would ever use out loud. Celia checked her eyebrows with the seriousness of a surgeon reviewing bad work.

"Left one is too thin," she said.

"Nobody's inspecting your eyebrows in here."

"That's because you don't know these people. They inspect everything. That one is still too thin."

Marcia took the brow pencil from the tray. She steadied Celia's forehead with one thumb and filled the arch in small strokes while the old woman breathed through her mouth, sour with medicine and the little sweetness that had started living under her tongue these last weeks. Dying had a smell once it committed itself. It was not rot exactly. It was more like fruit gone soft in a bowl no one had the heart to throw out.

Celia watched her in the mirror. Her hair had been set at the beauty shop on Monday, and even flattened from sleep it still carried a stubborn curve at the ends. She'd rather have gone to the salon herself. She'd said so every day since she stopped being able to.

"Use the good lipstick," Celia said. "Not the dead-lady one your Elena keeps bringing."

"You're not dead."

"Then don't paint me like I'm dead."

Marcia capped the pencil, found the coral tube in the little silver cosmetics case, and ran color over Celia's dry mouth. Celia pressed her lips together, then frowned.

"Again. The top lip looks frightened."

There were women who became easier once their bodies turned against them. They got grateful. They got soft. Celia had gone the other way. Each new indignity sharpened her. She complained about powder texture, soup temperature, the priest's breath, the size of the pills, the angle of light on her bedspread. She also made Marcia laugh at least once a shift, usually against Marcia's will. It was an irritating gift.

When Marcia got the brief changed and the fresh gown over Celia's shoulders, she helped her back to bed. The room looked out over a clipped lawn and a bougainvillea hedge that never seemed to collect dust. Even the sunlight in Coral Gables came filtered, respectable. There were no roosters, no bass crawling through drywall, and no neighbor calling across a yard about who had eaten whose mangoes. There was just the soft hum of central air and, farther down the hall, Elena's voice giving instructions in the kitchen like every object in the house had to justify the space it occupied.

Celia settled against the pillows with a little hiss of pain she tried to hide behind annoyance. The effort cost her more now. Her wrists were narrow enough that Marcia could circle one with thumb and forefinger. The first week Marcia worked there, Celia had slapped those fingers away from a button she preferred to fasten herself. That had been almost three years ago. Now Celia waited for help and then resented the waiting.

"You're quiet," Celia said.

Marcia smoothed the sheet where it had wrinkled under Celia's hip. "I'm working."

"You're thinking. The working part is with your hands."

There it was. Celia had a way of seeing the exact place Marcia wanted to keep covered and tapping it once, lightly, just to prove it existed.

"Everybody thinks while they work," Marcia said.

"Not like that. That's counting." Celia shifted her eyes toward Marcia's handbag on the chair. "Something is short."

Marcia almost laughed, because what in her life was not short. Cash was short. Time was short. Tiana's patience with the house was running short. Micah's summer idleness would soon turn into some other kind of trouble. The red code notice on the apartment door had cut the whole season down to a bad joke. But she only said, "You want tea before your pills?"

"I want my daughter to stop arranging my death like centerpieces for a luncheon."

From the hall Elena called, "I heard that."

"Good," Celia called back, not raising her voice much. She did not need to. The house carried her the way some people carried perfume. "Bring me the pink cardigan. The blue one makes me look like a hospital wall."

Elena appeared in the doorway with the cardigan over one arm and a yellow legal pad tucked against her side. She had changed out of whatever she had slept in and into white linen pants and a sleeveless navy blouse that cost more than Marcia let herself guess, but she'd left her hair in the rough knot from the night before. A black pen held the knot in place. Each time it started loosening, she pushed the pen deeper with two fingers and kept talking. The skin around one thumbnail was torn raw. She worried it when she thought nobody was looking.

"Morning, Marcia," she said. "Did she take any water?"

"Some. Not enough."

"Nobody drinks enough in this family except the dead." Celia held out a hand for the cardigan. "And don't put me in that freezer after lunch. If I die cold, I'm haunting this house."

Elena put the cardigan on her mother with the same brisk care she used on pill bottles and doctor phone calls. No wasted motion, no softness for show. She tugged the collar straight, checked the water cup, and wrote something on the legal pad before Marcia had fully stepped back.

"Dr. Feldman is coming at one," Elena said to Marcia. "And the hospice coordinator at two-thirty. I'd like to keep the afternoon quiet."

The word hospice entered the room and sat there. Celia rolled her eyes hard enough to make herself cough.

"See?" she said when the cough passed. "Centerpieces."

Marcia reached for the water cup. Celia waved it off, then took it anyway because her pride had not yet found a way to live without thirst. As she drank, thin and careful, Marcia watched Elena flip to a clean page on the legal pad. She had lists for medications, lists for linens, lists for cousins coming by with casseroles nobody ate. Last week she'd started labeling drawers in the kitchen. Spoons. Towels. Batteries. As if the house had begun slipping away and could be pinned down with handwriting.

Marcia had been waiting all morning for an opening that would not sound like begging. She had rehearsed three versions on the first bus and rejected all of them. One sounded desperate. One sounded like a lie. One sounded too much like gratitude paid in advance.

Elena glanced at the clock on the mantel. "Can you change the bed again after her pills? Then maybe just settle her? I don't think we'll need you through the full afternoon."

There it was, and it was not even cruel enough to deserve anger.

"All right," Marcia said. The word came out smooth. Years of practice. "Miss Elena, I wondered if there might be any extra time later this week. Or if there was any way to run part of Friday's check ahead. Just a little. I can make it up over the weekend."

Elena did not pretend not to understand. Marcia gave her that much credit. The younger woman's face changed in one quick, almost invisible way, the way people looked when they had already begun arranging their refusal into something they could live with.

"I'm sorry," Elena said. "No on the advance. Payroll is through the agency and they've already closed this week. And I don't have extra hours right now. I'm actually pulling hours back while we're figuring out what the doctor wants."

"Only for this week?" Marcia asked. She hated the smallness in the question as soon as it left her mouth.

"For now." Elena pressed the pen flat against the pad, then lifted it again. "Maybe next week is different. Maybe not. I don't know yet."

Celia made a sound that was almost a laugh and almost a spit.

"Ay, Dios," she said. "Listen to all that clean language. The woman asked for money, Elena. You don't have to dress the no in a blazer."

"Mami."

"What? I'm dying, not deaf."

Heat moved under Marcia's skin, mean and useless. She kept her face arranged. That was the trick. Her mouth stayed polite, her eyes blank, her shoulders loose.

"It's fine," she said. "I shouldn't have asked in front of you."

"You should've asked sooner," Celia said. "Need doesn't get more elegant with waiting."

Elena blew out a breath through her nose. Up close the fatigue showed in the gray half-moons under her eyes. She had been sleeping in this house for days. Her phone kept lighting on the bedside table with brothers, nurses, priests, and a son in Tallahassee who wanted updates but not duties. She looked exactly like what she was: a daughter with a calendar and a mother leaking out of it.

"Marcia, if I hear of anything once this is over, I'll ask around," Elena said. "You know I'd tell you if there was room here. But right now we're paying for the overnight nurse consult, the equipment, all of it. It's not that I don't want to help."

Equipment, consult, help: the words came out ironed flat. Marcia looked past Elena at the unopened oxygen tank by the wall, the legal pad, the bottles and cartons lining the dresser. Even the ending in this house had line items.

Celia held out her palm. "My purse."

"No," Elena said immediately.

"What do you mean no? It's my purse."

"And you're tired, and tired is when you start solving everybody with cash."

"I'm not confused. I'm generous."

"Same damage, different name."

"Marcia," Celia said. "Bring it here."

Marcia's eyes went to the chair before she could stop them. Black leather, gold clasp rubbed pale where a thumb had worked it for years. She knew that purse better than she wanted to. She had carried it into cardiology, podiatry, the salon, Sunday Mass. She knew the middle pocket held the green wallet, and the green wallet held cash because Celia considered leaving the house without bills in her bag a kind of public nakedness.

"Leave it," Elena said.

"It's my money," Celia said. "Bring me my damn purse."

Marcia crossed the room anyway. Obeying the patient, she told herself. Not reaching. The strap bit into her palm, heavier than it had any right to be. When she set the purse on the blanket, Celia's fingers fumbled at the clasp and failed.

"Open it," Celia said.

"Marcia," Elena said, and now the softness was gone.

The clasp snapped under Marcia's thumb. Inside sat the green wallet, a coral lipstick, pharmacy receipts, and the flat bright edge of cash. One twenty showed, then another, and a folded ten tucked under them. Fifty dollars. Groceries, bus fare, light.

"Take it out," Celia said. "If I have to listen to poverty in my own bedroom, somebody should at least leave paid."

Marcia slid the bills free. The paper dragged against her damp thumb. For one second she held the money over Celia's blanket, not looking at either woman, already feeling the shape it would make tucked inside her bra on the ride home.

Elena stepped to the bedside. "Put that back."

Heat broke across Marcia's face. The bills stuck to her fingers. Celia watched her with bright, mean amusement; Elena watched her the way people watched a hand too close to a drawer. Marcia laid the money on the blanket, smoothed it once, then shoved it back into the wallet hard enough to dog-ear one corner. She shut the purse so hard the clasp snapped like a tooth.

Elena did not thank her. What she gave Marcia instead was a flat, measuring look that made the room feel newly divided.

"No, Miss Celia," she said. She lifted the purse off the bed and put it back on the chair, farther than before. "Take your pills."

"Bossy girl."

"Take them."

"You hear that, Elena? She likes me best when I obey."

Marcia almost smiled despite herself. Celia saw it and gave a little triumphant nod, lipstick bright against all that failing skin.

After the pills, Celia drifted for a while, hovering just short of sleep. She lay with one hand on top of the blanket, fingers moving now and then as if still sorting cards in some old game Marcia had not been invited to learn. Marcia changed the bed again because the pad had leaked at the edge and because work was cleaner than standing in the kitchen feeling the no settle inside her. She rubbed lotion into Celia's calves, powder under the breasts where the skin stayed damp, and tucked the cardigan straight across the shoulders.

When she bent to lift the laundry bag, Celia opened one eye.

"Your husband working?" she asked.

The question was not casual. Celia did not waste curiosity on casual.

"Some," Marcia said.

"Some is a bad amount."

"It's the amount I've got."

Celia's mouth twitched. "You still have your pride too. I can see that from here. Terrible burden. Heavy thing to carry on the bus."

Marcia looked up. The old woman's pupils were cloudy but fixed on her with embarrassing accuracy.

"Rest," Marcia said.

"Mm. You too, if they ever let you."

By eleven-thirty Elena had already set out forms for the doctor and moved two framed family photos off the side table to make room for a machine that had not arrived yet. She was staging the end, just like the outline in Marcia's head had feared. A blue oxygen tank stood by the wall in its carton, unopened, as if the future could be tolerated in packaging but not in use.

Elena walked Marcia to the kitchen, speaking in the low practical tone people used for contractors and clergy. "I'll text you tonight about tomorrow. Morning should stay the same. Afternoon, I'm not sure."

"All right."

"And Marcia?" Elena waited until Marcia looked at her. "I meant what I said. If somebody asks me for a caregiver once this is over, I'll pass your name first."

Marcia nodded because to do anything else would have required a speech and speeches were expensive. On the island counter sat a bowl of nectarines ripening untouched, a stack of hospice pamphlets facedown, and three kinds of imported crackers nobody in that house had the appetite for. The refrigerator gave off its steady expensive hum. Marcia could have stood there naming the cost of the room: granite, a gas range, an ice dispenser that worked every time. She did not. Arithmetic done in anger still had to come out correct.

At the front door Elena touched her arm. "I'm sorry."

Elena's fingers were cool and dry on her arm, there and gone before the touch could turn into promise.

"I know," Marcia said.

Behind them Celia called, thin but sharp, "Bring the coral tomorrow. If I end up meeting Saint Peter, I'm not doing it pale."

Elena shut her eyes again, just briefly. Marcia let herself smile that time. It cost nothing, which was good, because she did not have another kind to spare.

Outside, the heat hit her full in the face, raw and wet and honest. The walk from the Alvarez house to the bus stop was only seven minutes if she moved hard, but Coral Gables had a way of stretching distance with prettiness. The sidewalks were broad and empty. Hibiscus hedges held themselves like women with old money. Sprinklers clicked over lawns so green they looked artificial. A man in white shorts jogged past with a dog wearing a bandanna cleaner than Micah's school uniform on picture day.

Marcia kept her work bag high under her arm and held herself to an even pace. A delivery van idled at the curb while a woman in tennis whites took her time working a gate code. Up the block, a nanny steered a double stroller with one hand and a sweating iced coffee with the other. Marcia stayed at that same unbothered speed until the corner hid the house. She passed a driveway where two landscapers were loading clipped branches into a trailer and caught one of them looking at her shoes, the sensible black pair she only wore for work because the bus dust ate everything cheaper. His glance held no insult. Just recognition. Service shoes knew one another.

At the stop she sat under a buttonwood tree trimmed so neatly it gave almost no shade. Her blouse stuck to the small of her back. She took out her transit card and checked the balance on the little screen by the shelter: $4.25. It was enough to get home if the transfer behaved, but not enough for another round trip after tomorrow unless she reloaded it.

The bus came heaving cold air and diesel. She climbed on, nodded to the driver, and took a seat halfway back where the vent blasted too hard. Her damp skin went chilled at once. Across from her a nanny in pink scrubs was texting with both thumbs while a toddler slept against her chest, mouth open, one fat hand knotted in the drawstring of her top. Two seats up, a man in paint-spattered whites dozed with his head knocking the window every few blocks and never waking enough to fix it.

Marcia set her bag on her lap and opened the zipper to the small inside pocket. Inside were her wallet, lip balm, two receipts from Presidente, a church envelope, and the partner notebook. She touched the notebook and then took her hand away, as if other people on the bus could tell what it was by temperature alone.

She started with the fixed numbers because panic liked fog and she preferred columns.

Rent was eleven-fifty.

Light was ninety-three and some change if she paid before the red print started.

Phone was seventy-eight because Micah swore he needed data for school even in summer and Tiana needed a line that did not cut out when colleges called. Delroy always said they could lower it next month. Next month was a liar.

Groceries, if she cooked proper and stretched meat with lentils and enough rice to insult nobody, maybe one-forty for the week. Less if she let them complain. More if Micah kept eating like a boy trying to outrun his own height.

Then came bus fare, gas for Delroy when he got a day, laundry, the school money Tiana pretended not to need yet, and the money for moving that no one was saying out loud in front of the children, though the red notice on the apartment door said it well enough on its own.

Income came softer, always in maybes: her Friday check if Elena kept tomorrow's full shift, Delroy's promise of something from a patch job in Hialeah unless the man paid him in excuses again, and Tiana's little check, which Marcia hated touching because every dollar the girl earned already had one foot pointed north, toward paperwork, bus fare, and whatever waited past Miami.

Her thumb worried the edge of the partner notebook while live oaks striped the window with shade and then gave her back to harder sun. Yvette's handwriting filled the first pages, dates and names and small tidy figures. Marcia's draw came in July. It had lived in her mind so long it had started feeling less like money than weather, not guaranteed, not controllable, but coming if the season held.

That was only if the season held.

She took out the church envelope. On the front she had written in pencil two weeks ago: `Yvette - July`. It felt too light. She had already robbed it for bus fare, then for cooking oil, then for bus fare again, each theft from herself small enough to forgive while she was doing it. Any sane advice said stop. Pay the bill that had teeth this week. Keep cash where she could touch it. But if she shorted Yvette now, Yvette would not curse her. She would do something worse: understand, slide Marcia's draw back, and let some other woman with a cleaner week take July in her place. July was the only pile of money near enough to matter: rent, a deposit if they had to run, the first school money for Tiana. That option was gone. The hand had to be made whole.

More bodies shouldered in at the next stop with shopping bags and trapped heat. A woman got on carrying a foil pan wrapped in two dish towels. Garlic and baked cheese spread through the aisle, rich enough to hurt. Marcia's stomach answered with a flat little turn. She had eaten two crackers at Celia's with the morning pills because the house always had crackers and because fainting in rich people's bathrooms was bad for employment. Now she wished she had stolen a nectarine, not because of hunger, but because the bowl had been full enough not to notice one missing. Keeping July whole meant somebody else in her house would have to miss instead. The light bill could go one cycle late if she called before the shutoff notice printed. She could buy the cheaper oil and let them taste the difference. She could tell Micah no more cold drinks from the store and tell Delroy that if he wanted cigarettes he could smoke ambition. She could stretch one chicken over two nights and walk one way to church if the transit card ran out. She had done uglier things for less.

Humiliation from Elena's kitchen had already hardened into assignments. Tomorrow she would give Yvette the full amount due before Delroy could start talking around it. Tonight she would strip the borrowed bus money back out of the grocery money and call the light company for one more week. If Elena cut tomorrow short, fine. If Delroy brought in cash this week, part of it would go straight into the next envelope before rice, before phone, before any bill that could be delayed without a man appearing at the door.

She would not tell the children how close the math sat to breaking yet. Fear made young people stupid in different directions. Tiana got colder. Micah got louder. Neither one of them needed to see how much of the summer was balanced on women passing envelopes after church and pretending that trust was stronger than misfortune.

Little River announced itself before the turn: salt, old fryer grease, damp cotton, somebody's cheap coconut lotion giving up under the heat. The cold air had gone thin and useless. The partner notebook stayed in her hand for the rest of the ride. She did not grip it. She held it. She ran her thumb over the cardboard cover until the edge softened with sweat. She slipped the notebook back into her bag as carefully as if it were breakable and gave the rest of the summer its rule. Part of it was already spoken for in her head: whatever Tiana needed to carry north without looking hungry or lost. July was the door she had: thin wood with a cheap lock, maybe swollen from rain already. Until it failed, every other bill in the house would have to wait its turn outside it.


# Chapter 6

Outside the student union, the sidewalks still held the night's damp in their cracks, but the sky had already gone that hard white-blue Florida liked to put up before noon, a color with no mercy in it. Tiana had come up from Miami on the early Saturday bus, before the heat got rude. Now she crossed campus with her document folder tucked under her arm and the strap of her bag burning a line into her shoulder. Families moved around her in small, practiced clusters, carrying the kind of ordinary equipment that had started to look luxurious to her: refillable water bottles, envelopes clipped together, printed campus maps, a parent who knew where to stand next.

Nobody was cartoon rich. That made it worse.

One girl walked with a father in a pest-control polo and a mother still wearing hospital clogs, both of them arguing softly over whether the student union was the brick building or the glass one. They looked tired, underpaid, a little lost. They had still come together. They still had a somebody to say you go get your ID while I hold the place in line.

For one stupid minute she hated them for having infrastructure.

Then she hated herself for that too, because she knew exactly what her mother had done to get her here at all. Marcia had ironed her blouse on the kitchen counter with the little travel iron that hissed and spat rust at the edges, then folded two ten-dollar bills into the back sleeve of the folder and told her to eat something that involved protein instead of just sugar and panic. Delroy, pulling on his boots in the half-dark, had kissed the side of her head and said, "Everything line up, baby girl. Go make them people know your name." He had said it the same way he said a tire only needed air when the tread was already showing wire. She had chosen, because she wanted the day, to hear promise instead of style.
