At the check-in tables outside the student union, a student in an orange polo held out a name tag and a lanyard that said ORIENTATION in block letters. He had a shaved line through one eyebrow and the impatient, underfed look of somebody who had been up since five and had already decided every family in the state would ask him where the bathrooms were.

"Parents and guests are heading into the auditorium on the right," he said, not looking up yet. "Students go left."

"It's just me," Tiana said.

He looked up then, quick and embarrassed. "Right. Sorry. You're good. Sign here."

The pity never lasted more than a second. That was part of what made it cut. Nobody lingered over her lack long enough to feel ashamed of it. The whole place had been built for a version of family she could walk through but not trigger, like one of those grocery-store automatic doors that stayed shut if you were too light.

She signed her name, pinned on the badge, and followed the arrow for students. The lanyard ribbon dragged at the back of her neck. In the ballroom, the AC ran hard enough to make the damp at her temples go cold. Round tables had been set with plastic folders, campus maps, handouts about housing, dining, financial aid, honors, first-year success. She sat beside a girl with soft-pink acrylics and a fresh school sweatshirt folded over her chair.

The girl offered her a mint.

"You came up from Miami too?" she asked.

"Little River."

"Damn." The girl smiled. "My mom nearly cried about the drive from Orlando."

Tiana smiled back because the line invited it. "Then she would've died on the Turnpike."

The girl laughed, and for a few minutes it was easy. The dean on stage welcomed them. A student panel answered questions. People laughed in the right places. Tiana wrote notes she did not need because the writing itself calmed her down: office hours, add/drop, move-in, meal plans, book grants, honors advising. Facts sat still on paper even when the life under them did not.

When the panelists talked about residence halls, the room shifted. Students sat up. Parents started whispering to one another and circling dates in their packets. Tiana felt the word dorm move through her again, that private electric thing it had become since graduation night. The electric part was not the cinder-block room, the twin bed, or the shared bathroom with girls from Tampa and Broward and somewhere in New Jersey. What she wanted was a key and a lock and one hard little rectangle of air that belonged to her body. A door she could close without hearing Micah's game through the wall, without smelling frying oil in her pillow, without anybody asking where she was going or when she'd be back. This is your room. Shut the door.

She let herself have the morning anyway. At honors advising she got a sample first-semester schedule printed for her, fresh paper warm from the machine. The adviser, a woman with a silver braid and sneakers she had decorated with little green alligators, told her she had done very well and would do well here if she kept asking for what she needed before things got late. Tiana nodded as if that sentence did not have a whole second meaning pressing under it. She folded the schedule neatly into her folder and ran her thumb over her own name at the top. For one bright, reckless stretch of an hour she could almost mistake progress for arrival. She was on campus. She had a badge. People kept calling her an incoming student. The word incoming had direction in it.

By lunchtime the concrete was throwing heat back up at everybody's knees. Tiana bought a chicken wrap that sweated through the paper and a bottle of water that cost more than it should have. She sat on a low wall and ate fast while heat pressed through the seat of her skirt. She looked at her phone. Marcia had texted three words and three red hearts: You eat yet?

Tiana sent back a picture of the campus lawn and wrote, Ate. It's nice here.

Marcia replied almost immediately. Good. Ask every question.

Delroy had sent a thumbs-up and a fist earlier that morning. Nothing since.

When she unfolded the schedule again, one line sat heavier than the rest: Financial Aid and Scholarship Verification, room 214. Orientation had spent all morning trying to make college feel settled. Room 214 looked like the part that would decide whether any of it counted.

* * *

The aid office waiting area smelled like toner, cold coffee, and wet clothes drying too slowly under AC. Students sat in molded chairs beneath posters about access, excellence, and deadlines. A television in the corner ran a silent campus slideshow while captions crawled along the bottom about summer disbursement dates. Tiana checked in at the front desk, gave her student ID number, and got handed a slip of paper with 63 on it. The woman behind the glass had lashes thick enough to cast a shadow on her cheeks and a tiny silver screwdriver tucked behind one ear, as if she had been fixing her glasses between students.

"Take a seat, baby," she said. "They're moving."

They were moving the way a wound moved when somebody kept prodding it to see if it still hurt.

Numbers jumped on the screen. Fifty-seven. Fifty-eight. Sixty-one. A father in cargo shorts kept tapping a highlighter against his knee while his daughter practiced saying "subsidized" under her breath like a new vocabulary word. A girl with a perfect low bun cried soundlessly beside a mother who had already stopped pretending not to be irritated. Nobody made a scene. That was the office's genius. It kept everybody in the same fluorescent key where panic looked like paperwork and shame sounded like printers.

When 63 flashed, Tiana stood so fast the folder edge sliced the web of skin between her thumb and index finger. A bright little line rose there. She licked it once, tasting paper dust, then followed the sign for cubicles.

The counselor in cubicle four wore lilac reading glasses on a beaded chain and a beige wrist brace under her cardigan. A half-eaten clementine sat on a napkin beside her keyboard, peeled with the neatness of somebody who liked to keep bitterness out from under the nails.

"Tiana Campbell?" the woman said.

"Yes."

"I'm Ms. Herrera. Have a seat."

Her voice was not unkind. It was worse than unkind. It was practiced. She typed with two fast fingers, clicked through screens, nodded once at whatever rose up in front of her.

"Okay," she said. "I'm seeing two issues here. One is residency verification. The permanent resident card we have on file expired in March."

Tiana had already taken the photocopy out of the folder. She slid it across. The copy looked old and grayer than she remembered, the lamination glare caught forever in the machine's bad light.

"My father said the renewal was submitted," she said. "He handled it."

Ms. Herrera glanced at the copy, then back to the screen. "Do you have the receipt notice for that? The I-797, if it was filed?"

"No. I thought the updated card would be coming."

"Okay." Ms. Herrera clicked again. "Right now I can't see a pending update in the system. If the renewal's in process, we can usually work with the receipt notice while immigration takes its time. But I need proof that the renewal was actually filed."

Actually filed: the phrase fell into Tiana's lap and stayed there.

"What's the second issue?" she asked, because the first one had already started spreading through her body and she wanted all the bad news at once if possible, the way you ripped off tape.

Ms. Herrera turned the monitor slightly. On the screen was Tiana's name, her student number, and under Requirements a red list:

Proof of current permanent resident status or receipt notice pending renewal.
Parent 2017 IRS tax transcript or verification of nonfiling.
2017 parent W-2s.
Signed parent verification worksheet.

The bullets were so tidy. The damage in them had no smell.

"Your FAFSA was selected for verification," Ms. Herrera said. "That happens all the time. For the 2019-2020 aid year, we need your parent's 2017 tax information and the signed worksheet before we can finalize aid packaging. Until both issues clear, your file stays on hold."

"Hold for what exactly?"

"For federal aid, need-based institutional aid, and final disbursement of your scholarship package."

"I already got the scholarship."

"You got the offer." Ms. Herrera looked at her over the tops of the lilac frames, not stern, just exact. "An offer isn't a disbursement. Right now it's provisional because your file isn't complete."

Tiana stared at the red list. She could feel the room narrowing around the plastic chair, the AC vent overhead blowing straight at the damp place between her shoulder blades.

"If I get these today," she said, "does that fix it today?"

Ms. Herrera gave her a look that contained no contempt and, because of that, landed harder. "If you upload everything cleanly and if the residency piece checks out, the hold can be reviewed. But no, not today. We're in July. Nothing moves today."

Nothing moves today.

Of course that was how the ruin came. It did not announce itself with a slammed door. It arrived as a queue number on a screen.

Tiana forced herself to ask the questions in order, because she could hear Marcia in her head saying if you want help from an office, leave with a list and a name.

"What happens if it doesn't clear before August?"

"Then your aid won't disburse on time." Ms. Herrera tapped the screen once with a capped pen. "Housing sees that. Billing sees that. You stay in pending status until the file's complete."

"Pending means what?"

"Pending means pending."

"For the school it does." Tiana heard her voice sharpening and hated it and kept going anyway. "For me, what does it mean?"

Something changed in Ms. Herrera's face. It wasn't softness. It was recognition of the gap between office language and rent language.

"It means you're not done yet," she said. "It doesn't mean canceled. But you need to move fast, and you need original, usable documents. Not stories about documents."

The humiliation of that almost made Tiana flinch.

She swallowed. "Can I print this?"

"I'm printing it now."

The printer coughed to life behind the cubicle wall. Ms. Herrera took the page when it came through, checked it, then slid it across. Her nails were clipped square and bare. On her ring finger was a white line where a ring had either been removed for work or removed for life.

"Call whoever handled your paperwork," she said. "Ask for the receipt number first. Then ask for the 2017 tax transcript and W-2s. The worksheet's online. If your parents didn't file taxes, that's a different route, but it's still paperwork. Don't let anybody tell you the school can just see it somehow."

The sheet in Tiana's hand was warm, newly born and already hostile. At the top, in block letters, it said UNSATISFIED REQUIREMENTS. Her name under it looked less like ownership than accusation.

"Thank you," she said, because she had been raised correctly.

"I know," Ms. Herrera said, and for the first time she sounded tired instead of professional. "I know, honey."

Outside the cubicle, the waiting room carried on in its low electric hum. Somebody laughed too loud near the front desk and then stopped. The girl with the perfect bun was still crying, but now she was talking too, words coming fast in angry little bursts while her mother stared straight ahead. Tiana stood beside a potted plant with dusty leaves and called Delroy.

He answered on the fourth ring with traffic and machinery roaring around him.

"Baby girl."

"I need the receipt number for the resident-card renewal."

"The what?"

She closed her eyes. "The receipt number. From when you filed it."

There was a beat in which she could hear him move the phone, maybe from one ear to the other, maybe away from his mouth so he could buy himself half a second.

"Why you need that right this minute?"

"Because the aid office can't verify anything without it, and they don't have any 2017 parent tax documents either. They have nothing, Daddy. They have a photocopy of an expired card and that's it."

"All right, all right. Slow down."

"No."

He made a small sound, almost a laugh, the one he used when he wanted a room to get friendlier than it was. "You up there sounding like police."

"I'm sounding like a person whose file just got put on hold."

"Hold don't mean dead."

"It means they won't finalize my aid."

"Them offices always say dramatic things. I sent what needed sending."

Tiana opened her eyes and stared through the office window at the parking lot beyond, heat already wobbling over the asphalt. Parents moved between buildings with campus folders tucked under their arms. Nobody out there knew her life had just shifted shape in a cubicle with a clementine on the desk.

"Then give me the receipt number."

"I don't have it on me. I'm working."

"Where is it?"

"Home. Somewhere safe."

The lie was not in the words. It was in how quickly he reached for safe, that pretty useless word, the same way he said soon when he meant not yet and handled when he meant stop asking me in front of people.

"And the 2017 tax transcript and W-2s?" she said.

"We'll print whatever they need."

"From where?"

"From online."

"With what account? Who set it up?"

He went quiet. The noise around him kept going: reversing truck, somebody shouting in Spanish, metal knocking metal. Under it she heard what she had not wanted to hear all day. It was not confusion. It was emptiness. There was no paper in his hand, no number, no plan, only delay wearing his voice.

"Daddy."

"Tiana, listen to me. Don't get yourself worked up in front of them people. Come home. Tonight we sort it out."

"Did you file it?"

Another beat followed, longer this time.

"Come home," he said again, and now the humor was gone. Shame had shortened him. "We'll talk home."

She hung up before she could say something that would break open in the wrong place.

Her thumb had started bleeding again where the folder had cut her. She pressed the warm requirement sheet against it and watched a faint pink crescent bloom through the corner by Parent Verification Worksheet. She almost laughed at that. Her blood knew where to land.

At the front desk she asked, in the most even voice she could assemble, if there was anywhere on campus she could upload documents if her family sent them later tonight. The woman with the screwdriver behind her ear told her yes, there were computer labs, but if the receipt notice did not exist then no amount of emailing would change the situation. She said it kindly. Tiana wanted to throw the potted plant through the television.

Instead she walked out into the heat.

The afternoon had gone full Florida now, thick and blunt and wet enough to wear. Her blouse stuck under her bra. The lanyard felt like a punishment ribbon. She sat on a bench outside the building because standing took more performance than she had left. The requirement sheet was already damp at the edges. It said aid hold, pending review, no disbursement until the file was complete. Housing would see that. Billing would see that. Every clock between July and move-in had started ticking in a language she hated.

Nothing moves today, Ms. Herrera had said.

The cruelty of offices was that they could be truthful and still miss the body. To the office she was a pending file, maybe a follow-up in five business days if the queue moved. To Tiana the problem had teeth. It bit through timing first. Orientation had been the day college was supposed to harden into matter. Instead the whole thing had gone filmy again, as if Gainesville itself were only a screensaver the school could shut off the minute her documents failed to load.

She looked down at her badge. INCOMING STUDENT. The plastic sleeve had already fogged from sweat.

What was she actually incoming to?

Marcia texted again: How aid go?

Tiana stared at the words until they blurred, then locked the phone instead of answering.

The bus back to Miami did not leave for another hour and a half.

* * *

By the time the bus hissed into the station that evening, the paper in her folder had gone soft at the corners from being handled too much. She had spent the ride south not crying, which took more work than the morning had. The man next to her had slept with his mouth open all the way to Fort Pierce. At one stop a woman got on carrying a foil pan that filled the whole bus with baked macaroni and onion. Two rows up, somebody watched fight videos without headphones until the driver threatened to pull over. The world went on with its same cheap mess, which felt insulting. She had wanted, just for that day, some sign that a hold on her future should alter traffic patterns.

Near Wildwood she had texted Malik because there were some humiliations that could not be carried all the way home untouched.

You around later?

He had taken twelve minutes to answer.

Class at seven. What time you get in?

She told him. He wrote back, I can catch you first.

Now, stepping down from the bus into diesel air and old gum heat, she saw him leaning against his silver Accord under a flickering light. He had changed out of his work shirt but not his pants, navy scrub bottoms with one knee gone paler than the other from too many washes. His backpack sat by the tire. In one hand he held a bottle of water, and with the other he was stripping the label off it in a clean white spiral, the way he always did when he was thinking or waiting or trying not to call somebody again.

He straightened when he saw her. He did not wave big. That was one of the things she liked about him. He never behaved as if public space needed more witnesses than it already had.

"You look cooked," he said when she got close enough.

"That's flattering."

"It's accurate."

His voice came out flat when he was worried, not cold but leveled. He picked up her bag before she could say not to, took one look at her face, and shut the trunk again without putting it in. "You want to sit first or move?"

She almost said move. Motion still passed for control if you didn't look too close. Instead she said, "Sit."

They got into the car with the doors open. The seat held the day's heat. Malik handed her the water. It was still cold enough to hurt her teeth.

"Thanks."

"I was already down here." He jerked his chin toward his backpack. "I got class in a little bit. So don't make me miss it for some vague nonsense. What happened?"

That was why she had texted him. He did not give her a soft Are you okay for the record. He went straight to the thing under the performance.

Tiana took the requirement sheet out of the folder and handed it across. He read faster than people expected him to. His eyes moved once down the page, then back to the top.

"Resident card expired," he said. "2017 parent tax transcript. 2017 W-2s. Verification worksheet. That's the whole fight?"

"That's enough fight."

"I'm asking."

She watched the station lights reflect in the windshield. Her own face hovered there faintly, badge still hanging off her neck like proof from a day that had revoked itself.

"The school says they can't clear aid or housing without proof the renewal was filed," she said. "Daddy told me he handled it. I called him from outside the office and got safe somewhere in the house like that means anything. Same with the tax stuff. There's no paper. There's just him talking."

Malik looked up from the page. "You telling your mother tonight?"

The question hit hard enough to make her turn. "Why would you jump to that?"

"Because if he's lying, he gets more room by keeping it between the two of you."

"I'm asking him first."

"That's not the same as protecting her."

"I know that." She heard the sharpness in her own voice and did nothing to hide it. "I need to hear what comes out of his mouth when I put the list in front of him."

Malik folded the page once, careful not to crease the printed boxes too badly, then opened it again. "All right. Then say that clean."

She let her head fall back against the seat. The station loudspeaker crackled something nobody could understand. A woman in pink slides dragged two little boys past the car, each boy holding one handle of a grocery bag between them and fighting anyway.

"I sat there all day being an incoming student," she said. "Badge, campus map, little honors schedule with my name on it, people saying welcome. Then one woman clicked around on a screen and I was nobody coming anywhere. I was a list. I was a maybe."

Malik's thumb kept worrying the peeled strip of bottle label until it snapped. "Yeah," he said quietly. "That part's real. The office made you pending. Your father made it personal."

The sentence landed and stayed. She could not argue with it without lying on Delroy's behalf one more time.

She looked down at the scrub pants, the backpack, the spiral of label in his hand. "You were really going to class."

"I still am, unless your father makes me commit a felony on the drive over."

That pulled a sound out of her, rough and alive enough to count as a laugh.

"What class?"

"Human growth and development." He made a face. "Which sounds fake and then they hit you with fifty pages on attachment theory."

"That tracks."

"I knew you'd be rude."

He tossed the label strip into the cup holder, then finally looked at her full on. He had a tiny nick under his chin from shaving too fast and a line of wear at the bridge of his nose where his cheap sunglasses sat every morning on the drive to the rehab clinic. She liked that line. It made him look less polished than the care in him actually was.

"What are you doing when you get home?" he asked.

"Asking him once."

"Asking what?"

"What he did. Where the paperwork is. When he plans to stop talking to me like I'm a child he can entertain past the hard part."

"Good." He reached into the back seat and found a pen under a workbook, clicked it, and tapped the requirement sheet. "Ask for the receipt number first. If he changes the subject, ask again. Then 2017 tax transcript. Then 2017 W-2s. Then who signed what. Don't go in there with ten speeches. Go in there with nouns."

She took the pen from him and wrote on the margin of the page:

Receipt number
2017 tax transcript
2017 W-2s
Worksheet

The handwriting looked too clean for the feeling under it.

"You always this bossy?" she asked.

"Only when people act like fury is a filing strategy."

"Maybe that's how it works at my house."

"At your house it might be foreplay for yelling. Not the same thing."

She cut him a look. He lifted a shoulder.

"You called me," he said.

"I know."

"So either you wanted a witness or you wanted me to lie to you. I can do one of those."

That landed somewhere low and hot in her, not because it was romantic, though some part of it was, but because witness was the word. He had named the thing she had not wanted to admit. She had not texted him for rescue. She had texted him because somebody besides her needed to know the day had happened exactly as it had happened. That the office had reduced her and she had still sat upright in the chair. That her father's voice had emptied out in her ear.

"A witness," she said.

"All right."

Neither of them moved for a while. The station lot emptied and refilled around them. Somewhere a bus hissed, knelt, rose. Malik checked the clock on the dash, grimaced, and rubbed at the line between his brows.

"If he starts performing, leave," he said.

She looked at him. "Leave to where?"

"Out the door first. Figure out the geography second. Call me before you let him talk you numb."

Something low in her chest tightened at that, not from rescue exactly. From being given an exit and being trusted to use it.

"You hungry?" he asked.

"Not in a way food helps."

"That wasn't the question."

She thought about the chicken wrap at noon, the stale pretzels at three, the coffee smell on the bus that had turned her stomach. "A little."

He reached into his backpack and came up with a crumpled brown bag from the Haitian bakery near his job. Inside was a beef patty, flattened at one corner but still warm in the middle.

"I forgot I had this," he said. "Eat before you go start war."

She took it. The pastry left grease on the requirement sheet when she set the paper on her lap. Somehow that felt right. Documents should have to live in the same world as appetite.

"What if he says he'll fix it?" she asked after a few bites.

"Make him use dates," Malik said. "Soon isn't paperwork."

"What if he lies?"

"Then at least you'll hear the shape of it sober."

"You sound like an old woman."

"My grandmother raised me till I was nine. You want these skills or not?"

She smiled despite herself, flaky pastry sticking to her thumb. He looked relieved but did not make a thing of it. He never tried to collect gratitude while it was still wet.

"You need to go," she said.

"In a minute."

"You'll miss attachment theory."

"Tragic."

She finished the patty and folded the empty bag small. Outside the car, Miami had gone the color it got just before full dark, everything outlined but not yet softened. She could feel home waiting at the other end of the drive, the garage apartment with its thin walls and one overhead light that made every argument look cheaper than it already was.

Malik started the engine. The AC coughed awake.

"I'm taking you there," he said.

"I didn't ask."

"No. You looked like bus stations should've been outlawed. Close enough."

On the drive north he did not fill the space. Traffic on the expressway moved in thick red streams. Billboards rose and went past: personal injury, urgent care, Jesus, debt relief, a beauty school promising transformation in eight months or less. Tiana held the page of requirements in both hands like it might still change wording if she watched it hard enough. It did not. At a light near Little River, she saw two girls in fast-food visors smoking behind a dumpster, their laughter bending around the cars. The neighborhood came back piece by piece: botanica signs, chain-link fences, the fried smell from the jerk stand on the corner, men at the bus stop standing farther apart than the shelter required because heat turned other people's skin into extra weather.

When Malik turned onto her block, he lowered the music that had barely been there to begin with.

"Call me after," he said.

"After what?"

"After you find out whether this is a mess or a betrayal."

"That sounds dramatic."

"It sounds specific."

He pulled up half a house down from the stucco place so he would not be in the exact line of sight from the main house windows. Another thing he understood without having to be taught.

The light was on in the apartment. Through the thin curtain she could see movement, one shadow crossing and then crossing back. Delroy's shape, tall and loose even when tired. Micah's voice rose once, laughing at something. Ordinary sound. The house had the nerve to be ordinary.

Tiana kept her hand on the folder a second longer.

"You got the list?" Malik asked.

"Yeah."

"Then go in there with the list."

She opened the car door. Night air hit her damp face. The cut on her hand had reopened in the drive and left a small rust mark on the corner of the page, just beside receipt number. She stepped out with the folder tucked hard under her arm, the day still clinging to her in sweat and printer ink and diesel. Behind the curtain, Delroy laughed again, easy as if paper had not been eating through her all day. The apartment was still moving to a Saturday rhythm she had missed, and the ordinary noise of it made her angrier.

She shut the door and walked toward the apartment with the list in her hand, ready to stop him from laughing.


# Chapter 7

Earlier that Saturday, while Tiana was still out and the apartment still believed in next week, Marcia had spent the money so many times in her head it already felt warm from handling. First and security on a place with a real front door, not a converted garage somebody else could decide to call illegal whenever it suited them. The college money she had not named to Tiana because hope got loud in that apartment, and loud hope turned into appetite. She would clear the light bill and the little late fees that bred in the dark when you let them. The numbers changed depending on which office wanted blood first, but the total never moved: five thousand eight hundred dollars, enough to turn July from a cliff into chores.

Inside her handbag she carried three white envelopes with her block printing in the corners: RENT, SCHOOL, LIGHT. She had folded them flat before leaving, the same way she folded medication schedules for Celia Alvarez, crisp enough that even bad news had to stand up straight. Writing the labels ahead of time felt half like discipline and half like temptation. She had done it anyway. A woman could not wait on miracle and then pretend she had no use for it once it came.

People with checking accounts liked to shelve partner among quaint old-country habits, beside rum cake and airport barrels. Marcia never corrected them. Let them keep their little smile. A hand was not folklore. A hand was what people built when the bank charged thirty-five dollars because a paycheck landed one day wrong, when credit asked for paper the poor could not spare, when cash disappeared too easily inside a marriage or a family or your own hungry head. Twenty-nine names, a hundred dollars every week, and Marcia had carried two numbers because one number was grocery hope. Two numbers could move a family.

Her mother had bought a refrigerator in Spanish Town on partner money, paid the rest of the cycle under a zinc roof that leaked over the sink, and never once called it luck. In Miami, Marcia had used a Christmas hand years ago to buy Tiana's crib without putting one more thing on a store card. Yvette had understood what it meant when crib money and grocery money had to come out of the same sore pocket. Yvette kept the book in blue ink, no pencil, no uncertainty, and called late people by Wednesday before their excuse had time to grow pretty. She ironed even the house dresses she wore to collect money, and one of her eyebrows always sat a little higher than the other; she did not quite believe anybody who said, Next week, sis, for sure. That was the face Marcia had trusted with two numbers.

The fellowship hall at New Testament Baptist had one wall unit that spat cold in short mean bursts and then gave up. The room smelled of Pine-Sol, old paper, and the curry goat somebody had served after Bible study the night before. Not all twenty-nine women were there, but enough had come to make the air thick. Miss Pauline sat with her airport parking visor clipped into church hair, still dressed for a Miami sun that was nowhere near this cinderblock room. Her ankles ballooned above white support hose, and she kept spearmints in a little cough-drop tin she snapped shut like a judge. Nadine had come straight from a night shift in bee-print scrub pants and white Crocs, a crescent dent still pressed into the bridge of her nose from a face mask. Claudette, who hemmed hotel uniforms for cash, kept passing her thumb over a loose stitch at her sleeve, worrying it harder each time somebody checked the door.

Marcia chose a folding chair near the table where Yvette usually spread the notebook, calculator, and stack of rubber-banded envelopes. The table was bare except for a Styrofoam cup sweated half empty and a church bulletin advertising youth choir rehearsal. She checked the clock over the serving hatch. It was ten after two.

"Traffic must've caught her," Nadine said, though her voice already carried a doubt she had not earned yet.

Miss Pauline made a small sound through her nose. "Yvette late is like funeral chicken left over. It happens, but it means something went wrong."

Nobody laughed. Even Claudette, who laughed at almost anything with a shape, only rubbed harder at the loose stitch.

Marcia took out her own notebook, the small black one she kept in the zipped side pocket of her bag. Every page carried dates, amounts, and Yvette's initials beside each payment. She had recorded each hundred the minute she got home, sometimes before she changed out of work clothes, while the apartment still smelled of bleach and frying oil and whatever bass line the neighbors had decided belonged to everybody. There were weeks that second hundred had come from ugly places: utility money, bus money, one of Tiana's paychecks shifted quietly from one future into another. The notebook did not blush. It only held the figures.

At quarter past, Nadine called Yvette and got voicemail. Claudette called next and got the same dead answer. Pauline called and left no message at all, just listened until the beep and hung up with a thumb hard enough to make the screen pop.

"Hospital, maybe," Claudette said. "Franklin's still doing therapy, no?"

"Then you send word," Pauline said. She shook out one spearmint and bit it in half. "If money is waiting in a room, you send word before the room goes bad."

Marcia said nothing. She had seen Franklin once after the stroke, in church, one side of his face gone slack and his left hand resting in his lap like it belonged to somebody he had not yet met. Yvette had sat beside him in a yellow blouse, jaw set, answering for both of them when people asked how he was doing. Marcia had gone home that day with pity for the woman and relief that pity was not yet necessary in her own kitchen. The relief came back now to shame her.

At two twenty-three, Pauline's phone pinged. The sound snapped through the room so sharply that every chair leg stopped moving.

"From her?" Nadine asked.

Pauline's thumb stalled over the screen. In her other hand the cough-drop tin snapped shut hard enough to hush the whispering by the door. "Voice note."

The women shifted in, chairs scraping, bodies drawing close with the intimate greed of people who knew bad news always arrived with details and wanted the details first. Pauline hit play and held the phone above the table.

Yvette's voice came out small but clear. There was hospital noise behind it, the rattle of a cart and a television turned too low to understand.

"Pauline, if everybody's there, play this loud. I can't take twenty-nine calls and say the same thing twenty-nine times. I took money from the hand. I did. Franklin's stroke hit, the mortgage was three months down, and I kept telling myself I'd hold from it for one week and put it back before Marcia's draw. Then rehab wanted cash and the bank pinned another notice on the door and I used more. I know what that is. I know what it makes me. I'm not asking anybody to call me good. I'm saying I wasn't going to let them take my house while my husband couldn't lift his own left hand. I will pay every cent back. I don't know how fast. Please don't come by the house and frighten the girls."

The room held for one stunned beat after the message ended. Then it broke all at once.

"Jesus Christ," Nadine said.

Claudette covered her mouth with both hands. "The girls are there by themselves?"

Miss Pauline slapped the phone down on the table so hard the Styrofoam cup jumped. "A stroke doesn't baptize a thief."

The word thief stayed where Pauline threw it. Nothing in the room reached to dress it up.

Nadine started talking at the same time Claudette did. One wanted the police. One wanted the pastor. Pauline wanted Yvette's sister. A woman by the door, Sonia from the usher board, said she had seen foreclosure take her cousin's house in Lauderdale and it was enough to make any person mad. Pauline wheeled on her fast enough that Sonia stepped back into the hymn-number board.

"Mad at who?" Pauline demanded. "The bank? Good. Be mad at the bank. Why am I supposed to pay for Wells Fargo in the name of Christian sympathy?"

"Because the man had a stroke," Claudette said, and her fingers had moved from the loose stitch to the cheap silver chain at her throat. "Because she must've been losing her mind."

"Everybody in this room joins partner because something is already wrong," Marcia said.

The sentence came out level. It surprised her a little. She had expected fury to arrive hot. Instead it came the way it did when she read dosage instructions to a patient who thought she could charm her way around medicine: level, stripped of comfort, impossible to sweet-talk.

The room quieted just enough for her to keep going.

"Nobody brings easy money into a hand. Every dollar in here already had a job: school, rent, somebody minding a child. If emergency gave you license to raid the box, partner wouldn't exist."

Nadine nodded hard. "Exactly."

But Claudette's face crumpled anyway. "Marcia, I know. I know. I'm only saying I can see the table where she did it. That's all I'm saying."

Marcia could see it too, and that was part of what made her want to break something. She could see Yvette's glass-top dining table under the slow fan, Franklin's pill organizer open beside the fruit bowl, one bank letter after another with language sharpened by people who never had to choose between public dishonor and private foreclosure. Cash bands would have sat on the table, not because Yvette was rich enough to stack money but because cash looked different once it stopped being theory and turned into one more month before the sheriff. Marcia could picture Yvette's hand hovering over the pile, telling herself borrow first, replace later, because every poor person she knew had once or twice survived by renaming theft temporary.

Understanding the decision did not soften it. It only made it uglier.

Pauline hit redial and got sent straight to voicemail. She cursed, low and inventive, then jabbed a finger at the blank screen, aiming the gesture straight at Yvette. "You don't send me apology in MP3 format. You answer."

Sonia had already started crying. She was near the end of the hand and had been planning to use her turn for school uniforms in August. Nadine stood over her with one hand on her shoulder, rubbing circles so fast they looked impatient. Someone near the back asked whether any of the money was left. The room had no answer. Somebody else said maybe Yvette had only borrowed from later turns, not today's. Pauline barked out, "Did you not hear the woman say before Marcia's draw?"

Heads turned, though not all at once. Worse than that, they turned one by one, with that careful human slowness people used when they had just remembered where the sharpest pain in the room lived.

Marcia had known they all understood it was her turn. She had not understood how different knowledge felt from attention. The pity landed first on her blouse, then on her face, then in the softening of bodies around her. Claudette pulled a chair closer. Nadine touched her forearm. Even Pauline's anger tried, for a second, to change shape.

"Marcia," Claudette said quietly. "Lord."

Marcia moved her arm off the table before Nadine's hand could settle there. "Don't."

The room understood her.

She was not proud enough to hate sympathy in general. She had taken casseroles, hand-me-down uniforms, advice she did not ask for. She knew the uses of community. But pity in public was a different thing. Pity in public asked a woman to stand still while other people took note of just how badly she had needed something. It made need communal before relief had any chance to become private.

Nadine swallowed. "All right."

"Play it again," Pauline said.

They did. This time the room listened for proof instead of surprise, and each woman leaned in on a different wound in the message: the mortgage, the stroke, Marcia's draw, the promise to pay every cent back, the girls at the house. Whatever mercy the message held landed in a different place for each woman.

By the time the voice note ended a second time, the fracture had settled cleanly through the room. One side wanted to march to Yvette's house and pull every curtain open until shame climbed out. The other side wanted to call the pastor first, or Yvette's sister, or the hospital social worker, hoping a better chain of adults could reorganize catastrophe into something reputable. Nadine wanted the police until Pauline reminded her there was no bank statement, no formal contract, only notebooks and cash and witnesses who would all sound ridiculous the minute they sat under fluorescent law. That shut Nadine up for almost thirty seconds.

"So what then?" she said. "We just let her eat us?"

"That isn't what I said," Pauline snapped.

"What did you say then?"

"I said the police aren't bringing the money back."

"Neither is church."

"Church can ruin her reputation."

"Reputation doesn't pay FPL."

The argument skidded around the room, hot and useless. Marcia heard only pieces. She kept staring at the table where Yvette's ledger should have been. The church bulletin had soaked up the sweat from the Styrofoam cup until the ink bled through the youth-choir photo. She thought of the three envelopes in her bag, their neat labels waiting for content, and wanted to laugh so badly the feeling nearly made her choke.

Claudette crouched beside her chair. "You all right?"

The question was kind. That made it worse.

"No," Marcia said. "But I'm sitting here."

Claudette gave a helpless little nod. She smelled faintly of camphor and fabric softener, the smell of clothes that had been mended instead of replaced. "If she pays it back piece by piece..."

"Piece by piece is not a draw," Marcia said.

That shut Claudette's mouth for a while.

Pauline started taking names of who had paid what and when, copying from notebooks onto the back of old church programs because outrage loved administration when administration was all it had left. Marcia opened her own notebook only because it gave her hands somewhere to go. She read the dates she already knew: March 8, March 15, March 22. Those were whole weeks of sweat, bus fare counted twice, one pair of shoes reheeled instead of replaced, one light bill delayed, one Saturday lunch skipped at work because hunger was cheaper than pride. The numbers sat there in blue ink, loyal and useless.

Nadine leaned over the table. "You had two shares, right?"

Marcia looked up.

Nadine's face changed the minute she heard how the question sounded. Too late.

"I mean," Nadine said quickly, "I remember Yvette saying you carried two numbers, that's all."

"Yes," Marcia said.

Nadine flinched. Across the room Pauline closed her eyes for half a second. Even Sonia stopped crying long enough to stare.

There it was again, her need passing from rumor into inventory.

Five thousand eight hundred dollars sat in her head like a verdict. It was enough to relocate, enough to put Tiana's future back on rails, enough to buy Delroy time he had not earned. It was enough that the loss itself now made her look foolish, greedy, naive, wrapped in all the little words people offered women after fraud, words that made trust sound like appetite and loss like greed.

She stood before anybody could try kindness a second time.

"Call whoever you want," she said. "Pastor, sister, hospital, all of them. Tell me if money appears. Until then, I need to go home."

"Marcia," Pauline said, and for once she sounded older than sharp. "Let somebody drop you."

"I know the bus route."

"That isn't what I mean."

Marcia pulled her bag closed. "I know."

She left while the room was still deciding whether pity or fury would run the rest of the afternoon. In the hallway the church's back door stuck at the frame and made her shoulder work for freedom. Outside, the heat slapped harder than it had any right to after air-conditioning that weak. Somebody's child was practicing scales in the sanctuary, the keyboard notes slipping through cinderblock one embarrassed at a time.

At the bus stop she took the three envelopes out and looked at them. RENT. SCHOOL. LIGHT. Her own handwriting, calm as a nurse. She held them until the sweat from her fingers softened the paper corners, then slid them back into her bag before anybody waiting beside her could read them and know one more thing.

The ride home was full. A man with paint on his jeans slept with his mouth open. Two girls in crop tops argued about a wig install in voices bright enough to cut the stale air. At every stop Marcia expected the shape of the afternoon to change, for the phone to ring, for Pauline to say they had found some of the money, for Yvette to call sobbing, for God to stop clowning and put something countable back in her hands. Nothing happened. The purse on her lap stayed light.

***

By the time she pushed through the side gate to the stucco house in Little River, the sun had dropped low enough that the landlord's place stretched across the yard in shadow. The gravel popped under her shoes. Somebody up front was frying fish. Bass thudded through a neighbor's wall, then cut, then started again with more conviction.

Inside the apartment, the table fan Delroy had taken apart sat in pieces on the counter, screws lined in a saucer like tiny silver teeth. He was bent over the motor housing in an old Dolphins T-shirt, reading it the way he worked on people, all patience, bluff, and the expectation that a sweet mouth could loosen anything. Micah was at the sink peeling the label off a bottle of ginger beer in one long strip, his headphones around his neck instead of on his ears. Tiana was not back yet. Her work shoes were not by the door, and the room still carried the thin confidence of a place that thought next week existed.

Marcia set down her bag and the small sack of rice she had bought out of habit on the walk from the stop.

Delroy glanced up first. "You get through?"

Marcia went to the sink and washed her hands because she could still smell the fellowship hall on them, Pine-Sol and other people's sympathy. The water came out warm. She rubbed longer than necessary.

When she turned back, both of them were watching.

"The partner broke," she said.

Micah frowned. "What does that mean?"

"It means Yvette used the hand."

Delroy straightened too fast and the fan housing knocked against the counter. "Used it how?"

"She sent a voice note from the hospital. Franklin's stroke, the mortgage, rehab bills, all of it. She said she meant to put the money back before my draw and didn't."

Micah stared at her. "So she just told everybody that and done?"

"That was the shape of it."

For a moment the apartment went quiet. Outside, a car alarm chirped twice and shut up. The apartment's little fridge coughed into its cycle.

Delroy put the fan screwdriver down carefully, which always meant he was trying not to sound as alarmed as he was. "How much are we talking?"

Marcia reached into her bag and laid the three empty envelopes on the table one after another. RENT. SCHOOL. LIGHT.

"Five thousand eight hundred dollars."

Micah's bottle slipped and knocked the sink. "What?"

"That was my turn," Marcia said. "Two shares."

Delroy stared at her. Micah laughed once, one hard unbelieving burst that died before it fully left him.

"You had fifty-eight hundred dollars in that thing?" he said.

"I had two shares in that thing because one share wasn't enough to move a family."

Micah's eyes dropped to the envelope marked SCHOOL. "School like Ti's deposit?"

"It was for her deposit, the housing mess, and the fees that breed the minute a child tries to leave poor. It was for this roof and for the light bill sitting under the sugar bowl. That money already had work."

Delroy dragged a hand over his jaw. "All right. Fine. Fine. Then we go find Yvette."

Marcia almost smiled at the stupidity of it. "Find her where?"

"We go to the hospital first and the house after. If we have to, we drag the pastor in too. She can't send a voice note and disappear."

"Disappear?" Marcia said. "She left a confession with a forwarding address. That doesn't turn into money because you raise your voice in a parking lot."

"So we do nothing?"

"I didn't say that. I said there's no cash tonight. Not from Yvette, not from church, not from anybody with a title and a phone tree."

Micah had stopped peeling the label. He held the damp strip between thumb and finger like a little dead thing. "Damn."

Delroy looked at the envelopes, then at her. "You should've told me it had reached that size."

She turned so slowly he had time to regret the line before she spoke.

"Told you so you could do what with it?"

He opened his mouth and closed it.

"You had another plan?" she asked. "You had hidden money? A landlord who likes us? A bank man waiting on your smile? Tell me now. I'm very ready to hear miracle."

Micah looked away.

Delroy's face tightened. Embarrassment always reached him before anger, just enough to show if you were married to him. "I'm saying a thing that large should've been household business."

"It became household business the minute it died."

"That isn't fair."

"Neither was today."

Micah cleared his throat. "I can call Dre, see if there's anything at the docks."

Marcia did not look at him. "No."

"I'm just saying, I could make a little."

"I'm not filling one hole by digging another under you."

Micah's mouth tightened, but he did not argue again. He dropped onto the bed by the wall and tugged one sneaker loose with the heel of the other, not taking his eyes off the floor.

Delroy pulled a mug from the cabinet and held it without doing anything else. "Give me till Monday. I'll sort something."

The sentence sat in the middle of the room unanswered.

Marcia knew that tone. Delroy used it the way he used a shim under a wobbling table leg, a quick little steadiness that could fool a room till the weight shifted again.

"Don't promise into the air before the girl even comes home," Marcia said.

Delroy frowned. "So what do you want me to say then?"

"The truth. And before Tiana walks in here, I want the true number on this table. Empty your pockets."

Micah finally looked up. "Mine too?"

"Everybody's," Marcia said. "I'm done living off guesses."

Delroy gave a short laugh that had no air in it. "Marcia."

She pulled out a chair and sat. "Now."

The mug touched the counter with a dull ceramic knock. Delroy dug into his jeans and brought out a folded twenty, a ten with one corner gone soft from sweat, three singles, a quarter, and a gas-station receipt. Micah swore under his breath, shoved off the bed hard enough to jar the frame, and turned his own pockets inside out: eight crumpled dollars, two quarters, one bus transfer, a bottle cap. Marcia added the four singles left after rice and bus fare.

Marcia pulled the church handkerchief from her bag, then the notebook, and set them beside the envelopes. Sweat and powder had turned one corner of the cloth gray. She flattened the little pile with both palms, counted twice, and wrote the number at the bottom of the page with her name. Forty-five dollars and seventy-five cents. The ink looked meaner than the truth itself.

"That's the backup?" Micah said.

"That's what this house has till somebody earns more," Marcia said.

Delroy stared at the little spread of money as if the right words might multiply it. "I said I'll fix it."

"Then fix it after she hears the right story," Marcia said.

Delroy picked the mug back up and went to stand by the sink. Micah dropped onto the bed again and shoved one foot halfway into his sneaker without tying it. Marcia left the cash where it was, not tucked into RENT or SCHOOL or LIGHT, too small for any of those names. The notebook stayed open beside it. The handkerchief lay gray at the corner. By the time Tiana came through the side gate, the room had already told on itself.


# Chapter 8

The back light over the stucco house had burned out again, so the path to the garage ran through the damp dark between the bins and the fence, past the smell of wet dirt and old cooking oil. Tiana could hear her family before she reached the door. They were not shouting. That was worse. Marcia's voice was low and exact, the way it got when she was still trying to keep the room from seeing her break apart.

When she stepped inside, the apartment already felt used up. The place looked smaller than it had that morning. The standing fan shoved hot air from one end of the room to the other. Marcia's partner notebook sat open on the table beside three empty envelopes and a church handkerchief gone gray around the edges with makeup and sweat. Her good blouse, the pale blue one she wore when she needed to look like a woman who had never missed a payment in her life, was dark under the arms. Delroy stood by the sink with a mug in his hand like he had been meaning to make tea for twenty minutes and had gotten no further than boiling the water. Micah was on the bed with one sneaker on and one off, phone face-down beside him, trying to look bored so hard it had become its own kind of fear.

Nobody asked Tiana how orientation had gone. Nobody asked where she had been after work. Marcia looked up once, then back down at the notebook as if her eyes could still find a number in there that would turn into mercy.

"Yvette use the money," she said.

She did not say hello. She did not say sit down. Her finger tapped the page where names and weeks marched in careful columns. "Not some of it. All of it. She use it for the mortgage and hospital and whatever else the Lord send through her front door, and now everybody supposed to stand there and clap for hardship."

Delroy shifted the mug from one hand to the other. "Her man catch stroke, Marcia. The woman drown too."

Marcia lifted her face. "Did I say she wasn't drowning? Did I say that?" Her voice was still controlled, but the control had gone thin. "I said she took five thousand eight hundred dollars that was not hers alone. I said she let us dress ourselves and come stand up in that hot hall like idiots."

Micah let out a breath through his teeth. "Five eight?"

"Yes, five eight," Marcia said. "You hearing numbers now? Good. Hear that one."

Tiana set her bag by the door and kept her hand on the strap a second longer than she needed to. Five thousand eight hundred dollars had been the bridge in the house, the invisible one everybody kept walking on because not looking down was the only way to cross. Marcia had talked about it without talking about it for weeks. When the rent got late, when the light bill got slid two days past safe, when Tiana said UF wanted this form or that fee, Marcia's mouth had tightened with the confidence of somebody protecting a date on a calendar. Mid-July was supposed to be the hand, the turn. After that they would breathe.

Now the notebook lay open like a dead thing with neat handwriting.

Tiana felt the old heat from the financial aid office return, the fluorescent buzz, the girl at the desk saying hold and verification and incomplete file as if Tiana had misplaced herself on purpose. The row of plastic chairs came back with it, the fathers in golf shirts, the mothers signing without reading because no form had ever learned to bare its teeth at them. Delroy had told her months ago he handled the renewal. He had told her to stop worrying him. He had told her papers took time.

She looked at him. He would not meet her eyes.

"Where's my receipt?" she asked.

Marcia blinked. "What receipt?"

Delroy finally looked up, and Tiana saw it then, the small recoil in his face, the animal knowledge of a trap he had built with his own hands. "Ti, not now."

"No. Now." Her voice came out flatter than she expected. "The aid office put a hold on my file because my card expired and the parent paperwork wasn't there. You said you sent the renewal months ago. So where's the receipt?"

Micah's head turned from one parent to the other, quick and hungry, like he hated the scene already and still could not stop listening.

Marcia straightened in her chair. "What renewal?"

Tiana kept looking at Delroy. "My resident card. He told me it was being processed."

Delroy set the mug down. "These people take forever."

"Then show me the receipt."

"I said it takes time."

"I didn't ask what it takes. I asked what office you sent it to."

Silence hit the room so hard the fan blades seemed louder. Outside, somebody in the main house laughed at a television. A dog started up down the alley and then gave up.

Marcia's hand flattened on the notebook page. "Delroy."

He rubbed the heel of his palm over his mouth. "Tiana, I was going to fix it."

Her back teeth met hard enough to hurt. Something practical and ugly locked into place inside her, like a drawer slammed shut. "So there is no receipt."

Delroy tried a smile, a weak piece of old equipment. "Baby girl, don't make it sound like I lose your whole life."

"Did you send it?"

He looked at the sink, the hot plate, the warped cabinet door over the microwave, anywhere but the document folder she had left on the table two days ago after orientation and had not had the nerve to open since. "Rent was short."

Marcia said, "What?"

Delroy's head came up fast, as if he had forgotten she was in the room with him. Tiana watched the confession move through him in stages. First came the wish that maybe he could still talk around it. Then came the useless charm. Then came the shame, stripped bare and irritated by the light.

"Back in May," he said. "Landlord was at the gate every damn morning. You was doing the hand. Light was due. Rent was short. I use the money and I said I'd put it back before anybody needed to know."

Marcia's chair scraped the floor. "You use which money?"

"The envelope for the renewal."

"How much?"

"Enough."

"How much, Delroy."

He swallowed. "All of it."

Marcia stood. The table was so close to the bed that her thigh hit the frame. Micah snatched his foot out of the way. Tiana could smell the sweat dried into Marcia's blouse, the stale powder under it, the fried onion from somebody else's dinner creeping in through the window unit.

"You took the money for her papers," Marcia said slowly, "and you decide this in your head by yourself."

"I decide we needed a roof."

"No. Do not start with me like that. Do not make this into you protecting us from weather." The notebook snapped shut under her hand. "You made household math without me. You made promise with her future and never bring it to my face."

Delroy spread his hands. "What you wanted me do, let the man throw we out in the yard in May? Tell Tiana, sorry about college, but at least your forms is on their way while your mattress sit under the mango tree?"

"I wanted you to tell me."

"And with what money you would answer me?"

The room went mean and intimate. Micah looked at the floor. The insult hit Tiana the way a bus seat hit when the wheel dropped into a pothole, not clean shock but impact traveling upward through everything under her.

Marcia laughed once, short and disbelieving. "So that's it. You borrow my hand before it turn. You spend her paper money before the emergency come in full. You make whole plans with money I didn't even know was already gone, then you stand up in here and ask me what money I would answer with."

Delroy's shoulders lifted, dropped. "I said I was going to put it back."

"With what?"

"Work."

"Which work?"

He opened his mouth and closed it again.

Tiana heard herself say, "So you lied because you had no plan."

"I had a plan."

"No, Daddy. You had a wish."

He flinched harder at that than at Marcia's voice. For a second she hated that she noticed. She hated that some part of her still knew the way his hurt folded inward.

Micah sat up. "So what now? Like, what actually now?"

Nobody answered him. Marcia moved one of the empty envelopes aside as if she could not bear to look at it anymore. Tiana stared at Delroy's hands. They were thick-fingered and clean-nailed, with a crescent of white paint still trapped near one knuckle from some small job that had not turned into enough money to matter. Those were the hands that had held her bike upright in the apartment lot, the hands that had tucked a twenty into her book bag junior year when she said she did not need lunch money and both of them knew she did. Those same hands had opened her envelope and spent her paperwork into rent.

"The aid office said they can't finalize anything without current documents," she said. "Not housing. Not the scholarship package. Nothing. I stood there looking stupid because I thought the problem was timing."

Delroy's voice went soft, almost clean. He did that sometimes when he was nearest begging. "Ti, listen to me. You can defer a semester if you have to. Plenty people do that. School will still be there."

UF came back to her as the dorm room she had pictured: cheap desk, cinderblock wall, the ugly little freedom of being somewhere nobody remembered her at twelve or thirteen or last week. He said it like a missed bus you could catch tomorrow.

"Don't tell me school will still be there." She had to stop and start again because her voice had gone sharp enough to cut the words in half. "Don't say that to me like I'm being dramatic about a piece of paper. That piece of paper is the difference between me existing where I need to exist and me staying right here while everybody says next time."

Marcia turned to her, and the rage in her face made room for something worse, a mother's recognition of a harm she had not even known to defend against. "He told you it was sent?"

"Yes."

"For how long?"

"Since spring."

Marcia shut her eyes. When she opened them again, the church handkerchief was in her fist. "You make me stand up in front all them women today with my face washed and my pocket empty, and all this time you had another hole cut in the boat."

Delroy took a step toward her. "Marcia, don't."

"Do not come touch me."

He stopped.

"You think this is just the money?" she said. Her voice had started to rise now, finally losing its manners. "Money is bad enough. Money is blood in this house. But you decide I don't get to know what danger we're in. You decide I must budget blind. Every time there is shame, you push me outside it and call that protection."

"I was trying to hold it till July."

"July come and look what July bring."

Micah muttered, "Damn," under his breath.

Marcia rounded on him. "And you. Wipe that look off your face. This is not free show."

He lifted both hands. "I didn't say nothing."

"You never have to say much. Your face run ahead of you."

Tiana stepped around the chair and took her document folder off the table before Marcia's hand or Delroy's hand or the whole house came down on it by accident. The cardboard was soft at the corners from being carried everywhere. Her name, written in black marker months ago, looked childish now.

"What is the plan?" she asked.

Delroy grabbed at the word plan like it might still float. "Tomorrow I go out early. I can pick up something. I know a man in Hialeah got drywall work coming. I can call around."

"That's not a plan," Marcia said. "That's morning talking."

"What you want from me tonight then? Blood?"

"No. I want the truth in full sentences."

He stared at her. For one ugly second Tiana thought there might be more, another envelope, another hidden hole, but he only said, "Rent was due. I panic. I thought the hand was solid. I thought if I keep the room over our head and put the money back before her card come due, nobody would feel the gap. I was wrong."

There it was, smaller than the lie had been. It was neither noble nor monstrous, just a man kneeling in the cheap church of his own bad judgment.

It did not help.

Marcia pressed the handkerchief to her mouth. Tiana knew that gesture. It was the one before crying or vomiting, whichever came first. Instead Marcia lowered the cloth and said, very clear, "I can't talk to you right now without saying things that'll crack this house wider."

Delroy nodded once, as if he had been offered mercy. Tiana despised him for taking it that way.

"I can fix it," he said, but weaker.

"You can't even name it," Tiana said.

She was at the door before anybody told her not to go. Maybe they knew better. Maybe the room had already used up all the authority it had for one night. The air outside hit her wet and warm. Behind her, Marcia said Delroy's name again, the whole name this time, flat with warning, and Micah said, "Mummy," in a voice he usually saved for ambulances.

Tiana kept walking until the side gate clicked shut behind her and the house stopped being a room and turned back into weather.

***

She made it to Biscayne before she decided not to turn around.

The bus shelter sat in front of a shuttered insurance office with bars over the glass and a plastic palm tree drooping in the dark lobby. Mosquitoes worried her ankles. The bench was tacky with old heat. She could still hear Marcia's voice in her ear, not the words anymore but the force of them, that high hard place anger reached when it had to drag humiliation behind it.

Tiana took out her phone. Her thumb hovered over Malik's name longer than pride liked. Then she typed, `You awake?`

He answered before she could regret it.

`Just got off. You good?`

`You good?` glowed in her hand. If she said no, it would become a story. If she said yes, it would become a lie. She went with neither.

`Need to get out for a while.`

The dots came, disappeared, and came back.

`Stay where you're at.`

The northbound bus hissed up first, then pulled away. The southbound one took seventeen more minutes. Tiana counted them by the changing color of the beer sign in the window of the store across the street as it went red, then dark, then red again. A man with two mops slung across one shoulder smoked beside the pole and never once looked at her. A girl in scrubs fell asleep standing up and woke furious at herself every time her chin dropped.

When Malik finally stepped down from the bus, he had his work shoes looped by the laces over one shoulder and a deli cup tucked under his arm. He wore black kitchen pants and a white T-shirt with a damp collar. His left thumbnail was split down the middle, and he worried it with his teeth while he searched the stop for her, a habit she had teased him about once and which he had not even tried to defend. When he saw her, he stopped chewing the nail and came over without rushing.

"You eat?" he asked.

She almost laughed. "Hi to you too."

"That wasn't a joke."

He held out the deli cup. It held mango, cut in thick rough pieces and dusted with salt and chili. The lid had fogged from the heat. Malik always wanted something sharp to eat after work, something that could push the grease and bleach taste off his tongue. It was such a private, unnecessary preference that it made her want to sit down on the sidewalk and cry.

"No," she said.

"Then eat."

She took one piece because arguing about fruit would have been ridiculous. The salt hit her split lower lip and made her realize how hard she had been biting it.

The bus came. They got on through the front and moved to the back where the air-conditioning worked too well and the windows rattled in their frames. Tiana sat by the glass. Malik dropped into the seat beside her and set the deli cup between them.

He did not ask what happened right away. He let the bus pull out into traffic. Little River slid by in pieces: the dark bulk of a tire shop, a botanica window crowded with white candles, men under a carport arguing over dominoes so hard their hands looked violent even in silhouette. The city at night always seemed to Tiana like it was telling the truth and lying at the same time. Every storefront said closed. Every person outside said the night was still collecting.

Malik leaned his forearms on his knees. "You trying to be somewhere specific?"

"No."

"You trying to not be somewhere specific?"

She turned her face toward the window. "You always do that?"

"Do what."

"Ask the one question that makes lying look stupid."

"Only when somebody asks me to come get them and then starts acting like they don't know why."

His tone landed flat and edged, more anchor than accusation, a refusal to let her disappear into fog. Under pressure, Malik's voice cleaned itself down to the blade.

"My father lied to me," she said.

"About what."

"Paperwork. Money. Same thing here." She scraped chili salt off her thumb with her nail. "He told me he handled my card renewal. He didn't. He spent the money on rent and kept talking like the papers were just moving slow."

Malik nodded once. "And you found out tonight."

"My mother found out too."

"That sounds bad."

She laughed then, once, ugly and brief. "Thank you. Very clinical."

"You don't need pretty words."

The bus lurched around a turn. Her shoulder knocked his. He did not move away. A bright ad panel over the opposite seats promised accelerated training for medical billing and coding, smiling women in scrub tops with the kind of confidence that came from never having had a document hold placed on your whole life.

"I just needed out," she said.

"Out of the apartment?"

"Out of my own head for ten minutes."

He looked at her now. "That all this is?"

There it was, the part of him that kept him from becoming a mattress with opinions. Tiana felt irritation rise because he had every right to ask and she had not wanted him to.

"I didn't say that."

"No, you didn't say much at all."

She sat back against the seat. The cold from the vent touched the sweat between her shoulder blades and made her shiver. "What do you want me to say?"

"Say it straight. You called me because you wanted me, or because I was the first door that wasn't yours?"

The deli cup rolled a little on the vinyl seat between them. He steadied it with two fingers and waited.

Tiana had spent most of her life getting rewarded for composure. Teachers liked it. Clerks liked it. Even white girls with easy mothers liked it, because a careful Black girl gave them something flattering to say at banquets. Composure did not help here. Malik would hear it for what it was, another polished object held up between them so he did not have to touch anything warm.

"Both," she said.

He breathed out through his nose. "Alright."

"You asked."

"I did."

"And I came anyway." Her voice dropped. "That's got to count for some honesty."

He rested his head back against the seat and watched the reflection of the bus lights slide over the window. "It counts. I'm just not trying to be the place you crash because home gets loud and then tomorrow we act like none of this happened."

She looked at his throat, the place where sweat had dried in a pale line. The aid office clicked back into her head, computer keys deciding if she existed in the right category. Her father saying school will still be there scraped through right after. Then there was Malik at orientation, standing outside with her afterward and not telling her to calm down, not once, just handing over his water bottle and waiting while she cursed out the federal government in a parking lot full of nice cars.

"I've been thinking about you anyway," she said.

He turned.

She hated how young the sentence made her feel, how close it was to a confession she would have mocked in somebody else. So she made it uglier, truer. "Not in some clean way either. I keep thinking about your mouth when I'm supposed to be reading instructions. I keep thinking if I could get out of my house and into a room with you, I might breathe different."

The bus noise filled the silence after it. Metal shaking. Someone coughing three seats up. The driver muttering at a car that cut too close.

Malik's eyes stayed on her face. "You hear how that sounds."

"Yeah," she said. "Desperate. I heard it."

"Like you want me to fix your lungs."

Her thumb pressed the salt deeper into her palm. "Like my house got so tight I came looking for air. That's true. So is wanting you."

"I can't do that."

"I'm not asking you to," she said. "I'm asking if I get to want one thing tonight without turning it into another damn case file."

She was tired of defending every ugly piece of it. Mango salt had dried in the lines of her palm.

"I still want you," she said. "That part isn't fake."

His expression changed. The flatness in it gave way to something warmer and more dangerous. "Alright," he said again. "That part I can work with."

They rode another ten minutes without much talking. At a red light, a man in a reflective vest ran across six lanes carrying a Styrofoam container like it held his real name inside. The bus lights made everybody look already tired from tomorrow. Malik took one piece of mango and chewed slowly.

"You can come over," he said at last. "Just don't come over to blank out in my room. If an hour's all you got, say so. If you stay, stay for real."

She nodded.

"And if you want me to take you home after, I'll take you home. I'm not putting a contract on this."

That almost made her smile. "Thank you for your flexible terms and conditions."

"See. Now you're irritating again. Better."

He bumped her knee with his. The bus kept moving south, then west, carrying them through blocks she only half knew, where the storefront churches gave way to dark duplexes and chain-link fences with bicycles tipped against them. She rested her head against the window and let the city jolt through the glass into her skull until the apartment behind her loosened its grip by degrees, not anything so generous as mercy, just distance.

***

Malik rented a room at the back of a low cinderblock fourplex where the paint had given up around the window frames and the hallway always smelled faintly of Pine-Sol and old frying oil. The woman in the front unit kept two hibiscus plants in buckets by the stairs and had tied grocery bags around them against the sun. At this hour the bags whispered in the dark.

Inside his room, the window unit clattered like a drawer full of silverware. A twin bed took up most of one wall. On the floor beside it sat a pair of steel-toe boots, a foam roller split at one end with duct tape, and a stack of folded black work shirts strapped with a rubber band. A legal pad on the milk crate he used as a nightstand held columns of numbers, not poetry, not sketches, just hours, rent, gas, and a list titled `Things my right shoulder is complaining about`, which made her bite the inside of her cheek to keep from smiling. There was a cheap speaker on the sill, a half-empty jar of peanut butter, and a photograph tucked into the frame of the mirror of Malik at about twelve in a soccer uniform too big through the chest, already looking skeptical of the camera.

"Nice place," she said.

"Don't lie in my room. It will start something."

He shut the door and set the lock. The sound tightened the air. The room drew in around them, narrower now, every wall one step closer.

He handed her a bottle of water from the little fridge under the window. She drank half of it too fast. The room was cool enough to raise gooseflesh on her arms after the street, but the cool carried the smell of male detergent, clean cotton, and the ghost of whatever he had cooked around all evening. Onion. Oil. Lemon cleaner.

"You want to talk first?" he asked.

She put the bottle down on the crate. "Not really."

"You want me to shut up then?"

"Maybe for a minute."

He nodded, and for a minute they stood there in the thin mechanical cold with the window unit rattling and the neighbor's television leaking game-show applause through the wall. Tiana should have felt foolish. Instead she felt overfull. The room was small enough that all the wanting in it could not spread out and pretend to be atmosphere.

Malik stepped closer. He touched her wrist first, almost formal, giving her time to flinch if she wanted. She did not. His thumb slid once over the inside of her wrist where the pulse beat under the skin, and then he put his hand at the back of her neck and kissed her.

They had kissed before, but this was the first one with a locked door behind it and nowhere respectable for the feeling to go. Tiana grabbed the front of his shirt and kissed him harder than she meant to. The taste of mango and salt and a long shift came off his mouth. He made a low sound, surprised into honesty, and she felt a mean little spark of satisfaction. She wanted to affect something. She wanted proof.

He pulled back first. "You sure?"

"Don't do that in a voice like a guidance counselor."

"I'm serious."

She looked at him. "I want you. Right now."

His thumb stayed at the hinge of her jaw. "If that changes, you tell me."

"I will."

He nodded once and kissed her again.

They got out of their clothes gracelessly in a room built too small for elegance. Her sandal caught in the hem of her dress and nearly pitched her sideways into the milk crate. He laughed and caught her by the waist before the water bottle went over.

"Damn," he said. "Slow down, college girl."

"Don't call me that right now."

He heard the edge in it and stopped smiling. "Alright."

She took the dress off herself and let it land over the chair with his work shoes. Standing there in her bra and underwear under his blunt little lamp, she expected shame to arrive with a sermon. Exposure came instead, hot and total. Malik looked at her like he had no reason to fake indifference. That steadiness did something to her more dangerous than compliments.

He put both hands on her waist. His palms were rough, warmer than the room. "You beautiful," he said, plain as report.

She nearly ruined it by answering with a joke. Instead she kissed the corner of his mouth and then his throat. His body felt worked on, not sculpted for anybody's gaze but used daily, shoulders tight from labor, stomach flat, a small old scar under his ribs she found with her thumb before she meant to. He sucked in a breath.

"What happened there?"

"Bike. Twelve."

"You were clumsy?"

"You ask too many questions in intimate settings."

"That's rich coming from you."

He laughed against her neck. The sound eased something. She reached behind herself, unhooked her bra, and tossed it toward the chair. His breath caught so sharply she heard it. His eyes dropped to her bare chest and stayed there for half a beat, stripped of every joke he had in him, before he bent his head and took one nipple in his mouth. The sensation hit her so suddenly she had to grab his shoulder. It cracked the whole day open in a register anger had never reached.

The bed protested when he sat them down on it. The sheet was cool from the window unit and smelled like detergent dried too long indoors. Tiana pushed him back and climbed over him before she could start thinking about the fact that she was eighteen and in a back room with a man she had wanted half out of curiosity and half out of terror. His hands slid up her thighs, under the fabric. When he found how wet she already was, he looked at her with an expression so sharp it almost embarrassed her into lying.

"Don't," she said.

"Don't what."

"Don't look proud of yourself."

"I look accurate."

She slapped his shoulder lightly. He caught her wrist, kissed the inside of it, then moved his hand between her legs again, slower now, giving her somewhere to put the heat besides panic. She dropped her forehead against his and let the room narrow to touch: the rasp of his thumb, the stitched edge of his pillowcase against her knee, the machine breath of the air-conditioner. When she came, the surprise was in its shape. Relief was mental. This was bodily, stupid, immediate. Her mouth opened on his name before she had decided to say it.

After, she rolled off him and laughed once into the crook of her elbow because the sound coming out of herself had startled her. Malik lay there grinning at the ceiling, breathing hard.

"You still with me?" he asked.

"Unfortunately."

"Good."

He sat up, opened the milk crate drawer he had rigged under the bed frame, and pulled out a condom. He checked the wrapper by habit, date first, then tore it with his teeth because his hands were shaky now.
