Something about that, the ordinary caution of it, the refusal of fantasy, made Tiana want him more. The moment kept its hard plainness: a thin square of latex in a hot little room where rent was due somewhere for everybody.

When he eased back over her, he kissed her first, slow and careful. She wrapped a leg around his hip and drew him closer. He pushed into her carefully. The first stretch hurt, then changed. She shut her eyes and then opened them again because she did not want this to turn into some soft blur she could later pretend had happened to another girl.

"Look at me," he said quietly.

She did.

The second thrust went easier. By the fourth she was breathing against his shoulder in broken little sounds she would have denied making if anybody else told her about them. Malik kept his face close to hers, jaw tight with concentration, and it was that concentration that undid her, the fact that he was not performing ease. He was there with her, in the work of it.

The headboard tapped the wall. He stopped, glanced toward the neighbor's television, then shifted and put one hand under her thigh to change the angle. The new angle made her bite him, not hard, just enough to leave a white half-moon that colored back in after.

"Shit," he said, and laughed under it.

"Sorry."

"No, you're not."

She wasn't. She pulled him back down. The room gathered heat despite the air-conditioner. Sweat ran from his temple into her hairline. Her calf cramped and uncramped. He kissed her mouth when she got too loud, not to silence her completely, just to take some of the sound and make it theirs instead of the building's. When she came again, it felt almost angry, an aftershock following the day's first disaster. Malik followed with his forehead pressed into the pillow beside her ear, his whole body going rigid for a second before it loosened all at once.

For a while neither of them said anything. The window unit rattled. Somewhere down the hall a toilet flushed. A car alarm started three blocks away and then cut off like somebody had strangled it.

Malik rolled off carefully and tied the condom, practical even now. Tiana watched him, the clean unromantic motions, and liked him better for them. He tossed it in the little trash can lined with a grocery bag and came back to the bed. When he lay down again, they did not touch right away.

That was when the fear arrived.

The fear rose from what she had just made true. His room still smelled like sweat and detergent and the lemon cleaner somebody used on the hallway tile. Her dress was on his chair. Her underwear hung off the bedpost by one strap. The world kept the same face, and something under it had shifted.

"You alright?" he asked.

"I don't know yet."

"Fair."

He turned his head on the pillow to look at her. "What happens tomorrow?"

"I go home."

"That's geography."

Despite herself, she let out a breath that almost qualified as a laugh.

"Do you want me anywhere near that mess?" he asked. "Phone. Car. None of it."

She stared up at the water stain near the corner of his ceiling. That was the new thing, the part sex had changed: his name now belonged near the wreckage at the apartment if she let it.

"Not near the apartment," she said. "My mother has enough fire to look at."

"Alright."

"But keep your phone on."

His expression eased, not into triumph but into something steadier. "That I can do."

"I wanted you before tonight," she said. She touched the scar under his ribs again, lightly. "Tonight just made me stop acting respectable about it."

His mouth shifted. "There it is."

"It's also humiliating."

"Maybe. But I can work with humiliating."

She laughed then, actually laughed, and the sound turned soft by itself. He moved closer and put his hand on her hip, not urgent now, just there. The touch settled her more than it should have. That frightened her too.

"You can stay till morning," he said. "I gotta be up by ten, but that's not morning."

"I should probably go."

"Probably isn't a decision either."

She looked at the room again. The list on the legal pad. The duct-taped roller. The soccer photograph. Malik had a life here that did not arrange itself around waiting for her crisis to come knocking. The fact of that made the bed feel safer than if he had belonged to nobody.

"I'll stay a little," she said.

"Alright."

He reached toward the crate, set a timer on his phone without looking at it, and slid back under the sheet. She did not ask what the timer was for until it went off nearly an hour later and he said, half asleep, "Need to check if the parking lot tow people are out. They got my neighbor twice this month."

Only Malik would interrupt a near-tender night to look out the blinds for a tow truck and curse softly about predatory fees. She watched him do it in his boxers, one hand rubbing sleep off his face, and felt the attachment deepen in a way that had nothing to do with sex and everything to do with seeing the shape of another person's practical worry.

When he came back to bed, she made room for him without thinking. That was worse than anything.

***

At five-thirty the room had gone from night to a colorless gray that made every cheap object look more honest.

Tiana dressed quietly. Her body felt both heavier and more available to her, as if somebody had taken a locked window and forced it open in the middle of the house. Between her thighs there was a dull ache that was less pain than proof. She washed up in Malik's narrow bathroom with its cracked mirror and a bar of soap balanced on a jar lid. When she came back, he was sitting on the bed pulling on his socks.

"I can walk you to the stop," he said.

"You don't have to."

"I know."

He did anyway. Outside, the hibiscus bags clicked in the breeze. The sky over the low roofs had started to bleach, but the heat was already gathering in the concrete. Workers were awake all over the block. A woman in scrubs was scraping frost off the windshield of a car that would be hot in thirty minutes. Somebody's radio played gospel too low to make out the words.

At the bus stop, Malik handed her the last of the mango from the deli cup and a bottle of water he had shoved into his backpack. "Text me when you get in."

"I might be walking into a crime scene."

"Text me if there are bodies too."

She almost told him not to make this sweeter than it was. She almost told him he had no claim on her morning. Both impulses were defensive and both were tired. Instead she said, "Okay."

The bus came with its windows filmed in dawn grime. She climbed on and found a seat halfway back. Malik stood on the sidewalk until the bus pulled away, one hand lifting once and dropping again, more acknowledgement than wave.

As the blocks unspooled toward Little River, guilt moved back into her a piece at a time. She refused the easy good-girl version, the one where sleeping with Malik turned instantly into regret. The guilt came from leaving Marcia in that room with Delroy's confession still sour in the air and Micah trying to joke the walls wider. It came from the fact that while Marcia sat up over a legal pad, scratching due dates into a future that would not stretch, Tiana had been on somebody else's sheets learning the breath she made when pleasure got ahead of caution.

She rested her forehead against the glass. The bus smelled like cold metal, old perfume, and someone's breakfast empanada. Men with lunch coolers got on in twos. A teenage girl in nursing-school scrubs reviewed flashcards under her breath. Biscayne at dawn looked scrubbed and exposed, the pawn shops and churches and closed barber shops all waiting for their signs to mean something again. The city never really slept. It just shifted from one set of laboring bodies to another.

Her phone vibrated once.

`You good?`

She typed `On the way` and sent it before she could turn the answer into something cleaner.

When she reached the side gate, the yard looked indecently ordinary. A pair of damp towels hung over the line behind the main house. Somebody had put out trash. The broken back light was still broken. Tiana stood there a moment with her hand on the latch, the whole night pressed into her skin under yesterday's dress.

She lifted the gate with both hands and eased it shut behind her until the latch answered softly.

Inside, the apartment smelled like coffee boiled too long, sweat gone stale, and the first heat coming back. Marcia sat at the table in one of her house dresses with a scarf tied back from her face. The partner notebook was gone. In its place sat a legal pad covered in due dates and crossed-out numbers. Micah was asleep under the fan with his phone loose in his hand and one foot kicked clear of the sheet. Delroy lay on the bed against the wall under the sheet, eyes shut with the deliberate slackness of somebody pretending sleep could count as absence. His boots were on the floor beside him. He had not gone anywhere.

Tiana set her bag down without a word.

Marcia looked up. Her gaze moved over Tiana's face, her hair, the dress she had worn yesterday and again this morning. Tired recognition settled there, then hardened into task.

"Go wash your face if you need to," she said. "At seven I want your folder on this table. When those offices open, we're calling every number in it, and your father is not stepping over this threshold before I hear exactly how much it takes to fix what he broke."


# Chapter 9

By seven the apartment had its usual morning smell of bleach, old grease, damp towel, and heat beginning again. Tiana sat at the table with a mug of bush tea, her phone face down beside it, and the blue document folder Marcia had made her bring out at seven sharp. The legal pad beside it was already scarred with due dates and copied numbers. Marcia had on one of her house dresses and a scarf tied back from her face, and the sight of her wiping the counter made Delroy feel accused in a way shouting almost never managed. She had a way of cleaning when she was furious that made every object in the room look guilty.

Dawn was still in his ears. He had lain under the sheet and kept his eyes shut while Tiana slipped back through the side gate, one soft click under her hands and then her careful steps past the window unit, past Micah's sprawled legs, past the place on the floor where the linoleum bubbled up like old skin. Delroy knew the sound of each person in that room. Tiana, these days, moved like the house was a test she meant to pass without touching anything. He had not asked where she had been when she set her bag down without a word, and he was not going to ask now. He had no righteous questions left in him.

Micah was still under the fan with one foot out from the sheet. Tiana had changed clothes, but her eyes still looked used, and not from crying. Delroy saw the same shirt from yesterday balled in the laundry bucket. He looked away before his mind started arranging things he did not want to know about his child.

He had twenty-eight dollars in his wallet, a quarter tank of gas, and the old car still starting if he did not ask too much from it. He had been turning the idea over since midnight while Marcia slept stiff with her back to him and Micah breathed through his mouth and the room held everybody's shame like steam. If they stayed in that apartment all day, the house would go back to being what it had been since the partner broke and the paperwork lie came out: a place where every sentence carried a bill or an accusation behind it. He could not fix the money by breakfast. He could maybe buy them six hours where nobody had to look at the walls.

"We're going to the beach," he said.

Nobody answered. The fan clicked once on its loose screw.

Marcia kept wiping. "With which money?"

There it was. Not loud. Exact first.

"Not South Beach. Virginia Key." Delroy made his voice easy. "I've got gas. We have food in this house. The children need some air."

Micah pushed himself up on one elbow. "You said children like we're six."

"You're fifteen and ugly in the morning. That's still child enough for me."

Micah snorted despite himself, but his eyes cut toward Marcia to see if the room allowed laughter.

Marcia wrung the rag over the sink. "What you mean, we have food? You mean the chicken I was saving for tonight? You mean the bread that is already going dry?"

"I mean the same food we'd eat here," Delroy said. "Only we eat it where the wind can touch us."

Tiana lifted her mug and drank without looking at him. "This some kind of apology production?"

The line was flat and educated in the way she used when she wanted to shame him cleanly. Delroy took it. He had earned worse.

"If it was, I'd charge admission. I'm saying let's go before all of you turn into salt fish right here in the room."

"We're already salt fish," Marcia said. "Only dry and expensive."

That almost got him smiling. Almost. He moved around the kitchenette, opening cupboards so he would have something to do with his hands. Standing still made him feel like a defendant. He found the cooler with the cracked white lid, the one missing its side handle, and set it on the floor.

"I can get ice."

Marcia folded the rag over the faucet. "You can get ice with which money?"

"The same money I've got. Marcia, please. I know the arithmetic. I'm not asking you to forget it."

She turned then. Her face was not loud either. That was the trouble. Loud you could answer. This look had measuring in it.

"No," she said. "You're asking us to act like we can rest because you woke up with a plan."

He wanted to joke first, as he always did. He felt the joke come to the front of his mouth and stop there. That habit had carried him through enough rooms that he still trusted it a second longer than he should have.

"I'm asking for one day that doesn't smell like panic."

Tiana's gaze flicked up at that. Micah pulled the sheet off his legs and sat fully awake now.

Marcia looked back at the counter, then at the freezer, then at the front door. Her eyes worked over each one like she could bully a fourth choice into the room. Delroy knew that look too. It was the one she wore when she was lining up all the bad choices and checking whether one of them at least came with a breeze.

"I'm not cooking a feast for your performance," she said.

"Nobody asked for feast."

"Good. Because all I've got is chicken thigh, bread, and some mango that needs to get eaten today."

"That sounds like beach food to me," Micah said.

Tiana let out a small sound through her nose. "Everything sounds like beach food to you."

"Because I understand joy, Sis. Try it sometime."

She finally looked at him, and Delroy saw the old pair of them again, from before school offices and college folders and this month's betrayals sharpened everybody into their own corner. Micah grinned, already half defensive. Tiana tried to hold her mouth straight and could not quite.

Delroy pounced before the room changed its mind.

"Good. Micah, get up and find the folding chairs. Tiana, bring the towels in from the line if they don't smell like rain. Marcia, let me deal with the ice."

Marcia gave him a look that said she had not granted him command of anything. Still, she opened the freezer.

"If this turns stupid," she said, "we're coming straight back."

"Fair."

"And don't go spending gas like it grows in the glove compartment."

"Gas doesn't grow anywhere. That's why the country keeps acting insane."

"Daddy," Tiana said, already tired of him again.

But she stood up.

The work of going turned the room ordinary by force. Micah found one beach chair and one kitchen chair that had to serve as a beach chair because nothing in the apartment matched long enough to remain a set. Tiana brought in towels stiff from line-drying and shook a drift of lint onto the floor. Marcia salted the chicken and heated oil because she would not send her family to water with only bread and sliced fruit, no matter how angry she was. Delroy rinsed the cooler in the yard with the hose and watched a palmetto bug struggle toward shade. The morning was already heavy enough that his undershirt stuck to his back. When he bent to lift the cooler, his right shoulder gave him the old warning twinge, sharp then sulky. He kept moving.

At the gas station he bought two bags of ice, a loaf of cheap wheat bread, and one case of off-brand orange soda with the last decent bills in his wallet. The cashier, a Haitian woman with a silver tooth and reading glasses hanging on a bead chain, looked at the sweating cooler in his trunk and said, "Beach day?"

"Trying."

She gave him a look like she understood trying as a verb. "Go early. Sun wicked today."

When he got back, Marcia had packed the chicken in foil and paper towel and put the mango in a margarine tub. She had also, without announcing it, changed into a blue T-shirt over her swimsuit. Delroy noticed because for all the anger in the room, there was still that. There was still his wife's body under ordinary clothes, still the memory of her on beaches when they were younger and had less money but more nerve.

Tiana came out in shorts and a long white cover shirt over a black one-piece that made her look older than eighteen and younger at the same time. Micah wore trunks and a Heat jersey and carried the speaker like it mattered. Delroy took the keys before anybody could ask whether the toll was worth it. He needed momentum more than permission.

The car smelled faintly of old vinyl, Armor All, and the fries Micah had eaten in it three weeks earlier. The air-conditioning pushed out a sour breath for a minute and then decided to try. Marcia sat in front with her bag on her lap like she did not trust the day enough to put it down. Tiana and Micah got in the back without touching shoulders.

"Nobody brought cards?" Micah asked as Delroy pulled away from Little River.

"You're going to a beach, not prison," Tiana said.

"Same heat. More gulls."

Delroy kept his face on the road and let them talk. Houses gave way to wider streets, then to the causeway where the city opened like a fist. Water flashed on both sides, gray-blue first, then brighter where the sun struck clean. The air through the vents changed. Even inside the car he could smell salt and something green and tidal under it, the sour-sweet rot of sea grass baking back to life.

Micah rolled his window down the last inch. "Damn."

It was only that one word, but Delroy felt the room of the car loosen around it.

Tiana leaned her head against the glass and watched the sailboats out by the basin. Marcia did not say anything for nearly ten minutes, which from her felt as close to surrender as he was likely to get.

At the gate Delroy paid the fee before anybody could ask if he was sure. The ranger handed him a receipt limp with humidity. Virginia Key opened ahead in green and blue layers: sea grape, palms, a strip of white glare where the beach began, and beyond that water moving like something large and asleep. Even the parking lot felt different from home. At home, heat pressed down from walls and engine blocks and other people's breath. Here the wind kept passing over them. Nobody had earned it. Nobody could own it.

Micah was out of the car first. Tiana came slower, then stopped when the wind hit her bare legs under the cover shirt and lifted the hair off her neck. Delroy saw her shoulders loosen before her face did.

He took the cooler and hated the way his shoulder objected. The sand started hot and then gave way under his feet, loose and cooler a few inches down. He set up under a patch of shade cast by a crooked sea grape. Marcia spread the towels with the same capable irritation she brought to everything that still needed doing. Tiana shook out the chairs. Micah had already kicked off his slides and run to the waterline.

"Don't drown before we finish setting up," Marcia called.

"If I do, tell school I was absent for a legitimate reason."

"I'll tell them you were foolish in public."

Delroy laughed. Marcia did not quite, but the corner of her mouth gave a little.

He stripped down to his trunks and went toward the water, rolling his sore shoulder once before he pretended it did not matter. The first wash around his ankles was warm on top and cool below, and then another wave came high enough to slap his calves and bring a thank-you up behind his teeth. He let the salt take it. By the time he reached his waist, the sea had gone cool under the skin of heat. Salt held him up where gravity at home always seemed to remember exactly how much he weighed. He dove forward, came up slick and blinking, ears full, nose burning, sun hard on the back of his neck.

Micah splashed him from the left. "Old man, race you to the buoy."

"You want me to embarrass you in front of these tourists?"

"You already embarrassing me in front of everybody."

They went anyway, not to the buoy because it was farther than Micah had judged, but out to where the water turned from clear green to bottle-dark and the bottom went uncertain. Delroy let the boy win by half an arm and both of them knew it. Micah came up laughing and sputtering.

"You cheated."

"By being stronger? That's not cheating. That's genetics."

"That's delusion."

When they came back in, Tiana was at the edge of the surf with her shorts rolled and her phone held up uselessly against the glare. Micah lunged and flicked water at her knees. She yelped and jumped back.

"Micah!"

"What? Beach baptized you."

"I'll actually drown you."

"In twelve inches of water? You'd have to work harder than that."

He did it again and this time she went after him, half mad, half laughing before she remembered she was not supposed to laugh so easily in this family anymore. The sound came out anyway. She grabbed the speaker from the towel pile and held it overhead while he chased her, yelling about his music. Then she buried one of his slides ankle-deep in wet sand and stood there grinning when he swore.

Delroy stood chest-deep and watched his children become briefly, miraculously, themselves in a way that had nothing to do with crisis. Micah hopping on one foot, Tiana refusing to hand over the slide until he admitted she was smarter than him, both of them brown and sunlit and still young enough to play at war without any official paperwork behind it. It hurt him, how much he wanted to freeze that minute. It also made him ashamed. Wanting one good minute did not erase the things he had done to deserve the bad ones.

Marcia came down at last with her sandals in hand. She did not step all the way in, only stood where the water licked over her toes and around her ankles. The wind pushed her shirt against her body. She closed her eyes once when a cooler wave climbed her shins. Delroy saw the exact second the relief reached her. Her shoulders, always carrying more house than anyone thanked her for, dropped maybe half an inch.

"Cold?" he asked.

"Good," she said, and that one word had more peace in it than anything he had heard from her in days.

They ate late enough that the chicken had gone only warm, not hot, and nobody cared. Grease slicked the foil. Sand got into the mango and they ate it anyway, sweet and fibrous, sucking it from their fingers. Micah made a disgusted face at the orange soda and drank two cans. Tiana sat with her knees up and let the sun dry salt on her shins in pale maps. Marcia finally set her bag beside the cooler instead of in her lap.
Before she took her second piece of chicken, Delroy saw her count what was left with one quick glance. Tomorrow was already on the sand with them, sitting by the cooler with its hand out.

Delroy started telling some story about a beach in Saint Ann when he was a boy, the road there half pothole, half promise, and Micah said, "Every story from Jamaica sounds made up."

"That's because Miami doesn't know how to be beautiful without charging parking."

"They charged parking here too," Tiana said.

"There she go," Micah said. "Miss Fine Print."

"Somebody has to read the receipt."

"You read receipts for fun. That's your kink."

Tiana laughed so abruptly she had to cover it with her hand. "Please don't ever use that word around Mummy again."

"Around Mummy?" Micah sat up and widened his eyes. "Interesting phrasing."

Tiana threw a wad of napkin at him. He ducked and let out that foolish high cackle he used to have before his voice dropped. Delroy laughed too hard, partly because it was funny and partly because he had not heard them bounce off each other like this since before the school hearing.

Marcia looked from one to the other and shook her head. "All two of you need is water and bad sugar."

"And excellent parenting," Delroy said.

"Don't push your luck."

He held up both hands.

It was when the afternoon had thinned into that drowsy, expensive-feeling hour, everybody salted and slower and almost fooled into believing the day had taken, that Marcia's phone started buzzing inside her bag.

She frowned before she even saw the screen. Work never called with good news on a day off. Delroy watched her wipe chicken grease on a napkin, dig the phone out through towel sand, and tip the screen against the glare. Elena's name flashed white across the glass.

"It's Elena," she said.

Marcia's fingers cinched around the phone. Out in the shallows Micah stopped kicking at the foam and turned. Tiana set her soda down so carefully the can gave only one thin click against the cooler lid.

She got up with sand stuck to the back of one thigh and walked a little away from the towels out of habit. Work calls always pulled a person a few feet out of family life. Delroy saw her turn her back to the water and press two fingers into the free hand she kept at her side.

"Hello?"

He could hear only Marcia's side at first. The wind kept stealing half the words.

"Yes... Yes, this is a good time." A pause. "Oh."

Just that. Oh. Small enough that if he had not been watching her face he might have missed the way it emptied.

She listened. The children quieted without being told. Even Micah knew the difference between ordinary interruption and the kind that made grown people straighten.

Elena's voice crackled tinny from the phone when the wind shifted. It was clipped and controlled, the same kind of voice that wore linen in grief and still found the right envelope. Delroy had met her only twice, but he remembered the way she never wasted a syllable even when thanking somebody. He caught fragments now: "this morning," "hospice nurse," "very peaceful," "wanted to let you know myself."

Marcia said, "Was anybody with her?"

Another pause.

"I see."

Her eyes went to the water then, not looking at it so much as through it. Delroy got up and walked toward her, not too close. He did not want to arrive like a man claiming rights he had misplaced.

Elena kept speaking. Delroy heard the rustle of what might have been paper on the other end, or maybe one of those thin expensive bracelets women wore because they never washed dishes in them.

"No," Marcia said. "No, I understand. Thank you for calling me."

Then, after another beat, and quieter, "Yes. If you can do that, I'd appreciate it."

She listened one last time, said goodbye, and lowered the phone. A thin wash of water reached her feet and dragged back, leaving wet sand pasted over her toes before she put the phone in the bag.

"Marcia?"

She looked at him with the blank surprise of somebody remembering he was attached to the day.

"Celia dead," she said.

He had known before she said it. Still, the words landed heavy. The old woman had been dying for months, maybe longer. That did not make the sentence lighter. He pictured the little tight mouth, the dyed hair Marcia complained about fixing, the way Celia had once told him in her front yard that Cuban coffee in Miami was getting worse because everybody had become lazy. He had liked her for that, though liking was too big a word for what employers and husbands of workers mostly did with each other.

"Lord," he said, because there was nothing smarter ready.

Marcia laughed once, ugly and brief. "Don't start with Lord."

"I wasn't preaching."

"Good. Don't."

She moved past him toward a crooked sign near the dunes and sat on the low wooden border around it. Delroy stayed standing until she looked up like she would tolerate him there if he did not say anything foolish. Then he sat a little apart, sand grinding under his thighs.

Out by the towels, Tiana had one arm around her knees. Micah stood in the shallows, looking at them and then away, old enough now to know when to keep his distance but not old enough to hide how badly he wanted instructions.

Marcia took the phone out again, looked at the blank screen, locked it, unlocked it, then put it back.

"She went this morning," she said. "Elena said the nurse was there. Elena was on the way. By the time she reached, they had already cleaned up half the room."

Delroy waited.

"She hated people seeing her mouth open when she slept." Marcia stared at the water, trying to rebuild the room in her head with the bed already stripped halfway bare. "Always saying, Marcia, don't let me lie there looking common."

The imitation brought the old woman up whole: sharp, painted, annoyed even by her own body failing.

"I'm sorry," he said.

"I know."

She rubbed her thumb hard over the side of her forefinger, the way she did when soap or bleach had dried her skin raw. "You know what Elena said after? She said she'd make sure the agency processed my remaining hours and somebody would put my scrub jacket aside for me."

There was no self-pity in her voice. That made it worse.

"That woman," Delroy started.

"No." Marcia shook her head. "She wasn't cruel. That's the ugly part. She was trying to do correct. She said she knew how much I cared for her mother. She said Mama always asked if Marcia was coming. She said it all decent."

She looked down at her own hands. The knuckles were a little swollen from work and bleach and carrying everybody.

"But that's how it goes. You wash somebody's backside six days a week. You trim their nails when the daughter can't bear to look too long at the yellowing. You know which swallow means pills going down and which one means they tuck it under the tongue to spit out later. You know where the good bras are and which nightgown will make them feel less dead before the dead part arrives. Then they die, and by afternoon you're payroll again."

The wind pressed her shirt flat. He could smell salt and sunscreen and, faint under it, the fried chicken grease still on their fingers.

"Marcia."

"Celia used to cuss me if I put the pale lipstick on her. She wanted coral. Coral, in that bed, with the oxygen rubbing her cheek raw. Stubborn old woman." Her mouth trembled and hardened around it. "And she had money enough to be vain all the way down. Good for her. I mean that. Good for her. But don't tell me not to count the work in it too. I'm not one of those people who can afford grief with no arithmetic."

He put his hand near hers first, not on it. When she did not move away, he rested it lightly over her knuckles. Her skin felt hot from the sun.

"You don't have to split them apart," he said.

"Have to? I don't have to. It just comes together." She breathed in through her nose. "I liked her. That's the stupid thing rich people count on. They let you in near enough to care and not near enough to belong. That old woman knew my children's names. She knew when my shoulder was acting up before you knew. She knew if I had been crying because my face went shiny right here." Marcia touched the crease beside her nose. "She also paid me because I changed her diapers. Both are true."

Delroy looked out toward the water where Tiana had gotten up and was now pretending to rearrange the towels. The beach around them kept going. Somebody's speaker farther down was playing old dancehall. A little boy ran past with a plastic shovel. An airplane moved low over the bay, caught the sun silver, then went ordinary again. It offended him, how the world could keep its same volume.

"Do you want to go there?" he asked.

"Now? For what? To stand in the back with the staff and watch the family cry where the furniture is too expensive for my wet clothes?" She shook her head. "Maybe tomorrow. Maybe if Elena asks. Today I only know that tomorrow morning my bus route to Coral Gables belongs to nobody."

He heard the job loss inside that more clearly than if she had said wages. He also heard the part underneath it: the vanished routine, the woman whose body had structured Marcia's days, the house where she had been both invisible and necessary.

"We'll figure the work," he said.

Marcia turned to him then, not angry, which almost hurt more.

"With what?" she asked. "Tell me with what, Delroy. I'm not trying to insult you. I'm asking with what."

He had no answer that did not sound like his old tricks wearing a fresh shirt.

"All right," she said after a second, because she had seen the emptiness in his face. "Leave it."

That was worse than blame. Blame still expected something from him.

Back at the towels, Micah asked, "Mummy all right?" in a voice too casual to be real.

"Celia died," Tiana said before Delroy could answer, her voice flat and exact. "The woman she works with."

Micah's expression changed. Delroy watched the boy make the adjustment from not understanding to understanding enough. "Damn," he said softly.

Marcia picked up the cooler handle. "Pack up."

Nobody argued. The day folded fast after that, chairs collapsing, wet towels slapping sand, sunscreen bottle rolling under the seat. Delroy loaded the trunk while his shoulder burned cleanly now, no longer warning but statement. When he shut the trunk he looked once at the water. The relief was still there for other people. It had not lied. It had only been temporary, which was not the same thing.

The drive home narrowed to the blinker, Micah's sleep whistle against the window, and the hiss of tires over the bridge. Tiana watched the causeway go backward and kept pressing her thumbnail into the damp label on her soda can until it peeled up in tiny curls. Marcia held Celia's house key in her fist all the way to Little River. Delroy drove and felt the old appetite rise in him, the stupid masculine hunger to do something hard enough that maybe the room would look different afterward.

At home Marcia put the cooler in the sink and stood there longer than washing required. Then she changed clothes without shutting the bathroom fully and came out in a dry skirt and blouse, pulling on the version of herself that still knew how to think in straight lines.

"I'm going to call the agency in the morning," she said to no one and everyone. "And I need to see if Elena wants me there tomorrow."

Tiana asked, "Do you want me to come with you?"

Marcia shook her head. "Not unless I say so."

Micah leaned against the fridge. "That the old lady who used to send the Cuban crackers?"

Marcia's mouth moved. "Yes."

"She was funny."

"She was difficult."

"Funny too."

Marcia sat down at the table with the house key, a packet of tissues from her bag, and her phone. Delroy saw her looking not at the screen but at the nick in the brass key where years of being shoved into the same front lock had shaved it thin. He knew that look. She was not crying yet because crying took privacy and the apartment charged rent for every inch of it.

He could not help in there. Not with words. Not with touch. Not with promises, which had become a kind of debt in his mouth.

"I'm going out for a minute," he said.

Marcia did not ask where. That stung too.

Outside, the evening had fallen into that Miami half-dark where the air stayed warm and damp enough to wear. Somebody two houses over was frying fish. Bass thudded from a passing car and went away. Delroy stood by the fence first, hand on his hip, and stared at nothing until doing nothing started to feel like a sin.

He took out his phone and called Claude.

Claude picked up on the fourth ring with domino noise in the background, hard tiles kissing harder tile, men arguing over points as if numbers had mothers.

"Star," Claude said. "You remember I exist now?"

Claude always sounded like he had just bitten into a joke and expected the rest of the room to do its share. Delroy pictured him exactly: cap pushed back, eyes too knowing, that little crescent scar beside his mouth whitening when he grinned.

"I remember debt regular enough."

"Good. Debt likes attention. What you need?"

Delroy looked back at the lit square of his own window. Tiana's shadow crossed it, then Micah's.

"That demolition work," he said. "You still have space?"

Claude went quiet long enough to make the dominoes behind him seem louder.

"I got space," he said. "Question is if you have shoulder."

Delroy rolled the arm once and felt the catch like a rusted hinge.

"I got enough."

"Enough for a real site or enough for talk?"

"Claude."

"No, I'm asking proper. This is not patching drywall for some white woman with a yappy dog. This is dust, stairs, concrete, men young enough to believe their knees love them. You come, your four hundred starts paying back from that same day. If you break, you break on your own time."

There it was. Friendship itemized, same as always. Delroy almost loved him for the honesty.

"When?"

"Monday. Five-thirty. Old medical building off Twenty-Seventh. Bring gloves if you have them. Bring boots if you still own a pair worth anything. And don't come with no heroic foolishness. Foreman don't pay for style."

Monday. Too soon to heal anything. Soon enough to look like movement.

"I'll be there."

"You sure?"

Delroy listened to the house behind him, the muffled clink of Marcia setting a glass on the table, Micah laughing once at something Tiana said, the small sounds of people still living inside trouble because stopping was not on offer.

"I'm sure."

Claude exhaled through his teeth. "All right then. If you disappear, don't make me come collect in front of your woman. I hate domestic theater."

"You love domestic theater."

"Only when I'm not in it. Monday, star."

The line clicked off.

Delroy stayed by the fence a minute longer with the phone warm in his hand. His shoulder had started to throb from the day, from the cooler, from swimming harder than he should have because for half an hour the water had made him feel younger than the truth. He lifted the arm once to test it and pain ran from the joint down to the elbow bright as a wire. He let it drop.

Claude would start taking the four hundred back the minute Delroy showed up Monday. It would be dust, stairs, concrete, and men young enough to trust their knees. If the shoulder tore all the way, it would tear on Delroy's own time, for free. He stood there and took the full shape of that before he put the phone away.

Inside, Marcia had turned on the small lamp by the table. The apartment looked almost gentle from the yard, which was another lie buildings told. Delroy went back in.

Micah was in the shower. Tiana was drying the cooler with a dish towel. Tiana's blue document folder sat under the edge of the rice tin to keep it away from Micah's elbows, one split corner lifting in the fan. Marcia sat with Celia's key beside her hand and a notepad pulled close, already writing columns: agency, rent, landlord, church sister who knew somebody at Jackson, numbers turned into a ladder because she did not know any other way to climb. When she stopped, it was only to press the heel of her hand against her eyes before the pen went down again.

He filled a glass from the tap and set it by her elbow without interrupting her math. He did not tell her about Monday, or Claude taking his cut from the first hour, or the old medical building off Twenty-Seventh, or the way his shoulder had already begun to spit heat down his arm.

Delroy went to the closet and pulled out his old work boots. The leather was cracked white at the bend and one lace had been knotted back together three times. He set them by the door with the toes turned toward the wall.

Marcia glanced at the boots, then at him.

"You found something?"

He kept his face plain. "Maybe."

Her eyes held on him for a beat, measuring whether maybe was worth the space it occupied. Then she went back to the page.

"Wake me if you leave early," she said.

"I'll try."

That too might have been a lie. He had not decided yet.

He sat on the edge of the bed and listened to the shower run, to Tiana humming under her breath without knowing she was doing it, to Marcia turning pages in the notepad, hunting the first number that would finally behave. Salt dried on his skin. Sand scratched the backs of his knees. His shoulder pulsed with its own weather.

By the door the boots looked meaner than he remembered. He left them there, set the alarm for 4:18, and put the phone face down in the dark.


# Chapter 10

The phone buzzed face-down at 4:18, and Delroy trapped it under his left hand before it could rattle a second time. The old work boots he had set by the door the night before waited with their toes to the wall. Heat still sat in the garage apartment's block walls, along with the bleach sting from yesterday's mop water and the old frying oil that never quite washed out of the curtains. He dressed in the dark until the shoulder made that impossible. Then he clicked on the stove light and worked his right arm into a clean shirt one inch at a time, jaw locked, the cuff brushing the enamel sink because he could not lift higher without the whole side of him going hot and mean.

On the table, under the edge of the rice tin so Micah would not knock it down, lay Tiana's document folder. It was blue plastic, split at one corner, tabbed with index-card strips cut so straight they looked measured. Inside were a birth certificate copy, school letters, and aid printouts. The stack had the neat, desperate look of something that had already been asked to prove too much. Delroy kept his eyes on it long enough for the pain in his shoulder to get mixed up with the other thing, the older thing, the one that had not stopped working on him since the night he admitted where her document money had gone and tore the house open. He had taken money from that future and turned it into rent, light, groceries, bus fare, into days bought one at a time. It had been necessary at the time and shameful after. Both truths could sit in the same chair and stare at him.

Marcia spoke before he heard her move. "You leaving now?"

Her voice came from the bed, flat with tiredness rather than sleep. She had not been resting anyway. Celia had been dead less than two days. Grief and money had crowded the room so completely there was no good corner left to lie down in.

"Claude said five o'clock pickup," Delroy said.

Marcia turned over. In the stove light her face looked smaller and harder, the skin under her eyes a little darker than yesterday. She had slept in the church T-shirt she used for housework, one hand still under the pillow like she had been searching there for a number that might save them. "Claude says plenty."

"It's one day."

"One day can still mash up a man."

He reached for the boots by the door. The motion dragged a line of pain from the back of his neck to the point of the shoulder, but he kept his mouth easy. He joked first. That was the old habit and the only one he still trusted. "If one day could mash me up, Miami would've finish with me long time."

Marcia pushed herself up on one elbow. "Don't sweet-mouth me this morning. If that arm isn't right, say it plain."

He sat on the chair to lace his boots because standing on one leg had become something his body sometimes argued with now. "It's right enough."

"Right enough for what?"

He opened his mouth to answer. Rent came up first, then food, then Tiana's new look: mouth locked, eyes gone distant, all the softness burned out of her the night he admitted where her document money had gone. The words jammed behind his teeth. He shrugged with the left shoulder and kept his eyes on the knot in the lace. "For a day."

Marcia followed his glance to the blue folder on the table. She did not look at him when she answered. "Bring back money, Delroy. Don't bring back story."

That landed cleaner than if she had shouted. He nodded once, because anything else would have sounded like pleading, and stepped out into the dark little yard where the air felt like wet cloth laid over his face.

Little River was awake in pieces. A rooster somewhere behind the stucco wall carried on like it had a stage. Bass leaked thinly from a car turning the corner. Somebody had already started frying something heavy in old oil. Delroy locked the door softly, not because the neighborhood was quiet but because softness was the only kindness he had handy, and walked to the avenue with the shoulder pinned still inside the shirt, building his whole stride around not letting it swing.

Claude's pickup waited in the gravel lot behind a closed tire shop, tailgate chained with blue rope where the latch had gone bad. Claude stood beside it in a pale green fishing shirt too clean for the hour, shaking clove lozenges out of an old film canister into his palm. He had the same laugh lines as always and the same habit of making every arrangement feel casual until money came into it and the edges sharpened.

"Look who decide to be a working man again," Claude said. He tipped two lozenges into his mouth, crunched them, and held the canister out. "Want one? Helps with dust."

"Dust isn't here yet."

"Dust is always here. It just changes address."

Delroy took one. The clove hit sweet first, then medicinal. Around the truck, six other men waited with lunches in grocery bags and shirts already damp at the spine. A compact Honduran man with a gold wedding band looped on his bootlace instead of his finger kept tightening the knot, then checking it with two fingers at his throat, not trusting the tie to hold. A kid in a Buc-ee's cap, too young for the gray at his temples but wearing it anyway, checked his phone between every sentence because his girl's water had broken sometime after midnight and he was waiting for an auntie to text. Nobody here looked built for spare dignity. Everybody had packed enough of it to get through dawn.

Claude clapped Delroy on the left shoulder, mindful enough to miss the right. "You good for machine today?"

The real question sat under everything: did Delroy's body still qualify as something worth loading into a truck?

He made his face insulted. "What kind of question is that?"

"The kind I ask because if I tell Manny I brought him a man for the skid and the concrete breaker, I need that man to still be a man at ten o'clock."

Delroy rolled the bad shoulder a quarter inch and stopped before it showed on his face. "I can run machine."

Claude watched him a second too long. In the labor yard months ago he had seen enough to know the lie's shape. But Claude was a middleman because he knew when to hear bad truth and when to leave it alone. He looked down at his phone, thumbed a message, and said, "Then don't make me look stupid."

"You ever look stupid, is by your own effort."

That got a laugh out of two men. Claude liked a room better once it had given him something back. He jerked his head toward the bed of the truck. "Come on then. We're gutting an old fourplex off Seventy-First. Bathrooms, kitchen walls, little back slab. Owner wants it ready for whoever's coming behind us to make it luxury for people who call mold character."

The men hauled themselves into the truck bed or squeezed into the cab and the bench seat somebody had bolted along the side. Delroy climbed more carefully than he wanted anybody to notice. The truck pulled out, and his shoulder had already started its deep, familiar muttering, the warning noise it made when a day was going to cost more than it paid.

Miami before sunrise had a brief softness to it that Jamaica mornings never needed. Here the roads had to cool off from yesterday's punishment before the city could pretend to forgive itself. They crossed under a rail line, passed a bodega where a woman in a pink bonnet hosed the sidewalk in the dark, and then the light came up mean and quick, silver on the warehouse roofs, exposing everything half-finished.

Claude kept one elbow out the window and talked over the engine about the job. Manny wanted speed. The owner wanted no neighbors calling the city before the walls were down. There were hard hats in a crate, but nobody should expect one that matched his head. If a pipe still had water in it, cap it with your thumb and call for help. If the little Bobcat started sticking in reverse, slap the right lever, not the left, unless you wanted to kiss the chain-link fence.

"And wet the cut before you saw," Delroy said.

Claude glanced over. "That's if anybody remembered a hose."

"You telling me they didn't?"

Claude clicked his tongue. "I'm telling you what kind of morning it is."

The tires crunched into the site, and Delroy felt the lie settle deeper into him. It was not a big theatrical lie. It was the everyday one poor men carried to work sites and interviews and landlord offices: yes, I can lift that. Yes, I can stay late. Yes, I can make it on what you're paying. Yes, this body will hold because I have told it to.

The fourplex had once tried to look cheerful. Mint paint clung to the cinder block in sheltered squares. A sign over the front walk still said PALM COURT APARTMENTS, though only PALM C URT remained. The back slab was cracked into islands around an old rusted barbecue pit. Somebody had already punched out most of the windows. Concrete dust coated the hibiscus leaves at the edge of the lot gray as flour.

Manny came out of the shadow of the dumpster carrying a Spider-Man lunch box whose red had faded almost orange. He wore mirrored safety glasses and had a clean line of sunscreen rubbed only halfway into the ears. Somebody at home had told him to put it on, and he had obeyed just enough to say he listened. He did not look like a man who owned anything here except the clipboard in his hand and the authority to make other men's bodies hurry.

"Claude," he said. "You brought me seven?"

"Six and Delroy." Claude grinned like the joke was too obvious to explain.

Manny snorted once. "Which one is Delroy?"

Delroy raised his left hand.

Manny's eyes went straight to the set of his shoulders, the old-site appraisal that measured age, mileage, and risk in one look. "You run machine?"

"Yeah."

"Concrete breaker too?"

The smart answer would have been something smaller, like drywall, cabinets, or debris sorting. But the smart answer was not what had dragged him out of bed before dawn.

"Yeah," Delroy said again.

Manny looked back at Claude. Claude's mouth barely moved, but the movement meant what it needed to mean: he'll do. In that small movement, Delroy sold the part of himself he could not actually spare.

Manny handed out dust masks from a torn box. There were four for seven men. The straps on two were snapped and tied back together with blue painter's tape. "Use these till they die," he said. "Hard hats in the crate. Watch the live wire in unit three till Tito kills it. Keep the debris moving. Owner wants that slab gone before lunch."

There were no harnesses, no proper respirators, and no water line run out for the saw. Men in cracked hard hats and taped masks were supposed to make that enough.

Delroy took a cracked yellow hard hat and a mask that smelled faintly of somebody else's sweat. Claude saw him looking at the breaker, the heavy electric jackhammer leaning against a wall like a threat with a cord attached.

"Use your left where you can," Claude said quietly.

"You getting tender on me?"

"I'm getting practical. Different disease."

Then Manny shouted for the first load and the morning became noise.

* * *

By eight o'clock the inside of Delroy's nose tasted like wet cement and old paint. They had started in the back units, ripping out vanities swollen from leaks, prying tile off bathroom walls, feeding busted cabinet doors and lengths of pipe down a plywood ramp into the dumpster. The dust mask turned damp and useless almost immediately. Sweat ran from his scalp into his eyebrows and carved pale lines through the powder on his face. Each time the breaker bit into the slab, the vibration ran up both arms and lodged under the right shoulder blade like a bolt being tightened there.

He learned the job's shortcuts in the order they threatened him. Manny did not shut power fully before sending men into the walls. The cut water line in unit two was plugged with a pair of vise grips and hope. The plywood ramp flexed under loaded wheelbarrows because nobody had braced it right. The Bobcat's reverse stuck exactly the way Claude had said, with a short ugly jump before it obeyed. When the saw came out to score the patio, they had no hose, so the blade screamed through dry concrete and threw a gray cloud over the whole yard thick enough to erase a man's mouth while he was still talking.

Every shortcut shaved a minute and pushed the risk closer to skin.

Delroy worked the breaker first, knees bent, weight spread, the machine punching through the slab in stuttering bursts. He had used better ones on better crews. Back in Jamaica, road jobs had their own foolishness, but at least the foolishness did not dress itself up. Miami put a vest, a clipboard, and a contractor's sticker over the same gamble and called it professional.

The first time the shoulder slipped, it was almost nothing. A hot skip under the skin when the bit caught rebar and kicked sideways. He let go with the right hand for a breath, shook it out low where nobody would see, then set back in.

The Honduran man with the wedding band on the bootlace came up with a wheelbarrow of busted block. Up close Delroy saw that the man had written two little girls' names in marker on the inside brim of his hard hat. Sofi and Alma. The sweat had blurred the letters but not enough to lose them.

"You want switch?" the man asked.

Delroy wiped his mouth with the back of his glove. "You saying I look bad?"

"I'm saying I know that face."

"Name?"

"Fermin."

"Delroy."

Fermin tipped his chin at the breaker. "Five minutes each. Better than hospital."

That was the closest thing to kindness most job sites offered: not pity, not comfort, just somebody adjusting the work so maybe you got to go home with one more useful piece still attached.

They switched. Delroy took the wheelbarrow and immediately regretted being proud. The ramp up to the dumpster was slick with dust and broken tile grit. The load wanted to drag left. He compensated with the right arm and felt the shoulder light up again, a warning electric and thin. He kept walking. In the dumpster Manny wanted the cinder block stacked to one side, metal to the other, because salvage brought back a little money and owners always respected a man more when he could make profit out of ruin twice.

The kid in the Buc-ee's cap worked beside the Bobcat, tossing splintered studs clear of the bucket. Between loads he checked his phone with gloves on, awkwardly, then tucked it back into a zip bag inside his shirt. He was waiting for the baby and for news while breathing dust under a cracked hard hat. Delroy liked him for that and resented him a little too, the way older men sometimes resented youth for still having emergencies that sounded like beginnings.

By ten the shirt on Delroy's back was salt-stiff. His hands had gone numb from the breaker. His right arm no longer lifted cleanly above his waist without a lag, like it had to be convinced each time. Claude drifted in and out of the yard, checking another crew across the street, answering calls, hauling a bundle of contractor bags from one site to the next. He was not the boss, but every time Manny barked an order Claude turned it into something men would still climb onto.

Manny came around the side of the building with the clipboard and no patience left. "Need this patio opened up now. Inspector might come by after lunch."

Claude pointed at the score lines. "That cut's too shallow."

"Then break it deeper."

"With what time?"

"With the time we're wasting talking."

Manny walked off before the sentence finished. Claude looked at Delroy, at Fermin, at the kid with the phone. Something almost like apology crossed his face and disappeared. Then he clapped his hands once. "All right. We break, load, clear. Delroy, take the breaker. Fermin, barrow. Junior, keep clear of the edge when the slab starts to move."

Delroy took the breaker back.

The patio slab ran behind the units in one cracked strip, maybe eight feet wide, poured years ago without enough steel in it and patched twice since. Roots had pushed up one corner near the barbecue pit. The saw cuts looked pale and shallow, more suggestion than plan. Delroy set the bit in the line and leaned.

The machine hammered his wrists, his elbows, the sockets of his teeth. Dust came up so hard it coated his tongue. He tasted concrete, rust, yesterday's coffee turning sour in his stomach. Fermin rolled the loosened chunks back in loads, boots slipping, one hand always lifting to touch the bootlace at his throat where the ring sat. Junior worked the Bobcat now, following Manny's shouted directions with the frightened concentration of somebody driving expensive damage in a lot full of cheap men.

Delroy kept waiting for the shoulder to fail cleanly, with a clear announcement. Bodies almost never gave you that courtesy. They gave you a dozen warnings and then made you act surprised.

The first section broke loose near the barbecue pit, and they loaded it. The second section broke jagged and left rebar teeth sticking up. Delroy had to use both hands to lever the breaker clear when it jammed. Something shifted in the shoulder then, a wet little click, and the whole arm went light for half a breath. He dropped the tool, flexed the fingers, picked it back up before Manny could turn around and see.

Claude caught that much. He crossed the lot fast, wiping sweat with the hem of his shirt. "Leave it."

"One more pass."

"I said leave it."

But Manny was already yelling from the porch of unit three, "Need that path open. Dumpster swap's at noon."

Claude looked toward him, then back at Delroy. Manny had already turned away, making the choice small and fast. Claude swore under his breath. "Fine. Two minutes. Then off it."

Two minutes was enough.

Junior had the Bobcat too close to the patio edge. Anybody looking straight at the slab could see it. The bucket nudged a section Manny wanted loosened and the cracked strip shivered all the way down its length. Fermin shouted something in Spanish. Delroy turned, breaker still running, and saw the section under Junior's near tire crumble faster than the machine could back off.

Everything after that happened in pieces that refused to line up into one smooth memory. The Bobcat jerked. A barrow half-loaded with broken concrete tipped toward the kid. Fermin jumped clear and lost one boot in the dust. Delroy let go of the breaker with his left hand and threw his right arm out to catch the wheelbarrow handle before the whole load went over onto Junior's legs.

The shoulder tore with a deep, sickening pop, not loud in the air but loud inside him, like a heavy staple ripped through wet cloth. Heat flooded his neck. The arm stopped belonging to him. The wheelbarrow slammed sideways anyway, concrete chunks exploding over his shin and the patio. Delroy went to one knee, then both. The breaker hit the slab and rattled in a wild circle until Fermin kicked the cord free.

For a second there was no pain, only the fact of wrongness. His right hand hung at an angle it had no business hanging. Dust settled into the sweat on his chest. Then the pain arrived whole, huge and bright, a pressure so total it made the yard narrow to one rectangle of white sky over the broken patio.

"Fuck," Junior said. He was standing, both legs under him, one shin cut open and gray with dust. "Fuck, fuck."

Claude crouched in front of Delroy. Up close he smelled of clove and diesel and the expensive deodorant he used when he wanted to remind people he was not just another labor-yard man. "Look at me."

Delroy did not want to. Looking at another man's face in that moment was too close to asking for help. But he did.

"Can you move the fingers?"

He tried. The hand twitched.

"Arm?"

Delroy tried that too and almost blacked out. The world flashed thin and metallic at the edges.

"No," he said.

That one word came out stripped bare. Shame had taken the story out of him.

Manny came down off the porch with the clipboard still in his hand. Junior started talking all at once, about the slab, the bucket, the wheelbarrow, everybody's names tumbling. Manny looked at Delroy's shoulder once, at Junior's shin, at the half-broken patio, and his eyes did arithmetic.

"Get them out the way," he said to Claude.

Claude stared at him. "He needs a hospital."

"Then take him."

"You calling it in?"

Manny's jaw shifted. "You want the county here? You want this whole site shut? I said take him if he can't work."

Manny turned back toward the porch before Delroy had even gotten fully upright. Claude was still swearing at his back when Manny started shouting for Tito to keep the debris moving.

Claude swore at his back, low and venomous. Fermin spat gray mud into the weeds and sat hard on the curb, clutching his ankle where the wheelbarrow had clipped him. Junior kept pressing his shirt against the cut on his shin and staring at Delroy, storing it away already, maybe for the child being born into his life.

Claude got Delroy to the strip of shade behind the dumpster. Somebody passed over a half-cold bottle of water. Delroy could not unscrew it one-handed. Fermin took the cap off and handed it back without comment. His own ankle was swelling over the boot.

"You hearing me?" Claude asked.

Delroy took a swallow. Concrete grit scraped his teeth. "I hear."

"I can drive you to Jackson."

That meant registration, waiting, bills, questions, maybe X-rays that named the damage in a language he could not pay to fix. It also meant Marcia learning about Claude before he had even figured out how to bring the morning home.

"No hospital yet."

"This isn't a yet shoulder."

"No."

Claude looked like he wanted to argue and knew argument would only be another thing Delroy had to withstand. He scrubbed both hands down his face, then stood and walked back toward Manny with the stiff, controlled stride of a man trying not to make his own position worse while somebody else's body proved exactly what the job was. Delroy watched them from the shade. Manny shook his head before Claude finished speaking. Claude said something sharper. Manny held out both hands, palms up, selling innocence, like the ruined shoulder had arrived on site by itself.

Fermin leaned his head against the dumpster. "They'll pay half if we're lucky."

Delroy turned to look at him. "You know that?"

"I know them." Fermin gave a short laugh and immediately regretted it, hand going to his ankle. "Half if lucky. Nothing if they feel offended."

The pain kept changing shape. It throbbed deep in the joint, then sent little burning wires down to the elbow, then settled heavy at the top of his arm like somebody had driven a hot wedge into him. He held the forearm close to his belly because any swing of it made nausea rise. Under the pain sat the colder knowledge that he would be off ladders for a while. Paint buckets up back stairs, AC units wrestled through narrow landings, boxes of tile carried two at a time to make some contractor remember his name, all of it had already started sliding out of reach. A bad shoulder crossed whole kinds of work off the table.

Manny finally came over with an envelope folded twice. The sun had already reached the shade line and pushed them all hotter. Claude took the envelope, opened it, and his mouth tightened.

"What?" Delroy asked.

Claude did not answer right away.

"What?" Fermin said this time, flat and unsurprised.

Claude crouched again. His film canister had left a ring of clove dust in his shirt pocket. "Boss is saying you both left the site early."

Delroy almost laughed. The sound came out as air. "I left?"

"I'm telling you what he's saying."

"And what are you saying?" Delroy asked.

Claude met his eyes. That was the decent part of him, the part Delroy had trusted when he took the four hundred months ago. Claude would at least look at you while explaining the shape of the knife.

"I'm saying I pushed him for something. This is what he released today."

He slid the money into Delroy's left hand. Three twenties and a five, soft with sweat. Concrete dust stuck to the edge of the bills. Delroy stared at them until the amount stopped feeling like a joke and settled into insult.

Sixty-five dollars paid for the morning's pain and the shoulder hanging worse than it had at dawn. It was sixty-five dollars toward a rent hole that had once been Tiana's document money.

"The rest?" he said.

Claude's face closed a little. "There is no rest today."

"You mean there is no rest ever."

"Don't do that."

"Do what."

"Make me into the whole system because I'm the one standing here."

That irritated him because today's damage had Claude's fingerprints on it. Claude had driven him over, vouched for the bad shoulder, and put him back on the breaker after seeing the wince. Manny owned the site and the rules, but Claude was the man standing in front of him.

Claude lowered his voice. "I can settle with you on the four later."

Pity was worse than collection.

Delroy curled his fingers around the money. The shoulder answered with a bolt so bright he saw white spots. "Keep your later."

Claude exhaled through his nose. "At least let me run you home."

That, Delroy accepted. Pride had already spent too much of him.

They rode in silence most of the way. Claude drove with both windows down because the truck's air-conditioning had died the previous summer and he had never fixed it, preferring to tell people a breeze made a man honest. At a red light he pulled the Spider-Man lunch box from the floorboard where Manny must have left it, looked at it, and set it in the back without comment. The lunch box sat there like evidence of another man's ordinary life among all this breakage.

When they reached Little River, Claude stopped one block short of the apartment.

"You need a clinic," he said.

"I need more than clinic."

"You know what I mean."

Delroy opened the door with his left hand. Getting down out of the truck nearly folded him. Claude watched without pretending not to.

"Text me tonight," Claude said.

Delroy laughed once, not kindly. "With what hand?"

Claude winced at that and let him go.

The walk from the corner to the garage apartment took longer than it should have. Children were yelling two yards over with the wild happiness of summer boredom. Somebody had draped wet jeans over the fence. The smell of bleach hit him before he got to the door, then frying onions, then mildew from the bathroom vent. Home did not look like relief. It looked like one more room where he would have to account for himself.

Marcia opened the door before he knocked twice. She took in the dust, the way he was holding the arm, the face he could not arrange in time, and stepped back without wasting one syllable on surprise.

"Sit," she said.

He sat on the chair by the table. Tiana was there with the folder open beside a legal pad, hair tied up in a scarf that made her look younger until she turned and he saw the set of her mouth. On the stove a pot of rice had been left covered to steam. The fan in the window clicked at the end of every turn.

Marcia fetched scissors from the drawer. "Can you get this shirt off?"

"Not pretty."

"I didn't ask if it was pretty."

Tiana was already on her feet, crossing to the freezer. She moved quickly when there was a task, more like Marcia than she would have enjoyed hearing. "There's one ice tray left."

"Wrap it in a towel," Marcia said.

She cut the sleeve seam from cuff to shoulder because peeling the shirt off whole made Delroy curse into his teeth. Concrete dust dropped onto the floor in gray flakes. When the fabric fell away, the top of the shoulder had begun to swell forward, a hard ugly rise where his body had gone out of order.

Marcia did not gasp. She leaned close, eyes narrowing the way they did over forms and bills and any other thing trying to cheat her. Her fingers, callused and cool, pressed lightly around the joint. Delroy nearly rose out of the chair.

"Don't touch it there," he said.

"I need to know what it's saying."

"It's saying leave me alone."

"It's saying you mash it proper."

Tiana handed over the towel with ice knotted inside. The cold landed and for one stupid second Delroy thought maybe it would fix something by force of cold alone. Then the pain settled back around it.

Tiana stood by the table, arms folded under the legal pad she had picked up without realizing it. "Did you fall?"

"Sort of."

"That's not an answer."

Her voice was controlled, the precise public one she used on people behind counters. Under it he could hear the strain, the effort not to let this become about him in the old way, with story and excuse taking all the air in the room.

"Slab shifted," he said. "Machine too close. I caught a barrow wrong."

Marcia's head snapped up. "Machine? What machine?"

"Little Bobcat."

"On a broken slab." She stepped back and laughed once, all disgust. "Of course."

He reached into his pocket with the left hand and brought out the money. That small act hurt more than it should have because it made the point so plain. A man came home half-broken and still the thing in his hand had to be counted first.

Marcia held out her palm. He dropped the bills into it. Concrete dust marked her skin. She looked down.

"That's all?"

"That's what came."

"For a whole day?"

"For a morning and a wrecked shoulder."

She flattened the bills against the table one by one, thumb dragging hard enough to leave damp tracks on the paper. Sixty-five dollars looked obscene on top of Tiana's papers, not because it was no money but because it was such a small, dirty amount to have asked this much body for.

Tiana stared at the bills, then at the shoulder. "You need imaging," she said quietly. "If that's a tear, ice isn't going to do anything except make tonight different from tomorrow."

"Tonight and tomorrow already different," Marcia said.

Delroy watched her do the math in real time. Celia's wages were gone. Tiana's shifts would not stretch far enough. Rent was coming like weather. Claude's four hundred still stood behind this morning, and now clinic money had to stand there too. A shoulder like this could take ladders, paint buckets, toilets, and boxes of tile out of next week's reach before the week even arrived.

"We can go to Jackson," Tiana said. "They have to take you."

Marcia looked at Delroy's hand instead of his face and touched two fingers to the pulse in his wrist. "Move them again."

He did. The last two barely answered.

"We're not leaving you in this room like this," she said. "Put on another shirt. The walk-in on the avenue closes at nine. If they won't see you, then we head to Jackson."

Delroy started to object, then bent forward because the room tilted when he tried to stand. Tiana was beside him before he asked, one hand at his elbow, the other lifting the folder farther from the dust and money. She took one of the cleaner sheets from her legal pad and laid it over the bills before the grit could grind into the paper underneath.

"Don't put that on the documents," she said.

The words set the room back into its ugly order: dust on the floor, money on the table, paper that still had to stay clean.

Marcia looped a dish towel under his forearm long enough to get him to the bus stop. At the transfer, the towel was wet through from ice melt and sweat. The clinic waiting room on the avenue smelled of mint disinfectant, old coffee, and the damp upholstery of too many chairs. A judge show ran on the television with captions nobody watched.

Behind the glass, a woman in lilac scrubs slid a clipboard through the slot. "Insurance card?"

"We don't have insurance," Marcia said. "He tore his shoulder up on a job this morning. He needs an X-ray."

The woman's eyes flicked to Delroy's arm, then back to the monitor. "The X-ray deposit is one hundred and twenty dollars up front. The shoulder immobilizer costs extra."

Marcia set the crumpled bills on the ledge one at a time. "This is fifty-nine after bus fare. You can see that arm hanging wrong. Can't somebody look first and bill after?"

"No, ma'am. Imaging won't be ordered without the deposit."

The bills stayed under Marcia's hand. For a second Delroy thought she might sweep them back into her bag hard enough to tear the lining. Instead she said, very exact, "So we can sit here broke in front of you, and he'll still leave with no picture of the damage."

"Ma'am, that's the policy."

Beside her, Tiana tightened her grip on the blue folder. "If we come back with the money tomorrow, do we start over?"

"We can note him today and give you the imaging order when the deposit's paid."

Delroy kept his eyes on the counter seam. The strip of metal was scratched white where years of rings and keys had knocked against it. He could feel other people in the waiting room listening without lifting their heads. "Put the money back, Marcia," he said. "Don't count our last dollars out for them twice."

A nurse leaned out when he tried and failed to straighten. She took one look at the shoulder and came over before the woman behind the glass could wave her off. "Sir, don't lift that arm again." Her badge said N. BAPTISTE. "I can't get you to X-ray without payment cleared, but that joint is angry."

"Angry?" Marcia said. "It damn near gone."

The nurse's mouth tightened in a way that said agreement. "It could be a bad rotator-cuff tear. Could be more. He needs imaging, but tonight all I can tell you is no work, no lifting, ice twenty minutes at a time, ibuprofen with food, and prop the arm if he can't sleep." She disappeared, came back with a photocopied sheet, and circled the shoulder diagram in blue pen. "If the hand goes numb, cold, or blue, take him to Jackson. Don't wait till morning on that."

That was all the clinic let them buy with humiliation. They walked back out with one photocopied page of instructions and Delroy's arm still hanging wrong.

The drugstore three blocks down had a canvas sling hanging on a peg near the ace bandages. Tiana compared the prices once, jaw tight, then took the cheapest one, a bottle of liniment, and generic ibuprofen. Between the clinic and the drugstore, that was the whole night's treatment: paper instructions and the cheap sling. They got home with the webbing already sawn a red line into Delroy's neck. She unwrapped the scarf from her hair, threaded it through the sling strap, and knotted it so the arm rode a little higher and the strap stopped biting skin.

Marcia tucked what remained of the money into an envelope that had once held the light bill and left the front blank because none of her labels fit anymore. Inside, the bills looked more like residue than earnings, the morning reduced to paper and still not enough.

"Tomorrow," she said, rubbing liniment careful and mean across the muscle above his collarbone, "you don't go nowhere lifting anything. You hear me?"

He was already stretched flat on his back because any turn of the torso made the shoulder spark. Tiana shoved a folded towel under the bad arm until the pull eased a little. The apartment smelled of rice, bleach, onions, menthol, and concrete dust now, home and job ground together so fine there was no separating them.

"I hear."

On the milk crate beside the mattress, Tiana squared the blue folder before she killed the light. On the table, under the white sheet of pad paper, the envelope made a low untidy shape. It looked smaller every time Delroy glanced at it, and even lying still he could feel how much heavier the arm had become.


# Chapter 11

Marcia kept the steam off while she ironed the blue blouse; the iron spat brown water when she trusted it. The apartment had already gone hot. Delroy still slept flat on his back with his bad arm propped on a folded towel, mouth open, the sharp stink of liniment and sweat spreading off him as steadily as heat off pavement. Tiana's document folder still stood upright on the milk crate beside her mattress, squared and orderly, as if papers could shame the world into behaving.

Marcia slid the photocopy of the red code notice, the last rent receipt, and the landlord's text into her handbag. She put in a pen that still worked and the envelope where she kept whatever cash could survive not being touched. The envelope felt weightless. That was the first bad sign of the day, though the day had started with Delroy trying and failing to get his T-shirt over his head without waking himself up from the pain.

He opened one eye when she snapped her bag shut.

"You going now?"

"Before office open proper. Maybe he'll be in a better mood if nobody else reach him first."

Delroy pushed himself up too fast and sucked air through his teeth. Even that sound annoyed her because it carried so much need in it. He looked tall even folded by pain, shoulder wrapped in the drugstore sling Tiana had improvised from an old scarf because the clinic wanted money before better equipment. His hair had flattened on one side and stood up wild on the other. Last week she might have loved him for how ridiculous that looked. This week it just looked expensive.

"I can come," he said. "Man to man sometimes works better."

"Your shoulder can't even manage shirt to man right now."

"Still have my mouth."

"That's exactly what I don't need in there."

He gave her the little smile he used when he wanted to turn rebuke into flirting. The smile had won him more mercy in life than sense ever had. Today it only made the room feel smaller.

"You don't have to make me useless before breakfast, Marcia."

"I don't have to make anything. The workman already do that."

The words came out harder than she meant them to. Micah shifted on the pallet by the kitchenette but did not wake. Through the curtain, Tiana's breathing changed, which meant she was awake and pretending otherwise. Marcia hated performing family life through cloth. She hated the room hearing what belonged in a marriage. She hated that hatred itself cost energy she needed elsewhere.

Delroy looked down at the sling and used his good hand to smooth the frayed edge. "If he starts talking about code and all that foolishness, tell him I can finish the repairs myself once this settle."

"You're not finishing your own shoelace this morning."

He went quiet. Shame shortened him faster than pain did. Marcia saw it happen and did not stop herself. That was the thing she had learned over the last month: pity was another luxury item, lovely to look at, badly priced.

She took the house key from the nail, slipped on the shoes she saved for church and offices, and stepped outside before anybody else in the room could need something from her.

Little River was only half awake, but the noise had already started. A bus sighed at the corner. Somebody's rooster sounded insulted by the light. Bass trembled through a parked Honda two houses down, low enough to rattle the chain-link gate. The stucco main house in front wore its cracks in the same places as always. The converted garage behind it, where the Campbells lived, looked as temporary as bad news.
