The landlord's office sat in the front room of a one-story building off Biscayne that pretended to be respectable with fake wood blinds and a plastic ficus by the door. The wall unit blew cold air that smelled faintly of mildew. Marcia sat beneath a sign about late fees and watched the receptionist peel the backing off a hard candy with the concentration of a jeweler. The receptionist had one glitter nail broken down to the quick and kept touching it with her tongue.

When the landlord came out, he was already holding Marcia's file. He wore a stiff pale polo tucked into trousers that had been ironed with military feeling. Reading glasses hung from a blue cord on his chest. He had the kind of face that never needed to raise its voice because the paperwork did the bruising for him.

"Ms. Campbell." He did not offer his hand. "Come in."

His office held a desk, two metal chairs, and three framed certificates that said nothing Marcia could use. On the corner of the desk sat a photo of a girl in soccer socks grinning with a mouthful of braces. Beside it lay a stack of notices face down, as if bad news became less offensive when it showed its back.

The landlord sat. Marcia stayed standing a beat too long, making him look up at her. Then she took the chair because standing had not paid rent yet.

"I won't waste your time," she said. "I need a little extension. A week. Ten days at the outside. My husband's hurt, and I just lost the care job I had. The lady passed."

He nodded once, not unkindly. "I'm sorry to hear that."

The sympathy landed and slid off. He turned the file toward himself, thumb on the paper clip.

"The problem," he said, "is not whether your situation is sympathetic. The problem is the balance on the unit and the city's compliance deadline. Once code enforcement has a file open, I can't keep doing informal accommodations. Everything has to be documented properly."

"Document it, then. Write that I asked. Write that I'm telling you money is coming."

"Money coming isn't payment."

"No, but it's closer to payment than me standing in here talking."

He gave her a tight little smile for that, the kind men gave when they wanted to reward spirit without surrendering anything material.

"Your current rent is past due, there are late charges attached, and because of the occupancy issue with that structure, any extension past Friday would require an additional security payment." He spoke with careful hands, flattening each page after he touched it. "That is the cleanest way for me to protect myself."

Marcia stared at him. "Protect yourself from what."

"Liability. The city. A tenant claiming I made promises I didn't make. You understand."

He said you understand the way church ladies said bless your heart before gossip. It came dressed as inclusion and meant obedience.

"I understand you want extra money I don't have."

"I want the account brought current. For any formal extension beyond that, yes, I'd need added security. Given the circumstances."

"What circumstances. That your garage was making you money till the city remember law?"

His expression changed then, not to anger exactly. More to the patient alertness of a man who had found the line and meant to stand on the safe side of it.

"Ms. Campbell, I am trying to keep this professional."

"Professional is a lovely word when somebody else is the one getting pushed out."

The wall unit kicked louder. Outside the office door a phone rang twice and stopped. Marcia could hear the receptionist laughing softly at something on her screen. The laughter traveled under the door and made the room feel staged, as if one side of life kept happening no matter what the other side owed.

The landlord opened a drawer and took out a printed notice. He did not slide it to her yet. He kept one finger resting on the top edge.

"What I can do," he said, "is hold off on filing until Friday afternoon. If you bring the balance close enough to current by then, we talk again. If you can't, I move to the next step. I don't want to surprise you."

He said surprise as if that were generosity.

"And if I bring half."

"Half keeps you half behind."

"You have people looking already?"

The question slipped out before she could stop it. He did not answer. He only set the paper down now, neatly, within reach but not touching her hand.

"I need to manage the property responsibly."

There it was: not a refusal, but the cleaner insult underneath it, the reminder that if she couldn't pay, somebody else would.

Marcia looked at the notice without lifting it. Her own name printed cleanly near the top. The unit described as if it were something she had borrowed wrong.

"My children sleep there."

"I know that."

"Do you."

He took off his glasses and polished them with the hem of his polo, quick precise circles, the gesture of a man buying himself ten seconds of not having to feel another person's life press against his own. When he put the glasses back on, his face had closed again.

"Bring me money by Friday, Ms. Campbell."

That was the whole sermon.

She picked up the notice. The paper was cool from his office and went damp almost immediately in her hand. On the way out the receptionist offered her a peppermint from the stuck-together bowl. Marcia nearly laughed. She would have preferred a knife.

Outside, the heat struck her full in the face. Across the lot a man in a yellow safety shirt was pressure-washing the walkway of an empty unit. Water ran gray into the gutter. Every pass of the hose made the concrete look new for about thirty seconds before the stains came back through.

Marcia stood with the notice and thought, not for the first time, that poverty made language dirty. Extension, accommodation, current, security. Words that sounded neutral until they sat on your chest.

By the time the bus came, the paper had gone soft around the folds where her fingers kept finding the same place.

***

Coral Gables looked scrubbed in a way that felt personal. The trees were trimmed before they could get wild. The houses kept their grief behind hedges. Even the garbage bins looked expensive.

Celia's house smelled wrong without Celia in it, not empty so much as stripped too fast. Somebody had taken away the hospital bed from the den, and the rectangle it had covered showed pale on the rug, a ghost cleaner than the rest of the floor. White lilies crowded the dining table and turned the air sweet enough to sicken a person. In the kitchen, aluminum trays from neighbors and cousins filled the counters: croquetas, baked ziti, pastelitos going hard at the edges because sorrow made rich people overfeed one another.

Elena stood at the island in black linen pants and a white blouse that had already wilted at the waist. One heel lay abandoned near the trash can. She was barefoot on one side and still wearing a nude stocking on the other, the foot flexing now and then against the cool tile as if she had a blister she refused to respect. Her phone sat faceup beside a stack of funeral-home papers. Every few seconds she straightened them even though they were already straight.

She looked up when Marcia came in through the side door.

"You didn't have to come today."

"I was nearby."

It was not a good lie. Elena glanced at the handbag, the office shoes, the face Marcia knew looked arranged for asking.

"Sit if you want," Elena said.

"If I sit down in this house right now, I might not get back up."

Elena gave the briefest huff of almost-laughter. It vanished quickly, embarrassed by itself.

"I've got your final check," she said. "And I wanted to talk to you before things got more chaotic."

Elena had said more chaotic, and Marcia nearly looked around for evidence. The woman had died; how much more honest could a house get.

Elena took an envelope from beside the papers and handed it over. Marcia did not open it immediately. She could feel the check through the paper, one thin rectangle pretending to be a bridge.

"Thank you," she said.

"There's also a number in there. A coordinator at an agency my friend uses for her father sometimes. They take private-pay cases all over the county. No promises." Elena's voice tightened on the last words, as if she knew promises were expensive things to say to women who cleaned up bodies for a living. "I texted her already. She said to call today because weekend shifts open fast."

Marcia slid the check out halfway. The business card tucked behind it was for an agency in Kendall whose logo tried too hard to look gentle. She put both back in the envelope.

"Weekend shifts open fast," she repeated.

"Sometimes nights. Sometimes live-in for three days. It depends on who cancels and who's dying."

Elena flattened the funeral-home papers so hard the thick cream stock bowed under her palm. She kept it there a second too long, then lifted her hand and squared the stack again. Marcia preferred that small loss of control to soft language. Celia had died. Nobody needed the sentence dressed for church.

For one ugly second Marcia resented the dead woman, not for being hard, not for talking to waiters as if the world owed her volume, not even for paying just enough to make quitting impossible, but for dying exactly when Marcia had needed her alive another month. The thought came and stayed long enough to be real before shame pushed at it. Shame did not erase it.

"Does the agency pay regular?" Marcia asked.

"My friend's mother likes the girl they send, so I assume she gets paid eventually."

"Eventually doesn't interest my landlord."

Elena leaned both palms on the counter. Her nails were cut short and bare. Marcia had only ever seen them painted pale pink before. Grief had reached even that far.

"I know," Elena said. "I don't have a clean solution for you."

"Didn't ask for clean."

They stood there with that. On the counter, a funeral brochure showed a silver cross and cream paper thick enough to survive rain. Marcia thought of the red city notice softening in her bag and almost laughed again.

Elena picked up the phone. "What's the best number for you right now?"

"Same one."

Elena typed, frowned, then hit send. "I told her you're reliable, you know how to transfer patients, you can handle meds, and you don't panic when old people get mean."

"That last one should go first."

This time Elena did laugh, a short unwilling sound that made her look younger and more tired. "My mother would've said the same."

Marcia looked past her into the den, at the dent in the rug where the bed had been. She saw herself there at two in the morning changing Celia's damp gown while the old woman complained that dying had ruined her skin tone. She saw the suction canister, the crushed ice chips, the tube of coral lipstick Celia insisted on before visitors. Labor stayed in a room after the body left. Rich people called that haunting when poor women called it work.

"If you hear of anything direct, not agency," Marcia said, "call me first. I can do bathing, lifting, wound care, dementia, all of it. I can cook if the people don't mind seasoning."

"I know what you can do."

The answer was simple. That almost made it kind.

Elena opened the refrigerator. Cold rolled out over the kitchen. "Take some food."

"No."

"Marcia."

Elena did not say it like charity. She said it like a woman with too much chicken and no stomach for one more casserole. Marcia hated that this made the offer harder to refuse. She looked at the trays and thought of Micah after school, eating like anger needed fuel. She thought of Delroy trying to swallow pain on a stomach lined with plain tea.

"Something small," she said.

Elena packed a foil container with arroz con pollo and plantains browned almost black at the edges, the way Celia liked them and Elena never had. She snapped the lid on, wiped condensation from the outside with a dish towel, and set it in Marcia's hands with the same brisk care she had once used passing over a bag of medication.

"Call the agency before five," she said. "Ask for Lina. If she doesn't pick up, call again. She's bad with voicemail."

"All right."

"And if you need me to confirm employment, text first. I'm going to be buried in family for the next few days."

From the dining room the lilies kept shoving their sweetness into the kitchen until the air turned almost rotten. Marcia pictured cousins in black linen crowding the den where the hospital bed had been, foil trays sweating on the counters, grief moving room to room while the bills stayed put beside Elena's phone.

"I won't waste your time," Marcia said.

Elena's mouth changed at that, barely. "Do what you need to do."

On the bus stop bench outside, Marcia balanced the foil tray beside her and finally opened the envelope. The check amount was enough to embarrass a person, enough to matter, and nowhere near enough to alter the story the landlord had already printed.

She looked at the numbers anyway. Rent. Late fee. Light bill. Delroy's pills. Tiana's bus card. Rice, oil, bleach. Every line item stepped forward at once, none of them ashamed.

She folded the check back into the envelope and took out her phone.

The first call to the agency went to a recording that sounded like it had been left by a woman wearing pearls. Marcia hung up before the instructions finished. She would call again. But first she went to the contact she had been trying not to count.

Kerene answered on the third ring, breathless, with a dryer roaring somewhere behind her.

"If you're selling me salvation, talk louder," she said.

"It's me."

"Cousin. Hold on."

The dryer noise muffled, then shifted. Marcia pictured her stepping out the back of some beauty shop into the wet heat, hand pressed to one ear, bright voice intact because bright voices got you treated better in new countries until they didn't.

"All right," Kerene said. "Tell me something good."

Marcia looked at the foil tray, the envelope, the bus route sign bolted crooked into the concrete.

"I don't have good today. I've got a question."

Kerene was quiet just long enough to prove she was not stupid.

"That kind of question."

"Yes."

"Say it plain."

Marcia rubbed the soft fold in the city notice with her thumb. "You still looking somewhere to stay."

"I'm staying somewhere now. I wouldn't call it looking if I had a better word. More like bracing."

"How bad."

"The lady from church has been kind enough for the story she tells on Sundays. But her sister arrived from Montego Bay last night with two children and an air mattress attitude. I can read a room, Marcia. My welcome is getting thin."

Despite herself, Marcia smiled. Then she stopped because smiling made this feel easier than it was.

"I've got space," she said.

The lie sat between them.

"You have a corner," Kerene corrected, not unkindly. "And if you're the one offering it, that means the rent is breathing down your neck."

"It already put its hand on my throat."

"How much can I help."

The question hit Marcia low, not because of the money but because of the quickness. Kerene had skipped gratitude and pride and gone straight to terms. That was respect disguised as roughness.

"If you come, you have to put in. I can't dress it up nicer than that."

"Don't. I didn't fly here to be anybody's delicate flower. I can put in every week if the salon girls stop acting like they invented braid spray."

Marcia heard the strain under the joke then, a raggedness around the edges like a dress hem beginning to fray. Kerene was newly arrived, without a proper room or a stable address, and America was already charging interest.

"It's one room," Marcia said. "Bathroom, kitchenette, us. Tiana has the little back corner with the curtain. That would have to stop being just hers."

"Ah."

That was all Kerene gave her, no complaint and no surprise, just a woman measuring the cost before she agreed to owe it.

"I don't want the girl hating me from day one," Kerene said.

"She might anyway."

"Fair enough."

Traffic hissed through a puddle at the curb though it had not rained. Marcia watched a boy in a school polo kick at the bus-bench leg with the side of his shoe, over and over, as if irritation were something he could polish into shine.

"I've got one suitcase," Kerene said. "One big bag of hair and brushes. And the folding cot Miss Althea says must leave with me today because she's tired of looking at it in her dining room. I can come tonight if you mean tonight."

"I mean tonight."

"Then tell me the truth before I show up. Is this help, or is this emergency."

Marcia looked at the agency card with its soft blue logo and the word compassionate in curling script. She thought of the landlord's finger resting on the printed notice.

"Both."

Kerene let out a breath that could have been a laugh if it had more joy in it. "Good. I hate when people lie first and explain later."

"You sure."

"No," Kerene said. "But sure isn't what I've got to work with."

That honesty felt better than comfort would have.

"Bring whatever can fit."

"That's what everybody in immigration and housing been saying to me since I land."

"Kerene."

"I'm joking. A little." The bright tone flickered back on. "Text me the gate. And don't apologize when I reach there. It wastes time."

After they hung up, Marcia sat another minute with the phone in her hand. She should have called Tiana first. She knew that. She knew it so clearly that the failure had already happened. But Tiana would ask what exactly was changing and where exactly her things would go, and Marcia did not yet have answers that sounded like a mother rather than a clerk.

She typed, `Come straight home after work. We need to move some things.` Tiana answered almost immediately.

`What things`

Marcia stared at the screen until the bus came.

She put the phone away without replying.

***

By the time she got back, the apartment smelled of pain cream, old fan dust, and the chicken she had set in the oven to warm through. Delroy sat in the plastic chair near the door in basketball shorts and no shirt, a fresh strip of white tape crossing his shoulder where Tiana had rewrapped him before leaving for her shift. The tape did nothing for the swelling. His right arm hung in the sling like a sentence being served.

He looked up from the dominoes box he had been pretending to reorganize one-handed.

"You talked to him."

"I talked. He heard paperwork."

"How bad."

The question brought the office back whole: the mildew air, the notice under the landlord's finger, Friday sitting there clean on the page while her own sweat turned the edges soft in her bag. The date struck low in her belly all over again.

"Friday bad."

Micah, sprawled on his stomach with his phone in front of him, let out a low whistle without lifting his head. "That's the kind that comes fast."

"You have homework?" Marcia asked.

"School don't even want me this much. Let's not lie to each other."

"Then find something quiet to do."

He rolled over and sat up. "You got food, though?"

Marcia uncovered the foil tray on the counter. Micah made a sound of reverence loud enough to be rude. Delroy laughed despite himself and then winced, pressing his good hand to the taped shoulder.

"Don't make me laugh," he said. "This house is hostile."

Marcia set plates out because feeding people was easier than speaking to them. The rent envelope knocked once against the inside of her bag where she had tucked Kerene's money beside Elena's check, rescue and embarrassment flattened together. By the time she said her cousin's name, she knew she was choosing shame on purpose because shame still cost less than the street.

"Kerene's coming tonight."

Micah straightened. "Which Kerene. The cousin with the good wigs or the one who sells perfume from her trunk."

"Good wigs," Marcia said.

"All right then." He grinned. "House upgrading."

Delroy's face changed more slowly. "Coming to visit."

"Coming to stay."

He looked at the curtain that marked off Tiana's narrow corner. He did not have to ask the next part. The room said it for him. Marcia saw the corner the way Tiana would see it coming in from work: the milk crate desk, the notebooks, the cheap twin XL sheets still waiting for a dorm that had not happened. She had already traded that little square of privacy away on the bus bench with one text and no answer after it.

"Marcia."

"Don't start."

"I'm not starting. I'm asking where."

"In the same square footage God already gave us."

"That's not an answer."

She turned then, hot with the knowledge that she was about to defend the theft before the person she'd stolen from was even home. "The answer is rent. The answer is Friday. The answer is your shoulder can't earn and my dead lady can't hire. Pick one if you want poetry."

Micah's grin disappeared. Delroy looked as if she had slapped him in public. For a second she wanted to take it back, not because it was false but because truth got ugly fast in a hot room.

He swallowed. "How much she putting in."

"Enough that I had to make the call."

That shut him down. Money reached him where pride usually blocked the road. He looked at the dominoes box again, lids and tiles all mixed together because his left hand could not sort neatly.

Micah cleared his throat. "I can crash by the kitchenette more regular. I don't care."

Marcia looked at him. He shrugged one shoulder, pretending generosity cost nothing.

"You care," she said.

"Not enough to be outside."

That was his better self, rough and quick and embarrassed to be seen working.

Tiana came in twenty minutes later with her work shirt damp down the spine and her hair pulled back into the tight puff she wore when she did not have energy left for vanity. The minute she saw the shifted milk crates and the curtain rod taken halfway down, her face altered.

"Why is my room open."

Marcia dried her hands on the dish towel. "Come inside first."

"I'm inside."

Even angry, Tiana's voice came out pressed and office-ready, the careful English Marcia had fought into her so no clerk or manager could hear Little River and decide the rest. Tiana set her tote by the door without taking her eyes off the back corner.

"What's happening."

"Kerene needs somewhere to stay. She'll be here soon."

Tiana looked from Marcia to Delroy, then to the half-lowered curtain and the milk crate that held her extra notebooks, skin products, and the cheap twin XL sheet set she had bought on sale in June because she had once believed in August.

"Soon meaning what."

"Soon meaning tonight."

"You might have told me before you started dismantling my room."

"If I'd waited till you got home, the room wouldn't have grown another wall."

"That's not what I said."

"It's what the day said."

Tiana laughed once with no humor in it. "Of course. The day said it, so nobody had to ask me."

Micah stood up. "You want me to move the crate or not, because I feel like this about to become an argument where nobody eats."

"Move it," Marcia said.

Tiana bent and grabbed the other side before Micah could. "I can lift my own things."

The crate scraped the floor. Bottles clicked inside. Marcia watched her daughter carry her life two feet to the left and set it down beside the kitchenette trash can. It was not the distance that hurt. It was the casualness forced on the motion, as if futures could be tidied into new corners whenever rent demanded.

"Where am I sleeping," Tiana asked.

"Here still. You'll both be in the back."

Tiana looked at the cot leg already catching the curtain and at her milk crate parked beside the trash can. "So my desk's in the kitchen and two women are supposed to fold up under that window."

Delroy tried for softness. "It's a temporary thing, baby girl."

She turned on him with a precision that made Marcia want to look away. "That cot's already eating the window, and my things are by the trash can."

Nobody had anything better than that.

The knock came just then: two quick raps, then a third after a pause, as if the hand at the door had thought better of seeming eager. Micah got there first.

Kerene came in sideways because of the cot. It was folded flat and tied with pink extension cord, one metal leg working loose and clanging against the doorframe. A hard-shell suitcase rolled behind her with green yarn knotted around the handle to identify it. She wore jeans, a white ribbed tank, and a mustard headwrap that had slipped enough to show the clean black braid lines at her scalp. The heat had beaten the brightness from her makeup, but her mouth was still painted a brave reddish brown. One sandal strap had rubbed the skin above her heel raw and she was pretending not to limp.

"Look at this," she said, letting the cot thump against the wall. "I leave one dining room and arrive with furniture. I'm becoming American too fast."

Micah barked a laugh. Even Tiana's mouth twitched before she stopped it.

"Come in," Marcia said.

Kerene stepped fully over the threshold and looked around once, fast and complete. She wasn't judging so much as calculating, taking in the room, the bodies in it, the heat, the tension. Marcia respected her for not pretending she saw nothing.

"Delroy," Kerene said, giving him a little chin lift. "You look half handsome, half lawsuit."

"Only half handsome?" he said.

"The tape ruined the ratio."

That got the laugh from him too. It also bought her ten seconds of air, which Marcia saw and appreciated. Charm was work. Some people did it so prettily you forgot it had wages.

Kerene bent to unzip the suitcase, then hissed and straightened again, rubbing the back of her calf. "If anybody asks, this limp is fashion. Miami buses are wicked."

She pulled out a loaf of hard dough bread wrapped in a grocery bag and held it up. "I didn't come empty-handed. That would've made my grandmother rise straight out the grave and slap me."

"You can set that by the stove," Marcia said.

Kerene's gaze found Tiana then, and the room sharpened around the two of them.

"You're Tiana." Kerene's voice lost some performance. "You've grown right past all the photos."

"So have you," Tiana said.

It was not rude. It was not warm either. Marcia could live with that. Warmth asked too much too soon.

"This is your space I'm trespassing in," Kerene said. "I know that already."

Tiana folded her arms. "You're not trespassing if my mother invited you."

"Fine. Then I'm being legally inconvenient."

Micah snorted. Delroy put his head back against the chair as if the laugh cost less than the silence would have.

Marcia took the bread and set it on the counter. When she turned back, Kerene had already dug a folded wad of twenties from the side pocket of her suitcase. The bills were smoothed but not flat, beauty-shop money, edges soft from being tucked and untucked all day.

"Take this first," she said quietly, holding it out to Marcia. "Before I sit down and start acting like a guest."

Marcia did not move.

"Kerene."

"Take it. I got more work lined up this week if the girl in North Miami stops pretending she can braid faster than me. This is what I've got in hand."

The room watched. Even Micah understood enough to become suddenly interested in the loose metal leg of the cot.

Marcia took the money. It was not enough to fix Friday. It was enough to feel. Worse, it was enough to count.

"All right," she said.

Kerene nodded once, transaction done, and immediately reached for the cot as if movement could wipe away the intimacy of cash. "Show me where this glamorous bed going."

The back corner had once been a garage storage niche. Delroy had hung a tension rod years ago and Marcia had sewn a curtain from two different cream sheets, neither one white anymore. Tiana's mattress lay against the wall under the small window. Above it, thumbtacks held up a campus map of Gainesville, a pharmacy receipt with dates circled, and a glossy postcard from UF that showed laughing students carrying books nobody in Marcia's house could yet afford. Beside the mattress sat the plastic bin where Tiana had been storing bought-a-little-at-a-time escape: twin XL sheets, a set of navy towels, shower caddy, cheap flip-flops for communal bathrooms she still hoped to meet.

Kerene saw all of that in one glance. Her face did not change, but the charm slipped quiet for the first time.

"You sure," she asked Marcia, so low only the nearest people could hear, "this is where you want to put me."

"No," Marcia said. "It's what I've got."

That honesty seemed to steady them both.

They moved fast because slowness would have made the indignity ceremonial. Micah hauled the bin to the top of the wardrobe. Tiana took the campus map down herself and slid it between two textbooks without folding it. Delroy tried to stand and help with the cot, failed halfway up, and sat again hard, jaw tight with humiliation. Kerene pretended not to notice him failing; that was another kindness. She and Marcia unfolded the cot together. The metal frame snapped open with a report like a bad idea becoming official.

"Jesus," Micah said. "That thing sound like jail."

"Then sleep polite and it won't arrest you," Kerene said.

The cot fit only if Tiana's milk-crate desk moved out and the plastic drawer tower shifted nearer the window, blocking half the breeze when any breeze came. Once they wedged it in, the back corner no longer belonged to one girl dreaming north. It belonged to need. Need took up room differently. Need had elbows.

Tiana stood with her toiletry bag hanging from two fingers and stared at the arrangement.

"So that's it."

Marcia wiped her forehead with the inside of her wrist. "For now."

Tiana lifted the toiletry bag and pointed with it at the curtain snagged on the cot frame. "It doesn't even close anymore. My desk's by the stove. There's nowhere in here that's still mine."

"And you think I don't see that?"

Tiana looked at her then, full on, not as daughter to mother but as one woman taking the measure of another under pressure.

"No," she said. "You know rent. You don't know what it feels like to come home and watch the place where you kept yourself get turned into overflow."

The sentence hit with surgical cleanliness. Marcia wanted to answer with age, with sacrifice, with every meal she had gone light on so the children could eat heavy. She wanted to say You think I don't know displacement because I am the one holding the tape. She wanted to remind Tiana who had measured those twin XL sheets against sale circulars and hidden them from Micah so he would not use them to wipe his sneakers. She wanted to say a hundred true things.

Instead she said the truest ugly one.

"If I had another room, you'd have it. I've got this one."

Tiana blinked. That hurt her more than anger would have. Anger gave her something to fight. Facts only sat there.

Kerene picked up the toiletry bag gently from Tiana's hand because Tiana had loosened her grip without noticing.

"Listen," she said, not bright now, not performing. "If this feels bad, it should. It's bad. I'm still grateful and it's still bad. Both can stay in the room."

Tiana let out a breath. "I know you're not the problem."

"No," Kerene said. "But I'm definitely the furniture."

This time the laugh that moved through the room was small enough not to betray anybody. Sometimes that was the best a family could do.

Marcia turned back to the stove because if she kept looking at Tiana's face she would either apologize or harden, and neither one would feed anybody. She sliced the hard dough bread, warmed the chicken, stretched the plantains by cutting them thinner than honesty. From behind her she could hear Micah asking Kerene if the salon had any customers who tipped stupid. She could hear Kerene answering that American women paid forty dollars to look effortless and then acted surprised when effort cost money. She could hear Delroy trying his charm again, slower now, careful not to sound like a man too injured to offer anything but stories.

At the table, which was really a card table with one leg shorter than the others, Kerene ate like a person who had been careful all day and had run out of patience for caution. She closed her eyes on the first bite of chicken and said, "Thank God for dead rich people's casseroles."

Marcia nearly choked. Micah laughed so hard he slapped the table. Even Tiana covered her mouth.

"You really don't waste time," Delroy said.

"I came from a house where people were grieving with ham," Kerene said. "I'm not romantic about class. Pass the bread."

The room loosened by one notch, not much but enough that everybody breathed a little differently.

Later, after Micah had dragged his pallet closer to the kitchenette and Delroy had swallowed the last pain pill with tap water he pretended was rum, the apartment went through the awkward business of becoming night in its new shape. The curtain no longer closed clean because the cot nudged the rod at an angle. A triangle of space stayed open near the floor no matter how Marcia tucked the cloth. Through it she could see Tiana's bare feet near the mattress and Kerene's suitcase standing upright like a witness.

Marcia washed the foil pan from Elena's house because she could not stand waking to rich-people aluminum in her sink. The water ran warm almost immediately. Behind the curtain, voices dropped low.

"You can take the cot and I'll stay on the floor tonight," Kerene said.

"No. Then my mother will come in here feeling guilty every ten minutes."

"She already feels guilty every ten minutes."

"I know."

Silence. Then the squeak of the cot as Kerene sat on it, testing.

"Miami really does charge for everything," Kerene said quietly. "Bus, room, food, dignity. Breathing feels next."

"You noticed that fast."

"I'm from Jamaica, not the moon."

That got a breath of laughter from Tiana. Marcia stood very still with her hands in the dishwater.

"You were really going to Gainesville?" Kerene asked after a moment.

"I still might."

"Good."

"Maybe."

"Also good. Maybe is still alive."

Marcia dried the pan slowly. The words were ordinary. The ache under them was not. She thought of the plastic bin on top of the wardrobe, of the twin XL sheets bought one payday at a time, of Tiana's folder leaning now against the refrigerator because there was nowhere else flat enough to keep it safe. She thought of taking money from Kerene's hand and feeling relieved before ashamed. She thought of Delroy in the next room, breathing through pain he had bought trying to become useful again.

When she switched off the main light, the apartment rearranged itself into shadows and sound. Delroy's breath came rough and drugged from the mattress near the wall. Micah's headphones leaked a tinny snare pattern until Marcia told him once to turn it down and, miracle of miracles, he did. A plane moved somewhere far off, low enough to make the window tremble. From behind the curtain came the rustle of two women who were kin and strangers at once, each trying not to take up more than her share of an impossible square of air.

Marcia lay down beside Delroy without touching him. Before she did, she peeled bus fare and one extra dollar from the envelope and set them aside for Micah to carry to the school office in the morning. The rest of Kerene's money sat folded inside the rent envelope under her pillow, close enough to count by feel, too little to save them and too much to dismiss. Friday waited only a handful of sleeps away. The room held six different breaths now and not one place where a person could keep a future entirely to herself.


# Chapter 12

The trophy case outside the school office held dead football seasons under glass, and the front doors nearby kept breathing out weak air-conditioning that smelled like copier heat and old floor wax. Micah stood beside it with the back of his T-shirt stuck to him from shoulder blade to waistband, pretending to read the names etched into little brass plates from years before he was old enough to care. August had that particular Miami meanness where the air did not move unless it was being paid to. It was easier to look at dead football seasons than at the office window where Ms. Francis could see him waiting.

Marcia had set aside the bus fare the night before. In the morning she sent him with that, one extra dollar for a cold drink if the heat got at him, and exact instructions. Go to the office, pick up the packet, come straight back. Don't stop by the docks. Don't stop by anybody's house. Don't let Dre carry you nowhere stupid. At the sink she had counted the bills twice before folding them into his palm, her fingers cold from rinsing out somebody else's old yogurt container to save for leftovers. Behind her, Kerene had leaned over the hot plate and curled one eyelash with the back of a teaspoon because she still had not replaced the metal curler she swore TSA had stolen. Tiana had been digging through the dresser drawer that was not really hers anymore, moving her document sleeve from one stack of shirts to another like the papers might get legs and leave without her. The room had been full before breakfast. Micah had understood what Marcia meant without her saying it plain. The house did not have room for one more problem wearing his face.

The office window slid open with a tired scrape. Ms. Francis looked at him over half-moon reading glasses that had been repaired at one hinge with clear tape. Her desk fan was turned straight at her chest, making the laminated saint card pinned beside her monitor flutter. "Micah Campbell."

He pushed off the wall and went up to the opening. "Yeah."

"Your mother didn't come?"

"She had somewhere to be."

Ms. Francis gave him the look adults used when they wanted a child to understand that his people had failed before she said it out loud. Then she reached below the counter and produced a fat yellow envelope with his name scrawled across the front in black marker. Through the paper he could already see the hard rectangles of forms, the kind that wanted initials in six places and a signature where the school had highlighted a lie in fluorescent pink.

"Behavior contract, reentry acknowledgment, probation conditions, class issue report." She tapped the stack with one long acrylic nail painted pale blue, tiny silver cross glued at the tip. "Every highlighted line needs a parent signature. Every page. If she misses one, they'll send it back and you'll sit in the dean's office till somebody can come."

He took the envelope. It was damp at the corners from somebody else's hands. On the front, under his name, a red stamp said DISCIPLINARY REVIEW.

"I know how to carry papers," he said.

Ms. Francis's mouth pinched. "Then carry them home, baby. That would be a start."

He walked out before he said something that would make the envelope thicker next time.

Outside, the heat hit like a hand over the mouth. The lunch bell had already rung for somebody, and the courtyard was noisy with first-week bragging, girls with fresh knotless braids taking photos against the mural wall, boys in clean sneakers walking the long way around puddles so nothing got on the white. Micah kept the envelope folded against his thigh so nobody could read the stamp. He still felt them reading him anyway. The dean's office had a way of leaking. You could walk out with a packet in your hand and half the school already knew you had been turned into paperwork.

Dre was leaning by the bike rack in a faded navy Saints cap so soft the brim bent in the middle. He had his house key hanging on the same bright-green shoelace his grandmother had made him wear all summer after he lost three copies in one month and had to sleep on the porch once. He saw the envelope and whistled low.

"Damn," he said. "That thing thick. They gave you homework for being Black."

Micah snorted before he could help it. "Shut up."

"I mean it. That packet look like they want your blood type, your first sin, and your mama's signature in triplicate."

"They want her to sign where they already decided I'm a problem."

"Same thing."

Dre straightened from the rack and fell in beside him. He always moved like he was arriving one second before the joke. Today there was sweat darkening the collar of his gray shirt, and his laugh landed a little too fast, like he was trying to get in front of something.

"You going straight home?" Dre asked.

"That's literally the mission."

"Mission from who."

"From the woman who pays rent in my house."

"That's crazy. I thought maybe some other woman had stepped up."

"Man."

Dre grinned. "All right. Straight home. I was only asking because if we cut behind the old laundry lot, it's faster than the bus stop, and I'm not standing in that sun to smell them boys' armpits."

Micah looked toward the avenue. A line was already forming at the bus stop, kids shoulder to shoulder in the weak strip of shade, everybody loud because silence at school always sounded like weakness. He could take the bus like Marcia told him. He could hold the packet under his arm and sweat through the yellow paper while people shoved and laughed and tried to look over his shoulder. Or he could walk the back route and be home sooner. He had maybe twenty dollars in cash hidden in a sock for clippers oil and a phone recharge, and he did not want to spend bus fare twice if the transfer failed. The family had gotten so tight around money that even Marcia's extra dollar felt hot against his thigh.

"It's faster?" he said.

"By like ten, fifteen minutes."

"You said five yesterday."

"Heat changes distance."

"That's not a real thing."

"It does if your skin works."

Micah shifted the envelope to his other hand. Across the street, a patrol SUV crawled past the corner store slow enough to count faces. Nobody reacted. In Little River the cops moved through like weather: not surprise, exactly, just something you noticed before deciding whether you needed to hide from it.

Dre saw him looking and clicked his tongue. "Come on. We cut through, grab a drink at Mr. Vega's if he's open, and you're home before your mother starts calling your sister to ask where you at."

"My mother doesn't need Tiana to find me."

"Everybody needs Tiana to find something."

That one landed a little wrong. Tiana's folder flashed through his head, traveling all morning from drawer to bed to grocery bag to nowhere, and then the sight of Kerene's folding cot opening where Tiana used to stack her dorm things. He shoved both images down before they could turn him soft.

"Fine," he said. "We cut through and then I'm gone."

"Look at that. Youth making positive choices."

"You should be a guidance counselor."

"I'd be great. I already know all the bad routes."

They headed along the side street, away from the front of the school and the buses and the girls taking pictures. The narrow strip of shade beside the chain-link fence was crowded with bottle caps, wet leaves, and the chalky white droppings of birds that liked the power line. Somewhere behind the auto shop a bass line thumped from an open trunk, the same eight seconds of dancehall looping because somebody was testing speakers or patience. Micah loosened his grip on the yellow envelope and let himself believe, for half a block, that the day might stay the size Marcia had asked for.

The lot behind the old laundry used to have a cinder-block building on it. Now it was broken concrete, shoulder-high weeds, and a mural half-scraped off a wall that still showed one blue wing and the edge of a halo. People cut through there all the time. Men from the barber shop smoked there, kids ducked through it to avoid the avenue, and old women with grocery carts crossed it because the sidewalk buckled worse than the dirt path through the middle.

Dre hopped the shallow curb and kept talking. He talked when he was happy and when he was scared, and with Dre those two moods wore a lot of the same clothes.

"My grandma says if I fail algebra again she's putting me in church every Wednesday till Jesus himself comes grade my papers."

"Jesus would fail you too."

"That's hate. That's exactly why your packet so thick."

"You better stop looking at it."

"I can't. It keeps calling to me. Open me, Dre. Read how the system loves your friend."

Micah shoved him lightly with the back of his wrist so the papers would not bend. Dre laughed. Then the laugh cut off.

A cruiser swung broadside at the mouth of the lot, tires spitting grit. Another came in from the alley behind them before Micah fully understood what he was seeing. Doors opened. Boots hit concrete. The first command came before either boy had said a word.

***

"Hands. Let me see your hands. Right now."

The older officer had one hand on his holster and the other out flat like he was stopping traffic. Sweat had darkened the neck of his vest, and a nicotine patch was peeling up from the skin below his ear. The younger one moved fast to the rear, mirrored sunglasses hiding his eyes, jaw shiny with fresh razor bumps.

Dre's shoulders jerked up. "We ain't doing nothing."

"Hands where I can see them," the younger cop said. "On the wall. Now."

Micah lifted both hands. The yellow envelope slid against his palm. For a second his brain noticed everything stupid and small at once: the grit under his slides, the sour smell of old detergent baked into the lot, a fly walking the wet rim of a crushed soda cup. Then the bigger understanding arrived behind it: there were two cruisers, two Black boys, and no room to explain why his mind had put those facts in that order.

"Turn around," the older officer said.

Micah did it. He did it right away. He put both palms on the hot stucco wall at the edge of the lot and spread his fingers. Beside him Dre muttered, "Man, my grandma's key gonna brand me through my shirt," and tried to laugh, but the sound shaved down to something thin and useless.

"Matching description from a strong-arm two blocks over," the younger officer said. "One of you reaches, one of you runs, this gets worse. You understand me?"

"Yes, sir," Dre said quickly.

Micah kept his mouth shut. He had learned something in the dean's office, and then unlearned it on the bus, and then learned a different thing in the neighborhood. Sometimes the first answer out of your mouth became the part they hated most.

The older cop stepped in close enough that Micah could smell mint gum fighting old coffee. "Empty your pockets."

"My hands are on the wall."

"Then tell me what's in them."

"Phone. Bus card. Gum."

"Any weapons?"

"No."

"Drugs?"

"No."

Beside him, Dre said, "All I got is my phone and lint. You can keep the lint if you want it."

"Shut up," the younger officer snapped.

Dre shut up so fast his teeth clicked.

Micah heard a radio crackle from one of the cruisers. Male juvenile. Black. White shirt. Gray shirt. Last seen heading south. The description was so broad it could have netted half the avenue. He stared at the wall and felt the heat pressing through it into his palms. A lizard darted out from a crack near the base and disappeared under a beer can.

"Step back one at a time," the older officer said. "Slow."

Micah stepped back. The envelope slipped from under his arm. It hit the ground, burst at one corner, and three forms slid out over the dust. One page turned faceup. DISCIPLINARY REVIEW. PARENT ACKNOWLEDGMENT. His own name lay flat under the sun like something pinned for display.

He bent his head only far enough to look at it; he did nothing else.

"Eyes front," the younger officer barked, and the back of a hand hit between Micah's shoulder blades hard enough to pitch him forward.

His cheek slammed the wall and skipped off the rough stucco. Pain flashed white and immediate. By the time he caught himself, he could taste blood. The younger officer grabbed both wrists, yanked them down, and ratcheted a cuff onto one hand.

"I didn't do anything," Micah said. He heard the protest too late to swallow it.

"Then stop resisting."

"I'm not resisting."

The officer drove him sideways toward the hood of the cruiser anyway. The metal was so hot it felt alive. Micah's face hit the edge where hood met grill and something opened under his eye with a sharp wet sting. He heard Dre say, "Yo, he said he ain't-" and then stop when the older cop swung toward him.

"You want to go down too?" the older one said.

"I'm already here, man." Dre was trying to smile. He sounded twelve years old. "I'm not going nowhere."

The older officer patted him down in quick, punishing slaps. The green key lace bounced against Dre's chest with every shove. "Keep talking," he said. "See how that works for you."

Micah's free hand was flattened across the hood. Blood from his cheek had started to slick the silver paint. He could see it in the warped reflection below his fingers, a red smear cutting through the ghost of his own face. He tried to turn his head away from the heat and the metal and the officer pushed him harder.

"Other hand."

"It's under me."

"Then move it."

Micah moved it. The second cuff closed so tight his fingers tingled at once. The paper on the ground stirred in a weak breeze and one sheet skated under the front tire.

"Where's the bag?" the younger officer asked.

"What bag?"

The pressure on the back of his neck increased. "Don't play with me."

"I don't know what bag you're talking about."

"Woman got her purse snatched outside the check-cashing place. Two males. Your size. Your clothes. Where is it?"

"I came from school," Micah said, his mouth flattened against the hot metal. "That's school papers."

"Everybody comes from school when I stop them at three o'clock."

The lot had gone strangely quiet around the noise. Micah realized it was not actually quiet; it was the kind of attention that felt like quiet because every other sound had arranged itself around it. The bass from the auto shop was still thumping, but farther away now. A bus hissed somewhere on the avenue. Somebody said, "Lord." Somebody else said, "That's Marcia's son." He did not know which hurt worse, being made into a suspect or being recognized while it happened.

He twisted just enough to see the edge of the sidewalk beyond the weeds. People had stopped there. Mr. Baptiste from the barber shop was standing with a broom in his hand, black apron still tied over his shorts, hair clippings stuck to the hair on his shin. Miss Pauline from church had one pink foam roller still clipped in her bangs and a plastic Family Dollar bag cutting into the crook of her arm. A little boy in a Spider-Man backpack was staring openmouthed until the woman with him turned his shoulders away. Nobody stepped into the lot.

Miss Pauline took out her phone.

The officer saw Micah looking and shoved his head back down. Fresh pain tore across his cheek. This time blood ran warm along the side of his nose and dripped from his jaw to the hood.

"Keep your face down."

Micah wanted, with a force that scared him, to jerk free just to prove that his body still belonged to him. He did not do it. He fixed his eyes on the curve of the hood where the blood was spreading and thought about Marcia counting bills at the sink, about the small mean dignity of following one instruction exactly. He had almost made the day that small. That was the part that made him feel sick.

Dre was still talking. He could never help himself. Only now the jokes had gone strange and brittle, coming apart at the edges.

"Officer, for real, you can check my messages. I was literally trying not to die in summer school all week."

"You think this is funny?"

"No, sir. I think my grandma is gonna think it's funny when I come home late and she locks me out, though."

The older officer spun him, put him against the second cruiser, and cuffed him too. Dre sucked air through his teeth. "Damn. Damn, all right. That's tight."

"Good."

Micah watched the reflected crowd grow in the cruiser window. Two girls from school had paused on the far sidewalk with their phones held at chest height, screens dark and ready. Their mouths were shaped with that hungry little shock people wore when something bad was happening to somebody they knew but not to them. Tomorrow the story would come back whether anybody posted or not: his split cheek, the yellow envelope by the tire, those girls pretending their screens were nothing.

The younger officer dug into Micah's pockets, pulled out his phone, the bus card, the gum, and held up the folded one-dollar bill Marcia had sent for a drink if he got dizzy in the heat.

"Big score," Dre said automatically.

The younger officer looked at him. "You really want to keep performing?"

Dre stared straight ahead. "No, sir."

That was when Micah knew Dre was scared for real. The sir had dropped out of him earlier like something learned. Now it came back from deeper than pride.

The radio crackled again. A different description this time. Red jersey. Bicycle. Older. The older officer put a hand to his shoulder mic and listened without expression. The younger one stayed where he was, palm flat between Micah's shoulders like he did not trust gravity to do the job.

"Victim says no bike?" the older cop said.

Static. Then something Micah could not make out.

The officer looked at the boys, at the envelope on the ground, at the tiny pile of useless things from Micah's pockets on the hood. For one second Micah thought embarrassment might cross the man's face. It did not. What crossed was annoyance, thinner and meaner.

"How old are you?" he asked.

"Fifteen," Micah said.

"You?"

"Sixteen," Dre said.

"Either of you on probation?"

Micah stared at the papers by the tire. "School probation."

The younger cop gave a short laugh through his nose. "That tracks."

The older officer ignored him. He pressed a finger to the mic again, listened, then said, "Turn around."

Micah did not move.

"Turn around," the officer repeated. "You're being released."

They said released as if they had been holding something they owned.

The younger cop uncuffed him first. The metal peeled off skin already ridged red. Blood had dried sticky along Micah's cheek and into the corner of his mouth. When he straightened, the world tipped sideways for a second from heat and adrenaline and the hard clamp he had kept on everything inside himself. He steadied a hand on the hood. The older officer uncuffed Dre, then stepped back like proximity itself had become inconvenient.

"Next time when officers give commands, you move faster," he said.

Dre laughed once, high and unbelieving, then turned it into a cough.

Micah said, "I moved."

The younger officer slid his sunglasses down just enough for Micah to see his eyes. They were pale brown, flat, and tired in a way that made the whole thing uglier. It was not rage or panic. It was just another stop under bad sun.

"You kept talking," he said.

"You asked questions."

The older officer cut in. "Take your things and go home."

Mr. Baptiste had already stepped into the lot by then, not close, but closer than before. He bent and picked up the yellow envelope with two fingers, careful of the blood that had spotted the front. One of the forms was wet at the corner where it had landed in something dark and sticky near the curb. Miss Pauline was saying into her phone, "Yes, Marcia, right by the old laundry lot. No, baby, come. Come now." Her voice carried because she had never learned how to have private worry.

Micah wanted the ground to split and take him with the weeds and the broken bottle glass.

Mr. Baptiste held the envelope out. His pinky nail was still grown long on one hand from the old barber habit, lacquered clear. "Take your papers, youth."

Micah reached for them. The younger officer flinched toward his belt before remembering the scene was over. That tiny movement burned almost as bad as the cut.

"You all right?" Mr. Baptiste asked.

It was a stupid question, and it was the only one available.

"I'm good," Micah said.

He was not good. Blood had dried tight from cheek to jaw, and one side of his face had started pulsing in time with the heat. His wrists felt peeled. He said good because people were listening, and because the word cost less than the truth.

Dre wiped his upper lip with the back of his hand. "Man, that's some bullshit."

"Language," Miss Pauline said automatically, then looked at the blood on Micah's face and covered her mouth with her fingers.

The cops got back in their cruisers without apology and without any explanation somebody could take home and put in a sentence that made sense. Tires ground against the broken concrete. Then they were gone, leaving the lot to its regular trash and sun as if nothing had happened there that would still be happening later.

Marcia arrived three minutes after the cruisers left, half-running from the corner in nursing shoes she still wore even though she was between jobs. Her blouse was damp down the spine. She must have come straight off a bus because she still had her tote bag crossbody, the zipper open, papers and a plastic water bottle knocking against her hip.

She took one look at Micah and counted the damage the way she counted bills: blood first, drying from cheek to jaw; then the swelling climbing under his eye; then the red ridges at both wrists; then the dirt ground into the front of his shirt. The tote banged once against her hip and she was in front of him, fingers under his chin, thumb pulling his lip down to check his teeth while her other hand skimmed his collar and ribs for more blood.

"Spit," she said. "You lose any teeth? You seeing double? Who touched him?"

Miss Pauline started in at once. "The younger one slam him on the hood and then the wall and I called you same minute because I said no-"

"Which officer?" Marcia asked, eyes still on Micah.

Mr. Baptiste lifted his chin toward the avenue where the cruisers had gone. "Badge number maybe on the call log if somebody got it. It happened fast."

He smelled the outside on her, hot fabric and bus metal and the bleach she used to clean Celia's bathroom still living in her skin weeks after the job was gone. When she tipped his face toward the light, the skin under the cut pulled so hard he hissed.

"Look at me properly," she said.

He did. Her pupils flicked over the swelling, the blood, the grit stuck in the scrape. Then to his wrists. Then down to the envelope in his hand. That last part made something uglier pass through her mouth and disappear.

"Did they charge you?"

"No."

"Did they say why?"

"Matching description."

She let out one breath through her nose. "Of course."

Dre shifted beside him, suddenly smaller without the jokes doing their work. "Miss Marcia, we was just cutting through. We wasn't even doing nothing. They rolled up and-"

"I know you weren't," she said, not unkindly. She looked at the green lace with the house key bouncing against his chest, then at the sweat pasted to his cap line. "Go call your grandmother before she hears it from somebody else."

"Yes, ma'am."

He hesitated, like he wanted to say more, then touched Micah's shoulder with two fingers and headed for the sidewalk, already digging for his phone. Halfway there he looked back and tried on one more weak grin. "Don't let them put alcohol on that. You gonna cuss in front of your mother."

Micah almost smiled. It hurt too much.

Marcia turned to Miss Pauline. "Thank you for calling me."

"You know I had to."

"I know."

That was all the block got from her. She did not make a scene or shout after the cops who were already gone. Marcia locked her face smooth again, but the narrow muscle in her cheek jumped once and kept jumping.

"Can you walk?" she asked him.

"Yeah."

"Don't lie."

"I can walk."

"Fine. We're going to the clinic."

He looked at her then, really looked, and saw the calculation begin behind her eyes before she said it. Bus fare there and bus fare back were the easy parts; the fee at the window came next, then maybe medicine, maybe stitches, maybe an X-ray if his face was broken in some way they could not see yet. All of it moved behind her expression while the neighborhood watched. She reached into her tote, took out the small bottle of water, wet a corner of a paper towel from the envelope Mr. Baptiste had handed back, and cleaned the blood from under his eye enough to see the cut better. The towel came away pink, then red.

"It's not deep enough for emergency," she said, mostly to herself. "Walk-in first."

"Mummy, it's fine."

"Don't tell me what fine is with your face open."

The clinic was across town on a strip with a tax office, a botanica, and a storefront church whose windows were painted to look like cloud light. The air-conditioning inside hit Micah so hard his skin pebbled. The waiting room smelled like disinfectant trying to cover mildew, and a television bolted high in the corner played a daytime court show with the volume turned low. Under the plexiglass at the front desk a laminated sign listed fees in neat black print. New patient visit: $90. Wound care extra. Imaging separate.

Micah looked away from the sign fast, but not before the number fixed itself in him.

The receptionist had red reading glasses on a beaded chain and one heel tap worn white at the back of her shoe. She passed over a clipboard without looking up. "No insurance card?"

Marcia's voice went church-polite. "No insurance."

"Payment is due before the provider sees him."

"How much for just the visit?"

"Ninety."

There it was again. Ninety dollars was a whole bag of groceries, most of a light bill when the light bill still got paid on time, and more than half a week of whatever haircut money Micah had been scraping together two heads at a time.

Marcia took out her wallet. She kept her cash in different folds, and Micah knew those folds the way other boys knew game stats. There was a grocery fold, a bus fold, and a rent fold that was thin as tissue now. She peeled bills out one by one under the receptionist's patient eyes: two twenties, then two more, then a ten, then another ten from a side pocket where she hid emergency money so small it barely qualified. The woman counted it twice, stamped the intake sheet, and slid the clipboard back.

"Fill this out and take a seat."

Micah stared at the line asking for reason for visit. Three answers rose at once: matched description, police, because my face cost ninety dollars now. Each looked worse in writing than it already felt. Marcia took the clipboard from him and wrote for him in her tight practical print: facial injury after fall.

He looked at her.

She did not look back. "Sit down."

"That isn't what happened."

"I know exactly what happened." She kept writing. "Sit."

He sat. The vinyl chair squeaked under him. Across the room a toddler in only one shoe was asleep with his head in his auntie's lap, mouth open, while she ate salt crackers from her purse and watched the court show like it mattered. Somewhere in the back a printer started, stopped, started again. Micah pressed the folded gauze packet the nurse had handed him at intake against his cheek and felt the sting answer up into his eye.

Sorry sat in his throat with all the rest of it: that he had only taken the shortcut because it was faster, because the bus line was long, because he had been trying to make the errand smaller, not bigger. When the cop said matching description, the phrase had sounded almost casual, the way people said afternoon shower, like a thing that just happened in the weather of certain bodies. None of it came out. In the waiting room his shame felt less like speech and more like something packed under the skin with the gravel dust.

Marcia finished the forms and sat beside him. Up close he could see sweat dried white in the seam of her blouse. Her tote bag rested between her feet, one hand looped through the strap as if even here something might get taken.

"Does your head hurt or just the cheek?" she asked.

"Mostly the cheek."

"You feel sick?"

"No."

"You see double?"

"No."

She nodded once. She always started with practical questions and left feeling for later if any room remained.

"Miss Pauline said they had you on the hood."

"Yeah."

"And everybody saw."

It was not a question. He stared at the courtroom TV where a man in a cheap suit was lying badly about a car note.

"Yeah," he said.

Marcia sat with that a second. Then she said, quieter, "Lift the gauze."

He did. Blood had soaked a rusty moon into it. She took a fresh square from the little paper sleeve and pressed it to his cheek herself, gentler than he expected.

"Hold it there."

He held it. Her thumb had a tiny nick near the nail from some house job or kitchen knife or cheap plastic package that had fought back. He stared at that instead of her face.

"Mummy."

"What."

"You put fall."

She looked straight ahead. "The man at the desk didn't ask for truth. He asked what got us through the window fastest."

"So that's it?"

"For now, that's it."

For now was grown-up code for leave it ugly and call it handled. Everybody on the block had already seen his face on the hood, so what was a lie on a clipboard supposed to do, un-happen it? He bit the inside of his cheek, tasted fresh metal, and let the gauze drink more blood while the clinic printer spit out somebody else's bill in the back.

After a while the television changed segments and the air-conditioning clicked into a louder gear. Marcia reached into the side pocket of her tote and found the yellow envelope. She flattened the bent forms on her knee, smoothing each page with the side of her hand. His blood had dotted the corner of the parent acknowledgment form and feathered the ink on the red disciplinary stamp.

"They still want this back Monday?" she asked.

"Yeah."

"Then they'll get it back Monday."

He watched her read the highlighted lines. Behavior expectations. Reentry conditions. Parent cooperation. Student accountability. All the clean words institutions used when they wanted to describe pressure without naming who got to apply it. She did not make a speech. She only kept smoothing the pages flat, even the one tire had nearly taken, until the paper stopped trying to curl.

At the desk, the receptionist called another name. A woman rose, gathered up her purse and pharmacy bag, and went through the door. The fee sheet under the plexiglass caught the fluorescent light so hard it looked wet.

Micah pressed the gauze tighter. His cheek throbbed. His wrists burned. Behind all of it was the bright fixed image that would not leave: his own face in the silver hood, flattened and red, while people he knew watched from the sidewalk and decided how to carry it home. The officers had uncuffed him and driven off, and the neighborhood had exhaled, and still the day kept charging them for what it had done.

When the nurse finally opened the door and called his name, Marcia stood first. She tucked the forms back into the envelope, slipped the receipt into her wallet, and lifted her tote. Ninety dollars gone before anyone had even touched the wound clean. Micah rose with the gauze pressed to his face and followed her down the bright hallway while Marcia kept her hand looped through the tote strap, the clinic receipt folded inside her wallet against what was left of the bus money.


# Chapter 13

While Marcia and Micah were still at the clinic across town, Tiana climbed the outside stairs to the immigration-help office on Fifty-Fourth with the manila folder under her arm already gone soft at the corners. The office sat above a tax place and beside a beauty-supply store with three bald mannequin heads in the window, each of them wearing a wig that cost more than the sneakers on Tiana's feet. Somebody had taped printer paper to the glass door downstairs.

FEDERAL FEES BY MONEY ORDER ONLY.

BRING ORIGINALS.

NO EXCEPTIONS.

The words had the bluntness of rules written for people who were already tired. Inside, a window unit pushed out a cold that never reached the waiting chairs. Four people sat with packets in their laps, all of them holding paper the way church people held envelopes when the amount inside mattered. A baby in yellow socks sucked the metal snap on her mother's purse. A man in a work shirt kept rubbing a photocopy flat against his knee as if the ink would settle if he smoothed it hard enough.

Tiana signed her name on the sheet clipped to the desk and took the last plastic chair. On the counter somebody had left a jar of pens with fake daisies tied around it. The flowers were gray with dust. She opened her folder again even though she already knew what was in it: the hold notice from UF, the scholarship letter that still looked expensive and unreal with its dark-blue seal, a copy of her expired resident card, and the yellow legal-pad page where she had written every number she had been told to call. Most of the numbers had sent her to other numbers. One woman in Gainesville had told her, in a voice so crisp it seemed pressed, that if Tiana wanted to preserve aid eligibility she needed proof of a pending renewal and a corrected residency file. She had said preserve as if the thing in danger was produce.

Tiana had written it down anyway: proof of pending renewal, corrected residency file. Every phrase sounded like it belonged to somebody whose mother kept things in labeled drawers and whose father mailed forms the week he said he would.

Her phone buzzed with a message from her manager asking if she could pick up Saturday. Tiana stared at the screen a second too long, doing the math by reflex. Two extra shifts would get her close to the filing fee if she stopped buying lunch on break and if the light bill did not come in ugly and if Micah's clinic bill stayed where Marcia had guessed it might. It would not be enough. It had not been enough all summer. The arithmetic of this house always changed shape right before the answer.

"Tiana Campbell?"

She looked up. The woman in the doorway wore office slacks with lilac sneakers and had one of those jawlines that made even resting look disapproving. Her wig sat a little too far back from her forehead. A roll of antacids and a stapler shared the desk behind her.

"Yes."

"Come."

The office was barely an office, just a desk, two chairs, a humming printer, and a corkboard crowded with flyers for citizenship classes, tax help, and jobs cleaning airport hotels. The woman waited until Tiana sat before she held out her hand.

"I'm Miss Gumbs. You brought whatever the school gave you?"

Tiana passed over the hold notice and the scholarship letter. Miss Gumbs read fast, her lips tightening only once, then turned the papers toward Tiana and tapped the hold line with one square fingernail painted a thin shell pink.

"They're not saying no yet," she said. "They're saying they want the file able to survive being checked by somebody who doesn't know you and doesn't care."

Tiana let out a breath through her nose. "That doesn't feel different."

Miss Gumbs gave her a brief look that was not unkind. "No, baby, it doesn't. Still different on paper." She reached for a printed checklist from a stack and split it into two columns with quick hard strokes. "Two tracks at once. Renewal on one side, school on the other. If you try to do this politely, one after the other, school starts without you."

She wrote as she spoke, quick block letters, not wasting loops on anyone's name.

"For the renewal I need the expired-card copy, birth certificate, passport, two passport photos, current address. Fee is five hundred and forty unless the waiver sticks."

"Five hundred and forty?"

Miss Gumbs tapped the page. "Don't look at me like I printed it in the back room. Can your household prove what money comes in, or is it cash, favors, and men saying they'll handle it?"

The number landed with the flat weight of rent. Five hundred and forty actual dollars could live inside their apartment for weeks. Tiana thought of the cracked blue jar near the stove where Marcia dropped bus change and singles and quarters like apologies. She thought of the twenty Delroy had pressed into Micah's hand for pain pills and then asked back for cab fare two hours later.

"Mostly the last one."

"Then we drag it into paper," Miss Gumbs said. "Pay stubs for who's working. Rent. Utility bill. Tax return if anybody filed. If nobody filed, we prove that too. You cannot send a federal office a sad story and hope they reward honesty."

Tiana almost smiled at that, but the smile died before it fully reached her mouth.

"For the school I need the renewal receipt once this gets filed, the expired card, one parent ID, income proof, and anything that links the family names cleanly. Marriage certificate if the names wobble. Birth certificate if they ask. Whatever stops them pretending you appeared from nowhere."

The room's cold air seemed to dry out only the skin on Tiana's face, leaving the rest of her damp. "They flagged missing parent paperwork in June. My father said he handled it."

Miss Gumbs looked at her over the top of the checklist. "Did he file it, or did he calm you down?"

Tiana looked at the desk instead of answering.

Miss Gumbs nodded once. Fathers who calmed girls down instead of filing papers plainly did not impress her. "All right. Then stop talking like the promise counts. We work from what is in your hands." She slid the checklist over. "Do you have originals?"

Tiana thought of the apartment, of papers moved three times in her memory and maybe more before that. Marcia kept things in grocery bags inside bigger bags, in old shoeboxes, in envelopes tucked into Bible covers, every important object hidden inside two less important ones so nobody could steal it and so maybe life itself would forget to ask. But for the oldest papers there was the cedar trunk under Marcia's bed. Tiana had seen it hauled from one place to another her whole life, all brass corners and scratches, too pretty to match any room they had ever lived in.

"My mother keeps everything," she said. "Just not where daylight can find it."

"Good. Daylight is overrated." Miss Gumbs pulled open a drawer and took out a second sheet, this one headed FEE WAIVER SUPPORTING DOCUMENTS. "Listen to me carefully. Do not bring me a sack full of old papers and expect me to make your family into a packet by miracle. Sort first. Originals in one pile. Copies in another. Income proof together. Identification together. You understand?"

"Yes."

"And do not mail originals unless the instructions say originals. Government offices lose things every day and then swear to Jesus they never touched them."

That got a small, involuntary laugh out of Tiana. Miss Gumbs smiled with only one side of her mouth. Humor, with her, came out rationed.

"You in school right now?"

"I graduated."

"That's not what I asked."

Tiana looked back at the hold notice. The blue seal of the scholarship seemed almost insulting now, so clean, so sure there would be a line from talent to arrival. "Not in the way I was supposed to be."

Miss Gumbs tapped the paper again. "Then let's keep the file alive. Alive wins most weeks."

She marked the cheap copy place, the passport-photo shop on Sixty-Second, and the lines on the fee-waiver affidavit people tended to leave blank and then regret. She did not waste syllables pretending the process was fair. She said if the receipt notice came, the school would have a number to enter and a date to hang hope on, and institutions respected numbers in a way they did not respect girls who spoke complete sentences in a pressed blouse.

By the time Tiana left the office her checklist had arrows all over it, circles around ORIGINAL and COPY, and one note in Miss Gumbs's hand: Do not delay waiting for perfect.

On the landing she stepped aside so a man carrying a toddler could pass. The child had a purple sticker on her shirt that said BRAVE VISITOR. Tiana stood there long enough for the humid air to melt the last of the office chill off her skin, then called Marcia.

It rang four times. On the fifth, Marcia answered with her breath already in motion. "What?"

Tiana could hear traffic and the scrape of bus brakes. "I found out what they need."

"And?"

"Everything. Birth certificate original. Passport. Copy of the card. Income proof if I'm doing the fee waiver. Maybe the marriage certificate too."

Marcia was quiet for one beat, and in that beat Tiana could hear the quick calculation she knew better than some of her own prayers. Which papers, where, whether they still existed, whether asking Delroy would turn into a fight, whether the papers were dry, whether the papers were legible, whether anything in this family had been allowed to remain simple long enough to retrieve.

"Then it's the trunk," Marcia said.

"Under your bed?"

"Where else? The cedar one. If you break the latch, you better leave the country before I come home."

"Mummy."

"I'm serious. The key is on the blue ribbon in my jewelry dish. You see the folder marked school, don't fling it all over the place. You hear me?"

Tiana looked down the stairwell, where somebody had swept hair and dust into a corner and left it there. "I hear you."

"And keep your brother out of it. He'll swear he's helping and then I won't find one blessed thing again."

"He's asleep half the day."

"Good. Leave him asleep." Marcia exhaled hard. "You ate?"

It was such an ordinary question that Tiana almost hated it. "Not yet."

"Then buy a patty or something before you come in here and start sorting your life on an empty stomach. Hungry people get careless with paper."

The line clicked dead.

Tiana stood with the phone still against her ear. Hungry people get careless with paper. It was exactly the kind of sentence Marcia made without noticing, household law disguised as irritation. Tiana slid the phone into her bag, flattened the checklist, and headed for the bus stop with the sense, thin but real, that the day had offered her a route. It happened to run straight through whatever her mother had buried.

***

The apartment smelled like bleach, hot fabric, and the fried fish somebody in the front house had overdone. When Tiana pushed open the door, Micah was dead asleep on the pullout with one sneaker still on, his phone dark on his chest. The swelling along his cheek had gone yellow at the edges, turning bruise into weather. Somebody at the clinic had taped gauze just under his eye, and sometime during sleep he had scratched at it hard enough to lift one corner. There was ointment on the milk crate beside him and a fast-food cup full of melted ice.

The television was on mute. A gospel station played softly from somebody else's yard through the thin wall. In the narrow strip of floor between Marcia's bed and the dresser, Kerene stood in a black skirt slip and one of Marcia's old T-shirts, pressing the collar of a blouse with her hair straightener. She had clipped the blouse onto a hanger and was working the plate over each crease with grim concentration.

She looked over her shoulder when Tiana came in. "Don't laugh. The iron dead, and I refuse to show up looking like I slept in a bread bag."

"I wasn't laughing."

"You were about to. Same thing." Kerene snapped the straightener shut and held the blouse up to the light. "Well?"

"It looks better than before."

"That's this blouse too. Better than before. Good enough till the bus and the heat start at it again."

She said it lightly, but the dry-cleaning plastic for her one interview outfit still hung from the closet knob, and at the head of the folding cot she had her passport tucked inside a freezer bag under the pillow, sealed against rain, sweat, and anybody else's fingers. She caught Tiana's glance and lifted one shoulder.

"Don't judge me. First month here, I checked for that passport in my sleep. Still do sometimes."

Tiana set down her bag. "The paper I need is in the trunk."

"Then why are you standing there like the trunk is a person you have to forgive?"
