She was standing there because it was Marcia's trunk, because it had traveled farther than any of them, because the little brass lock on it had always made Tiana think there must be things in there serious enough to require metal, and because once, when she was ten, she had lifted the lid an inch and Marcia had said, not in a voice meant for children, Leave that.

Instead she said, "She told me not to break the latch."

Kerene gave her a look. "So don't. Come."

Marcia's bed was low to the floor. Dust bunnies and a single hoop earring had collected under it. Tiana got on her knees and reached into the dark until her fingers found wood. The trunk dragged out with a groan that made Micah grunt in his sleep and turn his face deeper into the cushion.

It was heavier than it looked. It was actual cedar, the grain still red in places where it had not been scuffed pale. Its brass corners were dented from moves. The latch kept the shine of fingers. There was a sticker on one side from Norman Manley International, old enough that only KINGSTON still showed clear.

Kerene set the blouse aside and crouched opposite her. "Pretty thing."

"It's older than me."

"Most useful things are."

The key on the blue ribbon was smaller than Tiana expected. It stuck halfway, then turned. When she lifted the lid the smell came up at once, cedar oil and old paper and the trapped sweetness of something that had spent years closed in the dark, with the first damp edge of mildew waiting inside it. It smelled of storage and survival, of a life kept folded.

On top sat a crocheted doily yellowed at one edge, then a stack of church programs tied with ribbon, then folders, brown envelopes, a baby shoe wrapped in tissue, a pharmacy bottle full of buttons, and a cracked photo album. Nothing was in any order she could see, yet nothing had been thrown in carelessly either. Marcia had packed it the way some people braided hair, strand over strand until the whole thing held.

Tiana pulled out the first folder and found vaccination records, report cards, a copy of Micah's birth certificate, old dental receipts, and a booklet from a middle school science fair she had won and forgotten. The second held rent receipts from apartments she remembered by smell: the one with the mango tree and rats in the wall, the one by the canal that always made sheets feel wet. She made piles on the bedspread the way Miss Gumbs had told her, with originals in one place, copies in another, and income proof wherever she saw it. Trash went nowhere because in this house yesterday's useless paper had a way of becoming next month's proof.

Kerene worked beside her without making the moment instructional. She slid envelopes open with a bobby pin, read dates aloud, stacked duplicates, smoothed creases with the side of her hand. Once she found a packet of old salon appointment cards held together by a rubber band gone stiff and laughed under her breath.

"Your mother saved the card from every set she ever took out and every set she ever put back in. That's a woman who respects evidence."

"She respects not having to buy the same thing twice."

"Same thing. She just calls it common sense."

The joke should have irritated Tiana, but instead it steadied her. It made Marcia's hoarding look less like madness and more like a method nobody had bothered to respect.

Micah stirred and opened one eye. "Why does it smell like old church in here?"

His voice came out gummy with sleep. He pushed himself up on one elbow and immediately touched his cheek, not dramatic, just checking whether the side of his face was still his. The gauze had slipped low enough to show the red scrape underneath.

The sight of the lifted tape tightened something low in Tiana's stomach. The clinic had cleaned him up just enough to make the damage look organized, which somehow felt meaner.

"Go back to sleep," Tiana said.

"Can't. Y'all dragging furniture like bailiffs." He blinked at the trunk. "What's that?"

"Paper."

"Everything in this house is paper till it turns into a problem."

He let himself drop back onto the cushion with one forearm over his eyes. A minute later his breath went heavy again.

Tiana kept sorting. Her knees hurt against the tile. Sweat gathered at the back of her neck. A stack of Marcia's old pay stubs appeared inside a tax envelope, then a marriage certificate with the embossed seal half worn down from years of being pressed flat and put away again. There was Delroy's passport, expired. Tiana's own first passport, child photo clipped inside, her face rounder, suspicious already. Her original birth certificate in a protective sleeve with Jamaica stamped across the top in black letters so severe they looked angry. She exhaled slow through her nose, the kind of relief that did not make a sound because sound would have wasted time.

"Good?" Kerene asked.

"Good enough to keep going."

At the bottom of one folder, under Marcia's high-school transcript and a certificate from a home-health course, Tiana found a large white envelope with MIAMI DADE COLLEGE SCHOOL OF PRACTICAL NURSING printed in blue across the front.

She stopped.

The envelope had been slit carefully and then tucked back in among other papers, saved there in the dark. Inside was a letter on thick paper.

Congratulations.

She read the first line twice before the words arranged themselves into sense. Marcia Campbell had been offered admission to the January practical-nursing cohort. The letter listed the orientation date, the tuition deposit due, the required immunization records, white all-leather clinical shoes, navy scrub pants, and the warning that students arriving late would forfeit placement.

The list struck Tiana harder than the congratulations because it was ordinary in the exact way her own scholarship packet had been ordinary. It spoke in deadline language, a future made out of dates and shoes and money that had to exist by a certain morning. Somebody had circled the deposit amount in blue ink and written in the margin, Call about evenings? The handwriting was Marcia's, younger only in its roundness.

Kerene leaned over, read the header, and let out a soft hiss. "Your mother never told me this."

Tiana shook her head.

"She talks like she came into the world already tired and carrying bleach."

That almost made Tiana smile, and because it almost did it hurt. She pictured a younger woman on a bus with a nursing-school letter in her handbag and enough nerve to imagine white shoes staying white. Then Marcia's real life flooded back in borrowed scrub tops, pots on the stove, Micah being told not to stain his school shirt. The picture would not settle. Every version of Marcia she knew had been compact, strong-backed, already braced against somebody needing something. She had never once seemed like a person with an almost.

Tiana looked again at the note in the margin. Call about evenings? She could see Marcia making the question practical before it even had room to become hope.

"Maybe she got pregnant," Kerene said softly.

"With who, me or Micah."

"Either one would wreck a timetable quick."

Tiana slid the letter back into its envelope but did not put it away yet. Under it were a requirements sheet, a campus map, and a list of clinical supplies so precise it made her throat ache: bandage scissors, penlight, watch with a second hand. Marcia had folded the supply list small enough to disappear into a palm.

"I didn't know she even wanted that," Tiana said.

Kerene sat back on her heels. For once there was no joke waiting. "Maybe one bill hit, then another, then another. After that what was she going to say, move, I need white nursing shoes?"

From outside came the slam of a car door, a rooster protesting from somewhere up the alley, then the high laugh of the landlord's granddaughter. The room held its own heat. Tiana laid the nursing packet aside with more care than she meant to.

"Keep looking," she said. "I need the income papers."

They did. They found more pay stubs, an old W-2, a notice from FPL, and a church envelope with rent money amounts written on the back in Marcia's hand. There was also a thin folder labeled IMMIGRATION that turned out to hold copy after copy of things Delroy had once promised to mail. Tiana found her card copy there and hated how vindicating that was. Of course he had copied it. Of course he had meant, at least for one afternoon, to fix the problem before life or pride or cowardice took the errand away from him.

At the very bottom of the trunk, wedged beneath an issue of Caribbean Beat and an old baby blanket, was a long white envelope from a check-cashing place on Thirty-Sixth Street. Tiana almost tossed it onto the pile with utility receipts. Then Devon's name caught her eye.

DEVON CAMPBELL.

She slid out the contents. They were money transfer receipts, folded in half, the paper gone soft at the creases. Each one named Delroy Campbell as sender and Devon Campbell in Jamaica as receiver. The amounts came in blunt little stabs: 60, 80, 45, 100. One was dated in late June. Another came in July, two days after Yvette's partner money should have paid out. Another was from the week Delroy had sworn he was handling the renewal.

The room shrank. The air went gummy around the bed, the folding cot, the dresser with the drawer that never closed right. Tiana read the first receipt again, then the second, then the third, waiting for the numbers to blink into some harmless explanation. They didn't. There was no explanatory note, no speech about obligation, and no confession tucked in. There were only the little printed facts of money leaving this house and landing in another country under his son's name.

She knew Devon existed. Jamaica was full of cousins and half-siblings and names that entered the room at Christmas and left again before dishes were washed. But knowing a person existed was not the same as seeing the carbon proof of what had been chosen for him. Sixty dollars that could have been bus fare and clinic copay and groceries. A hundred dollars that could have been the beginning of her filing fee. Forty-five dollars, almost insulting in its smallness until she thought how many times smallness had broken them.

"What?" Kerene said.

Tiana handed her one receipt.

Kerene read it, then another. The brightness left her face without any theatrics. "Oh."

Recognition crossed Kerene's face first. That kind of recognition made Tiana angrier than shouting would have.

"You knew?"

"No." Kerene looked up sharp. "No, girl. I know the shape, that's all."

Tiana ran her thumb along the printed edge until gray transfer ink smudged her skin. The receipts had been bundled with a rubber band and pushed under blankets and old school forms. Hidden and saved. That was worse. Kept meant he meant to remember. Kept meant this was a line of decisions, not one panicked move.

Micah snored once, ugly and young. The sound made everything around it seem more exposed. Tiana imagined walking out to wherever Delroy had gone and throwing the receipts at his chest. She imagined laying them on the table for Marcia and watching the whole room split down its cheapest seams. She imagined the relief of simple anger, how clean it would feel for about twelve seconds.

Instead she sat there with Devons and dates in her lap and the nursing-school envelope beside her, and simplicity would not come back.

Marcia had once folded her own way forward small enough to bury. Delroy had been feeding another child with money he had let Tiana chase in circles. Neither fact pardoned either of them. It only ruined the clean version of blame.

Kerene spoke first, quiet enough not to wake Micah. "You can start a war right now if that's what you need."

Tiana looked at her.

"I'm not saying don't," Kerene said. "I'm saying if you start with him right now, your mother still walks in to the same mess and that form's still sitting there blank."

That was annoyingly sensible, which made it useful.

Tiana stacked the receipts back into order by date. Her hands were steady, which frightened her a little. Steady meant she was close to becoming cold. She slid the bundle back into the envelope and set it beside the nursing-school packet.

Outside, footsteps came up the alley. Delroy's cough sounded once near the side door, then stopped. Tiana's head jerked toward it.

Kerene put a hand flat on the bedspread, not touching Tiana, just there. "Decide first."

The knob turned. Delroy came in with sweat darkening the collar of his shirt and his right arm held stiff in the sling Marcia hated because it made him look more injured than he admitted to being. He had a bag from the pharmacy in his left hand and a domino tile in his pocket, the outline of it printing through the thin fabric like he needed the shape of a game near his thigh. He glanced from the open trunk to the papers spread everywhere and gave a short whistle.

"Lawd. Y'all searching my sins too?"

The joke arrived right on schedule, his first line of defense same as ever. Tiana looked at his face, at the graying around his temples she had only started noticing this summer, at the sweat drying white on his upper lip. For a violent second she wanted to put Devon's name in the room and watch what happened to his mouth.

Instead she said, "I'm finding what the school needs."

He nodded too fast. Relief was already trying to stand up in him. "Good. Good. That's good."

He set the pharmacy bag beside Micah's melted ice, leaned over to look at the piles, and winced when the movement tugged his shoulder. "Need any help?"

She did not need help. She needed him to have been different six months ago. She needed the money back. She needed the lie unspoken. Help was not the word for any of it.

"We're all right," Kerene said before Tiana could answer.

Delroy looked from one of them to the other. Some part of him must have felt the weather change because the joke left his face. "All right then."

He went to the sink and began trying to twist open the pain-pill bottle one-handed, the childproof cap resisting him with cheerful cruelty. Tiana watched the muscles in his jaw work. Then she picked up the marriage certificate and asked Kerene for another envelope. Keeping her hands busy was the only way to stop the day from dropping out from under her.

***

By dusk the table had turned into a filing system built out of whatever the apartment could spare, with a sugar jar holding pens, a butter knife lifting old staples, and two supermarket circulars folded into temporary dividers labeled ORIGINALS, COPIES TO MAKE, and INCOME. The passport photos still had to be done tomorrow, and the copy place on Sixty-Second would get the rest of Tiana's tip money, but the packet had a body now. That mattered.

Kerene sat opposite her in the blouse she had revived with the straightener, now buttoned and hanging open over shorts because she refused to sweat in good clothes any longer than necessary. She was painting her toenails with a bottle of coral polish so old the brush kept sticking, pausing every minute to read another line on the checklist.

"This fee-waiver statement wants every person in the household and how much they bring in," she said. "These people truly believe poor folks keep accounts like corporations."

"Miss Gumbs said if nobody filed taxes we have to prove nobody filed taxes."

"So now you have to prove what nobody's got. Nasty little trick."

On the stove, oil ticked under a pot where Marcia had set rice to warm and then gone to shower. The bathroom fan rasped like it had asthma. Micah was awake now, sitting sideways on the pullout with a bag of frozen peas against his cheek and his school packet unopened in his lap. Every few minutes he peeked into his phone camera to check whether the bruise had improved. It had not.

"You still look rough," Kerene told him.

"Thanks. That's healing me right up."

He said it without any real heat. Then he went back to studying his own face like he might negotiate with it.

Tiana filled in block letters where the form allowed it, writing her name and address, copying the A-number from the card, and copying the date of entry from the old passport. She hated how satisfying the act was. The pen moved cleanly, the boxes filled, and the information finally stayed where she put it. All summer she had felt chased by other people's unfinished errands. Tonight the page let her catch one.

Then she reached the household income section and stopped.

Marcia's pay stubs from before Celia died. Tiana's part-time wages. Delroy's irregular cash, impossible to document cleanly because men like him were expected to exist and labor without ever quite becoming records. The fee-waiver instructions wanted numbers and treated them like the whole truth. They never included late rent, debt to men who came to the door, clinic bills, food that had to stretch, the fact that one bad afternoon with police could cost ointment and ice and hours nobody would reimburse.

"Put what can be proved," Kerene said, reading her face without looking up. "Save the rest for when somebody's actually trying to help."

Tiana snorted.

"I'm serious. Start telling them the whole house and they'll send you back for one more paper just because they can."

That was one of Kerene's gifts, Tiana was starting to see. She could make a sentence ugly enough to be useful, not comforting. Earlier in the summer Tiana had mostly noticed the cot, the extra body, the way her own room stopped being hers once Kerene arrived with two suitcases and a smile too bright to trust. Now she watched Kerene blow on her toes and then tuck the polish bottle upright in an empty mug so it would not spill, and thought about the passport under the pillow, the careful collar, the way she had sorted a stranger's family papers like she believed order could be borrowed for an hour.

"When I came through the airport," Kerene said, still looking at her feet, "I had every number written three ways. In my phone, in my bra, in the back of my Bible. Immigration man asked one question and my mind still blank. I was there sweating in a jacket I should've thrown in the sea, trying to remember my own auntie's street number. So after that, yes, I keep everything. Boarding pass, receipt, stupid little baggage sticker. If a place wants proof you were standing in it breathing, you keep proof."

It was the most direct thing Tiana had heard from her all day. There was no shine on it and no laugh to break the surface.

"Did you think about going back?" Tiana asked.

Kerene twisted the cap back onto the nail polish. "First week? Every day. Second week too. Then I checked what I had left and realized even homesickness costs money. So I stayed."

Micah gave a short laugh and immediately winced. "That's bleak."

"It's accurate," Kerene said.

Marcia came out of the bathroom toweling off the back of her neck, hair wrapped tight, clean T-shirt already dampening at the collar. Her eyes went first to the piles on the table, then to the trunk pushed back under the bed, then to Tiana's face. She saw more than she asked.

"You found the birth paper?"

"Yes."

"Marriage certificate?"

"Yes."

"Pay stubs?"

"Some. The old W-2 too."

Marcia nodded and crossed to the stove. The room filled with the smell of reheated rice and onion.

Tiana felt the nursing packet's existence under the room like buried heat. Even with the envelope back in the trunk, she could feel where it sat, waiting to make her mother legible in a new and inconvenient way.

"Mummy."

"What now?"

"You ever get into nursing school?"

The towel stopped at the back of Marcia's neck for a beat that still managed to feel crowded. Then Marcia said, "You found old paper."

"I found your name on it."

"Then put it back where you found it."

"Why didn't you go?"

Marcia lifted the pot lid, let steam hit her face, and set it back down. "Because the deposit wanted cash, the program wanted daytime, and this family was already charging interest. That enough for now?"

The answer was more than Tiana had ever heard Marcia confess at one time, and it still felt trimmed to the bone. She felt the old childish urge to pry anyway, to ask which emergency had eaten the deposit and whether anybody had warned her mother that wanting something could turn into one more bill.

Tiana had no answer ready. "Tomorrow I need current pay proof," she said. "For the fee waiver."

"I can print the agency stub at Miss Pauline's if her internet is acting right."

"And if not?"

"Then I'll go to the library and harass the machine till it obeys me." Marcia took out plates. "You get the passport photos tomorrow too. Don't wait on that."

Tiana looked at the side of her mother's face. The nursing letter sat only ten feet away now, back inside its envelope in the trunk, buried again because dinner still had to be served and forms still had to be finished and nobody in this house could afford to let revelation take up the whole evening. She could press harder. She could ask whose need had come first, whose bill, whose bad timing, and turn the whole thing into another debt to be argued over.

The satisfaction would be immediate and also cheap.

Delroy came in from outside carrying a loaf of bread under one arm and a packet of store-brand ham in his good hand. He had taken off the sling but not because he was better; he just hated how it looked. He nudged the door shut with his heel and glanced at the table.

"You still at it?"

"Yes," Tiana said.

"All right." He set the bread down. "I can give you twenty toward the photos."

For one hot instant she saw the white envelope again, the neat columns of money transferred out of this house. Twenty toward the photos. As if money moved cleanly once it left his hand.

Instead she put her pen down carefully. "Keep it for Micah's prescription. I just need you not to handle anything for me unless I can watch it happen."

Delroy's face changed by degrees. First came offense, because he hated being spoken to like a boy. Then came shame, because he knew why. Then came a quick attempt at lightness he did not quite complete. "I said I was sorry for that."

"I know what you said."

The room went quiet enough for the bathroom fan to sound suddenly enormous. Micah looked up from his phone. Kerene watched the table like it had become a card game.

Marcia set a plate down harder than necessary. "Eat first, quarrel after."

She did not wait for agreement. She shoved the ham toward the middle, told Micah to get two bites down before the pill, asked Tiana what time the photo place opened, and told Kerene to kill the flame before the rice scorched. By the time Delroy got the bread bag open, dinner had become the job in front of them.

Delroy opened the bread bag with his teeth and said nothing else. That silence cost Tiana more than a fight would have. A fight would have let her spend the afternoon's discoveries all at once, turn knowledge into noise, watch people bleed where she could see it. Silence made her keep Devon's name behind her teeth.

They ate at the table because there was no room not to. Rice, fried eggs stretched over the top, sliced ham browned in the pan until the edges curled. Micah complained that chewing on one side felt stupid. Kerene told him stupid was free and therefore abundant. When Marcia asked what time the photo place opened, Tiana answered at once. When Delroy reached toward the checklist to read over her shoulder, she laid two fingers on it and kept reading until he pulled his hand back and asked for the pepper sauce instead.

After dinner Micah took his pain pill and fell asleep again with the television talking low. Marcia packed tomorrow's pay stubs into Tiana's folder and wrote Miss Pauline's Wi-Fi password on the back of an envelope in case the library printer failed. Delroy rinsed plates one-handed and dropped a spoon twice because the shoulder would not lift cleanly. Each clatter struck Tiana's nerves. She kept filling lines.

When the packet was as complete as it could get for one night, she carried the originals back to the bed. The trunk slid out more easily this time. Dust had loosened under its feet, and her hands knew the weight now. She laid the birth certificate in its sleeve, tucked the marriage certificate beneath it, replaced the passports. Then she picked up the nursing-school envelope and the remittance receipts.

For a moment she held one in each hand.

The nursing letter bent slow under her thumb. The transfer receipts crackled cheap and dry, already fading at the edges.

Tiana could keep the receipts. She could carry them in her own folder like ammunition. She could leave the nursing letter out where Marcia would have to see it and explain. Instead she put the nursing packet back where she had found it, under the certificates and old school forms, and slid the receipts into their check-cashing envelope before laying them flat at the bottom of the trunk.

It wasn't forgiveness or mercy. It was storage.

She closed the lid softly and turned the key.

When she stood, the room seemed no larger, the air no cooler. Nothing had been solved except what papers needed to go where. But the file was alive now. Miss Gumbs had been right about that much. Alive looked like a stack clipped together with a black binder clip borrowed from Marcia's church notebook, a tomorrow full of copies and passport photos and a printer that might or might not work, a future small enough to fit under her arm and still heavy.

Tiana slid the packet into her folder and pushed it under her pillow on the couch before lying down. Across the room Kerene was already asleep on the folding cot, one hand flung over her head, the freezer bag with the passport peeking from under the pillowcase like a ridiculous, necessary secret. In the kitchenette Marcia asked where the black binder clip had gone. Delroy found it near the sink and set it beside her hand without brushing her fingers.

The folder edge pressed into her cheek through the pillow, paper instead of softness and proof instead of comfort.

She closed her eyes and let the apartment keep going around her, the sink stacked with plates and Micah's white school polo still stiff where the blood had dried, waiting for whoever had the hands left to scrub it.


# Chapter 14

The next evening the phone started its crawling again while Marcia had both hands on Micah's school polo in the sink.

It jittered somewhere under the couch cushion where Delroy had shoved it, buzzing against the cracked vinyl with the same stubborn nerve a trapped roach had when bleach hit it and did not kill it fast enough. Marcia stood at the basin with Micah's white school polo sunk in gray water, working soap into the brown half-moon under the collar where his blood had dried after the clinic. The cheap lemon detergent had already roughened the skin across her knuckles. Every time the phone shook, the water in the sink gave a faint answer off the pipe.

"Answer it," she said.

Delroy lay lengthwise on the narrow bed in his undershirt, one forearm over his eyes, his right shoulder propped on a pillow that had gone flat months ago. "In a minute."

"You said that two calls ago."

"Then maybe I mean it serious now."

He smiled when he said it, not enough to show teeth, just enough to make it sound like weather instead of warning. That smile had bought him more time in this world than wages ever had. Marcia wrung out the shirt hard enough to hear stitches complain and hung it over the faucet for another soak.

The apartment held everybody's evening at once. Tiana sat at the little table under the window with her document folder open in square anxious piles: originals, copies, things still needed. She had clipped her hair up with a black claw clip and was reading a form so closely Marcia could see her mouth move on the instructions without sound. Micah sprawled on the floor with one sneaker off, one on, picking at the frayed lace tip with his thumbnail like the thing had personally offended him. The yellow gone loose under his cheekbone made him look older in some angles and stupidly young in others. On the folding cot, Kerene had one gold sandal in her lap and a spool of clear fishing line between her knees, mending the torn strap with the concentration of a surgeon because that pair still had to pass for good shoes on Saturday nights.

The room smelled of bleach, hot cloth, and the onions Marcia had sweated down for rice and tinned mackerel before the phone began its complaining. Through the thin wall came the usual Little River chorus: a radio bass line from the front house, somebody laughing too hard in the alley, a rooster with no respect for clocks. Heat stayed pressed against the low ceiling even after sunset started thinking about them.

The phone buzzed again.

Tiana's eyes flicked up, then back down.

Micah said, "Whoever that is, they got commitment."

"Mind your business," Marcia said.

"It's my business if it keeps vibrating like some cheap sex toy."

Kerene barked out a laugh before she could stop it. "Boy, everything in your mouth have to come out dirty?"

"Not everything."

Marcia shot him a look. He shrugged and leaned back on his elbows, playing bored so hard it made the room smaller. That was how fear worked in him now. It did not make him quiet. It made him stupid on purpose.

The buzzing died under the cushion.

Marcia turned back to the sink. For three seconds the apartment had peace.

Then someone knocked.

It was not a neighbor's knock or the quick double tap of somebody borrowing pepper or asking if the laundry line was free. This one had patience in it: heavy knuckles, no hurry, somebody on the other side who had already decided he would stand there until the door opened.

Delroy took his arm off his eyes.

Marcia looked at him. He looked at the turned-off fan, at the ceiling stain over the kitchenette, at anything but her.

"Who is it?" Micah asked.

"Nobody for you," Marcia said, wiping her hands on a dish towel already striped brown from onion skins.

She got to the door before the second knock and pulled it open only as wide as her body.

Claude Brown stood on the little patch of concrete between the apartment and the leaning chain-link gate, dressed too neat for the heat in a blue work shirt with the sleeves rolled to the same height on each forearm. He held a baseball cap in one hand and kept worrying the cheap purple bead bracelet riding tight on his other wrist, the kind little girls made at summer camp and forced on the adults they loved. Sweat had darkened the line down his spine. He smelled faintly of tire shine and bay rum.

"Evening, Miss Marcia," he said.

"Evening."

Claude's eyes moved once, quick, over the inside of the room: Tiana at the table, Kerene on the cot, Micah on the floor, Delroy half-risen from the bed. Whatever he had prepared to say changed shape in his throat.

"Delroy there?" he asked, though he had obviously already seen him.

"He there. What you need?"

Claude rubbed the bracelet again. "I need a word with him. Outside if possible."

Marcia felt Delroy behind her before she heard him. The mattress springs had their own language by now. She planted her hand flat against the doorframe, wanting one clean minute, one private breath to understand the danger before it stepped all the way in.

"Anything you have to say can say through me first," she said.

Claude gave her a look that was not disrespect and not comfort either. "I'd rather not, sister."

Behind her Delroy said, too easy, "Claude. Look at you. You find my place now? I thought you only knew job sites and domino tables."

He came up beside Marcia with his left shoulder leading, his right arm hanging careful. He had put on charm the way other men put on cologne. Marcia could smell the effort from him.

Claude did not smile back. "Brother, I've been calling you all day."

"Phone was in the room."

"You live in the room."

Micah snorted from inside. Marcia wanted to slap the sound out of the air before it spread.

"Take it round back," she said low to Delroy. "Now."

But the path round back was gravel, old buckets, a busted lawn chair, and one window that did not shut right. There was no private room left in this house. There had not been for months. Privacy had gone the same way as full paychecks and fresh fruit: a thing they still talked about as if it might walk back through the gate.

Claude lowered his voice anyway. "I need my money, Delroy."

Marcia heard the scratch of Tiana's pen stop at the table.

Delroy laughed once, too quick. "You come to my gate for that?"

"I came to your gate because you stopped answering."

"I said next week."

"And the week before that you said Friday. Before that you said after the job clear. Brother, school starting Monday. I already promised my granddaughter shoes. I can't keep floating you because you don't like shame."

The word money had not hit the room yet. Shame did.

Micah pushed himself upright. "What money?"

Marcia kept her eyes on Claude. "How much?"

Delroy said, "Marcia."

"How much?" she repeated.

Claude looked almost angry at her for making him say the number at her own door, though she knew the anger was at the whole damn position, not her. "Four hundred."

The number landed flat and heavy.

It was too much for a joke, too little for a bank, the kind of number that could survive only if nobody spoke plain around it.

Tiana set her pen down. Kerene's fishing line flashed between her fingers and went slack.

"Four hundred what?" Micah said.

"Micah," Marcia said.

"No, because everybody's acting like we got private offices in here. Four hundred what?"

Delroy's voice hardened. "Watch your tone."

"Then answer the question."

Claude exhaled through his nose and looked toward the alley as if maybe another life was available over there. "Cash advance. Before the demolition."

Marcia turned to Delroy slowly. The towel in her hand was still wet from Micah's shirt. Bleach water touched the inside of her wrist and ran cold to the elbow.

"You borrowed from him," she said.

"I handled it."

"No. You hid it. That's different."

Claude said, "Miss Marcia, I didn't come to pull your house apart. If he'd just call me back, I wouldn't be here."

Marcia believed him enough to hate the whole thing more. There was no real enemy standing on her step, no clean villain she could send away. Just another man short on breath and money, too proud to beg and too squeezed not to collect.

Delroy took a step onto the concrete. "Claude, come on, man. You know how this look."

"I know how it looks. That's why I gave you all day first."

"And I said I would deal with it."

"With what? Story?" Claude's voice cracked sharper than he meant it to. He checked himself, glanced at the room, lowered it again. "Brother, I watched you last Friday at the money counter on Twenty-Seventh filling out a slip for Devon. Don't stand here like cash hasn't passed through your hand."

The whole apartment changed shape.

Marcia did not move. Behind her, something small and metal clicked against the table leg. Tiana's binder clip, maybe. Kerene made a soft sound through her teeth that could have been pain or prayer.

"For Devon?" Marcia said.

Claude shut his eyes for one beat. "That part wasn't for the step."

Delroy swung toward him. "Mind your mouth."

"Then mind your debt."

Marcia heard Micah say, from somewhere behind her left shoulder, "Daddy been sending money to Jamaica?"

Nobody answered him.

Claude dragged his hand over his face. The purple bracelet flashed again, bright and childish against the dark hair of his wrist. "Look. Delroy. Call me by morning. That's all I'm asking now. Don't make me keep coming back here."

He put his cap on, then took it off again immediately, as if even that small piece of shade felt disrespectful in the middle of what he had done. His eyes met Marcia's for a second.

"I really didn't come for this," he said.

She believed that too.

"Morning," she said.

Claude nodded once to the room he had already cut open, turned, and walked back toward the gate. Outside, a car rolled by slow with windows down and dancehall leaking from it in a hiss of bass. Claude got into a faded silver truck with a pink child's umbrella hooked behind the passenger seat and sat there a second before starting it, both hands locked at ten and two like he was steadying something bigger than the steering wheel. Then he went.

Marcia shut the door.

No one spoke while the truck noise thinned away.

The onions on the stove had gone too far. She could smell the bitter edge.

She crossed the room, turned off the burner, and came back. Delroy had not moved from the middle of the floor. His shoulder pulled one side of him lower than the other when he got tired. Tonight the sag looked less like injury than confession.

"Tiana," Marcia said without taking her eyes off her husband, "take your brother outside."

Micah said, "I'm not five."

"Then act older than five and move."

But Tiana did not stand. Her hands stayed flat on the table, one over a sheaf of photocopies, one over the corner of the folder. Her face had closed into the careful blank she used in offices when she did not intend to give anyone the satisfaction of seeing confusion.

"No," she said quietly. "If you're doing this with the whole door open, don't start pretending we're children now."

Marcia felt the sentence strike, not because it was disrespectful but because it was true.

Kerene eased her sandal onto the cot and rose with both palms out, salon-soft, trying to charm the room smaller. "Micah, come help me shake the bath mat, nuh. I wash it and the thing still smelling rank."

"I'm good."

"You don't want fresh air?"

"I'm good."

Kerene looked at Marcia, asking whether to push. Marcia gave the tiniest shake of her head. There were moments when forcing children out only taught them to listen harder.

So they stayed. Micah worried the lace tip to white fuzz. Sweat crawled down Marcia's ribs beneath her bra.

"How long?" Marcia asked.

Delroy's mouth flattened. "Marcia, lower your voice."

She laughed then, a short ugly sound she did not recognize as hers until it was gone. "You want softness now?"

"I want us not to do this in front of everybody."

"That ship sail when Claude call out four hundred dollar on my step." She pointed toward the door. "How long?"

He looked at the floor. At the stove. At Micah. At Tiana's folder. Everywhere but her.

"It wasn't every week," he said.

"That wasn't the question."

"I sent a little when I could."

"How long."

"Since June."

June had been before the school office, before Tiana's aid file stalled, before the partner hand broke, before Micah came home with his face opened into somebody else's story. It had been the month when there was still a version of the summer Marcia let herself touch in secret: deposit paid, partner payout in hand, maybe another rental if they moved fast and begged right. Back then she had been walking other people's polished floors and telling herself endurance was an investment.

She crossed to the sink shelf first and lifted the little brass trunk key off the bent nail beside the dish towel.

Then she dropped to one knee and reached under the bed.

Delroy straightened. "What you doing?"

She dragged the cedar trunk out by its handle. The metal feet scraped across the concrete with a noise like a blade being sharpened.

Tiana inhaled, and the sound carried recognition more than surprise. Marcia had no room in her just then to sort that out.

"If you want numbers," she said, "we can count."

The trunk sat squat and dark in the middle of the floor, its brass corners dulled by years of hands and damp. It held birth papers, church programs, old school photographs, envelopes with dead return addresses, all the evidence a family kept because losing paper in America was like losing blood. Marcia shoved the key into the stubborn lock, turned it until the mechanism gave, then flipped the latches and lifted the lid.

Delroy said, "Leave that alone."

"Why? You left enough in here."

She went past the nursing-school letter she had not touched in years, past the old immunization card, past the passport copies, until her fingers found the thin stiff slips Tiana must have disturbed and then reburied. Yellow remittance stubs and white receipt copies, all of them crowded with names, dates, and transfer numbers. It was money made visible by carbon paper and bureaucracy, neat where the life behind it had not been.

She laid them out on the table one by one.

"Forty-five." She slapped the first down. "Sixty." Another. "Fifty." "Forty." "Seventy-five." The last one she held up between two fingers. "This one from last Friday, same Friday Claude watched you send it. Two hundred seventy dollar."

Micah whistled under his breath.

Kerene said, "Jesus," so low it barely made sound.

Tiana's eyes were on the receipts, not Marcia or Delroy, as if the numbers could still alter if she stared hard enough.

Marcia turned back to Delroy. "Two hundred seventy dollars in this house. Do you know what two hundred seventy dollars looks like in here? It looks like groceries for two weeks. It looks like the clinic and the bus and the light bill not getting cut ugly. It looks like me not stretching mackerel with onion skin and pepper water. It looks like not having a creditor at my door."

Delroy's jaw set so hard a pulse jumped near his ear, the dangerous shift where charm stepped out the back and left pride to finish the conversation. "He's my son."

"And these?" Marcia swept her hand at Tiana, Micah, then herself. "These are what? Lodgers?"

"Don't do that."

"Do what. Count?"

"Make it like I sent away rent money in sacks."

"What should I make it like?"

He came to the table, favoring the shoulder though trying not to show it. With his left hand he touched one receipt, straightened it, crooked it again, a useless gesture that gave him something to do besides defend himself with open palms.

"It wasn't all one thing," he said. "One time the boy needed help for his course fee. One time his mother roof lift and water was coming through the kitchen. One time he called because he was short, just short, and I had something in my hand. I sent it."

"The boy," Marcia said. "You mean Devon. Use the name if you're going to spend our food on it."

At that, Delroy looked up. Hurt moved across his face so fast it almost passed for anger.

"Our food?" he said. "Marcia, every dollar that came in here, I fought for too."

"And every dollar that stayed in here, I accounted for. That's the part you skip because hearing what this house costs shames you."

"No, the part you skip is blood."

"Don't use blood with me tonight."

"Why not? Because it make the room inconvenient?"

Marcia felt the bleach on her hands start to burn where the skin had split. She welcomed it. It gave her somewhere to put the heat.

"Don't play big philosophy now," she said. "Not after you hid it in papers under my bed."

"I hid it because if I told you, you'd say no."

"Yes. I would've. Because no was the right answer."

"Easy for you."

She stared at him. "Easy."

"Easy to cut off a son you don't hear on the phone."

"You think I don't know what obligation is?" Her voice rose despite herself, then sharpened finer. "I packed barrel with you when we had enough to pack barrel. I sent shoes and powdered milk and school socks. I did not object to your child existing. I object to you running a second household out of the bones of this one and leaving me to smile like a fool while you do it."

Micah muttered, "Damn."

Marcia did not look at him. "You," she said, and the boy shut his mouth.

Delroy dragged a hand over his jaw. "Second household. Listen to you."

"What should I call it? Because from where I stand, money left this room under your name for months while your daughter carry expired paper in a folder and your son cough blood into a clinic rag and I go out every day trying to make dignity out of pennies. What word fit that proper?"

Tiana flinched, not visibly if someone did not know her, but Marcia knew the little tightening at the corner of her mouth. Shame on that girl always tried to dress itself up as patience first.

Delroy heard it too. "Don't bring the children in it like I don't know what the children need."

"They're already in it. That's what you still refuse to learn."

He turned toward Tiana then, maybe for rescue, maybe for witness. "Ti. Tell your mother every man don't get to be father the same way. Some of us get left with phone calls and whatever cash make it through."

Tiana looked at him for so long Marcia thought she might say nothing.

Then she said, in that careful clean voice she used when she wanted the words to stand without her, "You don't get to use us to explain why you lied to us."

Delroy blinked.

Micah sat up straighter. The bruise on his cheek caught the yellow light and made him look punched fresh again. "Facts," he said.

Marcia heard the edge in the boy and knew it was fear wearing somebody else's jacket.

Delroy pointed at him. "Watch your little commentary."

"Why? You got whole secret payment plans and I can't talk in my own house?"

"Micah," Kerene said.

"No, because every week it's some new thing. Some old thing. Some thing we weren't supposed to know till after it already mash up."

Kerene's fingers bit deeper into Micah's shoulder. Tiana's binder clip clicked against the table leg. Marcia felt her jaw lock so hard her molars ached. It was not just this truth. It was all of them. In this family, bad news almost never arrived as news. It arrived as aftermath: a notice already posted, money already gone, a suspension already signed, bruises already blooming. Marcia had stood inside that pattern so long she sometimes mistook it for the way the house worked. Hearing Micah say it out loud made her want to sit down and hit somebody at the same time.

Delroy's voice dropped. That was how anger came in him when embarrassment had been worked hard enough. "I said watch your mouth."

"Or what?" Micah shot back. "You gone send my bus pass to Kingston?"

The silence after that was vicious.

Delroy shoved off from the table so fast the nearest chair leg screeched. He came around it with his left hand out and his busted right shoulder hitching high behind him, like he meant to snatch Micah by the shirt before the boy could spit another word.

Kerene moved faster than Marcia had ever seen her move. She caught Micah by the shoulder and yanked him half behind her. The fishing line still clung to one finger, glinting like something sharp.

"No," she snapped. The charm had dropped clean out of her voice. "No more."

Delroy stopped so short his boot skidded on the concrete. A receipt fluttered off the table edge and brushed his shin on the way down. But the whole room had already read the danger at once, and once danger showed its face you could not tell everybody they had imagined it.

Delroy looked from Kerene's body in front of Micah to Marcia's to Tiana's, and Marcia saw it hit him plain: not a single soul in the room had assumed safety first.

Shame took the color out of him.

No one said anything. They did not have to.

Outside, somebody's bass rattled a bottle in the alley. A bus sighed at the corner. From the front house came a burst of laughter from a television sitcom, canned and cruelly timed.

Delroy turned back to Marcia because she was the only fight he could still enter without losing completely.

"You hear one name and you make me monster," he said.

"No. The lie make you liar. The debt make you reckless. The rest you earning line by line."

"Everything with you have to sound like bookkeeping."

"Because somebody in this place has to keep the book."

He slapped the nearest receipt with the back of his fingers. "You want the full story? Fine. Devon calls me at six in the morning Miami time because he never keeps the difference straight. Half the time he starts with evening like the boy still thinks I live down the road. He don't ask pretty. He don't even ask smart. He circles it. Says he can manage. Says he's only checking in. Then he says the real thing at the end because pride does not disappear just because a person need. You know what it feel like hearing your own habits come back in your child's voice from another country?"

Marcia almost hated him most when he got close to the truth. He could make pain sound so human you had to work not to hand it absolution.

"Yes," she said. "I do. I hear your habits in Micah every day and it terrifies me."

Micah gave a tiny wounded laugh and then swallowed it.

Delroy went on as if he had not heard. Maybe he had not. Maybe once a man started defending himself, language narrowed to the tunnel ahead.

"The boy missed enough of me. I missed enough of him. Every time I tell him no, I hear the years I wasn't there getting bigger. So I sent what I had when I had it. Not to show off. Not to disrespect you. Because I can't be absent twice."

For one ugly second, Marcia understood him completely. She did not agree with him. She understood the guilt like a second spine. She understood how a voice on a phone from Jamaica could make a man feel like he still owed the sea itself. She understood too that understanding changed nothing in the cupboard.

She took the top receipt and pushed it at his chest. It fluttered down because his right arm did not rise fast enough to catch it.

"You were absent here while you were doing charity for guilt," she said. "You were absent when Tiana came back from that office and found out her father had used the money and lied. You were absent when Micah sat in that clinic with his face swollen. You were absent when Claude called and called and you shoved the phone under a cushion like if cloth could pay debt. Don't tell me about being absent twice. You found a way to be absent in stereo."

Kerene sucked air through her teeth. Even Micah looked at Marcia then like he had not known she kept knives that sharp.

Good, a part of her thought savagely. Let them all know.

And another part, smaller and meaner because it was frightened, knew the children were learning her too.

Delroy stared at her as if she had slapped him. Maybe she had, in the only currency left.

"So that's who I am now," he said.

"Who were you before? Tell me. Because Claude know your debts, Western Union know your business, the aid office know your negligence, and somehow I'm always the last damn person to get informed in my own life."

That was it, the line under the line, the old wound inside the new one. Marcia heard it as she said it and could not take it back. She was tired, yes. Angry, yes. But beneath both sat the humiliation she had been carrying for months like a basin on her head: men, offices, daughters, accidents, everybody delivering facts after they had already changed her choices. She was the planner only in the narrow window before reality arrived with a stamp and made planning into comedy.

Delroy understood that too. She saw it in the way his mouth opened, then shut. If he had apologized right then, clean and ordinary, maybe the night would have found a softer failure.

Instead he said, "You think you own pain because you inventory it."

Marcia laughed again, harsher this time. "And you think naming love excuses theft."

"Theft."

"Yes, theft. What else you call taking from this table without telling the people who eat off it?"

"I'm not a thief in my own house."

"Then stop moving like one."

Micah made a little startled noise, not quite a word. Tiana's face had gone pale under her brown. Kerene looked toward the door like she was measuring whether this was the moment to make everybody step outside and let the neighbors have the show.

Delroy leaned both hands on the table. The right one shook when the weight landed wrong and he pulled it back fast. Marcia watched him do it. Watched pain crack through the performance and vanish again.

"You know what," he said softly. "You want me standing here so everybody can count out my sins one by one. Fine. Claude four hundred. Devon two-seventy. Tiana's renewal money before that. Anything else? Put it on the table. Let's finish the auction."

Tiana stood so suddenly the chair legs screamed.

"Stop," she said.

Everybody turned.

She had one hand pressed to the document folder so hard the cardboard bent. Her eyes moved between them, not pleading, not even really angry anymore. The look on her face had gone past anger into an exhaustion eighteen-year-olds should not know.

"This isn't helping," she said, then corrected herself with a bitter little shake of the head. "No. That's too soft. This is making everything truer in the worst way."

Marcia lifted a hand toward her daughter, then saw Tiana tighten her grip on the bent folder and let the hand fall again. Anything she put into the air right then would have sounded fake.

Micah looked from his sister to his father. "You leaving?" he asked Delroy, too fast, as if by saying it first he could turn it into a joke before anybody else could make it a fact.

Delroy straightened.

He looked at Marcia one more time, and what she saw then was less rage than the point after shame when a person would rather step out into night with nowhere arranged than remain under the eyes that had measured him correctly.

"Yeah," he said. "Probably best."

Marcia did not tell him to stay.

That was the failure that would keep its teeth in her later.

He went to the dresser and yanked open the top drawer with his left hand. Socks, an old church tie, pain pills in an orange bottle, his charger, two work shirts. He packed badly because he was angry and because the shoulder made speed clumsy. The duffel bag slipped once when he tried to swing it up and Micah half moved, instinct more than forgiveness, then stopped himself so hard his whole body jerked backward.

Delroy saw that too.

He put the bag down, slower the second time, and zipped it.

"Where you going?" Kerene asked before she could stop herself.

He gave a laugh with no humor in it. "Miami full of places a man can sleep when he don't fit where he paying rent."

Marcia said, "You paying rent?"

It came out reflexive, crueler than she had even meant. Micah blinked. Kerene's thumb froze on the clear line threaded through the sandal strap.

Delroy nodded once like that line settled something for him. "Right. Forgot."

He bent for his boots. Wincing did not stop him. He shoved his feet in without untying them properly and nearly lost balance on the second one. Again Micah moved and again did not finish moving.

Tiana stood by the table with her folder clutched to her ribs, watching the man who had failed her in so many ordinary administrative ways become suddenly, absurdly, just a man trying not to grunt while he put on work boots with one bad shoulder. Marcia could not know all of what moved through her daughter right then, but she could see it was not simple hatred. Simplicity had gone out of the room with Claude's truck.

Delroy slung the bag across his left shoulder, picked up his phone from under the couch cushion, and looked at Marcia.

"You could've talked to me different," he said.

The sentence was so small compared to the wreck around it that Marcia almost choked.

"You could've talked to me at all," she said.

He flinched, not because the words were loud but because they landed where he had no cover.

Then he opened the door and stepped into the damp night.

No one followed him. No one called him back. They listened to his boots on the concrete path, then the gate, then nothing.

For a long while after, the apartment kept the shape of a person who had just left it. The bed looked wider and poorer. The air seemed to move wrong around the empty space in front of the dresser. On the table, the remittance receipts fluttered under the fan's weak chop beside Tiana's college forms, transfer numbers and checklist boxes sharing the same patch of fake wood as if that had always been the story.

Kerene was the first to move. She sat back down on the folding cot, picked up her sandal, and stared at the clear line threaded through the broken strap without seeming to remember what came next.

Micah said, "So that's it?"

No one answered him.

Marcia gathered the receipts into one stack, then put them back down because touching them made her feel dirtier, not cleaner. The bleach had dried on her hands. When she flexed her fingers, the skin pulled.

Tiana slowly sat again. She opened her folder, closed it, opened it once more. Her eyes were bright but dry. "I still need the originals for tomorrow," she said.

That nearly broke Marcia. Not the argument. Not Delroy walking out. That sentence did. The office would open in the morning with fluorescent lights and a clerk's blank face and no respect at all for what had just been split open in this room.

"We're not losing another day to paper," Marcia said. "Ti, give me the folder. Micah, every receipt off the floor. Kerry, the freezer bag with the passport."

They moved because command was easier than grief. Micah dropped to a crouch and started gathering the slips Delroy had knocked loose. Kerene reached under her pillowcase and came up with the crackling plastic bag. Tiana slid the folder across the table with both hands, careful, like if she pushed too hard the whole thing might scatter.

Micah's phone lit first on the milk crate with a weather alert; Kerene's answered from the cot a second later, both screens shrilling before the thunder reached the roof. Marcia shoved Delroy's charger and pain pills aside, spread the passport, the birth certificate, and every other original with a seal into the space he had left, and kept the trunk key looped around her finger while the cheap screens kept screaming.


# Chapter 15

From the patch of floor he'd slept on, Micah watched Marcia write a storm list on the back of a church envelope as if the pressure of her pen could stretch what they had. Her handwriting got tighter when she was angry; this morning it looked like she was trying to cut the paper. Cheap screens buzzed with the weather alert on the dresser, on the milk crate, beside Kerene's folding cot. On the TV balanced crooked on top of the little fridge, a woman with hurricane hair and perfect teeth stood in front of a map of South Florida and talked about watches, feeder bands, storm surge, all the ways water could stop being ordinary. The morning light had gone the color of dishwater. The air wasn't moving so much as holding itself back.

"Water, bread, batteries," she said, mostly to herself. "Candles if the price isn't stupid."

Tiana stood by the bed they called hers though it had stopped being only hers weeks ago, pulling her document folder up from the floor where she'd kept it within reach all night and stacking it on top of her jeans. She had on one of Malik's old T-shirts, hanging off her in a way that made her look younger until she lifted her face and the room remembered not to mistake her for a child.

Kerene sat on her cot in bicycle shorts and a bleach-white tank top, tying a satin scarf over her hair with the speed of somebody who had learned to make herself presentable in bus-stop glass. One hoop earring was missing, so the other one flashed by itself when she turned. "Miami people love a panic," she said. "You'll see. Same people who never buy canned food in daylight suddenly turning survivalist."

"We don't have money to critique culture," Tiana said.

"I can critique for free."

When Micah pushed himself up, the skin along his bruised cheek pulled tight. The police had left him that souvenir a week ago, and the swelling had eased to the point that everybody stopped looking at it as soon as he walked into a room, which was worse in its own way. He went to the sink and splashed water on his face. The faucet coughed warm before it turned cool.

It had been three nights since Delroy walked out. For three nights Marcia had moved around the apartment like she had tightened every screw in her own body. For three nights Tiana had talked in that trimmed-down way that meant she was spending whole paragraphs privately. Micah had expected shouting to keep echoing after the fight. Instead the place had gone meaner than shouting. It had gone careful.

Marcia counted bills onto the counter: fives flattened by old sweat, singles, a little hill of quarters from the bowl over the fridge.

"Thirty-two dollars and seventy cents," she said. "Bus fare comes out of that too, so don't let anybody sweet-talk you into nonsense."

"Who is 'you'?" Micah asked.

"You if you ask foolish questions. Tiana if she decides plain water is beneath her. Kerry if she starts shopping like a woman with options."

Kerene pressed a hand to her chest. "Me? I'm a daughter of strict economy."

"You say that in lipstick."

That got the smallest crack at the corner of Marcia's mouth. Micah saw it and felt something in him unclench before he could stop it.

Marcia looked at him. "Put on a shirt. You're coming with your sister."

"I know how to buy bread."

"And I know how fast you can turn one errand into a disappearance."

"That was one time."

"Try not to make me nostalgic for simpler failures," Tiana said.

He almost snapped back, but the TV woman was now standing beside a spinning cone in the Atlantic, and something about all that bright official color made him want to prove he could do one ordinary useful thing without being escorted like a little boy. He found a clean-enough T-shirt under the cot, pulled it on, and took the envelope from Marcia.

"What if the batteries are gone?" he asked.

"Then the batteries are gone." She handed him two canvas grocery bags. "Bring back what the money can carry. Not what your pride wants to carry."

Outside, Little River looked like it always looked before weather people on TV started sounding excited: low stucco houses sweating under the sky, chain-link fences, a rooster shouting like he had not read the forecast, bass from somebody's car trunk making the puddles in the cracked street skin over and shake. But there was a second layer to it now. Men dragged patio chairs under eaves. A woman in pink rollers hauled cases of bottled water into the back of a Lexus with dealer plates. The whole block was trying not to admit who had prepared yesterday and who was pretending today still counted as early.

Tiana walked at a pace that made conversation expensive. Micah kept pace beside her and swung the empty bags at his knee.

"You think it'll hit?" he asked.

"I think if it doesn't, people will be mad they spent money." She adjusted the strap of her purse. "If it does, they'll pretend they knew all along."

"You always talk like a guidance counselor when you're stressed."

"You always get funnier right before you say something stupid."

He grinned despite himself. That was almost normal. He'd take almost.

The Family Dollar on Twenty-Ninth had already been picked thin. Aisles looked chewed. The battery rack held nothing but odd sizes for hearing aids and one dented flashlight hanging there naked without its packaging. The canned-food shelf had gaps like missing teeth. Two old men argued in Spanish over the last case of water while a little girl sat in a shopping cart chewing on the cardboard corner of a cracker box. Somebody had broken open a pack of candles and taken half.

Micah went straight to the bread and found four loaves left, all of them the kind that flattened if you looked at them wrong.

"Get two," Tiana said.

"That leaves no money for everything else."

"There's no everything else."

She was right. He hated that she was right in the brisk, adult way she had gotten lately, like reality had decided she got to be rude first. They found one gallon of water on the bottom shelf behind a pyramid of sports drinks nobody in their apartment could justify, a jar of peanut butter, two cans of black beans, and a bag of rice so small Micah almost put it back out of insult.

At the register he kept waiting for the total to climb past what they had. Thirty-one twelve. He put the coins on the counter fast, before the cashier could look bored in a way that felt personal. Outside, Tiana stood under the narrow awning and looked at the plastic bags like they were evidence from a crime scene.

"No batteries," Micah said.

"No shit."

"No candles either."

"No, I noticed the darkness coming."

Rain hadn't started yet, but the wind had found the flags on the used-car lot and was whipping sharp snaps out of them. Micah wanted to say they should've done this yesterday, should've had a father who stayed home, should've had money for stupid things like extra food before TV women started drawing cones over the map. He said none of it. He took the heavier bag and started back.

The apartment sat behind the main house, one low converted garage with its thin little strip of yard and its concrete step darkened by old mildew. From the gate Micah saw the plywood first, two mismatched sheets leaning against the wall like oversized cards. Then he saw Delroy.

His father had on the faded Dolphins cap he wore when he wanted to look busy instead of guilty. Sweat had pasted his T-shirt to the middle of his back. The right shoulder sat a little lower than the left, as if the body had gotten tired of pretending symmetry. In one hand he held a grocery bag. In the other, a loose handful of nails. He looked up when the gate scraped.

"Took long enough," Delroy said, too light, too easy.

Micah stopped. Tiana stopped harder.

Marcia came to the doorway with a dish towel in her hand. The threshold locked up around her. The TV was still talking inside, that cheerful weather voice floating out through the screen door like it belonged to another family.

Delroy lifted the grocery bag a little. "I got batteries. D-cell. A couple candles too, though one pack bust open. A brother from the church had scrap plywood from a kitchen job. I told him I'd help him haul tomorrow if the world's still here."

Marcia's face did not change. "Tomorrow is ambitious."

"I like ambition. It gives the day shape."

Tiana laughed once through her nose, not because anything was funny. "You would show up with jokes."

He looked at her, and for a beat the charm slipped. "I showed up with this first."

Micah watched the truth of that settle heavier than any apology Delroy had left unsaid. A grocery bag with batteries in it could beat a speech every time. Marcia stepped back from the doorway.

"Bring them in before rain catches them," she said. "Lean the plywood by the wall, not on the bed."

The space she left was narrow as a blade, but it was space.

Delroy nodded once and bent for the plywood. The motion caught in his shoulder halfway down. He tried to hide it by turning it into a stretch, but Micah saw the mouth tighten. He saw Tiana see it too and look away.

Micah put down the grocery bags and grabbed the lower edge of one sheet before anybody asked him.

"Watch your hand," Delroy said automatically.

"I know where my hand is."

"Good. Keep it."

They carried the plywood inside together, awkward at first because neither of them wanted to adjust to the other's pace. The board bumped the doorframe and shaved off a little curl of white paint. The apartment seemed even smaller with Delroy back in it, as if his absence had been a space of its own and now that space had been taken back by his body, his smell of sun and cut wood and whatever borrowed couch or borrowed room he had been sleeping in.

Kerene looked into the grocery bags, then into Delroy's. "Only a storm could make all of you men bring home provisions in daylight."

"Put my name in the book of miracles," Delroy said.

"Not till I see if the batteries work."

Micah set the rice on the counter and felt the mood of the place rearranging itself around practical things. There was bread on the table, water in the sink to chill what little it could, plywood against the wall, and weather outside getting grayer by the minute. Nobody had forgiven anybody. That would've been corny and impossible. But there was work now, and work made lying harder. You either had the thing in your hand or you didn't.

He could live with that for one day.

* * *

The power went out just after three, not with drama but with a tired little blink. The fan died first. The TV lost the weather woman mid-warning and turned the room black in a way that showed how much noise the screen had been making even when nobody listened. The hush pinned everybody where they stood. In it they could hear the neighborhood react: one child cheering, somebody cursing, a car alarm doing two offended chirps and quitting.

"Well," Kerene said. "Now the fridge becomes a countdown."

Marcia opened it and stared into the weak cold. "We cook what can spoil."

"We?" Kerene moved her gently aside with her hip. "No, make way. If we're about to sweat through the apocalypse, we're not doing it on sad food."

She tied her scarf tighter and started pulling things out with brisk little sounds of disapproval. There was half a cabbage, one onion soft on one side, three eggs, a half stick of butter wrapped back in paper, a can of mackerel, leftover rice in a pot, two plantains with skins gone more black than yellow, and a scotch bonnet pepper sleeping in a margarine tub. She lined them on the counter like she was judging a competition no one else had wanted to enter.

"This is enough," she said. "It's not abundance, but it's enough."

"You say that about everything," Tiana said from the bed, where she was plugging her phone into a dead wall socket out of habit and then catching herself.

"Because everything wants to pretend it's less than it is. Hand me that knife."

Micah took the knife first. "What I get?"

"You get to cut onion and not take off your thumb. That's a serious assignment."

"Why do I always get vegetables?"

"Because if I put you near hot oil you'll flirt with the flames."

Delroy, sitting on the overturned bucket by the door and testing the batteries in the old lantern, snorted. "She's learning the boy."

"Please," Kerene said. "The boy introduced himself."

The match scratch sounded loud in the dark. Marcia lit one of the broken candles and jammed it into a chipped coffee mug. The flame did not do much for the room except prove how close everybody was standing to everybody else. Sweat gathered fast under Micah's shirt. The apartment held heat like a grudge. Outside, the wind was stronger now, carrying somebody's radio in pieces through the walls.

Kerene set the plantains to fry first. The smell of hot oil changed the whole place. Hot oil and browning plantain put a hard bright edge on the air, something alive that shoved back at the blackout. She moved around the kitchenette with quick elbows and small authority, tapping Micah's wrist with the flat of the knife when he cut the onion too big, stealing Tiana's church program to fan the burner when the flame sulked low, telling Marcia not to hover unless she intended to season something.

"Don't waste all the gas," Marcia said anyway.

"If the storm takes the line tonight, you'll regret caution more than flavor."

"That sounds like something a woman says right before she empties my cupboard."

"Your cupboard was already in mourning."

Micah laughed, then regretted it because laughter in that apartment lately felt like siding with somebody. But Marcia only shook her head and reached for the salt. The candle showed the shine on her forearm, the calluses in her palm. She looked ready to snap and too practical to bother.

Kerene dumped the rice into the pan with the mackerel and let the grains pop and hiss in the butter. Then onion, cabbage, pepper, all of it turning fragrant so fast Micah's stomach cramped at the smell. She mashed the soft side off the onion against the cutting board with her thumbnail and flicked it away.

"At the salon yesterday," she said, "this lady wanted me to do knotless braids down to her backside while she sat there watching storm coverage and drinking a frappuccino. I said, 'Miss, if a tree falls on your head, at least the braid part will stay neat.' She never laughed. Women with money hate a joke unless they bought it."

Tiana smiled despite herself. "How much was she paying?"

"She wasn't paying enough to hear my private comedy."

"You still did the braids, though," Micah said.

"Of course I did the braids. Poverty is not an artistic movement."

That one got Delroy. He laughed himself into a cough. Even Marcia let out a sound that could've become a laugh if she trusted it. The lantern flicked on in Delroy's hand then, weak but working, and he lifted it in triumph.

"Light," he announced. "Behold the modern age."

"Don't blind us with luxury," Tiana said.

He set the lantern on the table. The battery light was uglier than candlelight, more honest about faces. It showed the yellow-green fade of Micah's bruise, the tired shadows under Tiana's eyes, the new gray at Marcia's temples he was not sure had been there in June. It showed Delroy thinner than usual, chin more hollow, and that made Micah angry in a direction he couldn't name. A man could disappear for three nights and come back looking hungry for sympathy. That hardly seemed fair.

Kerene slapped his hand away from the plantains with the spoon before he touched one.

"You'll live."

"I might not."

"Then die after serving."

"Kerry, stop bullying the help," Delroy said.

"The help is stealing."

"That's a family business," Micah muttered.

The words left a mark as soon as he said them. Tiny, but there. Tiana looked down. Marcia's face closed one notch. Delroy rubbed his jaw and let the joke around his mouth sag.

Kerene moved fast, quicker than the silence. "Then the family business says plates," she announced. "Nobody is standing up in this heat to have emotional revelations. Sit."

She fed them out of the little pot like she had cooked for a full house all her life and intended to keep doing it until the house respected her for it. The rice and cabbage and salted fish stretched under butter and pepper farther than Micah would've thought possible. The plantains were fried at the edges so dark they tasted almost smoky. She gave each of them half a boiled egg, cut neat so it looked like more. Micah ate too fast and burned the roof of his mouth. He did not care. The food had weight. It made his hands stop shaking in that small embarrassed way they had been doing all afternoon.

Kerene sat down with her own plate, crossed one leg under herself, and ate with appetite instead of performance. That mattered more than Micah would've expected. She didn't play martyr. She didn't do that fake auntie thing where the woman cooking kept saying she was full already while watching everybody else clear the pan. She took the softest center piece of plantain for herself and added a sliver more pepper to her rice.

"This needs avocado," she said around a mouthful. "American people don't respect avocado. They refrigerate it till it tastes like furniture."

"Every storm chapter of your life has a menu critique?" Tiana asked.

"Obviously. Otherwise what is memory for?"

"To remember who ruined it," Marcia said.

Delroy glanced up. Micah's fork stalled halfway to his mouth, waiting for the room to split back open with grease on the plates and storm light at the window. Marcia only pointed with her fork at the cabbage.

"This is good," she told Kerene. "You overcooked the onion, but the rest is good."

"From you, that's almost a bouquet."

"Don't get greedy."

Micah watched the exchange the way he'd watched adults examine a tire before a long drive, testing it with little kicks, deciding if it would hold. The apartment still ran so hot his shirt clung to him. The power was still gone. Rain hadn't even started in earnest yet. Nothing was fixed. But everybody was chewing instead of accusing, and the noise of forks against plates, of Delroy asking for a little more pepper, of Tiana actually answering him without ice in her voice, changed the air more than the fan ever had.

When the plates were empty, Delroy got up and took them to the sink without asking if anyone wanted the gesture. Marcia did not thank him. She also did not tell him to leave them there. She passed him the dish soap by sliding it across the counter with two fingers.

Micah saw that and looked away before it could turn into anything tender. He wasn't ready for tenderness from either of them. He wanted facts. The soap bottle in Delroy's hand. The hot pan cooling on the stove. The lantern holding its weak circle over the table. Outside, the first spit of rain hit the plywood by the wall with the sound of fingers drumming for patience.

* * *

By dusk the storm had moved from television to property.

The sky pressed low over the neighborhood, green-gray and swollen, and the palms beyond the fence had turned their undersides out like frightened animals. Rain came in quick slants, then stopped, then came again harder. The main house had not opened up once all day; its shutters were already closed, and the car in the drive was gone. That felt about right.

Delroy stood the first sheet of plywood under the front window and squinted up along the frame. "Not a perfect fit," he said, "but perfect is for people with budgets."

"You say that about a lot of ugly work," Marcia said from inside.

"And still the ugly work stands."

Micah picked up the hammer before his father could ask him to. The handle was slick with old use. Delroy had brought a coffee can half full of nails, bent ones mixed in with straight. He sorted through them with his left hand because the right kept flexing open and shut at his side.

"We need a stud here and here," Delroy said, tapping the wall. "Don't waste the nails in the thin part."

"I know what a wall is."

"That's not the same as knowing how one lies."

It was the kind of line Delroy liked, halfway between instruction and a joke about life. Micah should've rolled his eyes. Instead he watched his father tap the frame again, listening for the deeper knock. Delroy had always known where things would hold. In other men that would've made him seem reliable. In Delroy it only made the failures hurt more. If you knew where to place weight and still put it wrong, what did that say?

They lifted the plywood together. Rain speckled Micah's forearms. The board was heavier than it looked, one corner swollen from having lived some former life in somebody else's kitchen. Delroy braced it with his shoulder and made a face before he could stop himself.

"Leave it," Micah said.

"Leave what?"

"The hero business. Just hold the bottom."

Delroy's face tightened with the old reflex, pride reaching for a line. Then the wind shoved a wet breath through the yard and he gave a short nod.

"Fine. Hold it level."

Micah planted his sneaker against the concrete lip and raised the hammer. The first nail bent. The second went in crooked.

"Don't beat it like it insult your mother," Delroy said. "Hit straight."

"Maybe the nail started it."

"Again."

Micah hit straighter. The board answered with a hard good sound. Delroy moved his hand an inch, pointing with two fingers instead of lifting the whole arm. He had gone quiet now, no stories, no extra charm. Micah had forgotten that version of him existed. The one who knew the work and had stopped decorating it.

From behind them Tiana held the lantern out through the doorway so they could see the nail heads. Its yellow beam shook when the wind caught her arm.

"If that board flies off," she said, "I'm blaming both of you equally."

"That sounds democratic," Delroy said.

"That sounds accurate."

Kerene appeared behind Tiana with a bath towel draped over her shoulder like a corner woman at a fight. "Don't let the rain in after all my cooking. I refuse to feed water."
