Marcia set a second mug candle on the windowsill inside. The little flame bent every time the air found a crack. "Micah, watch your thumb."

"Everybody in here obsessed with my hands."

"Because your hands still have some use left," she said.

He drove the next nail and felt the sting jump up his arm. Something about the sting helped. It kept him in his own body instead of in the bad places his mind wanted to run: the police car hood hot under his cheek, the sight of Claude in their doorway, Delroy's voice going loud and then gone. Right now there was only wet wood, hammer weight, his father's low corrections.

They did the front window, then the side one by the bed. The second sheet barely covered it. Delroy found an old shelf board behind the kitchenette and used that to patch the gap.

"That's going to hold?" Micah asked.

"Long enough if the storm has manners."

"And if it doesn't?"

Delroy looked out at the darkening yard. "Then we learn something."

That was almost a joke, but not enough. Micah slid the shelf board into place and Delroy passed him the nails. Their hands touched, brief and damp. Delroy's palm was rougher than Micah remembered, and hotter.

The bathroom window was worst. Narrow louvers, ridiculous for weather, impossible to board from the inside without climbing half into the sink. Delroy tried to reach above it, hissed under his breath when the shoulder caught, and dropped his arm.

Micah pretended to study the coffee can of nails until Delroy said, flatly, "Take it."

There was no charm in it, no performance, just that.

Micah climbed onto the toilet lid, one sneaker on porcelain, the other wedged against the tub. He held the scrap board with his left hand and hammered with his right while Delroy steadied his calf so he wouldn't slip. It should've felt childish, getting balanced by his father like that. Instead it felt exact. Necessary. The man's hand on his leg said: don't fall, not right now, not for something stupid.

"A little higher," Delroy said.

"My arm ain't telescoping."

"You talk too much for a workman."

"You hire me anyway."

Delroy's mouth twitched. "Cheap labor."

"Family business," Micah said, and this time the words landed differently.

They got the last nail in just as the rain changed from scattered to serious. It came hard, flattening the hibiscus by the fence and sending a burst of cold spray through the gap under the door. Tiana swore and yanked the lantern back. Kerene grabbed the towels. Marcia was already rolling up the mat.

Micah jumped down from the toilet and nearly slid. Delroy caught his elbow with the good hand.

"Careful."

"I said I know where I am."

"You keep proving that's negotiable."

Micah should've pulled away. Instead he let Delroy's hand stay on his arm for one extra second before stepping back. The apartment smelled of wet concrete now, and cut wood, and the hot iron tang of a storm finally making good on its threat. His father looked tired as shit. He didn't look noble. He didn't look tragic. He just looked used up in a way Micah recognized from mirrors.

Delroy flexed the bad shoulder once, testing the damage like a man counting money he already knew was short. Micah saw him do it and said nothing. The silence between them had changed shape, slack now instead of cocked, built around a task finished together.

For one night, maybe that was enough.

* * *

The real weather came after dark.

By then the apartment had been remade into a smaller version of itself. The mattress had been dragged away from the windows. Towels were stacked by the door. The lantern sat on the table and the emergency flashlight on the milk crate, with one candle in the bathroom and one on the counter. The little fridge was sealed shut to preserve its last bit of cold like a secret. Every phone was dimmed low. The boarded windows took away what little outside view they had, so the room felt less like shelter than the inside of a shut box set gently in a floodplain and forgotten.

Rain hammered the plywood in uneven bursts. The wind hit between those bursts, then piled in with the rain and bullied conversation out of the room. Micah sat on the floor with his back against the bed and one knee up, listening to the boards report on every hit. Across from him, Tiana had her folder inside two plastic shopping bags and tucked under her pillow as if paperwork could sleep safer than people. She was supposed to take the originals to the office handling her renewal in the morning, and the thought of weather knocking that appointment sideways had her jaw set hard. Kerene had changed into an oversized T-shirt with a dancehall singer's faded face on the front and rubbed coconut oil into her knees because, she said, bad weather did not excuse ash. Marcia stood over the stove without cooking anything, just needing a place to put her hands.

Delroy stayed near the door on the overturned bucket. He was in a spot where he could help if the latch failed and still pretend this was not him coming home, only a man taking cover where his family happened to live.

The first leak started over the sink. A dark bead on the ceiling, then another, then a steady tap into the dish drainer.

"Pot," Marcia said.

Delroy was already up. He moved the drainer, slid the soup pot under the drip, adjusted it half an inch so the water hit center. He did it without asking. Marcia watched his hands and not his face.

"That seam was bad from June," she said.

"I know."

"Apparently."

He took the shot and let it stay there. Outside, something metal banged loose and kept banging. Micah felt the noise in his teeth.

Kerene looked around at all of them and clapped once. "Nobody is going to sit in separate corners like murder suspects. Come closer. If this roof plans to embarrass us, it can do it to a group."

"You really think you run this house because you fried two plantains," Tiana said.

"Three. And you're all depressing."

Still, she dragged her cot nearer the table. Tiana slid down off the bed and sat cross-legged on a folded blanket. Marcia wiped her hands on her skirt and lowered herself onto the edge of the mattress with the careful exhale of somebody whose back had been working since dawn. Delroy hesitated at the bucket until Micah heard himself say, "The lantern doesn't reach over there."

It came out like logistics, but Delroy took it for what it was. He moved closer and set the bucket by the wall.

For a while they only listened. The neighborhood had gone strange under weather. There was no bass and no roosters. There was just rain, wind, and now and then the human sounds that survived both: a baby crying in the main house, somebody shouting for a dog, a burst of laughter from down the block that sounded almost insane and then, because of that, useful.

Micah picked at the label on the lantern battery pack lying on the table. The adhesive came off under his nail in damp gray curls. He wanted to say something funny. He wanted to say something that would prove he wasn't sitting there scared. But the fear had weight tonight, heavy and crowding, like an extra person in the room nobody had made space for.

Delroy cleared his throat. "When Gilbert come through Clarendon," he said, "my mother make us all sit under the table because she swore the table was the strongest thing in the house."

"Was it?" Kerene asked.

"No. The table broke one leg before midnight. We spent six hours holding up righteousness with one hand."

Micah laughed before he could help it. "That sounds like your side."

"It absolutely is," Marcia said.

Delroy pointed at her with one finger. "Yet you married into it."

"Everybody makes one bad investment."

The line could've cut. Instead it sat there with a little heat and then cooled, because the rain thudded harder just then, because the soup pot rang sharp with each new drop from the ceiling, because nobody in that room had the energy left to turn every truth into a weapon.

Kerene leaned back on her palms. "In my grandmother's yard, when a storm was coming, she used to kill the fattest rooster first. Not for safety. For soup. She said if the roof went, at least people should go with a proper meal in them."

"That's practical theology," Delroy said.

"That's island logic," Kerene corrected. "Very different."

"You say that like Miami doesn't have its own foolishness," Tiana said.

"Miami has no storm culture. Miami has retail."

"That's true," Marcia said. "I saw one woman buying twelve loaves of raisin bread like cinnamon was structural support."

Even Tiana laughed at that, folding forward with it. Micah stared at her. It had been days since he'd heard that sound from her without an edge hiding inside it. The lantern put yellow half-moons in her eyes. In that light she looked like what she was: eighteen, tired, funny, still so young that laughter could take years off her in one hit.

"What'd you buy?" Micah asked her. "Before we met up. You went out yesterday."

"Shampoo."

"For a storm."

"My hair still exists in weather, Micah."

"You would've saved us if the roof flew off looking moisturized."

"Somebody in this family should have standards."

Kerene pointed at her. "See? Survival."

The wind hit the plywood broadside then, a long shove that made the whole wall complain. The candle flame went flat. Marcia's hand found the mattress beside her, fingers spread, not dramatic, just bracing. Delroy looked toward the window automatically, already calculating whether the nails would hold, whether the frame would go first, whether a man with one bad shoulder could do anything about it if it did.

Micah felt the same calculation move through him, faster and dumber. Could he get the trunk high if water came? Could he hold the door? Could he carry anything useful if Marcia started naming what mattered? He hated that fear came dressed like homework.

Kerene saw his face. "Don't make that expression," she said.

"What expression?"

"The one that says you're auditioning for man of the house. The house doesn't need audition tonight. It needs bodies with sense."

"She's right," Marcia said, before Micah could get defensive. "Nobody in here is proving anything to weather."

Delroy gave a small sound that might've been agreement or might've been the start of apology. It died before becoming either.

Rainwater had started running under the back threshold in a line thin as string. Tiana was first to spot it.

"Micah."

He got up with the towel before she finished the word and pressed it to the gap. The concrete under his bare feet felt slick and cooler than the room. Delroy joined him, kneeling badly, and together they shoved the towel down against the crack with a yardstick from the corner.

"We need another one," Delroy said.

Micah held the first towel in place with the stick while Kerene tossed a second from across the room, accurate as a pitcher. Delroy caught it left-handed.

"Good arm," he said.

"That arm pays rent in tips," she said.

They packed the second towel against the door. The water slowed, though it didn't stop. That felt like half of life lately: it wasn't fixed, only slowed.

When Micah sat again, Delroy stayed on the floor this time, back to the wall, knees up. Close enough that Micah could smell the rain in his clothes. Closer than they had been in weeks without somebody yelling. Delroy rubbed his shoulder once and looked over.

"You swung that hammer decent," he said.

Micah shrugged. "It's a nail."

"No. It's a wall. Different thing."

That was the kind of sentence Delroy could ruin by talking past it. Tonight he let it stand there by itself. Micah looked down at his own hands. There was a tiny crescent of dirt under the thumbnail, a splinter in the side of his index finger. It was proof.

Tiana leaned her head back against the mattress. "If they close that office tomorrow and the power's still out, I'm going to lose my mind."

"You can lose it after breakfast," Marcia said.

"Very caring."

"I'm full of care. That's why I schedule your breakdown."

Micah barked a laugh. Delroy did too, surprised into it. Marcia rolled her eyes as if the line had slipped out by mistake and she refused responsibility for any pleasure it caused.

Kerene stretched her legs and studied the leaking ceiling. "First thing I'm doing when I get a room with real money is buying a lamp for every corner."

"A lamp?" Tiana said.

"Many lamps. Wasteful lamps. Lamps that don't match. I want to come home and turn on light just because I can. Not because I've got to choose which part of my life deserves electricity."

Nobody mocked that. Maybe because the storm had stripped the sentence of any chance to sound silly. Maybe because everybody in the room understood wanting something small and excessive so badly it became almost holy.

Micah let himself picture it, not the lamp but the room it belonged in. There would be room to turn around without touching somebody else's grief, outlets you could waste one. He did not let the imagining get too big. Big was dangerous.

The rain shifted again, harder now, and the yard drain outside made a choking, gulping sound. Micah had heard that drain all summer and never listened. Tonight it sounded like a throat trying to swallow too much.

Marcia stood and checked the window boards with her palm, then the plastic bags around Tiana's folder, then the candle stubs, making one last inventory of a life too small for inventory. Delroy watched her do it. He did not offer instruction. He only reached for the soup pot when it got near full and carried it to the sink.

When he set the pot back down, Marcia shoved the straight-backed chair toward the door with her foot. "Sit there," she said. "If the towel slips or the water starts coming in harder, you wake everybody first. You don't open that door. You don't go outside trying to be a hero."

Delroy looked at the chair, then at her. Rain thudded on the plywood, rattling the mug candle. The soup pot kept ticking under the leak, and Marcia stood with one hand braced on her hip, listening toward the door as if she could hear the towels failing before anybody saw them.

"All right," he said.

He dragged the chair into place without another joke and sat facing the threshold. The scrape of its legs across the concrete ran through the room like a line being drawn. Micah followed the lantern light across all their faces as it moved: Kerene bright-eyed and shiny with heat, still somehow looking game for tomorrow if tomorrow came ugly; Tiana tired and sharp and young in the same breath; Marcia compact and unsurrendered; Delroy damp at the temples, quieter than Micah had ever known how to ask him to be. The storm kept thumping at the plywood, testing every cheap fix in the place. Still nobody drifted off alone. Nobody picked back up the fight and fed it.

When the next gust hit, they all looked up together. Delroy stayed in the chair by the door, one hand resting on the yardstick pinning down the towel, exactly where Marcia had put him.

Later, when the candle by the sink had gone to a blue nub and the lantern had been traded for the emergency flashlight on the milk crate, its beam thinned to the color of weak tea, Micah stayed awake on his blanket near the boarded window listening to rain on wood, water in the pot, Marcia's breathing from the mattress edge, Tiana lying still beside her with one arm over her face, Kerene turning once on the cot in the satin bonnet she'd tied on before sleep and muttering at somebody in a dream, Delroy shifting carefully in the chair by the door so the bad shoulder would not wake him all the way, and Tiana's phone buzzing once under the pillow with some useless alert nobody bothered to read.

Outside, the drain kept trying to swallow the yard and failing. For now the boards held, the towels held, the roof mostly held. Micah lay there with the wet towel smell in his nose and the hammer sting still faint in his palm, listening to Delroy breathe from the chair by the threshold and waiting for him to be the one who'd wake them if the water won.


# Chapter 16

The water won before dawn. What woke Marcia was not thunder. Thunder had been arguing with the roof since midnight. What woke her was the toilet talking back.

It gave one deep animal gulp in the bathroom and then another, a wet choke from the pipes. She sat up before she was fully awake. The emergency flashlight on the milk crate had gone dim to a coin-sized halo, enough to show the room in pieces: Tiana on the mattress with one arm over her face, Micah sprawled on a blanket near the boarded window, Kerene folded on the cot in a satin bonnet the color of mango skin, Delroy still in the chair by the door with his neck bent wrong and his bad shoulder hugged up high even in sleep. Rain slapped the plywood outside. The room smelled of damp socks, cold frying oil, and the sweet-rotten mildew the garage always breathed when weather sat too long on it.

Marcia swung her feet down and stepped into water.

It was already over her ankles, warm at first touch and then colder where the current moved around her shins. She sucked air through her teeth. Not rainwater alone. There was drain in it, dirt in it, the faint bleach sting from yesterday's mop bucket gone dirty in the night.

"Up," she said, too low for the size of the problem. Then louder: "Everybody up. Now. Water's in."

Micah jerked like he had been caught in class. Tiana came awake angry, already frowning. Kerene pushed herself upright and grabbed for the cot frame as it slid a little on the wet floor. Delroy was on his feet last because his shoulder slowed him half a second, and half a second suddenly mattered.

"From where?" Tiana asked.

"From every damn place." Marcia was already moving.

The back door rattled under the rain. Water was pushing under it in a steady flat sheet, not dramatic, not one big wave, just relentless and sure of itself. The bathroom drain had begun to burp brown around the edges. The boarded window by Micah's blanket dripped from the sill in a hard little stream. Overhead the ceiling above the kitchenette had turned the color of old bruises.

Marcia did not look at the mattress first, or the little television on the crate, or the fan that had died with the power hours ago. She went to the top of the fridge and snatched down Tiana's blue document folder. From the cabinet above the sink she took the mug full of bottles and blister packs: Delroy's shoulder tablets, ibuprofen, Micah's antibiotic cream from the clinic after the police split his cheek, the cheap allergy pills that kept Kerene from rubbing her eyes raw. From the flour tin under the counter she pulled the partner notebook and the envelopes tied with a rubber band that had already gone gummy in the humidity. Only after those did she reach for clothes, one armful from the clean pile she had not yet gotten to fold, because clean or not, they were dry for now.

"Garbage bags," she said. "Not the thin white ones. The black."

"We used most of them," Tiana said, already knee-deep in the cabinet under the sink.

"Then use what's left."

Micah had his speaker in his hands.

Marcia cut him a look so sharp he put it down without argument, though his mouth twisted around one anyway. "Get your school bag. Get the clippers too if you can find them. Not that speaker."

"The speaker cost more than those clippers."

"And the clippers can earn. Move."

He splashed off, muttering, "Man, this is some bullshit," but he moved. That was enough.

Kerene, still half asleep and all bone under an oversized T-shirt, had one hand on the cot and one on the little zip case where she kept tweezers, lashes, edge control, and the tiny sharp scissors she wrapped each night in a washcloth so they would not poke through her bag. "Tell me what to lift," she said. "Don't just cuss me out with your eyes."

"Your passport first," Marcia said. "Then your work things. Not the cot."

"America's trying to wash me right back before I even get steady," Kerene said, frightened enough to sound bright.

"Don't turn it into a joke. Lift."

Delroy had gone straight to the cedar trunk. Even before he bent, Marcia saw the stupidness of it. The trunk was heavy dry. Wet, with the bottom already wicking up water, it might as well have been a piano. But he put both hands on the brass handles and tried anyway, jaw tightening, shoulder jumping under his shirt.

"Leave it," Marcia said.

"I'm not leaving this."

"You can't lift it."

"Then help me."

His voice still had the old public warmth in it, as if persuasion might make flesh stronger. It almost angered her more than panic would have. She shoved the clothes bundle into Tiana's arms and went to the trunk.

The water swirled darker around its feet. When she knelt, her knees disappeared. The metal latch was slippery under her thumb. Inside, paper smell rose up at once, the dry dusty smell of saved years turned nervous by damp. She did not have time to honor any of it. She took what she knew by feel. The clear sleeve with birth certificates and old immunization cards. The brown envelope of remittance receipts she had once wanted to throw in Delroy's face and then had not. The nursing-school letter with the fold gone white at the center from being opened and put away and opened again over too many years. A packet of church programs with funeral dates written on the back in her own hand. She shoved them all into a pillowcase.

"What about the rest?" Tiana said from above her, voice already thinning.

"If the rest goes, the rest goes. Hold this open."

Tiana held the black bag wide. Her hair scarf had slipped and one side of her face was wet, whether from rain or sweat Marcia could not tell. "My books are under the desk."

"Take the folder first."

"I've got the folder."

"Then take the books you need, not all the books in the world."

That stung Tiana enough to shut her mouth. Good. There was no room in the storm for wounded feelings unless they could float.

Over by the window, Micah found his backpack and his clippers and then, because he was still fifteen and still himself, tried to hook the speaker strap over his shoulder too. Delroy saw it and barked, "Boy. Leave that foolishness."

"Everybody else get to save their life dream."

"Your life dream better fit in one bag then."

It was the kind of line Delroy would once have landed with a grin, turning command into banter so no one had to call it obedience. This time there was no grin under it. Micah stared at him one second too long and then dropped the speaker on the table hard enough to make the flashlight jump.

Rain struck the roof in a sudden new way, heavy and close. Marcia looked up. A dark sag had formed in the ceiling over the kitchenette, heavy with collected water. She opened her mouth to warn them, but the ceiling moved first. A square of plaster gave way with a crack like a plate snapped in two. Then everything above it came down at once: gray mush, dirty rain, pink insulation, one strip of wire swinging loose and dripping black water. The collapse hit the counter, bounced off the hot plate, and exploded across the floor.

Kerene screamed. Micah swore. Delroy lunged without thinking and caught Micah's shoulder with his good arm just as the boy started toward the noise. The movement yanked something ugly through Delroy's bad shoulder. Marcia heard the sound he made before he swallowed it.

Now the room had no dry side. Water pushed under the door and from the broken ceiling in two different temperatures, two different colors. The floor rug lifted and folded against her calf like something alive. Rice from the split bag under the counter floated out in pale soft worms. A spoon rack clattered loose and vanished under the bed.

"Outside," Marcia said. "We're not fighting this in here."

"The trunk," Delroy said again, stupid with pain and stubbornness.

"The papers out, not the trunk. You hear me? Not the trunk."

This time he heard the tone under the words. He nodded once, breathing through his mouth.

They worked ugly and fast. Tiana knelt at the desk and dragged out three books swollen already at the corners: a composition notebook, the binder where she'd kept scholarship printouts and bus schedules, and the paperback she'd been rereading so often the spine had become a white seam. She left the rest when water licked over the bottoms and blurred the ink on a page before her eyes. Micah dug under the mattress for his sneakers, cursed when one came up trailing black water, then stuffed the clippers, a phone charger, and two shirts into his bag with the quick angry efficiency of a boy who did not want anybody seeing what mattered to him. Kerene took her passport from the plastic sleeve inside her tote, then doubled back for the denim jacket she wore to salon calls because it made her feel dressed even over cheap clothes. Delroy grabbed the toolbox and then dropped it for the medicine bag when Marcia glared at him. That, more than anything, told her how bad the room had become. Delroy believed in tools the way other men believed in prayer.

Marcia stood in the center long enough to lose more than she could count. The little stack of plates from Woolworth's her mother had sent with her when she first rented in Miami. The fan. The folded towels. The church shoes in the clear bin under the cot. The clean sheets she had changed just yesterday because she wanted one decent thing while the storm passed. She watched the hem of one sheet drink brown and knew, with the flat accuracy pressure gave her, that laundry soap cost less than a mind full of grief. If they got the bodies, the papers, the medicine, and enough clothes to look like people in public, the rest could drown.

She hated how quickly she learned that.

"Micah, take the flashlight."

"It barely works."

"Then hold it close."

"Tiana, zip that bag. Zip it all the way. If water gets in after all this I'll box you myself."

"I'm doing it."

"Kerene, where's your shoes?"

"On my feet."

"Good."

The door stuck from the swell of water in the frame. Delroy set his shoulder against it out of habit and nearly went down. Micah, seeing it at the same moment Marcia did, wedged in without a word. Together they forced it open. Rain hit them sideways, cold enough to reset the skin. The narrow strip of yard between the garage and the house had become a shallow brown runnel carrying leaves, cigarette butts, and somebody's escaped flip-flop toward the alley. Plywood scraps from last night's boarding leaned drunkenly against the chain-link fence. The air smelled of wet dirt ripped open.

They passed the bags out one at a time. Marcia went last because she always went last. She took one final look inside and wished she had not. The mattress had gone dark and lumpy. The cot had shifted crooked. Water from the broken ceiling hammered the counter where she'd stood rolling dough a week ago as if the room had been reduced to that one correction. On the doorjamb, the red code notice had gone soft at the edges, the county seal washing into a pink smear.

Outside, they crowded under the mean shelter of the house eave, six inches of dry at best. Tiana clutched the document folder under her shirt. Micah held the flashlight in his mouth while tying his shoes with wet fingers, then gave up and shoved his feet into them loose. Kerene's bonnet was collapsing on one side. She ripped it off, wrung it once, and tucked it into her bag with a face so disgusted Marcia would have laughed another day. Delroy stood in the rain rather than the shelter, as if being wet by choice might count as usefulness.

"Phone the landlord," Tiana said.

"Already trying."

Marcia had left three messages during the night when the leaks first got bad. No answer. She called again now and got the same calm machine voice asking her to leave details. Details. As if he needed narration. As if the water would wait while she arranged it into language.

She tried church next because there were only so many numbers a poor woman got to have for trouble. Sister Paulette answered on the fourth ring sounding wide awake, because women like that slept with one ear still in the world.

"Marcia?"

"We're taking water."

Past tense would have been a lie. The water was still at work, still taking the room from them as she stood there.

"How bad?"

Marcia looked back through the open door at the trunk filling from the bottom up. "Bad enough."

Paulette did not say pray first. She said, "Stay where you can be seen. I'll call around."

"Don't call around too wide."

Paulette paused just long enough for the truth to sting. "I hear you. Let me do what I can."

Marcia thanked her because she had been raised right. Then she put the phone down and watched the rain walk in and out of the room that had been theirs.

By dawn the storm had not ended so much as lost its temper. Rain still fell, but in strips instead of sheets. The worst wind had moved on. What it left behind was uglier because it could be counted. There was mud rucked up along the threshold. There were wet clothes tied in garbage bags like bodies waiting their turn. There was Delroy shivering in a shirt he had insisted on keeping for work because the better ones had been nearer the broken ceiling. There was Tiana trying to smooth the edge of her folder and failing because paper, once buckled, told the truth forever. There was Micah staring at the open doorway with a look Marcia knew from his baby years, when fever had taken away all his jokes and left only insult. There was Kerene redoing her hair under the eave with two fingers and a broken compact mirror because she could not bear to meet calamity looking entirely defeated.

Marcia could count all of that. Counting made a thing real. Counting also kept her from crying until it would do nobody any good.

***

When the landlord finally came, he arrived dry from the knees up.

He wore grocery bags tied over his loafers and carried a narrow umbrella that covered his head and almost nothing else. His guayabera was clean enough to resent. He was a small man with a careful mustache and the habit of pinching his lower lip before saying no, as if refusal required concentration. Behind him came a younger man in a city poncho with a clipboard held inside his shirt.

Marcia stepped out from the eave before Delroy could.

"Morning," the landlord said, and let the word do the dirty work of pretending the day had begun normally.

"You got my messages."

"Service was in and out."

"You got them now."

He looked past her to the garage, to the broken ceiling, to the red notice softening on the frame. His eyes did not stay on the family long. Men like him liked damage better when it could be called structure.

"The unit is not habitable," he said. "Given the storm intrusion and the existing county issue, nobody can reoccupy this space at this time."

"At this time?" Delroy said. "So when the rain lets up, we go back in, clean it, and keep moving."

The landlord pinched his lip. "No, sir. Not until I have an inspection and know what liability I'm dealing with."

"Liability," Micah said, not quietly.

"Micah," Marcia said.

The younger man with the clipboard had a Dolphins tattoo washing blue at the edges on his forearm. He kept glancing at the collapsed section of roof with the alert boredom of somebody who had already seen a hundred poor people's emergencies and filed them all under inevitable.

Marcia kept her voice level because once she lost it, she would lose other things too. "We need to get the rest of our things out before they mildew through. The trunk. The bedding. The books under the desk."

"I can't authorize entry until I know the electrical situation."

"The power's been out since last night."

"That doesn't make it safe."

"Safe?" Delroy laughed once, raw. "Safe would've been fixing the leak when she called you the first time."

The landlord turned to him with that small, pained patience landlords wore when they wanted to sound civilized while stepping on your throat. "Sir, we had already discussed that the accessory structure had compliance issues."

"Compliance issues," Tiana repeated. She was hugging the folder so tightly the cardboard bowed. "You mean the code notice you used to keep taking rent anyway."

That got the younger man to look at her properly, with interest instead of pity. Young Black girl, articulate, soaked, furious. A whole category of trouble in one glance.

"Miss," the landlord said, "nobody is trying to make this harder than it is."

"It looks like exactly that," Tiana said.

Marcia felt the answer rise in herself too hot and too satisfying. She killed it because they still needed something from him, even if it was only the shape of the no. "What are you offering us?" she asked. "I can see what you're withholding. Tell me what you're actually offering."

The question made him blink. Good. Let him work for his phrasing.

"I can let you know when the county or insurance says the structure can be entered for salvage. If there is any prorated balance after damages, we'll have accounting review it."

"Accounting," Micah said. "Man, shut the-"

"Enough," Marcia snapped, and this time he stopped.

Delroy stepped forward into the rain. "You can come watch me pull out a few bags now. I don't need your money. I need ten minutes."

The landlord's eyes flicked to Delroy's shoulder, the wet shirt, the desperation he was trying to stand over. "I cannot let you in there and assume that exposure."

There it was. The roof was only the excuse. The real barricade was paperwork, liability, the point at which poor people's bodies started mattering as evidence.

Kerene, who had stayed quiet too long to be calm, said in a bright voice that had a blade under it, "So what you're saying is, our passports and drawers can mold up in there, long as the paperwork on your desk stays dry."

For the first time the landlord looked embarrassed, and the feeling skimmed off him almost at once. He adjusted the umbrella and said, "There's space at the New Testament Baptist fellowship hall. A lot of displaced residents are being routed there."

"Routed," Micah said.

"Church people are already assisting," the younger man offered, as if that sweetened anything.

Residents. The word turned them from family into a problem somebody could sort by cot and clipboard. Marcia let it stand because county mouths fed categories before they fed people, and right now a category might be the only thing between them and another day in the rain.

"When can I know about the rest of our things?" she asked.

"I'll contact you."

"Today."

He pinched his lip again. "As soon as possible."

She had heard enough deadlines in that tone to know a disappearing one when she met it.

The church van came before the landlord left, which made the whole thing feel staged by God and bureaucracy together. It was an old white passenger van with NEW TESTAMENT BAPTIST peeled in blue vinyl across the side. Sister Eudora climbed down first. She wore white church sneakers under a navy skirt because her bunions had long ago overruled elegance, and she carried a plastic grocery sack full of hotel soaps that she gave away like communion. Behind her came Deacon Lewis, a broad man with rain beading on his eyelashes and a cinnamon toothpick tucked in one cheek even this early, because he had been trying to quit smoking since before Marcia met him and had never chosen one substitute when seven would do.

"Lord, Marcia," Eudora said, taking in the bags, the children, the doorway, the whole public wreck of it. Her hand went straight to Marcia's forearm, dry and warm, steady rather than pitying. "Come. We have space for now."

For now. Honest woman.

"We're not the only ones?" Marcia asked.

Eudora gave a small snort. "Baby, after last night? Do you think Miami only flooded your address?"

Generous and exposing in one sentence. That was church too.

Deacon Lewis had already taken the heaviest bag from Tiana and was arguing with Delroy over the cedar trunk.

"Leave it till they clear entry," the deacon said.

"That trunk has our papers."

"The papers you could reach are here. The trunk itself is not going to grow legs and walk to Hialeah."

"You don't know what's in there."

"I know your shoulder is nonsense. Give me the toolbox instead."

Delroy almost refused on instinct. Then he handed over the toolbox. Marcia saw the humiliation land and stay there. No joke came after it.

As they loaded into the van, the neighborhood collected itself enough to watch. A woman from two blocks over stood under her patio awning in a shower cap and called, "Y'all all right?" in the tone people used when they already knew the answer and wanted the right to repeat it later. Two boys on bikes slowed just to see who had been carried out. Micah ducked his head and climbed in so fast he smacked his knee on the van seat. Tiana got in upright, like a girl boarding a campus shuttle and not a shelter van, and the effort of the performance made Marcia love her hard enough to hurt.

The New Testament Baptist fellowship hall had the smell of lemon cleaner spread over wet fabric and too many bodies. Fluorescent lights flattened everybody the same. Cots ran in rows between the stackable banquet chairs and the rolled-up choir risers. At the far wall, women from the kitchen ministry were lining up foil pans of stew and rice under handwritten signs that said TAKE ONE and PLEASE SHARE. Somebody had already claimed an outlet strip near the stage and turned it into a little power economy of phones, chargers, and bargaining. Children ran until they were hissed at. A baby cried with the offended stamina of a baby who did not understand why sleep had been rearranged around adults' failures.

Marcia stopped just inside and felt the room read them.

The room did not read them cruelly. Cruelty would have been easier. It read them with recognition, which was worse. Wet family. Little River people. The garage apartment behind the cracked stucco house. By evening there would be three versions of how bad it was and two versions of whether Delroy had come back before or after the storm. By tomorrow someone would remember seeing Tiana at awards night and make the comparison with a tenderness that humiliated all by itself.

At a folding table near the entrance sat a volunteer Marcia did not know, a thin woman in a church polo with red cat-eye glasses and a handwriting grip so tight it whitened her knuckles. A little silver thimble hung on a chain at her neck like jewelry. She asked names, ages, and any medical needs in the same brisk voice she might have used for a bake sale sign-up.

"Head of household?"

The volunteer did not lower her voice. Under the fluorescent hum, with the line of wet families stalled behind them and the intake sheet sticking to Marcia's wrist, the title made the whole room gather itself behind her name. Marcia flattened the damp edge of the form with her palm and said, "Marcia Campbell."

"Adults?"

Marcia listed them. When she got to Tiana, the woman looked up. "Eighteen?"

"Yes."

"Adult then."

Tiana bent the softened folder edge deeper under her thumb. Adult then. The form had no box for daughter on her way to college, no line for a girl almost out the door. In here she was just another adult on a cot under fluorescent lights because deadlines and weather and men and money had all found the same weak seam.

They were assigned four cots and one folding church mattress in a corner near the choir robes closet, close enough to the bathrooms to hear every flush and every child dragged there half asleep. Privacy had not followed them. It had only changed uniforms.

Sister Eudora brought towels still warm from a dryer somewhere in the building and distributed them like verdicts. "You dry your head first," she told Kerene. "Your hair catches cold before your feet do." Then, lower so the men could half hear and half pretend not to, she pressed a bundle wrapped in a grocery bag into Marcia's hand. Sanitary pads, two clean pairs of underwear from donations, a travel deodorant, one soft bra in a size that might or might not be right. Careful mercy. Mortifying mercy.

"Thank you," Marcia said, because gratitude cost less than refusing.

Kerene took her towel and, for one unguarded second, pressed it to her face instead of her hair. When she lowered it, she was smiling again. "If anybody has a decent edge brush to donate, send them to cot four," she said.

Eudora barked a laugh. "Girl, do you think catastrophe means we stop being vain?"

"If it does, I don't want that salvation."

Even Marcia smiled at that, a brief easing that kept the room from turning entirely against them.

Delroy sat on the cot nearest the wall and leaned forward with his forearms on his thighs, shoulder hanging. He had gone very quiet, which on him was louder than most men shouting. Deacon Lewis brought him a Styrofoam cup of sweet coffee without comment and stood there long enough to make taking it seem ordinary. A kindness done right left no debt you had to perform in public.

Micah refused food at first. Then he saw the size of everybody else's spoonfuls and went back with his plate. "I'm just saying," he muttered to Tiana as they came from the line, "if one little church lady posts me in these donation shorts, I'm deleting my whole face."

"Then don't act like an idiot in donation shorts," Tiana said.

"That's hate."

"It's strategy."

The fact that she answered at all meant she was not gone under. Marcia filed that away with the other survivals.

There was nowhere to put the wet papers except out where everybody could see them, so Marcia claimed the end of a folding table under a standing fan. She spread the contents of the pillowcase and the bags across it one by one, easing apart certificates, receipts, and damp church programs before they glued themselves together. Tiana brought her folder and opened it. The outer cardboard had gone soft at the corners. Inside, the forms were still readable, but the ink on one photocopied page had feathered, and the plastic sleeves had trapped beads of dirty water against the names and dates. She slid the UF housing printout under a borrowed Bible at the far end of the table and kept glancing at the strip of paper left showing. Micah emptied his backpack and discovered one math notebook pasted shut from the bottom six pages up. He swore once, under his breath this time, and set his clippers on the dry end of the table like the only thing in the bag still fit for work.

The partner notebook was worst.

The cover had held long enough to betray them. Inside, several pages had taken the water straight through the spine. Amounts Marcia had written in her careful hand were now blue ghosts swimming into one another. Dates she had kept exact as rent had feathered at the edges. Yvette's phone number had run down the margin in blue drips. The envelopes were soft as bread.

Marcia touched one page and felt it try to come apart under her fingertip.

The page she had labeled AUGUST had gone first. She had written the steps down plain enough to survive panic: first month, last month, light deposit, truck, Tiana's dorm balance squeezed into the margin. Now the ink had blown outward into blue halos, the zeros bigger than the sums, and when she tried to lift the corner the paper skinned off against her thumb. That page was not drying back into usefulness.

She pressed it flat again anyway. Then she looked up at the cots, the bathroom line, the women spooning stew into foil, and understood the next arithmetic had changed. Not deposit. Not lease. Not how to stretch one rescue month into the next room. How many nights the church would keep them. Which papers had to stay readable. What story she would have to tell the school, the county, whoever came asking why five people from Little River were sleeping under choir robes. The old housing plan had drowned between the floor and the roof. This was the part after.

Tiana was looking at the nursing-school letter where it had slid from the pile. She did not touch it. She had seen it before in the trunk, but here under fluorescent lights it looked smaller, meaner, just one old white paper curling at the edges while stew smell and baby cries moved through the room around it.

"You should put that back in the sleeve," Tiana said quietly.

"I know."

"I'm just saying the fan's hitting this side harder."

Marcia nodded. The practical correction was her daughter's love language when things got too large to name. She accepted it in the spirit it was given.

Across the hall, a man argued into his phone about insurance deductibles while his mother snored open-mouthed on a cot. A little girl in a sequined church dress from some previous Sunday colored on the back of a funeral program with fierce concentration. Somebody's pot of curry goat opened in the kitchen and sent a hunger through the room so strong even full people turned their heads. Relief did not soften humiliation. It simply fed it and laid a blanket over it and asked if you were warm enough.

Marcia went to the line for food at last because not eating in church looked like pride, and pride ate worse than hunger. A young usher she recognized only by face, a boy with barber-fresh lines in his hair and one front tooth silvered from childhood, spooned rice onto her plate and looked carefully at her shoulder instead of at her eyes.

"You need anything else, Sister Campbell, they got toiletries by the office," he said.

"We have enough for tonight."

"All right."

He meant it kindly. She heard the care in the formality. She hated him a little anyway for being able to offer while she stood there receiving.

When she brought the plate back, Delroy had fallen asleep sitting up, the coffee cup empty between his feet. His head had dropped back against the cinderblock wall, mouth open, face older than she had wanted to admit even in anger. Micah was on his phone under his towel, one leg bouncing. Kerene had somehow made a donated oversized T-shirt look intentional and was helping an elderly woman on the next cot untangle a chain from her hair scarf while talking low and fast enough to keep the woman laughing. Tiana was still at the table with the papers, reading every line as if force of attention alone could flatten them dry.

Marcia set her plate down untouched for a moment and pressed the partner notebook open with both hands.

The fan lifted the corners of the wet pages. They kept trying to curl back into themselves. She flattened them again. If she let go, the pages would buckle. If she pressed too hard, they would tear. It was delicate, annoying work, the kind no disaster movie respected because there was nothing grand in it. Still it was the work the day had left her.

Above the murmur of the hall, Sister Eudora was somewhere in the kitchen scolding someone for putting wet towels on a polished pew. Deacon Lewis laughed at something too tired to be funny. A child asked for their mother in that lost, casual way children did when they assumed the answer would come. Marcia kept one hand on the notebook while the fan worried at the spread of papers.

By night the fellowship hall had become its own weather. The overhead fluorescents were dimmed but not shut off. Snoring, muttering, phone light on faces, the hiss of the industrial fan, toilets flushing without shame, coughs stitched from one end of the room to the other. At some point Tiana peeled the borrowed Bible off the housing printout, slid the page back into her softened folder, and carried both to her cot. Marcia sat awake in the plastic chair by the papers because she could not trust sleep with so much of their life laid out drying in public.

The ink kept drying lighter than it had been written. The pages kept holding the waterline.

So did she.


# Chapter 17

By morning the overhead lights over Tiana's cot were back at full buzz. The fellowship hall had already used up its night.

They had never fully gone dark, only dimmed enough to turn everybody's skin the color of old paper. Children were crying in three different corners with three different pitches. Somebody's phone kept spitting the same gospel hook from under a blanket because the owner was still asleep. The industrial fans church men had rolled in after the storm pushed warm air from one end of the room to the other and spread every smell instead of solving any of them: bleach, wet sneakers, mildew lifting out of donated towels, coffee gone bitter on the burner, the sweet rot of floodwater that had dried into clothes and would not quite leave.

Tiana sat on the edge of her cot with her knees braced around her document folder. The plastic sleeve had buckled at the corners where the water had gotten in. Her immunization record looked as if it had been steamed. The housing printout from UF had gone soft, dried, and then stiffened again with a faint tide line across the middle. She had flattened it overnight under a borrowed Bible and it still curled upward at the edge like it wanted to escape her hands.

`LATE CHECK-IN APPROVED THROUGH MONDAY 5:00 PM,` the email said. `Failure to arrive within this window may result in release of your housing assignment for Fall 2019.`

The phrase may result was the kind of language institutions used when they had already decided what they were going to do and wanted the sentence to look less like a blade.

Malik had texted at six-thirteen.

If we're doing it, be ready by ten. I got the car till tonight.

She had not answered. She had stared at the message until the little gray bubble beneath it stopped feeling like language and became only time. Ten o'clock meant pack what was left dry, get dressed in something that did not smell like church floor and ruined drywall, get to the parking lot, point the car north, and hold the day steady with both hands until Gainesville. What sickened her was not impossibility but the opposite. The trip did not ask for anything noble, only nerve and a borrowed car. It hurt because possible things could still be lost.

Across from her, Kerene was sitting cross-legged on the folding cot, tying a green scarf around her head with the care of somebody dressing for a better room than the one she had. She had cut the scarf from the lining of an old skirt and the satin still caught the light. Her mouth was bare of lipstick for once, which made her look younger and meaner.

"Miss Doreen say if we don't move these bags from under the cots she's going to start throwing things in black garbage sacks," she said, not looking up. "That woman loves a rule the way some people love a man."

"Then move them," Tiana said.

"I'm moving them. I'm also cursing her privately. Both things can happen."

Kerene bent to drag her duffel closer with her foot. Even half asleep she made room like a person who had been temporary in too many places to trust anything left unattended.

Near the coffee urns, Miss Doreen was indeed moving down the aisle with her clipboard. She wore white church shoes with thick orthopedic soles and a cloud of White Diamonds that reached people before she did. A peppermint clicked from one side of her mouth to the other while she checked names and cot numbers and reminded everybody, again, that the side classroom was for mothers with babies and not for "private visiting." The shelter had been open only three nights and already the rules had multiplied like damp.

Tiana was looking for a moment to call housing without the whole hall hearing the shape of her leaving when Micah's phone buzzed hard enough to rattle against the metal chair beside his cot.

He had his hood up though the room was hot. The bruise from the stop two weeks ago had gone from plum to a faint ugly yellow under his cheekbone, but it was still there if you looked. He dragged the phone into his hand, squinted, and then laughed in a way that wasn't attached to anything funny.

"What?" Kerene asked.

"Nothing." He was already typing. "Y'all internet-famous now. Congratulations."

Tiana looked up. Micah held the phone too low and too close to his body, which was how she knew he wanted somebody to ask.

"Micah."

"It's stupid."

He said it too fast. She stood and crossed the narrow strip between the cots before he could tuck the screen away.

The photo was a screenshot from the church's own Facebook Live. She recognized the fellowship hall banner in the back and the metal serving table in front of it. Somebody had been streaming donations the night before, narrating over boxes of canned goods and diaper packs and bottled water, and in the background Micah was caught in a moment that had looked ordinary until it became evidence. He was bent over the table, reaching for a pack of socks, his T-shirt rucked up at the back, one knee showing through his basketball shorts, his face tired and half turned so the bruise caught the light. On the original church stream he would probably have been nobody in particular, just another body in need. On the screenshot somebody had circled him in red and written `LIL MIKE AT HURRICANE HILTON` across the top. The comments below were already filling.

Damn that's him fr

Boy in line for church drawers

Tell Dre come get his mans

Tiana felt the top of her scalp go cold. The room got smaller around the phone.

"Who sent that?"

"Everybody," Micah said. His mouth twisted. "Whole city doing journalism now."

He tried to take the phone back and she let him. Making him wrestle for it would only give the shame more shape.

Delroy looked over from the coffee line. He had slept in yesterday's jeans and a white undershirt gone gray at the collar, and he was shaving off three days of beard with a disposable razor and a paper cup of water because he refused to let the shelter see him looking like it had won. "What happened?"

"Nothing happened," Micah said.

"That don't sound like nothing."

Micah gave him the bright, ugly grin he used when he wanted to get cruel before somebody else could. "You want me to airdrop it to you, Daddy? Probably your church friend stream it."

Delroy crossed the room then, coffee forgotten. Tiana turned the screen toward him before Micah could stop her. Delroy's eyes took in the image, the caption, the comments. She saw the exact second his face stopped being offended on principle and became something more private. Embarrassment came to him before anger. It always did.

"Which fool post this?"

"I don't know." Micah shoved the phone into his pocket. "And I don't care."

"You care."

"Man, everybody got phones. What you want me to do, fight Facebook?"

Miss Doreen glanced over at the rise in their voices. Nearby cots had already gone a little quieter. The shelter had a way of turning every family argument into community theater without anybody admitting they were watching.

Tiana said, low, "Take it down if it's on your story."

"It ain't my story."

"Then report it."

He laughed again, meaner this time. "Yeah. Instagram going to save me. Good idea."

It would have been almost manageable if he had cursed or shouted. What scared her was how light he sounded, as if the humiliation had filed him to a meaner point instead of sinking in. That lightness was his worst look. It meant he was already half gone.

Marcia came back from the women's bathroom carrying a plastic caddy with soap, a comb, and the edge of her patience showing plain. "Why everybody making noise so early?"

"Nothing," Micah said.

"A picture online," Kerene said at the same time.

Marcia looked from one face to the next and understood enough without seeing the screen. Her mouth hardened. "Who took it?"

"Somebody from church Live," Tiana said.

"Jesus." Marcia set the caddy down so hard the comb jumped. "Blessing people for an audience."

Micah gave another little shrug, the performed kind. "I said it's fine."

"It isn't fine," Tiana said.

"Then don't look at it."

He snatched his hoodie from the cot and pulled it on. Marcia said his name in warning. He had already started walking.

"Where you going?" she asked.

"Outside."

"For what?"

"Air."

"You get ten minutes," Miss Doreen called from the aisle, hearing only the exit and not the reason for it. "Minors don't just roam. We already talked about that."

Micah saluted her without turning around. "Yes, ma'am."

The whole hall heard the insolence. A few people laughed. He kept moving.

Tiana watched the back of his neck disappear through the fellowship-hall doors. She almost followed him then, but instead she looked down at the email still open on her phone. Ten o'clock meant Malik and the car; five p.m. meant the late check-in window closing. She had the sudden ugly thought that came before she could stop it: of course it had happened today. Of course the family emergency had found the one clean square of time with her name on it and put its heel there.

She hated herself a little for thinking it and did not have time to hate herself properly.

Marcia picked up the caddy again. "Let him cool off."

Delroy was still looking at the doorway. "No."

Marcia turned to him. "No what?"

"No let him cool off. That boy shame-hot right now. Shame make people stupid."

"He's going to the parking lot."

"Maybe." Delroy rubbed at the bad shoulder without seeming to notice he had done it. "Maybe not."

Tiana checked the time. It was seven twenty-six. Her phone buzzed once in her hand with another message from Malik.

Need me there early?

She looked at the words and then at the door Micah had just gone through. The shelter's air pressed warm and used against her face. Somebody's toddler had started wailing because the pancake mix had run out. Miss Doreen was scolding a man for trying to charge two phones at once. Kerene was cursing softly while wrestling a zipper that had rusted with floodwater. The whole hall breathed against itself. There was no corner in it where a girl could decide her future without an audience.

Twenty minutes later Micah still had not come back.

Delroy did not posture about it. He did not announce himself head of anything. He put on his work boots, asked what Micah had on besides the hoodie, checked the pockets of the chair for the boy's charger, looked once at the cot, and said, "Come."

Marcia frowned. "Where?"

"Out."

"Tiana has to make calls."

"I know what she has." He looked at Tiana then, directly, and the look hurt because it meant he had not forgotten. "I also know if we wait till dark and have to bring police in it gets worse."

Marcia's jaw moved. She hated him most when he was right in a way that cost her.

"I'll stay here," she said. "If he comes back, I'll call."

Tiana's folder was still on the cot. The plastic sleeve caught the fluorescent light like a wink. Malik's message sat unanswered. If she walked out now, the hours would start bleeding. If she stayed, Micah would be out there with everybody in Miami laughing at his need and no one from his own house looking for him yet.

She picked up the folder, shoved it into her tote, and followed her father into the morning.

Outside, the air hit with the wet heaviness of water that had left but not forgiven. The church parking lot was crowded with donated pallets, black contractor bags, and the cars of people who had come to help without having to stay. The storm debris on the curb looked almost festive from a distance until she got close enough to see ruined mattresses, swollen particleboard, and a child's pink rain boot lying by itself in a gutter full of brown leaves.

Delroy headed east first, toward the bus stop and the strip of stores Micah haunted when he wanted to be visible without being home. He walked faster than Tiana expected for a man carrying that shoulder, but not so fast that it looked heroic. The pace only proved he had not come along to supervise.

"He has cash on him?" he asked.

"Maybe twenty. Maybe less."

"Phone?"

"Yeah."

"He answer you?"

She called. It rang until voicemail. The voicemail greeting was one of Micah's old joke ones, done in a fake radio voice: You reached a very important person. He can't come to the phone because capitalism is a disease. Leave something worth hearing.

Delroy almost smiled at that, then didn't.

At the bus stop a man in paint-splattered Crocs said he had seen a boy in a dark hoodie headed south half an hour ago, maybe. At the corner store the cashier said Micah had come in, asked for the Wi-Fi password, and left without buying anything. Delroy thanked everybody. He had a way of pitching his voice half an inch lower with strangers, not submissive exactly, but careful. Tiana saw the effort it took him and hated that she saw it.

By eight-thirty they had checked the basketball court behind the apartment complex on Sixty-Second, the alley where Micah sometimes got haircuts for cash, and Dre's building. Dre himself answered the door in basketball shorts and one sock, his hair flattened on one side. He had a school lanyard still wrapped around his wrist from some habit he never broke.

"He ain't here," he said before Tiana asked. "I sent him the screenshot 'cause I figured somebody was gonna anyway. I didn't post that shit."

"Who did?" Delroy asked.

Dre shrugged. "Some church lady streamed supplies and then people screen-recorded it. Couple boys from school got it. Probably gone by lunch once somebody else get embarrassed."

"That's supposed to help?" Tiana said.

He looked properly ashamed then, which made him younger. "I was just letting him know."

Delroy said, "If he call you, you tell him home. Not tomorrow. Home."

Dre nodded. "Yes, sir."

They kept moving.

The city had that washed-out brightness storms left behind, everything too clean on top and rotting underneath. Mangos split open in the gutters. Tree limbs leaned against chain-link fences like broken furniture. At an intersection a city truck was feeding branches into a chipper that screamed loud enough to empty thought out of Tiana's head for one full block. Her phone buzzed in her pocket twice, three times, and she let it. She knew the pattern by feel now: Malik, then an unfamiliar Gainesville number, then Malik again.

At nine-fifteen Delroy said, "Answer the boy."

"I know it's him."

"Then answer."

"I can't talk right now."

"You can talk and walk."

She stopped under the shadow of a shuttered botanica and looked at him. "Please don't do this like you know how to manage my schedule."

His mouth tightened. He took the rebuke without pretending otherwise. "Fair."

Then, after a second: "Still answer him."

She did, mostly to stop hearing her father say his name like he belonged in the conversation.

"Where are you?" Malik asked without greeting. The line was full of traffic noise and whatever engine he was sitting near.

"Little River."

"That narrows it down about zero."

"We're looking for Micah."

There was a beat. His voice flattened, which was how it did concern. "He left the shelter?"

"After some bullshit picture went around."

"You find him?"

"Not yet."

"What time do you need to leave if we're still going?"

She looked at the street, at a puddle holding the church roof upside down, at her father pretending not to listen while listening to every word. "I don't know."

"Ti."

"I said I don't know."

He let out air through his nose. "I'm by my aunt's job right now. I can hold the car till maybe eleven before it turns into me owing people."

"Okay."

"That isn't an answer."

"My brother's missing."

"I heard that. I'm not saying leave him on the sidewalk. I'm asking what you want me to do."

She opened her mouth to say, Keep the car till five. Give me one impossible afternoon where Micah comes back and Gainesville does not close. The ask died there. She could hear too clearly what it would cost him: a borrowed favor, more gas, more waiting, his whole day parked beside her family's latest disaster.

"I'll call you," she said, and hung up before he could make her more exact.

Delroy started walking again. "He sound like a decent youth."

"Please."

"Just saying."

She almost laughed at the absurdity of being furious and embarrassed in the same breath. He had that effect on her. "You don't get to be casual with him like you're not the reason half of this exists."

"Half?" He gave her a crooked glance. "You are generous today."

It was the kind of answer he would once have turned into a whole performance. This time he left it there. They crossed under the Metrorail overpass where the shade smelled like urine and wet concrete and old cigarettes. A woman with silver lipstick was selling bags of boiled peanuts from a cooler and fanning herself with a church bulletin. Somewhere nearby a radio was playing dancehall through blown speakers.

By ten-thirty the church had called twice. Micah was still not back. Marcia's first voicemail was all control: Call me the minute you hear anything. The second had something thinner in it. Tiana did not play that one a second time.

They checked the canal path, the field behind Micah's school, the dock road where Dre had once taken him for day cash. The places gave them pieces and not the boy. Somebody had seen him on a bench. Somebody had seen a hoodie on a bus. Somebody said a kid fitting that description had gone behind the old strip mall with his head down. The search turned into weather: everywhere around them, nowhere solid enough to hold.

At eleven-oh-two the Gainesville number called again. She answered because not answering had started to feel childish, and because Delroy was no longer pretending not to notice.

"This is Jasmine with UF Housing," said a woman whose voice had the bright sanded-down roughness of somebody talking through a lunch break. Tiana could hear keyboards behind her, another phone ringing, somebody laughing too close to the receiver. "I'm following up on your approved late check-in. We still need confirmation that you'll arrive by five today in order to hold the assignment."

Tiana had to step away from her father to hear. She turned toward a chain-link fence with bougainvillea strangling through it in magenta knots.

"I'm trying to make arrangements," she said.

"Okay. I do need to be very clear that if you do not physically check in by five p.m. today, we will release the space."

The word release was another soft one for something already dead.

"If that happens," Tiana said, hating the shake in her own voice, "what happens to my enrollment?"

"That question would go through admissions and financial aid," Jasmine said. The answer was neither cruel nor kind; it had the office-clean efficiency of a limit she had probably repeated all day. "I can note the file that you were affected by storm displacement, but I can't guarantee housing after today and I can't activate your room without arrival."

Tiana pressed her thumbnail into the soft plastic edge of the folder through her tote until it bent. "Okay."

"Would you like me to hold while you confirm transportation?"

The question was so ordinary it almost made her laugh.

"No," Tiana said. "Thank you."

When she hung up, Delroy was watching her from the curb.

"Five o'clock?" he said.

She nodded.

He looked up the street as if he might find extra hours hanging from a power line. "We keep moving."

He did not offer a lecture or some sudden burst of paternal permission. He only said it, and that usefulness made her angrier than either speech would have.

She opened the map one more time. If she called Malik that minute, if the traffic gave her one undeserved break, she could maybe still scrape into Gainesville by five. She locked the phone instead and stepped off the curb beside her father. If Micah was still out here, she was not leaving him to be found by strangers.

Around noon they bought two cold waters from a gas station and drank them standing up. Tiana could feel the day being eaten. Each time she opened the map north to Gainesville the blue route was longer than the last time. First the map said five hours. Then it said five hours eleven minutes. Then it said five hours twenty-three because of standing water near Ocala or construction or some other indifferent obstacle the phone explained in clean colors.

At twelve-forty Malik texted:

Need a real answer.

Ten minutes later he sent another text:

If you want the ride, say that. If not, say that too.

She put the phone back in her pocket. The thing she wanted was not a ride and not exactly school either. It was a life that did not demand an oath every time she tried to touch it.

By one-thirty Delroy had started moving with the stubborn limp he got when pain was chewing at him and he wanted nobody to see teeth marks. They were back near the old neighborhood almost without planning to be, passing the stucco house with the barred windows, the mechanic yard, and the corner where the church van used to pick up old people on Sundays. Tiana realized before he said it where they were going next.

"He wouldn't go back there," she said.

Delroy kept his eyes on the sidewalk. "If his shame bad enough? He might."

"Why?"

"Because there nobody can reveal him to himself. Place already know."

He had not come closer all day to speaking the real thing.

The main house looked almost cheerful in the sharp afternoon sun, the same cracked stucco painted a pale color that passed for clean from the street. The garage apartment behind it was another matter. The flood had left a water line halfway up the outside wall, a brown necklace around the building. Someone had nailed plywood over the side window. The landlord had chained the side gate but not well. Delroy worked the chain loose with his good hand and the practiced shamelessness of a man who had already paid for the right to trespass on what used to be his own life.

The yard hit Tiana low in the body. Every warped bucket and busted board still knew the outline of their old days, and seeing it again made Gainesville feel indecently far away, like a country she had only rented in her head.

"Micah," Tiana called as soon as they stepped into the side yard.

No one answered. The place smelled like wet insulation and sour mud baked by sun.

Then she heard music, low and tinny, somewhere behind the ruined washing machine that had been dragged near the fence after the storm. It wasn't even a full song, only the bass bleeding through a phone speaker turned down too far.

Relief flashed through her first, hot enough to make her dizzy. Anger crowded in right behind it. He had let them walk the neighborhood for hours while he sat close enough to answer.

Micah was sitting on an overturned bucket behind the machine with his hood up and his knees spread, elbows on thighs, staring at his deadened reflection in a strip of black water caught in a broken plastic bin. He had taken off one shoe and was scraping mud from the sole with a house key. Beside him lay a garbage bag with three things in it: a pair of sneakers he must have dug out, a damp PS4 controller, and the blue towel he had slept with as a baby and still refused to admit he had kept.

The towel caved something in her chest. He had dragged that thing by one corner when he was little, terry cloth dark at the mouth from sleep and spit. Seeing it in the bag beside the controller and the mud-stiff shoes made her relief feel mean.

Tiana stopped.

So did Delroy.

Micah didn't look surprised. He only looked caught. "Damn," he said. "Y'all got GPS on me now?"

"You want a joke," Tiana said. "Make one later."

He went back to scraping at the shoe. "I wasn't lost."

"Then answer your phone."

"Didn't feel like hearing everybody panic."

"Everybody panic because you disappeared."

"I went outside."

"For six hours?"

He threw the key harder than he meant to. It clinked off the washing machine and fell into the mud. "Why you yelling like I got kidnapped?"

Delroy moved first, not toward Micah's face but toward the fallen key. He bent badly, jaw tight, and picked it up with his left hand. The sight made Tiana's throat tighten. His shoulder had been bad for weeks, and now he was paying for Micah's shame with a body that barely had spare parts left. Then he lowered himself onto the cracked concrete block beside the bucket with a care that made her aware of every year in him and every minute still burning down in her pocket. He handed Micah the key back.

"You done?" Delroy asked.

Micah stared ahead. "Done what."

"Done hiding."

"I wasn't hiding."

"All right. Done sitting in stink water then."

Micah's mouth twitched despite himself. Tiana hated that it worked and loved it too.

"That picture got around school?" Delroy asked.

Micah didn't answer.

"School and everybody else," Tiana said.

Micah shot her a look sharp enough to skin.

Delroy nodded once, like he had expected no better from the world. "Church people don't know how to help quiet."

"Don't start," Micah said.

"I wasn't starting."

"Then don't make it a speech."

Delroy spread his hands. "Who making speech? I ask a question."

Micah rubbed both palms over his face, hard, as if he could scrape off the morning. When he spoke again the swagger had thinned. "I didn't want to be there with everybody looking at me like some before-and-after picture. Like if they drop enough canned beans on a table they get to own my face."

The admission came out bare and ugly, no joke wrapped around it, which was probably why she believed it at once.

Tiana felt something inside her shift against something meaner. She could still see the comments. She could also see the blue route to Gainesville shrinking toward impossible in her mind like a muscle going into spasm.

"So you came here?" she said.

Micah looked around the wrecked yard. "Yeah."

"Why."

He shrugged. "This place already knows we're broke."

The sentence was exact enough to make any softer version a lie.

Delroy's eyes closed for a second. When he opened them he did not try to tell Micah not to care. He did not tell him to man up or laugh it off or remember other people had it worse. For once he did not charm the truth smaller.

"I hear you," he said. "Still, you can't run everybody ragged because some little bastards got phones."

"I didn't ask y'all to come."

"No," Delroy said. "You made us."

Micah's face folded then straightened before it could become anything else. Tiana saw the child in him only in flashes now. Fifteen was mostly angles and performance with the boy buried inside like contraband.

Her phone buzzed.

One glance was enough. `UF Housing. Final reminder: approved late check-in expires at 5:00 PM today.`

The time at the top of the screen read 3:57.

If Malik left the church that minute, if traffic broke in their favor, if nothing else happened, she could maybe get to Gainesville after nine. The thought did not even feel close enough to ache. It felt mathematical, like a sum with too much missing from one side.

Micah saw her look at the phone. "That's school?"

She said nothing.

He looked away first. "Damn."

Delroy pushed himself up from the block and winced only after he was already standing. "Come. Both of you."

Micah stayed where he was another second, looking at the garbage bag. Delroy picked it up before the boy could decide he was too old for help. He hefted it with his good hand and motioned toward the gate.

"Bring the shoes if you want. Leave the mold."

It was such a fatherly sentence, so ordinary and unheroic, that Tiana felt tears sting behind her eyes out of sheer bad timing. She swallowed them. The yard was too ugly for crying. Everything would have smelled worse.

They walked back toward the church together. Micah dragged his feet at first, then not at all. Delroy carried the bag. Tiana called Malik and got straight to voicemail.

"It's me," she said. "We found him."

She stopped there, because the next sentence refused to pick a shape.

By the time they got back to the fellowship hall, Miss Doreen had switched from authority to sympathy and was pressing sweet tea into Marcia's hand. Marcia set the cup down untouched when she saw Micah. She did not run to him. She held herself rigid for one second, which was worse, and then gripped the back of his neck hard enough to make the point before pulling him against her.

"You think I don't have enough to bury me?" she said into his hair.

"Sorry," he muttered.

"Sorry can't be the only thing you know how to say after."

Kerene, relief showing as irritation, took the garbage bag from Delroy and pulled out the blue baby towel without comment. She shook it once, smelled it, made a face, and set it aside to dry near the fan. No one mentioned that Micah had gone back for it.

Tiana had just enough time to wash the dirt from her hands in the church bathroom before Malik texted that he was outside.

The parking lot was all late light and broken asphalt and generator noise. Malik leaned against a gray Altima with his arms folded, one ankle hooked over the other. He had on a faded black T-shirt and jeans, and the first thing she noticed, absurdly, was that he had a clean duffel in the back seat and a case of bottled water on the floorboard like he had packed for both rescue and logistics. There was a crescent-shaped grease stain high on one thigh where he must have brushed against somebody's engine or some machine earlier in the day. He worried the edge of a folded gas receipt between thumb and forefinger while he waited, rolling it into a tight pale cord and flattening it again.

"You found him," he said.

"Yeah."

"He okay?"

"Embarrassed. Which for Micah is basically a medical event."

That got the smallest breath of a laugh out of him, then nothing. He looked tired in the plain way people did after a day that had actually cost them something.

"What time is it?" she asked, though she knew.

"Five twenty-two."

The time lodged under her ribs, heavy and useless as a brick.

He opened the passenger door anyway. Cool air spilled out, dry and unreal after the fellowship hall and the streets. "Get in for a second."

She did. The seat was cleaner than anything she had touched in days. She almost wanted to sleep there on principle.

Malik shut the door and came around to the driver's side but didn't start the car. The engine ticked softly under the hood. From inside the church parking lot the fellowship hall looked almost peaceful. You could not see the cots from here. Only the brick wall, the side door, the women from the kitchen carrying foil trays in and out.

"I called you three times," he said.

"I know."

"You could've texted one sentence."

"I know."

He nodded, looking through the windshield. "All right."

That all right was worse than anger. Anger at least made a scene you could answer.

She pulled out her phone. The email had come in at 5:03.

`Because you did not complete check-in by the approved deadline, your Fall housing assignment has been cancelled. Please contact Admissions and Financial Aid regarding your enrollment options.`

The cancellation email had stopped hiding behind soft verbs and maybes. The knife was clean now.

She read it once, then again. The words did not improve under attention.

"It's gone," she said.

Malik waited.

"The room. The move-in. The whole..." She gave up before the sentence made her sound stupid. "It's gone."

He looked over then, really looked, and the tenderness in his face made everything worse because it was not pity. Pity she could have fought.

"Let me see."

She handed him the phone. He read the email and gave it back.

"I'm sorry."

She laughed once under her breath, harsh and airless. "I hate that phrase."

"I know."

"Everybody keeps being sorry and nothing un-happens."

"Also true."

The car filled with silence. Outside, somebody wheeled a trash can across the lot, the loose lid slamming against the side in dull metal beats.

Malik said, "What do you want to do now?"

She turned on him too fast. "Now?"

"Yeah. Now."

"Nothing. I want this morning to not be this morning."

"Can't do that."

"Clearly."
